Now see whether he did not fittingly, in that part, receive so fitting a sign.

XIII. IF THE JUDGMENT OF GOD IS JUST, WHY WERE THE INFANTS IN SODOM BURNED TOGETHER WITH THEIR PARENTS?

So that the exceedingly impious crime of the Sodomites might be observed, their sin reached even to the killing of their own children, that no trace should remain of their origin. Was it not provided in their case that, living long, they should not follow the examples of their fathers? For those slain in another's cause are freed from Gehenna. For the parents are guilty both for themselves and for these, because, in order that their works might be blotted out, all their offspring was erased. For if children are gladdened by the good deeds of their parents and are commended by their parents' privilege, so also concerning the death which is inflicted for the parents' evils they cannot complain, so that, just as they are sharers in the gain, they may be so also in the loss. Likewise in Egypt too the children were punished for the harms done by their fathers, but so that it might avail toward their correction, so that—because for other reasons they had been unwilling to believe and venerate the Lord who was in Moses—being terrified by this plague they might confess with satisfaction, lest they should feel him more grievously angered.

This death of the children, therefore, is the crime of the fathers. For in whatever manner the children rendered up their owed spirit, being about to be accusers of their parents, because they could not overcome their treachery even by the blood of their children, therefore not for the future but for the present time the children found the evils of their parents. And is it not some kind of benefit to be without glory, yet also without guilt? For if certain men, set in their last hours, strive with prayers to come to the faith of the truth, so that, if not a crown, they may at least merit pardon, how will any be able to complain on this account—those with whom it was so dealt that they had neither anything for which to pray to be forgiven, nor were held exalted who had labored not at all? Compare now the torment of a single moment to the punishment of a long time; consider also that many labor and, because they cannot persevere, not only lose the fruit of their labor but are also punished with penalty; add too that it is difficult for those born of evil men and raised or living among the most wicked to temper their mind to following the discipline of God: see therefore whether it was not rather well dealt with in the case of the children of the wicked.

XIIII. HOW IS IT THAT GOD, WHO IS PROCLAIMED JUST, PROMISED THAT HE WOULD REPAY THE SINS OF THE FATHERS UPON THE CHILDREN UNTO THE THIRD AND FOURTH GENERATION?

Whoever doubts that the Lord does or says nothing unjustly is mad. And so, if the words of the Lord are not suppressed, but all the things that pertain to the same question are set forth, what seems obscure will shine clear, and what is thought to be by no means just will be reckoned pious. For whoever suppresses the words of the question is either unskilled or an evader, one who is more devoted to false accusation than to teaching. Furthermore, God did indeed promise that he would repay the sins of the fathers upon the children—but upon those who hate him, that is, those who, engaged in their fathers' evils, serve idols, just as their fathers did. For that man is an enemy of God who claims the honor of his name for a creature. For just as the children of the righteous, being themselves righteous, ennoble in themselves the lineage of righteousness, while they are exalted by both their fathers' righteousness and their own, so too the children of the most wicked, being themselves most wicked, make an increase of wickedness, because they are both the children of evil men and have modeled their wickedness to the ruin of many.

God therefore seems to have spoken with terror, but it is true, and reason bears this out. For who would not execrate an evil man and the son of an evil man with a double kind of curse, just as he judges a good man and the son of a good man worthy to be honored with a double merit? And for this reason he promised to repay unto the third and fourth generation, so that, if after an evil father the son does not follow the father's faithlessness, yet the grandson, proving an imitator of his grandfather in evils, may know that he will not be immune from the severity of the sentence; or if perhaps the grandson too, following not his grandfather's footsteps but his father's, loves the covenant of God, the great-grandson may not be unaware that the sentence reaches even to him, if he be an emulator of his great-grandfather's faithlessness.

By this manifestation of the law God wished to deter the impious fathers, so that, recognizing how much evil the pursuit of idolatry produced, they might be recalled—even by affection for their children—to reverence for the Creator, or that the children, fearing lest they incur the sins of the fathers, might obey the law of God. But they, giving their attention to wickedness, began to interpret this otherwise, saying: The fathers ate a sour grape and the children's teeth were set on edge—so that, secure about impunity, because the sins would be repaid to their children, they themselves nonetheless might have piety neither of spirit nor of flesh—hence it is said to them: not "the son shall die for the father" nor "the father for the son," but: the soul that sins, it shall itself die—and that they might learn that it would not go unpunished for them, but that the sins of the fathers are repaid to the children in such a way that, because in following the impiety of their parents they reproduced the evils which ought to have been blotted out, they are punished the more harshly.

After God showed what evil idolatry produces, he immediately added what good things follow those who love him, saying: and showing mercy unto thousands of thousands, to those who love me and keep my commandments, so that he might repay the good of a father not merely down to the great-grandson, but unto thousands of thousands. For instance, if someone from the seed of David loves the Lord — who, it is agreed, certainly lived a thousand years and more before — to this man the mercy of God is added, so that in his necessity he may deserve to receive mercy for two contributing reasons: because he himself is a worshipper of God and because he is from the seed of one who loved God. And in this the clemency of God is praiseworthy, that upon those who hold him in hatred he repays down to the third and fourth generation and not unto thousands of thousands, so that if the evil be drawn out into the fifth generation, from there it may begin again to have a head, so that from that point it may be counted down to the third and fourth generation.

But someone will say: 'it seems just that more should be repaid to the originator than to the imitator.' Upon the originator a fitting punishment is imposed; but that he says more is repaid to the sons is a penalty of the fathers. And if you inquire, by the coming of the law sinners become more guilty, as were both Lamech and those after Lamech. To some, however, it seems that the sins of the fathers were thus repaid upon the sons in that they were led into captivity on account of the fathers and remained there down to the fourth generation. But if this is true, then God repaid the sins of the fathers not only upon those who hold him in hatred, but also upon those who love his name, because both Daniel was carried off and the three young men and Baruch and Ezekiel, and Ezra was born there. Clearly, then, it appears that what we said above fits the proposed question.

XV. SINCE THE LAW PROCLAIMS THE JUST MAN, AND HE IS TRULY JUST WHO HAS FULL JUSTICE, YET SOLOMON, ON THE CONTRARY, SAYS: 'DO NOT BE JUST OVERMUCH.'

Excessive justice runs into sin, but tempered justice makes men perfect. For he is not without malice who is just overmuch, because if you were to respond to sinners for every single thing, there will be no lack of where you may sin. In short, God's justice is tempered. For he sometimes forgives sinners, sometimes is angry, sometimes repays not as much as they deserve; for he bears with them, so that there may be those who make progress. For the law, since it cannot soften itself, must be mitigated by us, so that it may be able to benefit those who act under it. This man, therefore, who is an imitator of God, is not just overmuch.

XVI. WHY WAS THE ANGEL, WHO WISHED TO KILL MOSES ON THE WAY, APPEASED BY THE CIRCUMCISION OF THE INFANT?

What Moses had done without deliberation, the appearance of the threatening angel corrected. And so, seeing what had been omitted through negligence, he ceased from his anger. For this reason, then, Moses saw the angel so hostile toward him and ready for vengeance, because the fault of an exalted man is a grave sin. For the more his person is regarded with esteem, the more even a slight offense of his is a most grievous sin. And so he was proceeding to the children of Israel, sent by the God of Abraham, yet he did not bear the sign of the righteousness of Abraham, in which he knew the Jews gloried—so that he would either be called a deceiver himself, or one not sent by the same God who had chosen Abraham. For while dwelling in Midian he had by no means circumcised his sons, whom he either ought not to have led with him to Egypt—he who was going to lead the others out from there—or else certainly ought to have taken along only when circumcised, on account of the things we have said above.

Therefore Zipporah, the wife of Moses—for Scripture speaks in a compressed manner—not being ignorant of the reason for which the angel seemed hostile to her husband, took it and circumcised her son, and the angel withdrew. For the anger was appeased by the same reason by which it had also been kindled. For Scripture speaks certain things in a compressed manner, which it wishes to be understood by implication from the reason set forth, just as that too which the Savior says, among other things, to the Jews: Why do you also transgress the commandment of God, that you may establish your tradition? For God said: 'Honor father and mother,' and: 'He who speaks evil of father or mother, let him die the death.' But you say: "Whosoever shall say to father or mother: 'Whatever gift there may be from me shall profit you,' and he has not honored his father or his mother." Without doubt the Savior said this by way of compression, so that it might be understood by implication that the priests of the Jews, while they were devoted to avarice, had spurned the law of God; for by an impious conspiracy they gave counsel to the sons that, though speaking evil of father or mother, they should offer whatever they had as if it were a gift to God, through which the sentence pronounced against those who curse would be dissolved, whence the sons, made secure, despised their parents against the commandment of God. So too Moses, when he saw the angel hostile to him, understanding the cause, showed his wife what ought to be done, so that the angel, who was seeking to kill him, might withdraw from him. This is understood by implication in this case, just as that too which we said above concerning the priests.

XVII. HOW IS IT THAT HE CALLS ACCURSED THOSE WHO HAVE NOT LEFT SEED IN ISRAEL, BUT THROUGH ISAIAH SAYS THAT IT IS NO HINDRANCE TO EUNUCHS THAT THEY CANNOT BEGET?

Since it had begun to be understood otherwise than had been intended, for that reason the prophet Isaiah showed afterward in what sense it had been said. For since this had been laid down on account of the offense against God's judgment, because those who were forsaking God were being struck down in this, so that, though they married wives, they might not leave offspring, human error so seized upon this that men supposed people to be guilty before God if any either were unwilling to take wives, or, having taken them, could not — as is usual — beget children, as though God sought nothing other than offspring, just as they are reproached through the prophet Malachi. Isaiah therefore, in order to console those whom a wrong understanding had saddened, taught that it does no harm if anyone either cannot have children or is unwilling, provided only that he keep the law of God. For the curse pertained to those who, though they could and would, were barren by the divine judgment standing in the way, so that they might not have the fruit of God's creation which they despised — so that when they saw either that they could not beget, or that they in no way possessed those they had begotten, they might recognize that it was the wrath of an indignant God and, being converted, might make satisfaction.

For holy men too, when they were desirous of children and did not obtain this, judged that their sins stood in the way, and grieved, being ignorant that it was of God's providence, for whom they were being kept. And so, by God's provision, both Anna conceived and bore Samuel, and Elizabeth John, and Manoah's wife Samson. Yet the spiritual understanding in this matter is this: that those are held cursed who shall not have left offspring that may see the Lord — that is, 'who shall not have instructed either a son or a servant or a neighbor to teach the fear of God upon the earth.'

XVIII. WHY, WHEN SAUL SINNED, DID HE ASK THAT PRAYER BE MADE FOR HIM THAT HE MIGHT BE FORGIVEN, AND COULD NOT OBTAIN IT, WHEREAS DAVID, WHEN HE SINNED, ASKED AND OBTAINED PARDON?

It is not fitting that God's judgment be called into question; for human weakness and inexperience ought to instruct itself by God's judgment in that which it does not understand, so that from God's verdict it may perceive the truth and that nothing else ought to have been done than what it sees the Lord to have done, whom it knows to be no respecter of persons. For if David's prayer was accepted and Saul's rejected, nothing must be thought to have been done by the Lord contrary to right. For knowing with what mind each of them had spoken, He accepted the petition of him whom He saw asking pardon with a troubled heart; but that other He disregarded, because grief for penitence had not touched his soul, since God is a hearer of the heart rather than of the voice. Hence it is said: 'Man looks upon the face, but God upon the heart.' Therefore we too are deceived, because pretense in words and countenance circumvents us; for what is in the heart, we cannot see.

For which reason it behooves us to follow God's judgment, who examines individuals according to the heart, in which lies each person's purpose. This too we find that the Savior did. For He did not receive the scribe who offered himself, but He called Levi, who was sitting at the tax-office, because the heart did not follow the scribe's words, whereas Levi, though silent, had in his heart what the scribe had in his words. But Jesus, who according to what is written knew what was in man, chose Levi. Moreover David did not wish another to be humbled on his behalf as Saul did, but he himself as a suppliant with a troubled heart prayed for pardon.

XVIIII. WHETHER ADAM, WHEN HE WAS MADE, HAD AN IMMORTAL OR A MORTAL BODY. (TO BE INVESTIGATED.)

God made man in such a way that, as long as he did not sin, he would flourish in immortality, so that he himself might be the author to himself either of life or of death: that, by keeping himself from sin, he might rejoice that by his own effort he was immortal, but that, if he became negligent, he might impute it to himself, because he had begun to be mortal. For as long as he continued in the Creator's law, he was worthy to eat of the tree of life, so that he could not die. For the body was not of such a kind that it seemed impossible for it to be dissolved, but the taste of the tree of life held back the corruption of the body. Finally, even after sin he could have remained indissoluble, if only he had been permitted to eat of the tree of life. For how did he have an immortal body, which was sustained by food? For an immortal being has need neither of food nor of drink. For food supplied strength, but the tree of life, in the manner of a medicine, warded off all corruption. For thus it was to man as it were an impregnable wall.

XX. WHAT IS THE MEANING OF THE SAYING: 'MAN ATE THE BREAD OF ANGELS', SINCE ANGELS HAVE NO NEED OF FOOD, INASMUCH AS THEY ARE SIMPLE IN NATURE AND FLOURISH IN SPIRITUAL POWER?

The bread which he calls the bread of angels is manna. Now manna is interpreted, 'What is this?' For when the children of Israel saw something white, like coriander seed, falling upon the ground, they said to one another: What is this? — which in the Hebrew tongue is said 'manni.' This bread, then, or food, was not created by the law of the world through a mixture of the elements, but came from above, provided by a spiritual plan. Nor indeed was it kept in the regions above, because it was created for a moment by the power of God for the nourishing of bodies. But it was called the bread of angels because it was created by that power by which the angelic nature subsists and lives. For without God there is life to none. Through the fact, then, that it is said to have come from heaven, it was called the bread of angels. For in order to record how great and of what kind are the benefits of God toward man, he spoke in this way, so that men, coming to know themselves, might give thanks to God, who governs his servants with such great care that he provides for them, while they are still placed in the flesh, if it be necessary, these things by which the angels live.

The apostle said among other things: and the rock was Christ, so that, because water flowed from the rock by the power of Christ, the rock was called Christ. In like manner, because the manna came from heaven by his power, the manna, that is the bread, is called Christ, so that what was then done may be reckoned to have been a figure of that thing which is now offered in the Church, so that the men — that is, the Jews — ate the bread of the Christian peoples, whom Scripture signified by 'angels.' And because the grace is now greater than it was among the prophets, and liberty was given through Christ, because where the Spirit of the Lord is, there is liberty, in comparison of the times and of the graces the Christians were called angels, with whose bread, which is Christ, the Jews — whom he called 'men' — were satisfied. Christ belongs rather to these, to whom he has both been revealed and who are deemed to be called by this name. In a twofold manner, then, the aforesaid question can be understood, so that it can be called both the bread of the heavenly angels and the bread of the Christians, according to what has been said.

XXI. WHAT IT MEANS TO HAVE MADE MAN AFTER THE IMAGE AND LIKENESS OF GOD, AND WHETHER WOMAN IS THE IMAGE OF GOD.

This is what it is for man to have been made after the image of God: that one made one, so that just as all things are from one God, so also from one man is the whole human race. But the likeness is this: that just as the Son is from the Father, so also woman is from man, in order that the authority of a single origin may be preserved. But the Son was born from God the Father ineffably and incomprehensibly, whereas woman, as we read, was made bodily from man, so that through her birth might arise. The Son of God, however, was born for this reason, that through him the creation might come to be. In this, therefore, there is a dissimilarity; for God too was born from God, but a female from a man, because in the former case there is simplicity, and simple could not be born except from simple, spirit from spirit, God from God. Therefore man is the image of God. For thus it is written: and God made man, after the image of God he made him. Whence also the Apostle says: a man indeed ought not to veil his head, since he is the image and glory of God; 'but a woman,' he says, 'let her veil her head.' Why? Because she is not the image of God. Whence again the Apostle says: but to a woman it is not permitted to teach nor to have dominion over a man.

XXII. WHAT IT MEANS THAT SOLOMON SAYS: 'JUSTIFY YOUR SOUL BEFORE YOUR DEATH,' AND IN THE PSALM: 'NO LIVING MAN,' HE SAYS, 'SHALL BE JUSTIFIED IN THE SIGHT OF GOD'?

He justifies his soul before death who, in the hope of the life to come, attains faith, lest he die impious. But Solomon speaks of the one who lives under the law and is not righteously converted. Such a one, therefore, if he correct his mind so as to walk in the ways of righteousness, justifies his soul before death. For Solomon gave this counsel on account of the judgment to come, lest anyone, careless about himself, die in his sins; but David spoke of the perfection of righteousness, because no one shall be justified before the Lord, or be worthy of the promise, unless he has died with righteousness. For as long as he lives, he does not yet have the full merit of things to come. Whence the Savior says: he who shall persevere unto the end, this one shall be saved.

XXIII. WHETHER SOULS ARE FROM PROPAGATION LIKE BODIES.

I think it unseemly if souls are said to be generated together with bodies, so that a soul is born from a soul, which does not even befit the soul itself. Or if indeed the heavenly powers were each made singly and from them the rest were born, can it also seem credible that from the one soul of Adam the rest are born? But it does not fit, because this was possible for God alone, that the simple should generate, nor would it be granted to the rest. This is so mystical in the matter of the Savior that it is thought incredible not only by the gentiles alone or the Jews, but even by those very ones who call themselves Christians; for both the Photinians and the Arians spurn this faith.

For if at the time when the body is sown the soul too is generated from a soul, we read that a rib was taken away from Adam, yet not that a soul was born from a soul; but if the rib had a soul with it, then it was not born, but drawn off into a portion. But not even this is written. For the prophet Zechariah says, among other things: who formest the soul of man within him. Nor does Isaiah, dissenting from these words, say: thus saith the Lord God who made thee and fashioned thee in the womb. If therefore it is fashioned in the womb, then it is bestowed upon a body already formed. For since it fills all the members of the body, it is said to be shaped within the body; and just as water, though it be without shape, yet when poured into a vessel appears shaped, so also the soul, though it be by nature incorporeal and simple, is as it were shaped into the body, giving life to each of the members. And this indeed Moses handed down more plainly, saying: if anyone strike a woman who is with child and she miscarry, if it be formed, let him give a soul for a soul; but if it be unformed, let him be fined a sum of money, so as to prove that there is no soul before the form. And so, if it is given to a body already formed, it is not born in the conceiving of the body, derived along with the seed. For if it exists together with the seed, and soul from soul, then many souls perish daily, since the seed by a certain flux comes to no birth.

But if we look more closely, we shall see what we ought to follow. Let us contemplate the making of Adam; for in Adam an example was given, that from him it might be understood that a body already formed received a soul. For He could have mingled a soul with the clay of the earth and so formed the body. But it was shaped by reason, because first the house had to be framed together and so the inhabitant brought in. The soul surely, because it is spirit, cannot dwell in what is dry; therefore it is carried in the blood. When therefore the lineaments of the body have not been framed together, where will the soul be? Or does it wander outside until it be sent in, since reason teaches that it is given in such a way as to animate the body, not to wander idle? But from what source it is given, let those say who think otherwise; from the male or from the female? If from the female, it does not fit, because there is something else in the example. For they propose that both the soul was given from the man along with the rib—which we have shown to be undermined by many arguments. Whence perhaps it might seem to be given from the female, chiefly on account of the Savior, whom we know to have been born of a woman without the embrace of the flesh, from the Holy Spirit. But if they think this, they grant more to women; for they transfer the authority of the man to the woman. For since they say that the origin both of the body and of the soul is from the man, they turn themselves about, so as to say that that which is the greater is from the woman, that is, the soul, but that which is the lesser from the male, that is, the body—although it is manifest that the order of the example handed down by God cannot be changed.

XXIIII. HOW IS IT THAT, WHEN MAN AND WOMAN ARE ONE FLESH, THE MAN IS THE IMAGE OF GOD, AND NOT THE WOMAN ALSO?

Man and woman are indeed of one substance, both in soul and in flesh, but the man is greater in degree, because from him is the woman, as the apostle says: the man is the head of the woman. For it is the cause that made the man greater, not the substance. For even in one body there are greater members and lesser ones, not by nature, but by order.

XXV. WHY DOES JOSEPH, AFTER HIS PROPHECY, ADJURE THE SONS OF ISRAEL THAT, WHEN THEY WERE FREED BY GOD, THEY SHOULD CARRY HIS ASHES OUT OF EGYPT?

It is settled that Joseph did not command this to the sons of Israel idly. For one who was able to prophesy must not be believed to have spoken without foresight. For since he was rather inclined toward devotion to God the creator, and knew, moreover, that he was venerated by the Egyptians on account of the administration by which he governed Egypt in its great want, he sought that after his departure the error should be taken away, being certain that it is the vanity of the common crowd to venerate the dead rather than the living as gods, lest the honor owed to the creator should be transferred to him. Here, therefore, he showed that he was free from the vain superstition of the Egyptians, when — lest it should advance to God's injury — he commanded that his ashes be carried away. And indeed we find that the apostles too followed this. For Paul and Barnabas, when they had seen that men wished to sacrifice to them, knowing that this was hateful to God, tore their garments, saying: What are you doing? We too are men like you; and thus they restrained the crowds.

XXVI. IT MUST BE INQUIRED CONCERNING ELISHA WHETHER HE OBTAINED THAT WHICH HE IS SAID TO HAVE ASKED HARSHLY FROM ELIJAH.

The holy Elisha, desirous of performing mighty works, drawn on by the example of his master, having received the power of asking whatever he wished, greedy for the good, asked that the spirit which was in his master might be in him twofold. To him the man of God Elijah said: You have asked harshly. For in his eagerness he did not consider that the disciple is not above the master. Elijah, however, knowing that he had asked this not from ambition for present glory, answered: Yet if you see me taken up from you, it shall be so for you. This sense must be weighed. But having, somewhat indignant, said: You have asked harshly, he added: Yet if you see me taken up, it shall be so for you. Did he not — since he had seemed to deny it to him — show that it would be for him as was fitting, not as he himself had asked? For scripture wishes much to be understood by implication, so that the sense gathered from the words may not be opposed to the reasoning of religion. For assuredly a harsh petition ought even to lose its merit; but because, being desirous of the good, he asked thus, God, who knows what gift is to be given to each one, did not suffer him to be deprived of his merits.

Let us therefore see whether Elijah's merit would allow that Elisha should deserve to receive a double gift of his. For it was said by the Holy Spirit through Zechariah, of what merit John was to be: He shall come, he says, in the spirit and power of Elijah — thus he shows John equal to Elijah — and the Lord says: Among those born of women there has arisen no prophet greater than John the Baptist. He did not say 'he is greater than all', but 'no one is greater than he', that is, 'he has an equal, he does not have a greater'. Elijah, therefore, is his equal, assuredly inferior to none; how then is Elisha greater than he, since John, than whom no one is greater, is equal to Elijah? For when Elisha came to the crossing of the waters, he did not receive honor from them, so that he might have been able to cross, until he had named the God of Elijah, so that by this the harsh petition might seem to be rebuked, since he was heard only by the name of Elijah, lest he should be thought to have received that which he had asked harshly, as though the dispensation of the spirit granted to Elijah were not sufficient for the merit of the time.

XXVII. WHETHER THE WITCH RAISED SAMUEL, AND WHETHER HE HIMSELF WAS SEEN BY HER AND SPOKE TO SAUL — THE NARRATIVE OF WHICH IS RECOUNTED IN THE BOOK OF KINGS.

I consider it an unworthy crime, if assent be granted according to the words of the narrative. For how could it come to pass that a man both holy by birth and just in the works of his life should be drawn forth by the magic art? Or, if he was not drawn forth, that he consented? Both of which it is repugnant to believe of a just man. For if he was brought forth unwillingly, justice has no advantage; but if willingly, he lost the merit of a spiritual man, which he had sought while set in the flesh — which is utterly absurd, since he who departs hence as a just man remains just. Furthermore, this is a deception of Satan, whereby, in order to deceive the many, he pretends to have even the good in his power. Concerning which the apostle, among other things, says: Satan himself transforms himself into an angel of light. For in order to produce the error in which he glories, he decked himself out in the guise and the name of a just man, so that he might lie away all profit from the hope which they were proclaiming to the worshippers of God, when he pretended that the just, on departing hence, were in his power.

But this deceives certain persons, that concerning the death of Saul and of his son he did not lie — as though it were a great thing for the devil to foresee, before the day, the fall of the body, since certain signs are wont to appear to those about to die, namely to those from whom the protection of God seems to have been withdrawn. How much more the devil, whom the prophetic oracles attest to have been lofty with angelic majesty, of whose greatness the apostle says: Or do you not know the height of Satan? What wonder, then, if he could foresee death drawing near at hand, since this is the very means whereby he deceives and wishes himself to be adored as though in the power of God? For Saul was made so senseless with such dullness that he took refuge with the witch. For, corrupted by reason of his sin, he betook himself to those things which he had condemned.

But if anyone, on account of the narrative and the things which are expressed in words, should think that they are not to be passed over, lest the account of the narrative be empty, he will indeed do rightly — provided, however, that he by no means drag this over to the account of the truth, but to the sight and understanding of Saul. For, having been made reprobate, he could not have a good understanding. For the historian described the mind of Saul and the guise of Samuel, expressing the things that were said and seen, passing over whether they be true or false. For what does he say? Hearing in what guise he had been raised up: he understood, he says, that this was Samuel. He reported what he understood; and, because he did not understand well, contrary to Scripture he adored another than the Lord, and, thinking it was Samuel, he adored the devil, so that Satan might have the fruit of his deceit. For this is what he strives for: that he be adored as though God. For if Samuel had truly appeared to him, the just man would by no means have permitted himself to be adored, seeing that he had proclaimed that the Lord alone was to be adored. And how could the man of God, who was with Abraham in refreshment, say to a man of pestilence deserving of the fire of Gehenna: 'Tomorrow you shall be with me'?

By these two tokens improvident Satan betrayed the cunning of his deception, because both he allowed himself to be worshipped under the guise and name of Samuel — contrary to the law and in the person of a man weighed down by sins, though there is a great distance between sinners and the just — and he lyingly claimed that Saul would be with Samuel the most just. Yet it can seem, even if the name of Samuel be passed over in silence, that Saul was going to be with the devil. For he passed over to the one whom he worshipped. Always, then, the devil, lurking under a veil, betrays himself, while he feigns those things which are abhorrent to the persons through whom he strives to deceive.

XXVIII. WHAT MUST BE SAID AGAINST THOSE WHO ASSERT THAT THIS WORLD IS MOVED NATURALLY FROM ETERNITY AND IS SUBJECT NEITHER TO A BEGINNING NOR TO ABOLITION.

That the world exists from eternity and owes subjection to none is improbable and impossible, since we see it to be corporeal, subsisting with such great diversity, whereas nothing can be divine and eternal except what is simple, in which no diversity is seen, but there is unity. For it does not even preserve the very alternations of the seasons, so that it is variable not only in its seasons and consists of contrary substances, but also in the very alternations of the seasons — whereas an eternal thing shrinks from diversity and can be grasped neither by touch nor by sight, being as it were incorporeal. But this world is subject to passion, because both water fights against fire, and fire, if it be greater, overcomes water, and earth, since it is by nature dry and cold, suffers burning like a corporeal thing. And so it is unfitting to call the world eternal, which, since we see it to be subject to passion and to fail in its several parts through old age, we believe also to be brought to an end.

What does he think concerning man, who supposes the world eternal? Certainly man had a beginning in the world: without man, then, for whom did the annual fruitfulness of the world profit? Or did it generate without any providence? And how did the eternal generate corruptible and mortal things, when from the eternal there ought to exist the eternal? And that which is visible and is perceived and is touched — by what audacity is it called eternal? And that which is administered by the alternations of the seasons, so that the very seasons sometimes do not preserve their functions — how is it called eternal? For eternity is not corrupted, is not changed.

XXVIIII. WHY WAS IT COMMANDED THAT CIRCUMCISION BE PERFORMED ON THE EIGHTH DAY?

It was commanded that circumcision be performed on the eighth day from birth for this reason: because, according to the number and the courses of the seven days, that same day is again found to be first, as it were after the sabbath, so that it is now reckoned not the eighth but the first. For because the salvation to come through Christ was predestined on the first day — which is therefore called the Lord's day, because on it the Lord rose again, who is after the sabbath — for that reason the figure of this salvation was given in circumcision, so that a renewal to come might be discerned in the law of circumcision. And because men were to be saved through faith, which was confirmed on the first day by the resurrection of the Lord, the figure of this matter was given in circumcision on the first day, which would also be a sign of the faith of Abraham. The reason, therefore, is complete: because the sign of the past faith signified on the first day was a figure of the future faith established on the first day.

XXX. IN PROVERBS: 'THE RIGHTEOUS MAN,' IT SAYS, 'IS HIS OWN ACCUSER AT THE BEGINNING OF HIS SPEECH': IN WHAT WAY RIGHTEOUS, IF A SINNER?

All truth is righteousness. For he who confesses what he is, is righteous — since he speaks the truth —, but sinners who defend their own offenses cannot be justified. Hence one of this sort is fittingly called righteous, because in declaring his sin to be forgiven, he assuredly asks it for himself, imploring the mercy of God. For he knows what is said in the law: confess your sins, that you may be justified. But what is it to confess at the beginning of one's speech, except to acknowledge not under compulsion, but willingly? For who, even fearing God, is without sin, since offenses are mingled even in thought, and sometimes we sin unwillingly? Yet it can also be said or understood concerning this: that he is called a catechumen, so that coming to the faith he may be justified. For when he asks to be changed, without doubt he confesses himself a sinner, that he may be justified.

XXXI. WHETHER A SERPENT BY NATURE SPOKE WITH THE WOMAN, OR WHETHER THE DEVIL, CALLED A SERPENT IN A FIGURATIVE SENSE, SEDUCED HER.

As far as pertains to the historical sense, it was a serpent by nature that was spoken of, which was wiser than the other beasts that the Lord God had made upon the earth. And indeed God passed sentence upon the serpent. For what wonder is it if the devil should be wiser than the beasts, concerning whom the apostle says among other things: or are you ignorant of the depth of Satan? But the serpent was by nature so much a serpent that it was said to it: upon your breast and your belly shall you crawl, and you shall eat earth all the days of your life. These things are far removed from the condition of Satan, since he is neither corporeal nor mortal. And if it be looked at more closely, the sentence given will be seen to have done no harm to the serpent, but in that condition in which it was made, it was commanded to remain. For because it was made a minister of Satan for subjugating man, to whom God had subjected all things, therefore God, in that subjection by which he subjected all things to man, checked the exaltation of the serpent, because it had begun to be a minister of pride. For man seemed to have been made under the serpent, through whom, having received its counsel, he despised the law that had been given. But that serpents are prudent, the Lord confirms in the gospel, saying: be wise as serpents.

Therefore, since it has become clear that a serpent truly spoke with Eve, this remains: that it be determined whether it could have been so prudent and cunning as to deceive her by trickery. For even if it was more prudent than the other beasts, it was nevertheless not more prudent than human beings, since no animal is rational except man. And so it is impossible that the serpent devised this subtlety. For although it is called prudent, yet not beyond its own nature; for it neither deliberates nor devises nor takes counsel. For this reason there is no doubt that it was the devil who circumvented the woman through the serpent. For mingling himself with the serpent, he acted through it as though through an instrument, so that the woman, knowing the serpent to be prudent, would not perceive the trickery of the hidden devil. And through this, though the sentence was given bodily, it fell spiritually upon Satan, because a sentence holds fast the guilty party. For the sentence was not given by a man, such that he might by chance destroy an innocent person through error or ill will, but by God, who assuredly cannot be deceived, so that his sentence does not hold fast one who is not guilty. For because, although Satan is invisible, he carried out his trickery bodily, the sentence appears to have been given bodily, so that it may hold him spiritually, whose deed drew forth the giving of the sentence. For, cast down from the most sacred seats of the heavens, the most shameless Satan writhes upon the earth, forever groaning.

But perhaps it may be said: 'And in what language, do you suppose, did the serpent speak to the woman?' By the serpent's mouth, of course. For if today there are those who understand the barking of dogs and the howling of wolves and the trumpeting of elephants and the crowing of young birds, why could not the woman, still untutored, all the more have known and understood the hissing of serpents, since we know that many people distinguish the voices of birds? For it is also established that the devil uses the tongue of that one whose body he has entered; nor would he otherwise deceive the woman. And through this he spoke by the serpent's mouth through the serpent.

XXXII. WE READ IN SOLOMON: 'THE RICH MAN AND THE POOR MAN MET ONE ANOTHER; BUT GOD MADE THEM BOTH': HOW THEN IS THERE NO RESPECTING OF PERSONS WITH GOD?'

Far be this assertion of impiety from the minds of the faithful. For scripture, lest it should teach that the poor man is to be despised and the rich man to be honored, shows that God is the maker of both — not indeed in poverty and riches, but in that which they are. For if they differ from one another in resources, yet not in the substance of their nature. For not because occasions bring about certain outcomes in the world — some men prosperous in riches, or others ill-fated in poverty — are those therefore to be looked down upon whom God has not humbled, or those to be looked up to whom the truth has not given testimony. Plainly those are to be despised who are corrupters of morals and sacrilegious against the law of God, but those to be honored who love God and are cultivators of discipline.

For these are held to be rich in God's sight who are of good conduct, and the more contemptible they are in the world, the more honored they are in heaven. For those who have the temporal advantage of increasing their means, if they recognize themselves and understand God's will — because he gave the earth to all and commands his sun to rise upon all and pours out his rains without distinction, whereas the unfairness of the times, or chance circumstances, or want, denies to certain people those things which God gave to all — let these very ones minister to them, so that, discerning God's will, they may be rich not only in the world but also in heaven, lest the riches of a brief time shut out the eternal riches; while those, indeed, whom they think should be despised on account of their poverty, if they contemplate God's coming judgment, will there be forever rich, in the place where the rich of this age shall be in need, repenting that they were not poor.'

XXXIII. SOLOMON SAYS: 'THE YEARS OF THE WICKED SHALL BE SHORTENED.' WHEREAS WE SEE SOME OF THE WICKED LONG-LIVED.

It would indeed have been fitting that all the wicked should not only be deprived of this light more quickly, but should not even enjoy the life of a single moment of time — they who, unmindful of their Creator, ascribe the authority over their whole life to the creature. But Scripture speaks of those wicked who, though they lived under God's law, were more inclined toward idols. For they are worse than the rest of the wicked; for knowing the Lord and despising him, they obey slaves. And that it was said concerning these the Apostle proves, saying: But we know that whatsoever the law saith, it saith to them who are under the law. The Gentiles, however, are not reckoned in the number of the living.

XXXIIII. IN SOLOMON HE SAYS: 'GOD MADE NOT DEATH', AND IN ANOTHER PLACE: 'GOOD AND EVIL, LIFE AND DEATH, POVERTY AND HONOR ARE FROM GOD'.

It is doubtful to no one that all good things are from God, but the evils which he mentioned are evils to us while punishment is exacted from us. For a penalty, although it be inflicted justly, nevertheless seems evil to the one suffering it. For also through the prophet God speaks to sinners, saying: I will do evils to you, that is, 'I will drive you with punishments'. But life and death are from God in this way, because he gave the law, which promises life to those who keep it, but death to those who despise it — yet that death which is called the second. For while he adjudges what each one deserves, from him comes either the acquittal unto life or the guilty verdict unto death. He is not, therefore, the inventor of death, but the judge, because the author of death is sin. How then could God make death, who knows not how to sin? But when he requites sins, death is said to be from him, though it is not from him, but from the one who sinned.

By the same reasoning poverty and riches are from God. For some, having the fear of God, spurn luxury, flee a prodigal life, and do not turn their eye away from the needy or the poor: people of this sort bring about an increase of goods. For it is written in Proverbs: Discipline takes away poverty and lowliness. But those, on the other hand, who do not have the fear of God, living more dissolutely and despising the needy, will be in want, as it is written, being not only negligent and improvident concerning themselves, but also despisers of the given law. In this way God makes the poor and the rich, because, since an offense against the law begets destitution, this is said to be done by God as the author of the law; and since some keep the discipline of the law out of dread, they become rich by God the author of the law.

XXXV. BY WHAT REASONING DOES DAVID, AFTER GOD HAD DEPARTED FROM SAUL, CALL HIM THE LORD'S ANOINTED AND SHOW DEFERENCE TO HIM?

David, not unaware that the appointment to the office of the royal order is divine, therefore honors Saul, who was still established in that same appointment, lest he should seem to do injury to God, who decreed the honor for these orders. For the king bears the image of God, just as the bishop bears the image of Christ. As long, therefore, as he is in that appointment, he is to be honored, if not for his own sake, then at least for the sake of the order. Hence the apostle says: Be subject to all the higher powers. For there is no power except from God; and those which exist have been ordained by God. This is why we honor even a pagan, provided he is placed in power, although he himself is unworthy, who, holding the order of God, gives thanks to the devil. For power demands honor, because it merits it. For it was for this reason that the dream of the coming famine was revealed to Pharaoh, and that Nebuchadnezzar alone, while others stood by him, saw the Son of God in the furnace of fire — surely not by his own merit, he who wished himself to be adored in an idol, but by the merit of the royal order.

XXXVI. IF 'THE SOUL THAT SINS SHALL ITSELF DIE,' HOW IS IT THAT ACHAN THE SON OF CHARMI SINNED AND THIRTY-SIX MEN WERE KILLED ON HIS ACCOUNT?

Scripture spoke in a different sense than has been proposed. For it did not speak of this death, which is indeed common to all, but of that which is called the second. For this latter takes up sinners; but to those who died in battle the sin of Achan was not imputed, but it harmed them in that, not being aided by divine protection, they did not prevail against their enemies. For they were not weakened on account of Achan, but because they were in no way fortified by God's help, so that, being unable to act bravely, they were killed. For they were few against many, whence they are also known to have been overcome. There is therefore no injustice in the will of God. For He has it in His power, if He wishes to help, so that if He is present, He is merciful, and if He is unwilling, He is just; for He is not a debtor to such men, that He should be held liable. For this is why Joshua the son of Nun, being grieved, tore his garments, understanding that the Lord had not without cause withdrawn His help.

XXXVII. HOW IS IT THAT THE DEATH SENT UPON JACOB CAME UPON ISRAEL, SINCE JACOB HIMSELF WAS CALLED ISRAEL?

He set two names of one people, so that from the names their merits might be understood. For assuredly he who was surnamed Israel was formerly called Jacob. For wrestling with the Savior, he understood that he whom he was seeing in bodily form was God, and therefore he was called 'a man seeing God.' Therefore the people whom he names Jacob, in this place and in this matter, he signifies as carnal, in the way that names are imposed on the newborn by their parents. But these, that is the Jews, on account of their evil deeds were not called Israelites, because they sacrificed to heifers and groves in Samaria by the zeal and command of King Jeroboam. But that people who in Jerusalem in the temple of the Lord offered sacrifices through the priests of God was called Israel. Samaria, therefore, on account of its impiety was handed over to captivity and death, but Jerusalem was preserved, because it had had kings who loved God. But when Jerusalem itself also followed the impiety of Samaria, the sentence given against Samaria overtook it.

Therefore it was said: God sent death into Jacob — that is, into the people of Samaria, who lived carnally, as we said above — and it came into Israel, that is, into the people of Jerusalem, because it followed idolatry, imitating Samaria; so that in a like manner it was led away into captivity by Nebuchadnezzar, just as Samaria too was by Shalmaneser, king of the Assyrians. This means plainly: the sentence given against the wicked found the good as well, once they had ceased to be righteous. For death sent into Jacob, as was shown above, reached Israel without passing over Samaria; but since the cause for which it was sent into Samaria was found in Jerusalem as well, when sent it came thither also, into that place in which the same cause disclosed itself; because vengeance follows the cause, not distinguishing the person.

XXXVIII. IF THE HORSE AND THE MULE HAVE NO UNDERSTANDING, HOW MUCH MORE THE EARTH, A THING WITHOUT SENSE? HOW THEN IS IT THAT THE EARTH IS SAID TO BLESS GOD?

It is not to be understood as the words sound. For it is a work which, although it be mute, is said to cry out the praise of its Creator; for it exhorts those who behold it to bless the Maker, just as every well-made vessel too praises its craftsman, seeing that it is read in the Psalms: Shall the dust, he says, confess to you, or declare your truth? In every senseless substance, therefore, it is not the will — which indeed is none — but the works that bless the Author. But if you wish to take this spiritually, understand the earth to signify man according to the saying of the prophet — for he says, Remember, Lord, that we are earth — whom the works of creation exhort to break forth into the praise of his Maker.

XXXVIIII. WHAT IS IT THAT IS READ IN SOLOMON: 'THERE IS HOPE,' HE SAYS, 'IN THE DARKNESS; A DOG IS BETTER THAN A DEAD LION'?

Tenebrae signify Gentile status and ignorance. He therefore wishes it to be understood that there is more hope in a Gentile than in an apostate, by that which he added, saying: A dog is better than a dead lion, because just as the lion is stronger than all wild beasts, so too Christianity is stronger than all sects. If anyone therefore withdraws from it, he loses salvation, so that he becomes worse than a Gentile. For it can happen that a Gentile believes and acquires the salvation which the apostate has lost. And if he does not believe, the apostate is still worse. Because it is worse to have lost salvation than not to have had it. And the Savior shows that a 'dog' signifies a 'Gentile,' saying: It is not right to take the children's bread and cast it to the dogs.

XL. WHAT DOES IT MEAN THAT THE PROPHET SAYS: 'REJOICE, BARREN ONE, YOU WHO DO NOT BEAR; BREAK FORTH AND CRY OUT, YOU WHO DO NOT GIVE BIRTH, FOR THE CHILDREN OF THE DESOLATE ARE MANY MORE THAN OF HER WHO HAS A HUSBAND'?

Since the children of the earthly Jerusalem apostatized from God, joy cannot be ascribed to her, but mourning. To the reproach, therefore, of this Jerusalem the prophet says that that higher Jerusalem ought to rejoice, whom the apostle calls our mother, because without groaning and pains she has many more children than this one which brings them forth carnally with tears — the Jews, namely —, while the former brings forth Christians through faith spiritually. He calls her 'desolate' for this reason, because she herself is the life which the first Adam deserted and followed death. When, therefore, men are regenerated, they return to that life which they had deserted. This life is Christ, because he himself said: I am the life. God the Father is also life, because the Lord says: As the Father has life, so he gave life to the Son. Without doubt the Holy Spirit too is life, since the Lord says: He, he says, shall receive of Mine. And so, whoever has received of life is life. The three, therefore, are one life. This is the life through faith in which we are regenerated. She herself is our mother, just as we also read in Genesis: because she herself is, he says, — that is, life — the mother of all the living. Who are they that live, except those who believe?

XLI. WHETHER THE SPIRIT, WHICH WAS BORNE OVER THE WATERS, IS TO BE UNDERSTOOD AS THE HOLY SPIRIT, BECAUSE IT IS SAID: 'THE SPIRIT OF GOD WAS BORNE OVER THE WATER'.

If the Holy Spirit is thought by some to be so called for the reason that he is read to be the Spirit of God, it is a weak and empty assertion. For not only does the order not permit this and reason refuse it, but the very words themselves prove feeble for establishing this claim. For with these words we frequently find something else signified in the Law; for among other things the Lord God says: My spirit shall not remain in these men, because they are flesh. And in what follows: But I will blot out, he says, everything from man even to the beast, because he was about to bring a flood upon the earth. Can the Holy Spirit possibly be understood here? Was he not speaking about souls? For not only our souls are called spirits, but also those of the beasts. For thus it is written: And all flesh died in the water, in which was the spirit of life. And in Ezekiel the prophet it is thus said, when God promises concerning the renewal of men, saying: Thus says the Lord to these bones: I will stretch skin over you and will put my spirit in you, and you shall live. Was he speaking about the Holy Spirit and not about the soul?

All heavenly things indeed are spirits, but they differ from one another, and God too is spirit, but far otherwise. Therefore every spirit is God's; yet it is not God, except the Spirit which is from him, whose designation is this, that he be called 'holy.' For men too are called sons of God, and Christ is called the Son of God; but there is a difference, because the latter is true, the former adoptive. So too among the spirit of God there is a diversity. For the Holy Spirit is from God, consubstantial with him; but the rest are said to be spirits of God, yet they are creatures. But neither is it in keeping with the order that the Holy Spirit should be understood to be borne over the waters, since reason hands down that there is a spiritual creature over the waters, and one which has above itself another more excellent creature, because creature differs from creature in brightness. For whatever is approached near to the seat of God, the ministries are brighter. Whence the angel Raphael says among other things: I am, he says, one of the seven holy angels, who stand and dwell before the majesty of God.

XLII. WHY DID THE ANGEL SENT TO SPEAK TO MOSES APPEAR ON THE MOUNTAIN IN FIRE AND IN A BUSH?

God is exalted; it was therefore fitting that he should appear on high, in a place that is near to heaven. And because fire always seeks the higher things, growing upward toward heaven, in it the Lord of the heavens ought to appear, who strives from the ground toward the things above. But in the bush the cause of transgressions brought it about that he appeared. For because he had come down to give the Law for the sake of sins, and thorns are signified among sins — for I waited, he says, that it might make a grape, but it made thorns — and the Law had not come at once to punish, but first to reveal, therefore the fire did not burn up the bush, that is, the Law when given did not make sinners guilty concerning the past. For because sins too are not of nature, but from accident, the material of the bush, that is, of the thorns, does not have a root armed with thorns. It was therefore done with reason that the Lord should thus appear to Moses.

XLIII. SINCE WE KNOW THAT ABRAHAM WAS FORBIDDEN BY GOD TO SACRIFICE HIS SON, WHY WAS JEPHTHAH, WHEN HE OFFERED HIS DAUGHTER AS A LIBATION, NOT FORBIDDEN, BUT DISCHARGED HIS VOW TO GOD WITH HUMAN BLOOD?

There is a great distance between the case of Abraham and that of Jephthah. First of all, indeed, Abraham was born legitimately, but Jephthah was a bastard; for he was the son of a harlot. Next, Abraham was of the best manner of life, but Jephthah was the chief of robbers. Abraham was a righteous man and proven in temptation; but in Jephthah there is no testimony of righteousness whatever.

And so Abraham was commanded to offer his son to God, and to show to men how greatly Abraham believed God, seeing that he did not hesitate to slay even his own son at His bidding. For he who, against the hope of the world, was able to bring about that he should be born of a barren and aged woman — it was not doubtful that, against the reasoning of the world, He could raise him up from the dead. By this deed Abraham was heaped up with righteousness. For God showed that He had not demanded the blood of the boy, but that His servant Abraham might by this deed be made greater. For He wills that those who love Him should suffer something, so that He may seem justly to advance them.

For Jephthah, a criminal and improvident man, by a foolish devotion promised a gift to God, saying: whatever shall meet me on my return from the slaughter before the doors of my house, this I will offer to God. Was he compelled to make the vow, or did he understand in what manner he ought to make a vow? For what if a dog or an ass had met him — which God forbade to be offered? Or if another man's son, or if his wife, so that he would render his vow exulting in another's grief? Therefore, by divine judgment, the improvidence of the impious man, in that he had made a vow to another's destruction, was turned back upon that very man himself. And Jephthah was so senseless that not even afterward did he recognize that he had foolishly made the vow, so that his error, which had begun from himself, might through himself also be amended. For he ought to have understood that God does not take delight in such a sacrifice, and to have prayed for pardon of his folly and to have offered something else which was not against the law. But the fool showed himself faithful — a parricide — although it is agreed that foolish faith not only in no way profits, but even does harm. And so in the case of his daughter he himself was slaughtered, whence also God dissembled at such a sacrifice. For if He had forbidden this, He would have seemed to wish him to offer something else to Himself, whereas He knew that, as unworthy, he ought not to offer. But his daughter made a gain, because, dying innocent amid the evils of her father, she was spared the punishment of hell, which perhaps by living long she could not have attained.

But that the triumph of victory attended Jephthah, it was not his own merit that brought this about, nor that of the people; for it is not read that they were suppliant to God, that He might give them a defender. Or did God send Jephthah himself, or address him? But because the foreigners were praising their own gods, as though it were these who had made the children of Israel subject to them, reviling the God of Abraham, therefore, for the honor of His own name, God took vengeance upon the foreigners — if not on account of these men, then at least on account of the merit of Abraham and His own eminence. Or shall a man be called worthy because the Spirit of the Lord leaped upon Samson while he was consorting with harlots, when this deed is understood to have been for the destruction of the foreigners?

XLIIII. AGAINST THE JEWS.

It is written in the prophet Isaiah: My house shall be called a house of prayer for all nations. But perhaps the Jews may say: 'The house of God, that is, the synagogue, stands open to all.' Nor do we resist this, provided however that, as always, the circumcised be joined to the law. For the law belongs to those who have been commanded to be circumcised after the manner of Abraham. Nothing new, therefore, could seem to be shown by this example of the prophet, since anyone coming from whatever nation was not, from the beginning of Judaism, forbidden to attach himself to the law. Nothing new could be reckoned to have been said by the prophet; indeed, it would be superfluous, if Gentiles have always been admitted to serve the God of Abraham and are found never at any time to have been forbidden. And it is true, since those who subject themselves to the law are proved never not to have been received. If, then, these things are so, the prophet seems to have given his admonition superfluously, since what was once handed down has always been kept.

And could anyone be found so dull as to say that the prophet spoke a thing so superfluous and empty? I do not know for whom it would be safe even to hear this and not to forbid it. For since the Jewish people, on account of the sins in which they were offending against the Lord by seeking support from idols, being often rebuked by the prophets, were unwilling to enter the path of penitence, so as to return to God, God, to their reproach, signified that he would open his house to all nations for prayer, so that, the Jews being cast off, he might show that he would set others in their place. For if the Gentiles were always admitted to the law, as was said above, how was the prophet saying that this would come to pass, unless because he signified something other than what had been commanded before? For he could not speak of a thing that was happening daily, as though it were to be in the future. Whence it is manifest that this makes for the ruin of the Jews, to whom the prophet showed that others, these being removed, were to be called to receive the gift of the grace of God, which had been promised to the Jews.

But what was promised, the prophet Isaiah teaches, saying: There shall come out of Sion he who shall deliver and turn away impiety from Jacob; and this is the covenant to them from me. This covenant, therefore, is the abolition of sins. For this is the new covenant, which was promised through Jeremiah, saying: Behold, the days shall come, saith the Lord, and I will accomplish for the house of Israel and for the house of Judah a new covenant, not according to the covenant which I disposed for their fathers in the day when I took hold of their hand, to lead them out of the land of Egypt; because they themselves did not continue in my covenant, and I neglected them, saith the Lord. This is their prevarication: when they made the image of the calf, then they corrupted the covenant. Whence also the tablets on which the law had been given were broken beneath the mountain, from the fact that they deserved to receive grievous precepts.

But because God is kind and merciful, he promised through the prophets that he would change the law, so that he might give another testament, which would differ from the one he had given before; so that not only what was burdensome would be changed, but also those things which had been given as a figure, that is, the sabbath and circumcision, so that, because they had long been oppressed, being relieved in all things they might as suppliants give thanks to God. And because, persisting in the ancestral transgression, they refused to turn their minds away from idols, he attests that the Gentiles, together with the remnant who kept the faith, should enjoy the testament which he had said would come; so that the faithless Jews, burdened in a twofold way, might be tormented; for they were both burdened for a time by the precepts and in the future will pay fitting penalties, because they were found faithful neither in the old nor in the new testament.

He said therefore that he would give a new testament, so that the old one, which had been given to the fathers, might cease. And through this, whoever serves God in this testament keeps neither the sabbath nor the new moons nor circumcision nor distinctions of foods, but has only the fear of God in faith, because he is not justified by the law, but by faith, as the prophet Habakkuk says: For the just shall live by my faith, so that he no longer uses many precepts to merit God, but few, Isaiah again saying the same things concerning this meaning; for he says: If the number of the children of Israel be as the sand of the sea, a remnant of them shall be saved. For a word completing and shortening in righteousness. For God will make a shortened word upon the whole earth. To the remnant, who persevered in the faith of the promise while the rest were unbelieving, he shows the shortened word of the law, that is, the new testament which he promised—which would not otherwise be called a testament, unless in like manner this too were confirmed by the testimony of blood, just as also the old testament, so that the name of both is one; but in the old the precepts are many, while in the new they are few. As he promised, so that, because he had said that Abraham would be the father of many nations, the sons might be justified in the same way as the father too had been justified, so that, when the things which had begun after Abraham's faith ceased, receiving the faith through which the same Abraham was justified, they might be sons of Abraham, as it was said by the Lord. Which indeed is neither wonderful nor unheard of, that men should be justified without care of the sabbath and circumcision and the rest of the commandments of this kind.

For it was not through this that Enoch pleased God, so that he was translated, nor was Noah, by keeping these things, found alone righteous in the flood, nor was Abraham himself justified after he was circumcised: for Abraham believed God, he says, and it was reckoned to him for righteousness. He was then circumcised, so that there might be a sign in him, because he had believed, when he was old, that he would have a son. Therefore it is not circumcision that commends Abraham, but faith. And therefore for believers faith suffices for justification, so that they are sons of Abraham in faith, not in circumcision, because for these there is no need of circumcision; for that which Abraham believed is not now believed. But the Jews were for this reason commanded to be circumcised, that they might have a sign of Abraham's faith, not of their own faith, but that they might be known as his sons according to the flesh: but if they should believe, they will be his sons also unto justification.

For what does it profit to call someone the son of a powerful man, if he himself is powerless — unless it rather does harm? For it is a disgrace for the son of a man of dignity to be without honor. For the circumcision of the flesh is of so little merit that God says through the prophet Jeremiah that he will take vengeance upon all the circumcised who have foreskins, if they have not been circumcised in heart. But he is not indignant with those who, circumcised in heart, are uncircumcised in flesh, because no one has pleased God without the circumcision of the heart. Whoever, therefore, are circumcised in the flesh signify the race of Abraham according to the flesh; but those who are circumcised in heart signify the race of God according to faith, because, circumcised from every worldly error, they profess God as their parent. What, then, does the circumcision of the flesh profit, when, if faith is lacking in the Jew, he will not be able to attain to the merit of Abraham? Or what harm does the foreskin do, since no one, except him who has been emulous of the faith of Abraham, has attained to his righteousness?

Vainly, therefore, do they glory in the circumcision of the flesh, when they see that it has no merit before God. And if, as has been shown, it is better to have faith than circumcision, the Gentiles are better than the Jews, because the former have faith, while the Jews have circumcision. And whom, do you suppose, does he acknowledge more as the sons of Abraham: those who are like him according to the flesh, or those who are in faith? But it is faith that bears witness to Abraham, and through this he glories not in the flesh, but in faith. And therefore he receives as his sons those whom he sees to be imitators of his glory. For how can he call those his sons in whom he sees an abhorrence for that whereby he pleased God and was justified? He will be able to judge these, then, not as sons, but as enemies. For those who spurn the faith through which he was glorified seem to be envious of him.

It is no wonder if the Jews are found to be distrustful, by no means accepting the New Testament, since we know that they were not faithful even under the Old. For they were always rebellious and insolent against God, whence they received such commandments, through which their necks, which were inflexible, might be pressed down. For they never wished to hope for good things from God, whose so great benefits and such marvelous mighty works they had come to know. Hence it is that among other things the prophet Ezekiel says: I lifted up my hand among them in the desert, that they should be scattered among the nations and dispersed in the regions, because they did not perform my ordinances of justice and rejected my commandments; and in what follows: and I gave them commandments that were not good and ordinances of justice, in which they shall not live in them. He shows for what cause commandments so many and so harsh were given to them: namely, that, because they proved ungrateful for so great goods, at the dictate of justice they might be pressed down by more severe commandments. For he called them not good, though they are just, because they did not avail them unto justification, but unto ruin; and a punishment, although worthily inflicted, is nevertheless not good to the one suffering it. Yet after these things, because God is merciful, he promised that he would give a New Testament, in which a shortening made of the law might justify those who believe, just as Abraham was justified, so that, with the new moons and the sabbath and circumcision and the other commandments ceasing — not those which are of the natural law, that is, murder, adultery, and such things — they might be justified in the same way as Abraham also was. For, faith having been renewed after the manner of Abraham, he willed that all things should cease, so that there might be such a time as had been when Abraham believed.

But perhaps the Jews may say: 'If the things which were given by Moses have been declared to cease through the shortening of the law, was circumcision given by Moses that it should cease? And through this we have the sign of our father, since we are sons of Abraham, which on account of the testimony of our lineage ought not to cease.' Circumcision indeed was to be kept in its own time, that is, before the New Testament, which had been promised, was given. But when, by the grace of God bestowing it, the burdens of the law were commanded to cease, what need is there to be circumcised? And for this reason a new law was given, that is, a spiritual one, so that carnal things might cease and those who receive it might take its sign, so that, just as under the Old they had the sign of Abraham, from whom the Old Testament began, so also under the New they might have the sign of the Savior, who is the establisher of the New Testament. For just as from Abraham they were called Hebrews, so also from Christ they are called Christians. Whoever therefore are still called Hebrews have not received the New Testament which God promised, bringing to nothing the benefit and mercy of God — God who, no longer willing his people to be reckoned by the name of a man, deigned to establish them under the name of his own Son.

By what audacity, then, do the Jews say that we use not our own law but theirs, when David says: I will confess to you among the nations, and again: rejoice, you nations, with his people? And to the prophet Jeremiah the Lord says: before I formed you in the womb, I knew you; and before you came forth from the belly, I sanctified you and set you as a prophet among the nations. Judaism certainly has its own designations; for that people is signified either in Jacob or in Samaria or in Jerusalem or in Judah or in Israel. But concerning the nations it is clear that he signifies them otherwise than as the Jews are.

Jeremiah, therefore, is our prophet. For although in all the prophets God has shown the nations to be sharers in his gift, yet to Jeremiah he speaks specially, because he gave him to us as a prophet. For this man signified the New Testament that was to come, which would benefit the nations more than the Jews, which now seems to have been made manifest. For since the preaching of the New Testament is in the whole earth, so rarely and with such difficulty is a faithful Jew found, that all the churches of the New Testament are named from the nations, just as it had been said in the prophet Hosea: I will call not my people my people, and not the beloved beloved. The Jews, therefore, having been rejected, he calls together the nations by that grace of mercy by which he deigned to call Abraham also. For not even did Abraham, uncalled, come to know the Lord. And so he who deigned to call Abraham, the same himself deigned also to call the nations.

Why, then, do they say: 'the law is ours,' when it is manifest that the gift of God belongs to all who are willing? Let this rashness of usurpation therefore cease, since the grace of God is common to all. For with what shamelessness do they deny that our Christ was promised in the law, when they see in him all the signs which the law foretold would come to pass? Since both according to the promise to Abraham all the nations are justified who believe in him, and according to the prophecy of Isaiah he was born of a virgin in Bethlehem from the tribe of Judah, as it had been promised, from the seed of David; and, according to what Jeremiah prophesied, he preached the New Testament which God had promised would come to pass; and after all the signs of his mighty works had been displayed, he humbled himself according to the sayings of the prophets, and having suffered all the things which had been foretold, on the third day he rose again, just as it is written in the prophet.

And if they should inquire into the time of the promise, just as it was shown to the prophet Daniel, they will find it fulfilled in the time in which Christ was born and suffered; for from the time of the first year of Darius king of the Persians up to the nativity and passion of the Lord and the destruction of Jerusalem, which was carried out by Vespasian, prince of the Roman Empire, the seventy weeks were fulfilled, which are 490 years. This reckoning is made clear through the reigns and through the successors of the empire, in the manner in which it is also found reckoned by Tertullian in the book which he wrote against the Jews — which, lest it should turn to his injury, since he reckoned rightly, we have passed over.

And who could be in doubt about this number, when now some three hundred years or so are found to have accrued beyond this number? It is therefore the mark of a shameless person, after so great a number has been added on top, to say of the coming of the Savior: "He has not come." For under the apostles, or at the very time when the Lord was living among men, the malice that contradicts the truth could conceal itself, so that, although it saw all the signs of the promised Christ in the Savior, it nevertheless claimed to be in doubt about the time, in order to hide its treachery. But afterwards, when many years began to pile up beyond the promised number, what shamelessness it is to deny that he has come, when it is known to have been fulfilled both by the signs and by the times! For when something is complete and does not overflow, some contentious person can say that it is not complete; but if it overflows, the contentious person's mouth is shut.

Thus the Jews too, in the time of the Savior, were able to say — though by way of deception — that the reckoning from the time of Darius up to the Lord Christ had not been correctly made, so that he might be said to be the one who was promised. For we go astray in the years and months and days of the emperors and do not reckon them out to precision. But once the times grew beyond the prescribed number of the years of Christ's promise, no excuse can be put forward for saying that the one who came is not himself the Christ — unless (which God forbid) the one who made the promise be said to have lied. For when the time of the promise has been measured out and it is denied that the one who was promised has come, of what else is the one who promised convicted than of falsehood? But that the one who made the promise is truthful, all the signs teach — the signs that were foretold and are known to be found in Christ. The Jews, however, while they blush to correct themselves, do not consider the coming judgment. For nothing is worse than for someone to deny that a thing is true which he understands is no lie.

XLV. ON THE IMAGE.

"Let us make," he says, "man to our image and likeness." Some think this ought to be understood from another passage of the reading, namely from that in which we read that God said: "Come, let us descend and confound their tongues," because, as far as appears on the surface, it is similar: for it is the utterance of one speaking in the plural number, but it differs in sense, since the reasoning too is dissimilar. For there he speaks of creating man, whom God wishes to be created to his image and likeness. But that which he says: "Come, let us descend and confound their tongues," pertains to this: that men should not understand one another; whence God may seem to have spoken to the angels, as a commander to an army. For he spoke jointly, because he was going to act through them, inasmuch as he was their creator, and on this account he says: "Let us confound their tongues." For when he says: "Let us make man to our image and likeness," he cannot be said to have spoken to the angels, because it cannot be said that the image of God and of the angels is one, as of a commander and his companions, since where the image is one, the dignity or nature cannot be dissimilar. Finally, he added: "And God made," he says, "man; to the image of God he made him," so that it was God who spoke and God who made — not another God or a different one, but the same God.

For what one said to one does not signify a different nature, but a person. For although God is one, there are nevertheless three persons; and therefore God is one, because, whether in the Son or in the Holy Spirit, it is the substance of the one God, since that which is from him cannot fail to be what he himself is.
