For the apostle spoke these things which you have brought forward into the question not in order to strike down free will, but in order to forbid a man to call into question the judgment of God; he said these things, proclaiming God just; for he knows on whom he ought to have mercy. And he who inspects the heart sees through the mind of the one who asks, whether he deserves to receive. Finally, he says through the prophet: This people honors me with their lips, but their heart is far from me. For it is just that the mind of the pretender be hardened. For he who calls the true false not through error but through malevolence, so that, understanding the good, he feigns that he does not know it to be good, in order that he may turn it into evil — to such a one this indeed ought to be granted, that he truly not understand the good, lest he be saved, which he does not will. For neither is it just that he be saved against his will, who spurns salvation not through ignorance but through malice or envy. By these things, therefore, free will is confirmed rather than destroyed, so that to each one it may be answered according to his own wish.

LXXVI. (LXXII). IT IS READ IN THE APOCALYPSE OF JOHN: 'GO', HE SAYS, (AND TAKE THE BOOK FROM THE HAND OF THE ANGEL AND DEVOUR IT, AND IT WILL MAKE YOUR BELLY BITTER, BUT IN YOUR MOUTH IT WILL BE SWEET AS HONEY). WHAT IS THIS BOOK, WHICH MAKES THE BELLY BITTER, THOUGH IT IS SWEET?

All the divine books are bitter, but to the faithless and carnal. For to idolaters it is unpleasant when they hear one God proclaimed in Christ. And Photinus at the same time is thoroughly frightened, seized with dread; Sabellius too blushes when he hears that the Father is a Father who cannot be called the Son, because from him are all things; but that the Son is on this account called Son, because he is not indeed himself the Father, but is from the Father, not the one from whom, but the one through whom are all things. Arius too is refuted, when he reads that Christ is the true Son of God; for without doubt he could not be called true, unless he were properly from God. Marcion also is convicted, learning that the Word was made flesh — he who thinks that flesh must be denied to Christ. The wicked Manichaeus too is confuted, seeing it written by the evangelist that the Savior not only adorned the vows of marriage by his presence, but even honored them with cups — he who bars the approach to marriage and exhorts to meeting together promiscuously.

These things are contained in the book which the apostle received to devour. This book is the Gospel which is titled ACCORDING TO JOHN, which is bitter not only to those who think ill, but also to the undisciplined, who refuse to be corrected, so that they advance for the worse. This revelation was made at the time when the apostle John was on the island of Patmos, banished by the emperor Domitian for the sake of the faith. Then he was in the spirit, so that he could see heavenly things, and while these things are shown to him, which are to come to pass because of impiety and debauchery and the rest. There was given to him a book which was indeed sweet in the mouth, but made the belly bitter, so that of those men who seem to be of one body, to some it was sweet. Those, because they are of upright profession, were signified in the mouth — for this is sweet in the mouth, which is true —, but to those who by heretical depravity live or think carnally, because of which they were also signified in the belly, it was bitter. For it will accuse them on the day of God's judgment. After the revelation, therefore, he was commanded to write the gospel on account of these things which we said above. And to the prophet Ezekiel likewise it was said, that he should take a book which was sweet in his mouth, when he was sent to rebuke the faithlessness of the people.

LXXVII. (LXXIII). WHAT IS THE MEANING OF WHAT SIMEON SAYS TO MARY THE MOTHER OF THE LORD, AMONG OTHER THINGS: 'THIS ONE,' HE SAYS, 'IS SET FOR THE FALL AND RESURRECTION OF MANY IN ISRAEL; AND A SWORD SHALL PIERCE THROUGH THY OWN SOUL, THAT THE THOUGHTS OF MANY HEARTS MAY BE REVEALED'?

Symeon, a holy man commended also by divine oracles, spoke through the Holy Spirit of what was to come upon men on account of Christ: that to those who seemed to themselves to stand firm through observance and expertise in the law, He would be a ruin — for through their distrust of the works of Christ they would fall, cut off from the promise of the fathers — but to those who were of almost no standing in the law, yet believed in Christ, there would be a resurrection in Israel, so that those who were previously unworthy and useless might become worthy of God, and those who were thought to be something might be rejected. This is what the Lord says in another place: For judgment I have come into this world, that they who see not may see, and they who see may become blind. And indeed it was not the doctors of the law, not the Pharisees, not the scribes who followed Christ, but fishermen, unlearned and rustic men. Hence it is that the Lord says: Father, I give thanks to thee, that thou hast hidden these things from the wise and prudent, and hast revealed them to little ones.

But as for what he added, saying: and a sword shall pierce through thy own soul, that the thoughts of many hearts may be revealed, this surely signified that even Mary, through whom the mystery of the Savior's incarnation was accomplished, would doubt at the Lord's death — yet in such a way that she might be strengthened by the resurrection. For all doubted, seized by a certain amazement, at the Lord's death. For who would not waver, seeing Him who called Himself the Son of God so humbled that He descended even to death? And because, as I have said, all wavering was to depart at the Lord's resurrection, he said the sword would 'pierce through,' not fall upon or strike some passing limb — so that, just as a sword thrust passing close beside a man causes fear, yet does not wound, so too the doubt caused sorrow, yet did not slay, because it did not settle in the soul but passed through, touching the hearts of the disciples as if through a shadow. And indeed Cleopas and Ammaus, going along the way in sorrow, say among other things to the Lord Himself, not knowing that it is the Lord: We were thinking that it was He who was beginning to deliver Israel. They doubted, but as soon as the Lord was recognized the doubt passed away. Similarly it was said also of Joseph, that iron pierced through his soul. This is to escape death, because to doubt concerning God or Christ is death, and thereby he who ceases to doubt escapes death.

LXXVIII. (LXXIV). WHAT DOES IT MEAN THAT THE PROPHET ISAIAH SAYS OF CHRIST: 'WHO DID NO SIN'; BUT THE APOSTLE, ON THE CONTRARY, SAYS: 'HIM WHO KNEW NO SIN,' HE SAYS, 'HE MADE SIN FOR US'?

As far as the sense is concerned, the questions are different. But the words are in one respect similar, and in another respect discordant. For "he made" and "he did not make" is a contradiction; but "he did not know" and "he did not do" are similar. The prophet therefore spoke in the person of Christ: that he did no sin, neither was guile found in his mouth; but the apostle speaks in the person of God the Father: that him — that is, Christ —, who knew no sin, he made to be sin for us. This is to be understood in two ways. For first he made him sin, when he willed him to be made flesh, so that he whom the lot did not touch might receive a body from sinful flesh, through which he is said to have been made sin. Then, when he offered him for sins, he made him sin. For the victim offered in the Law for sins was called sin.

Christ therefore did not do sin, as the prophet says, but manifestly he himself was made sin, as has been shown. But what is it to offer Christ for us, except to give power to those who wished to kill him? Which was granted for this reason, because it was going to be profitable, so that it might be the cause of his descending to the lower regions to despoil hell of the souls. For it is a superabundant and unheard-of sin to have killed him who not only had in no way sinned, but had also given life to many. The devil, made guilty by this sin, lost his boldness to contradict. For another similar thing is read in the epistle to the Galatians; for among other things: he was made, he says, a curse for us. Made by whom, if not by the Father? For by the judgment of God the cross of Christ is the curse of the Jews; for the death of the Savior cries out their sin. He therefore permitted himself to be fixed to the cross, so that his passion might profit us, so that, going forth from here with his sign, we might in no wise be held by the second death. For death fears even his servants, by whom it was conquered.

LXXVIIII. (LXXV). WHY THE SAVIOR PAID THE DIDRACHMA ONLY FOR HIMSELF AND PETER, AND NOT ALSO FOR THE REST OF THE APOSTLES, SEEING THAT THEY ALL FOLLOWED HIM WITH ALL THEIR OWN POSSESSIONS FORSAKEN?

The didrachma is understood to be a poll-tax on persons, not on estates. For the Savior possessed nothing in the world, although he is the Lord of the world, and when dead he is buried at another's expense; whereas we, to whom the world is foreign, desire to increase our resources, so that as we die we may attest — not only by voice but also by writing — that the world has been seized by us, so that by our own profession we may be condemned by him whose the world is. Hence the Lord: whoever, he says, does not forsake all things and follow me, cannot be my disciple. Whoever therefore so holds his resources that he does not place his hope in them, but is ready to cast them away for the faith, walks the way by which one goes to Christ our Lord.

The didrachma, therefore, was to be exacted from those who were conducting some business or devoting their labor to the crafts. But the Savior, who cared for none of these things, nor his disciples, was by no means one from whom it ought to be exacted. But because the hostile devil was always lying in wait, seeking an occasion, to see whether he might be able to bring down the Savior, he took hold of the minds of the collectors of the didrachma, so that they should do his will — namely, that coming to Peter, who was first among the apostles, they should say that the didrachma had to be paid by their master, though they were free from these burdens (for they were doing nothing in the world that belonged to the world) — so that, since there was no source from which he might pay, either he might be a stumbling-block to them, or at least by the lowliness of a subsidy he might seek someone from whom it might be paid. Then the Lord, in order to show that the devil is improvident and always contrives even against his own self, orders the apostle Peter to go to the sea and to open the mouth of the caught fish, and to find there the amount owed for the exaction; and when this was paid, he not only would not be a stumbling-block to the collectors, nor be brought down through the aid he had had to seek in order to pay, but would also display a sign of the greatest power, through which he might draw to himself those captured by the devil, so that the devil might be tormented by his own scheme and cunning. The collectors of the didrachma therefore say to the apostle Peter: does your master not pay the didrachma? By saying this, they confronted the master, so that he might pay on behalf of all the disciples.

But when the Savior orders it to be given for himself and Peter, he is seen to have paid for all, because, just as in the Savior all were included by reason of his mastership, so also after the Savior all are contained in Peter. For he appointed him to be their head, that he might be the shepherd of the Lord's flock. For among other things he says to the disciples: watch and pray, lest you enter into temptation; and to Peter he says: behold, Satan has demanded to sift you like wheat; but I have prayed for you, that your faith fail not, and you, when at length you are converted, strengthen your brethren. Why is there any doubt? He was praying for Peter, and was he not praying for James and John, to say nothing of the rest? It is manifest that all are contained in Peter, because in another place also he says: I pray for these whom you have given me, and: I will that where I am they also may be with me.

Now let us see what the didrachma is, that is, what measure of weight or of quantity it is, or in what substance * * * * * * * *

LXXX. SURELY ONE IS EITHER A SON OF GOD OR OF THE DEVIL: THEREFORE ONE IS ALWAYS A SON, BUT SOMETIMES OF GOD, SOMETIMES OF THE DEVIL. WHY THEN WE ARE BORN MUST BE INQUIRED INTO.

The Lord, since the children of Israel often transgressed after being led out of Egypt, being angry, gave a sentence saying that none of them would enter into the land of promise except Caleb and Joshua the son of Nun, who was earlier called Auses. But those who had been born in the desert, these he said would enter, because they knew not good or evil. Now, not to know good or evil is a certain natural simplicity, which has been instructed neither toward good nor toward evil, that is, an ignorance without malice. For we are born without understanding, but our nature has this good, that it is capable of learning the truth. But the son of the devil is he who, being born, is steeped in evil things, so that he pursues those things which are hostile to the Creator, asserting many gods and that sacrifice must be made to them as rulers of the world. If this one comes to his senses and, thinking according to his nature, betakes himself to the Creator, he will be a son of God.

And through this, those who are born are neither sons of God nor of the devil. For what does the Savior say to the Jews: you are of your father the devil, and the desires of your father you wish to do? You see, therefore, that men are made sons of the devil by their works and their profession; but that those are sons of God who, confessing that God is the proper Father of Christ, conduct themselves rightly. For thus God established our race, that, though born without understanding, we nevertheless have the possibility of learning either good things or evil, so that we ourselves may acquire for ourselves either evils or goods, so that we may rejoice in ourselves when, acting rightly, we are rewarded, or may impute it to ourselves, if, following depraved things, we are condemned.

LXXXI. THE APOSTLE SAYS: "WE ARE JEWS BY NATURE." HE SHOWS THEREFORE THAT JEWS ARE BORN OF JEWS — I DO NOT SPEAK OF PROSELYTES, WHO, IT IS AGREED, BECOME JEWS. FINALLY, THOSE WHO WERE BORN IN THE WILDERNESS WERE NOT CIRCUMCISED —: IF THEREFORE JEWS ARE BORN OF JEWS, WHY NOT ALSO CHRISTIANS OF CHRISTIANS? FOR PAGANS TOO ARE BORN OF PAGANS.

Not everything that is born is that which it is born from, or that from which it is born. For gold too is born from the earth, and yet it is not earth; and gems, and many other things; and food is born from wood, and an egg from a chicken. But as to why pagans are born from pagans, this is the cause: because every pagan is in ignorance, and he who is born from such a one is without understanding, both are ignorant. But there is this difference between the two: that the begetter is without doubt a blasphemer, and thereby a son of the devil; whereas the newborn infant does not know how to blaspheme, just as it does not know how to bless; yet it is a pagan, because it is without understanding. But from a Jew a Jew is born for this reason, because he is begotten under a devotion to the Creator, just as Adam said of Cain: I have brought forth a man through God. For just as pagans, undevoted to the Creator, give thanks to demons in all things that they do, so too the Jews, as suppliants of God the Creator, praise him in every act of theirs — despicable in this, that they do not know Christ, through whom are all things. In this way too a Jew is born from a Jew; for it is not, as it seems to some, because circumcision makes a Jew. For circumcision is a sign of Judaism, not Judaism itself, since circumcision is not righteousness in Abraham, but a sign of righteousness, so that those born of Abraham might have a sign by which they might be proved to be sons of Abraham — inasmuch as they were called by this name from Judas Maccabaeus, so that not only those of Judah, but also the rest, from all the sons of Jacob, were called Jews, because Judas was a leader to them. And therefore those born as Jews receive the sign, so that they may be understood to be what they are.

. 2. But a Christian is not born from a Christian, for this reason, because the very one who begets was not born, but made a Christian. Wherefore this one too, who is born, must be made, in order to be one. For he is not born through substance, but through faith, which comes to those already born. For ignorance is born with the flesh, but faith is afterward implanted by spiritual reasoning. But it is objected on the contrary: 'if indeed the one who begets was made, he ought now to beget that which he himself is, so that the dignity received might make a lineage. For senators beget senators.' But the dignity of senators has no merit with God, nor does nature itself, that is, substance, obtain the benefit, but the dignity turns on reputation and talk alone. And thereby the talk indeed runs on through the lineage of the flesh, but confers nothing other than an opinion of dignity, just as those too who are consuls or are honored with statues rejoice in vain. But when men become Christians, dignity comes to nature itself, so that the very essence of man may have the eternal benefit of incorruptibility, so that it may be not by opinion alone, but also in effect with God. This is like the case of an eloquent poor man who is in high praise and yet lacks bread or the other necessities. For this is true praise, if the dignity that is sought so profits him that he lacks nothing, while this is granted to his nature, that through itself it may live and live well with no support required; this is truly to become rich and glorious.

LXXXII. THAT THE PAGANS ARE SUBJECT TO THE ELEMENTS IS DOUBTED BY NO ONE: WHAT THEN IS IT THAT THE APOSTLE SAYS: 'WE TOO WERE IN BONDAGE UNDER THE ELEMENTS OF THIS WORLD'? IF THEREFORE THE JEWS ALSO WERE IN BONDAGE TO THE ELEMENTS, HOW DID THEY DIFFER FROM THE PAGANS?

It is evident that by the addition or subtraction or alteration of a syllable the sense is perverted. For this reason it is known to all that the pagans worship the elements, but the Jews served God not by means of the elements, but 'under the elements,' on account of the new moons and sabbaths and circumcision and other such things. For these are carnal; for whatever is visible is carnal and belongs to the elements. But because it was done at God's command, God was being served, so that one might come from the visible to the invisible and, having made progress out of this care, might attain to grasping greater things, so that, being trained in these things, they might be made worthy of the spiritual promises. In that part, therefore, which I mentioned above, the Jews served under the elements; from the other part they had a spiritual law, which both forbids sinning and exhorts to the love of the Lord God, who had been promised to them as one who would come to remit their sins.

LXXXIII. IF THROUGH CHRIST THERE IS SALVATION AND TRUE AND PERFECT KNOWLEDGE, WHY DID HE NOT COME EARLIER, SO THAT OUR FOREBEARS TOO, WHO WERE IN IGNORANCE, MIGHT LEARN THE TRUTH? INDEED, AFTER THE COMING OF CHRIST MANY MORE HAVE BEEN SAVED THAN BEFORE. WHENCE, IF HE HAD COME EARLIER, MANY MORE WOULD HAVE BEEN SAVED. IF THEN IT IS SO, IT SEEMS BLAMEWORTHY.

No slander ought to be brought against one who shows mercy. For it is in the power of the giver, when and how much he wishes to have mercy. For one of this kind ought to be exhorted and stirred by prayers, not called back from a good work by contention. Perhaps there would be no objection, if for this mercy some return were made to him. Would anyone find fault with a physician who expends his medicines and his service for free, or will his slowness be accused by those from whom he was not sought out?

These things indeed we have said according to reason, but the goodness of the Savior does not concern itself with these things on account of human weakness; for it follows its own nature in showing mercy. Therefore he came then, when he ought to have come, following the reasoning of his own will, not of our merits. For if you should weigh our merits, he ought not to have come. He came therefore when he knew both that help ought to be given and that the benefit would be welcome. For if help be given beforehand to one upon whom necessity presses, it will indeed be welcome, yet he will not be very able to know what has been bestowed upon him; but if you bring aid to one placed in the very midst of necessity, he will know what kind of benefit he has obtained. For one rescued from tribulation will render greater thanks, just as if you offer bread to one who is hungry; but if you offer it when he is not hungry, it will certainly not be so welcome.

Therefore it was rightly done that the Lord did not come before. For first he left the human race to make use of its own will, yet not without a witness of himself, because from the very fabric of the world and the yearly gifts, which he decreed should be furnished for human uses through the ministry of the elements, he willed that his own divinity be understood and feared, seeing that even from the propagation of the first man, and then of Enoch and Noah, knowledge of him was upon the earth. But when, through a certain languor of the human race, the knowledge of God had begun to grow obsolete among men and their morals were being changed, he deigned to choose Abraham, in whom there might be a pattern of renewed knowledge of God and of morals. And since reverence was still too sluggish, afterward through Moses he gave the law in writing, which would neither grow obsolete and would strike greater fear. For the fear is greater where the authority is manifest. And because, once the law was received, the nations spurned it, not subjecting themselves to it, nor did those who received it keep it, the Lord, moved by mercy, sent his Son, who, offering himself for them and destroying death, once remission of sins had been granted to all, might offer them justified to God the Father; for it would not have been possible to descend to the lower regions except through death. For neither order nor reason demanded that he who had not died should enter the gates of Tartarus.

Is it not the case that, if someone wishes in his own land to deceive barbarians, he changes himself into their dress, so that he may be thought one of them, and thus he explores in what way he may overthrow them? So it was done also to the devil. Since by an evil practice he had overthrown man, so that he might remain in death, a remedy was found by which he might lose those whom he was wrongfully holding: namely, that one might steal in upon him, and that the one whom he supposed he had killed as a man he might find, among the lower regions, to be God. And when this was done, being found guilty he lost all whom he was holding, and thenceforth the way to heaven was opened.

Behold how much the coming of the Savior profited, so that men, knowing from what peril they have been freed, might without ceasing render thanks to him! But perhaps it may be said, on the contrary: 'If many before the law and after the law, pressed down by sins, gave themselves wholly to the flesh and were worthy to remain in Tartarus, without doubt there were those who by devotion and reverence for the Creator bridled their life by the law of nature. Were not these too detained among the lower regions when they were discharged from this life? But if he had come before, after the death of Adam, with Adam freed—who had sinned first—he would have opened the way to heaven for the rest, so that, because they had lived well, having acknowledged the Creator, the deceased might be received into heaven. It is therefore a loss that Christ did not come before.' That it is a loss is manifest, but ask whether it is just or unjust. For losses are wont to be just, concerning which it is not permitted to complain. Did the thief, caught and condemned according to the law to fourfold, dare to complain? And through this, the loss which the human race suffered was justly done, wherefore it was also permitted.

Wherefore the Savior also ought not to have come at the very beginning. For, so that the devil might not grow proud, a man made by God was set against him as an enemy, in order that, fortified by God's aids—since he was unequal—he might resist him, having received a command. But the devil, with his customary subtlety, pretended not to know what had been commanded by God, and found Eve unstable; and afterwards, promising her that, if they should touch what was forbidden, he would confer divinity upon them, he circumvented them. Then the devil, having overcome man, triumphed; the latter, being conquered, subjected his own race to sin. For which reason it was unjust to take away the spoils from the victor by force, because God does justly whatever he does—and besides, since man had sinned grievously. For by consenting, against the command of the Creator, that he should become a god, he committed idolatry, whereby he sinned against God; and therefore he ought not to have been succored at once, seeing that he had not even repented. For the mercy would not have been welcome, had he not known what he deserved. For although God can do all things, yet he does that which accords with reason, so that he may persevere blameless.

LXXXIIII. WHY, WHILE OBSERVING THE COURSE OF THE MOON IN THE RECKONING OF EASTER, DO WE REPROACH THE PAGANS FOR OBSERVING LUNAR DAYS AND MEASURE?

Far be it that Christians should be said to venerate the stars—Christians, to whom a spiritual worship has been granted, so that, despising the things which are seen, they may be joined to those things which are invisible and supercelestial, so as to transcend all these things, being destined to be with the angels of God. For the worshipers of the stars will either be with those very stars or below them. For how can it come about that anyone should be above the things which he worships? And so the pagans do not observe the computation of the moon, but venerate the moon itself as a goddess, and, supposing that they have grasped the effects of its courses, what is to be done on certain days and what is to be avoided, they have determined to subject their life and conduct in a certain order to its dominion—and these, because they have dared to usurp these things outside the ordination of God, we frequently catch out in error. For God established these luminaries for signs of the seasons, whence we too keep the number of the moon, but do not worship it itself: so that from the fourteenth moon, which according to the law is for us the first, we may observe the reckoning of Easter. For God instituted all things full; and therefore, from the fourteenth up to the twenty-first, in these seven days it has been granted to us to celebrate Easter, so that of these seven some days may be enclosed from the Preparation up to the Lord's resurrection, so that neither the thirteenth may fall on the Passion, nor the fourteenth on the resurrection, nor the fifteenth—lest the Passion of Christ be before the first moon according to us, nor his resurrection be on any but the first day, on which the world was begun.

For all the times of the world are numbered by the courses of a single week, in which God composed the members and adornments of the world. For in six days he fitted out the world, and on the seventh he rested, which he called the Sabbath. For in these seven days the reckoning and number of the whole world is contained. For always returning into themselves they multiply the number of the times. For after the Sabbath it always begins again from the first day up to the seventh day, that is, the Sabbath, so that it may be learned that the resurrection of the Lord took place on the first day of the world's beginning, which day is called the Lord's Day. For the Lord made that one day, which in the evening, having unrolled into the sevenfold number, begins again, so that after the week it is the first. And this was so decreed from the beginning on account of the mystery of the Lord's incarnation and passion and resurrection. For in the body of the Lord almost the whole world rose again and was restored.

It is settled that we do not worship the moon, but that we keep the number which was established through the course of the moon. But the devil, who is Satan, in order that he might be able to lend some authority to his deceit and to color his lies with a fabricated truth, in the first month in which he knows that the Lord's mysteries must be celebrated—because he is of no mean power—established for the pagans mysteries that they should observe, so that he might hold their souls in error for two reasons: that, because the deceit comes before the truth, the truth might seem to be the deceit, as though prejudging the truth by its antiquity; and because in the first month, in which the Romans have the equinox, just as we also do, that very observance is kept by them, so that they too say that expiation is made through blood, just as we also say it is made through the cross. By this cunning he holds the pagans in error, so that they suppose our truth seems an imitation rather than the truth, as though discovered through emulation by a certain superstition. 'For that cannot', they say, 'be reckoned true which was discovered afterwards'. But because among us there is truth for certain, and this truth exists from the beginning, the signs of miracles and prodigies furnish testimony, so that, with miracle as witness, the wickedness of the devil may be made known. For since it alone is that which easily persuades men of these things, it is set against the cunning and the anticipation of the devil, so that it may reveal his pretense. For no one, even among enemies, would dare to deny that there is truth where miracle appears.

LXXXV. HOW IS IT THAT, ALTHOUGH IT IS ESTABLISHED THAT FROM DAVID DOWN TO THE DEPORTATION OF BABYLON THERE ARE SEVENTEEN GENERATIONS, THE EVANGELIST SAYS FOURTEEN, AHAZIAH BEING PASSED OVER, WHO IS AFTER JORAM THE SON OF JEHOSHAPHAT, AND JOASH THE SON OF AHAZIAH, AND AMAZIAH THE SON OF JOASH?

It should seem credible that the evangelist followed nothing but the sense of the law, and therefore not without reason were these men removed by the evangelist from the number of the rest. For the impiety of these men ran on in such continuity that their wickedness had no interval. From Joram, moreover, the wickedness that began ran on in this way all the way to Uzziah the son of Amaziah, so that there was no support on any side by which, for some part, on account of the father's merit, any one of them might remain in the number of the kings. For Joram, acting wickedly, was on account of Jehoshaphat's merit remitted into the number, and Uzziah, on account of Jotham's merit, was reserved in the number; but the wickedness of those three was continuous against God. Solomon too, on account of his father's merit, was remitted into the kingship, and Rehoboam the son of Solomon, acting wickedly, was on account of Asa's merit left in the number of the kings. But those three, acting wickedly, were shut in amid evils; therefore they were erased. For it is a worse example toward the ruin of the race when wickedness spreads itself out unbrokenly. And, to speak precisely: because Joseph is not from their line, they were passed over. For from Abraham the evangelist traced the generation of these, from whom Joseph draws his origin, to whom, betrothed, Mary bore Christ.

LXXXVI. WHAT IS IT THAT PROVES THAT MARY THE MOTHER OF THE LORD IS FROM THE TRIBE AND SEED OF DAVID?

We bring forward the words of a suitable witness. For the angel says to Mary among other things: and the Lord God shall give unto him the throne of David his father, and he shall reign in the house of Jacob for ever, and of his kingdom there shall be no end. How could David be called his father by a suitable witness, if Mary were not from the seed of David?

LXXXVII. IF GOD IS ONE, WHY IS THE HOPE OF SALVATION IN THREE, AND NOT IN TWO OR FOUR OR CERTAINLY IN THE ONE HIMSELF? AND WHY WAS THE TRINITY NOT PROCLAIMED FROM THE BEGINNING?

There is almost nothing new; for the Trinity is proclaimed in such a way that one God is believed. Nothing therefore has been added, but the mystery of the one God has been revealed. In the very first beginnings, however, it was not fitting that it be made manifest, because first a thing must be proclaimed and afterwards that which has been proclaimed must be revealed, so that you may know that there is a Father, from whom are all things, that the Son is so called, through whom are all things, and that the Holy Spirit is so named, through whom all things, which indeed are from the Father but through the Son, are regenerated unto faith in the one God. These therefore are indeed three, but there is one divinity. Reason therefore brings it about that they are three, not more nor fewer. For every number reaches up to nine, because the three, while they are mutually in one another, make in number nine, since in one there are three and three in one. For he who sees one, sees three, since one differs in nothing from the other. Three therefore are one and thrice three are one; that is, in the number nine there is perfection, so far as pertains to number, because from one are all things. For with one added — from which, or in which, the three that are counted are nine — they become ten or twenty. Yet one always comes up to nine and one is added, because nine are one, so that there may be the full perfection of unity.

LXXXVIII. IF THERE IS GREATER GRACE AND CLEARER UNDERSTANDING IN THE NEW THAN IN THE OLD, WHY DID THE PROPHET ISAIAH SEE THE LORD OF SABAOTH — WHO IS CHRIST, ACCORDING TO THE INTERPRETATION OF JOHN THE EVANGELIST — SEATED UPON THE THRONE OF MAJESTY (FOR HE SAID, AMONG OTHER THINGS: 'THESE THINGS SAID ISAIAH, WHEN HE SAW HIS MAJESTY AND SPOKE OF HIM'), WHEREAS IN THE NEW STEPHEN THE FIRST MARTYR SAYS THAT HE SAW JESUS STANDING AT THE RIGHT HAND OF GOD? WHAT IS THIS, THAT HERE HE SEEMS AS IT WERE A SUBJECT AFTER HIS TRIUMPHS, AND THERE AS IT WERE LORD, BEFORE HE HAD CONQUERED?

As the case presented itself, so too did the Lord show himself. For to the prophet he appeared as a king rebuking the people, and he showed himself to be what he was; for the cause of his deity was at peace. But that he should appear standing to Stephen, the slander of the Jews brought about. In Stephen's case the cause of the Savior was suffering violence; therefore, while God the judge was seated, he appeared standing, as one who was pleading a case, and because his cause is good, he was at the right hand of the judge. For everyone who pleads a case must needs stand.

LXXXVIIII.

THE SAVIOR SAYS, AMONG OTHER THINGS, CONCERNING THE HOLY SPIRIT, THAT 'WHEN HE COMES,' HE SAYS, 'HE WILL CONVICT THE WORLD OF SIN AND OF RIGHTEOUSNESS AND OF JUDGMENT: OF SIN INDEED, BECAUSE THEY DO NOT BELIEVE IN ME; OF RIGHTEOUSNESS TRULY, BECAUSE I GO TO THE FATHER AND YOU WILL SEE ME NO MORE; BUT OF JUDGMENT, BECAUSE THE PRINCE OF THIS WORLD HAS BEEN JUDGED.' HE SEEMS INDEED TO HAVE STATED THE REASON FOR CONVICTING THE WORLD, BUT IT NEEDS EXPLANATION.

Since the Jews did not believe the Savior, nor did the worldly powers — for he willed to manifest himself not only to men, but also to the princes and powers in the heavenly places, as the apostle teaches in the epistle to the Ephesians —, he showed that after his passion the Holy Spirit would prove that he had spoken true things. And this is 'to convict the world': to show it that those things are true which it refused to believe. For it refused to believe that the Savior had come from God. But the Savior, righteousness being preserved, did not fear to return to him who had sent him, and by the fact that he returned, he proved that he had come from there, because no one ascends to God except him who descended from God. The powers, therefore, seeing him ascend, were confounded, seeing to be true what they had spurned as though false. And so that righteousness convicted them, by which it was proved just that he returned whence he had come. And thus he convicts them of sin, because they not only refused to believe him, but even killed him. But of judgment he rebuked them thus, when he showed the prince of the world made guilty and crushed by him, in whose faith they had no part. For seeing souls go from the lower regions into the heavens, they recognized that the prince of this world had been adjudged, so that, made guilty, he lost in the case of the Savior what he had held.

These things indeed were seen while the Savior was ascending, but they were made plain and openly manifest when the Holy Spirit came upon the disciples. For the true reproof took place at that time, when after the passion, as a testimony to the Savior, there were publicly seen by the faithless the dead rising again, the lame running, the lepers cleansed, the paralytics made firm, the blind seeing, the deaf hearing, the mute speaking, those possessed by demons purged, and the sick, having recovered their health, giving thanks. In this manner the Holy Spirit convicted the world, because in the name of the Savior, who had been rejected by the world, it worked the powers of all healings.

XC. IF THE DEVIL HIMSELF IS SATAN, HOW IS IT THAT, AMONG OTHER THINGS, THE SAVIOR SAYS TO THE JEWS: 'YOU ARE OF YOUR FATHER THE DEVIL, AND YOU WISH TO DO THE DESIRES OF YOUR FATHER. HE WAS A MURDERER FROM THE BEGINNING, AND HE DID NOT STAND IN THE TRUTH, BECAUSE THE TRUTH IS NOT IN HIM. WHEN HE SPEAKS A LIE, HE SPEAKS FROM HIS OWN, BECAUSE HE IS A LIAR, AS IS ALSO HIS FATHER'?

"Devil" is not a proper name, but a common one. For in whomever the works of the devil are found, without doubt he must be called a devil. For it is the name of a work, not of a nature. And so in this place he signifies Cain as the father of the Jews, whose imitators the Jews, wishing to be, put the Savior to death. He says that this one, because he defiled himself with parricide and made himself guilty of death, did not stand in the truth. And from him the pattern of fratricide was given. He says that this very one, when he speaks a lie, speaks from his own, so as to show that each single person sins by nothing but his own will. But because he is an imitator of the devil, he added: because he is a liar, as is also his father. For this one, in order to condemn the first man to death, pretended that he did not know what had been commanded by God. Similarly Cain too, when questioned, feigned that he did not know where his brother Abel was, whom he had killed. Therefore in this place he called Cain a devil, but his father the devil, whose works he followed; for the son of the devil is a devil. But that devil, who is also Satan, has no father in his own malice; for he is himself the author to himself in evil. For he himself sinned first, and through this whoever have imitated him will be called his sons, and he their father. For indeed we have Abraham as our father in faith, because he himself first believed God, and through this we are reckoned by his name; for we are called faithful just as he too was.

XCI. IT MUST BE ASKED IN WHAT WAY, WITHOUT ARGUMENTS, PHOTINUS IS TO BE CONTRADICTED THAT CHRIST EXISTS BEFORE MARY.

Let us first inquire of John the Baptist, whom the Savior himself declared to be his witness. For he says, among other things, concerning the Lord: He who comes from above is above all; he who is of the earth is of the earth and speaks of the earth; but he who comes from heaven, what he has seen and heard, to that he testifies, and no one receives his testimony. Behold, John calls all men together with himself earthly by comparison with the Lord, because he testifies that the Lord came from the heavens, but that all the rest are of the earth, that is, from lower things. And the Lord himself speaks the same things among others, saying: For I have not come down from heaven to do my own will, but the will of him who sent me, the Father's. And again the Lord speaks among other things to this effect, saying: And now I say to you that I will ask the Father. For the Father himself loves you, because you have loved me and have believed that I went forth from God the Father and came into this world; again I leave the world and go to the Father. Then the disciples, seeing that he had spoken plainly, say to him: Behold, now you speak openly and utter no proverb; now we know that you know all things, and you have no need that anyone should question you; in this we believe, that you went forth from God. These things need no interpretation, since this indeed is the sum of our religion: that we believe Christ, before the flesh, to have been in the heavens with God as a son with the Father.

For if it was on account of an upright manner of life and heavenly teaching that he said he had gone forth from heaven and from God and had come into the world, then all the prophets and apostles ought to have said the same things about themselves. And because he said, again I leave the world and go to the Father, perhaps it may be claimed that he signified by this that the teaching, which had come from God, having left the world, returned to God. For they have nothing else to say. For if his having come from heaven and having gone forth from God and having come into this world pertains to the cause of virtue and teaching, not to the person of the Savior, when he says, again I leave the world and go to the Father, then he returns thither whence he had come; but we see that after the Lord's departure both virtue and teaching were in the world—you see therefore that it was not of virtue and teaching that the Lord spoke, but that the Lord spoke specifically of himself.

But that if you say the words belong to the power that worked in Christ, this same power calls God its Father. See, you run into the very thing you fear, because if the power of God is the Son of God, it is assuredly that power of which the Apostle says: Christ the power of God and the wisdom of God; this came forth from God and again returned to God. And so this same power, which is Christ, testifies that it casts out demons by the Spirit of God. This Spirit worked through the apostles, so that you may know that the Holy Spirit, through whom the Lord worked, after the Lord's departure worked here in the world in the apostles, so that it may appear more fully that the Lord spoke concerning his own person, because, having left the world, he returned to God. Or if you think that this power is not Christ, there will be two powers and two sons of God; and where is that which is read: the only-begotten Son of God, who is in the bosom of the Father, and again: for so, he says, God loved this world, that he gave his only Son for it? You now perceive that the power of God, which worked embodied in the world, is itself one — which is called both the power of God and the Son. And let us see what the apostles believed, or in whom, when they heard from the Lord: Behold, again I leave the world and go to the Father, they answered him: Behold, now you speak plainly and speak no proverb; now we know that you know all things, and there is no need for you that anyone should question you; in this we believe, that you came forth from God.

Now let us see whether the disciples were in doubt about the mighty works — whether they were from God, when they saw Lazarus, already stinking on the fourth day, raised up, the eyes of a man blind from birth restored, a woman freed from an issue of blood by the touch of the hem, the nature of water changed into wine — or about the person of the Lord. Plainly no one doubted about the mighty works, seeing that some said he was Elijah, others Jeremiah or one of the prophets. For when the Jews wished to stone him, Jesus answered them: Many good works have I shown you from my Father; for which of these works do you stone me? The Jews said to him: For a good work we do not stone you, but for blasphemy, because, being a man, you make yourself God. If therefore the Jews were not in doubt that they were the works of God, were the apostles in doubt?

Since, therefore, every doubt concerned the person of the Savior — for it was an unheard-of thing, one that had not burst into human comprehension, that struck men with amazement when they heard him saying that he had come forth from God and that God was his own Father —, for that reason the disciples, confessing that they had been satisfied by the plain declaration of the Lord's words, said to him: now we know that you know all things, and there is no need for anyone to question you; in this we believe, that you came forth from God. For if he did not truly come forth from God, the faith of the apostles is emptied out; but this cannot be, because it was approved by the Savior. For he answered them: do you now believe? — that is, "seeing such great signs, you did not believe." And so it was proven to the apostles that Christ had come forth from God and had come, so that through this coming-forth it should not be doubtful to believe that this one is the Son of God. For if no one has seen God, except him who is from God — this one has seen God, and if no one knows the Father except the Son — this is to have truly been with God and to have come forth from him and to have come; for no other could come forth from God, except this one who in the beginning was with God, since not any other knew or had seen God —, therefore it is not incredible for us to believe this one to be the true Son of God, just as John said in his first epistle: and we are, he says, in his true Son Jesus Christ. This is the true God and eternal life, and the apostle: who did not spare, he says, his own Son, and the evangelist: because he said, he says, that God was his own Father.

If, therefore, Christ is the true Son of God, how is he only a man? For he would not be called true, unless he were properly begotten from him. Or let Photinus say why he is believed to be truly the Son of God, if he is not true. Or what need was there to believe this one the Son of God, if he were one of the rest of the saints, who are worthy to be called sons of God? Or is there perhaps some unworthiness in this one, on account of which he could not be believed to be a son of God as the rest are, and is it therefore said, in order that this one, about whom it seems there is unbelief, may be believed to be the Son of God? For if he is more powerful than the rest, what need is there for it to be said, "believe that Christ is the Son of God," unless because it is commanded that he be believed otherwise than the rest — so that, since there are many who are called sons of God for the sake of holiness, this one alone may be believed the true Son of God, whence he is also called the only-begotten? For which of the saints dared to call himself a son of God — I will not even say a true one — except the Savior, aware of his own nativity that had proceeded from God? And how would he allow himself to be adored, if he did not know himself to be from God, since it is written: you shall adore the Lord your God and him alone shall you serve — seeing that the angel rebuked John, because he wished to adore him, saying to him: do not do it, for I am your fellow servant; adore God — and, since scripture testifies that God alone is to be served, the apostle: he who serves, he says, Christ, pleases God? Why? Because God and Christ, the Father and the Son, are one.

The Apostle acknowledges himself to be a servant of Christ, and he says to the Corinthians: do not become servants of men, and to the Galatians: Paul an apostle, not from men nor through man, but through Jesus Christ and God the Father, who raised him from the dead. He shows openly that Christ is God and man, so that he might demonstrate that his apostleship is not from man but from Christ, inasmuch as he is God, and from God the Father. And so in what follows he says: for neither did I receive it from man nor did I learn it, but through the revelation of Jesus Christ. What could be so plain? For he says that he attained the teaching of God not through man, but was taught by God himself. For we have received the teaching of God through men, but the vessel of election proves that he attained nothing from the apostles, that is, from men. For if, because it is the teaching of God, it is on that account not learned through man, what praise of his own does the Apostle attest, if he learned it in the same way as the rest of the peoples?

To what end also does he say: neither did I receive it from man nor was I taught it, but through the revelation of Jesus Christ? Who would doubt that in this place Christ is signified as God? But what wonder is this, when among other things he says to the Romans: whose fathers, and of whom is Christ according to the flesh, who is over all things God, blessed forever? What does 'and of whom is Christ according to the flesh' mean? For he would not say this, unless he were also according to God, so that according to the flesh Christ is from the fathers, but according to divinity Christ is from God, over all things God. 'But perhaps it may be said to pertain to the person of the Father.' Yet in this place there is no mention of the Father's name, and therefore, if it is denied to be said of Christ, let the person to whom it belongs be supplied. But why should it not belong to Christ, at whose name things heavenly and earthly and infernal bend the knee? This is to be God over all things; for besides these there are no others. Let Photinus now say whether these bend the knee to a man, or whether God decreed that those heavenly powers and holy angels should adore a man. But far be it, since God decrees nothing foolish, especially since it is not permitted for anyone but God to be adored; how much more in the heavens! For a king is adored on earth as the vicar of God, but Christ, after the office of vicar has been fulfilled in the dispensation, is adored in the heavens and on the earth.

For we read that the Word was made flesh, that is, that it was incarnated from Mary with the Holy Spirit as minister, and that this Word was in the beginning with God and was God, and that this too is called the Son of God. How then does that which was in the beginning and was God not exist before Mary? Even if, being blinded, you deny that this Word is God, nevertheless you do not deny nor are you ignorant that this Word is called the Son of God and that this was with God in the beginning. By what reasoning, then, do you say that it exists after Mary, which you read existed in the beginning? For in the Apocalypse we read concerning Christ: and his name is the Word of God, which word has many interpretations. For there is nothing that is of God which is not called God, but on account of the authority of the one God he called that which was born of him the Word, so that, led by this reasoning — by which we know that our words are not except from ourselves — we might believe that Christ is from God.

Nor indeed ought it to have been put otherwise, so that he might be believed to be from him and not outside of him. For in a similar way he is also called the power and wisdom of God, so that by the same reasoning he may be understood to be from him. For if he had said (God and God), he would not have signified that he was from himself, but he would have made two gods, which is contrary to unity. This is the reasoning in the divine books, that God who is signified, Christ, does not exceed the measure of the one God.

But how foolish is that notion of yours, Photinus, in that you so punctuate at the head of John's Gospel, that the Word may not be called God? For thus you read: In the beginning was the Word, and the Word was with God, and God was. This Word in the beginning was with God. What does this punctuation want for itself? What sense does it indicate? What rationale of piety does it signify, except only enmity against the Son of God? For what is the significance of one declaring: And the Word was with God, and God was? Is this then the wisdom of our law, to teach that there is a God? What tongue is there, what nation, what land, what sect, that would deny that God exists? And to what purpose does it say: And the Word was with God? Or what does this "Word" wish to be understood, unless it shows his rationale? For the Gospel is not written on account of God, about whom no one doubts, but on account of the Word about whom questions arise. For it is a mystery of God, which is therefore believed because it is not manifest to all. And therefore Scripture strives to teach what the Word is, saying: And the Word was with God, and the Word was God. This was in the beginning with God. This both agrees with reason and the declaration is sound. For it shows that this Word, which in the beginning was with God, was God, so that in the sacrament of the mystery of the eternal God, which was unknown from ages and generations, God the Word may be believed to have been with God the Father.

And because it comes into doubt among the carnal, who wish Scripture to speak according to the capacity of their own understanding, faith has been established, which, following the sense of Scripture, may merit a reward. To Solomon, when he had asked wisdom from God, this response was given by the Lord: Behold, he said, I have given you a wise and prudent heart, such as there was not before you, and after you there shall not arise a man like you. What shall we say? Is what God promised true? Indeed truly. Therefore no man shall be like Solomon. And what will it seem concerning Christ, who among other things says: The queen of the south came from the ends of the earth to hear the wisdom of Solomon: and behold, a greater than Solomon is here? Now choose whom you believe, Photinus, God or Christ, the Father or the Son. If you believe the Father, you accuse the Son; if you believe the Son, you accuse the Father. For if Christ is only a man, in vain has he set himself before Solomon against the promise of God; but if he is understood to be also God, worthily has he set himself before Solomon and the promise of God is undefiled, because no man has arisen like Solomon. For Christ shows himself to be superior for this reason, because he is God.

O the madness of Photinus, who refuses to confess Christ before Mary, though he hears with his own ears the one testifying that he exists before Abraham! For when the Jews were disputing with him about time, saying: You are not yet fifty years old, and have you seen Abraham? he answered them: Amen I say to you, before Abraham was, I am. He did not say, 'I am better,' but answered to what was asked, because he existed before the Jews supposed. Clearly Photinus cannot escape the curse of the law, because he places his hope in Christ, whom he calls a mere man, since he reads: Cursed is the man who has hope in man. But the apostle, knowing Christ to be God and therefore that hope is in him both in the present and in the future, says: If in this life only we are hoping in Christ, we are more miserable than all men. Therefore he in whom we are taught to have hope is not a mere man, because hope in man is vain. These few things we have said out of many; for it would suffice, if for a Photinus of good mind we had set down this last point alone.

XCII. IN WHAT SENSE IS IT UNDERSTOOD WHAT THE SAVIOR SAYS: 'PEACE I GIVE TO YOU, MY PEACE I LEAVE TO YOU; NOT AS THIS WORLD GIVES, DO I GIVE TO YOU'?

He who receives the peace of the Savior will incur the enmity of the world. For unless he has fallen into discord with the devil, he will not have peace with Christ. For no one can serve two masters. He therefore is at discord with the world who faithfully serves the law of God; fortified by the peace of Christ he crushes the darts of all his adversaries. For who would dare against him whom he knows to be a friend of the king? Yet the world gives peace otherwise than the Savior promised to give it. For the world gives peace either out of fear or out of entreaty; but the Savior, since he is stronger than all and certainly fears no one, gives peace unasked. This peace, therefore, is salutary, which is given for this reason, that it may be a fortification against enemies. Peace indeed is called by one name, but that of God and that of the world are distinguished by a wide interval. For the one is fragile, the other strong; the one carnal, the other spiritual; the one earthly, the other heavenly; the one is of necessity, the other of will. For Christ, needing no one, offers peace to the weak and the unarmed, the Lord to servants, the good to the wicked, God to men.

Rightly therefore he says: not as this world gives, do I give to you; for in saying this he wished to be understood the clemency of his good will. For whereas everyone gives peace in order to profit himself, this one gives it for this end, that it may profit not himself but those to whom he gives. Otherwise, then, the world gives peace than the Savior gave it, and the peace of the world does not teach a good life, does not urge toward patience, does not provoke to justice, does not exhort to mercy, does not promise eternal life. For whoever receives the peace of Christ will be a stranger to the vices of this world, which war against the soul.

XCIII. IT MUST BE ASKED WHETHER THE APOSTLES HAD THE HOLY SPIRIT AT THAT TIME WHEN THEY WERE WITH THE LORD, SINCE AMONG MANY THINGS THE EVANGELIST SAYS: 'THE SPIRIT,' HE SAYS, 'HAD NOT YET BEEN GIVEN, BECAUSE JESUS HAD NOT YET BEEN GLORIFIED,' AND IN ANOTHER PLACE: 'IF YOU LOVE ME,' HE SAYS, 'KEEP MY COMMANDMENTS, AND I WILL ASK THE FATHER, AND HE WILL GIVE YOU ANOTHER PARACLETE, THAT HE MAY BE WITH YOU FOREVER, THE SPIRIT OF TRUTH, WHOM THE WORLD CANNOT RECEIVE, BECAUSE IT DOES NOT SEE NOR KNOW HIM; YOU SEE HIM AND KNOW HIM, BECAUSE HE ABIDES WITH YOU AND IS WITH YOU.' WHAT IS THIS? HE DENIES THAT THE SPIRIT WAS GIVEN BEFORE THE PASSION, BUT HE PROMISES THAT HE HIMSELF WILL ASK THE FATHER TO SEND HIM. LIKEWISE HE ADDS THAT HE WAS WITH THEM AND ABODE AMONG THEM, BUT AFTER THE RESURRECTION HE IS READ TO HAVE BREATHED UPON THEM AND SAID TO THEM: 'RECEIVE THE HOLY SPIRIT,' AND AT PENTECOST THE HOLY SPIRIT IS READ TO HAVE DESCENDED UPON THE APOSTLES. I SEE SUCH GREAT CONFUSIONS IN THIS MATTER THAT I DO NOT KNOW WHAT OUGHT TO BE HELD.

In all these things which have been set forth there is not one single explanation. For the forms of the individual explanations have been indicated briefly, because the Spirit is indeed one, but has many gifts. When, therefore, the Holy Spirit is read of, his office too, in which he is signified, ought to be understood. For that he both was with them and was going to come is not false, but only if you refer this not to the person but to the nature. For Christ promised that another would come from the Father, so that, because their divinity is undifferentiated, in the presence of Christ the Holy Spirit might not be thought absent, and in the coming and appearing of the Holy Spirit Christ too might be reckoned present. Therefore, although he promises that he will come, he says: you see him, because he abides with you and is with you — so that the apostles might be secure, because after the Lord's ascension they were not going to have a lesser or degenerate shepherd or king for their protection. For there is no doubt that the Spirit was not given to believers except, as the evangelist testifies, after the Lord, rising with death overcome, was glorified. For they are the words of the Lord himself, triumphing and saying: 'John indeed baptized with water, but you shall be baptized with the Holy Spirit, whom you are also going to receive not after these many days.' At this time, when he had arranged to ascend to the Father, he seems to have spoken this.

Now that thing which the Lord is read to have breathed upon the disciples a few days after his resurrection, and to have said, Receive the Holy Spirit, is to be understood as ecclesiastical power. For because all things in the Lord's tradition are carried out through the Holy Spirit, therefore, when the rule and form of this kind of discipline is handed over to them, it is said to them, Receive the Holy Spirit. And because it truly pertains to ecclesiastical law, he at once added, saying, Whosoever's sins you shall retain, they shall be retained; if you shall remit anyone's, they shall be remitted unto them. This inspiration, therefore, is a certain grace which is poured into the ordained through the tradition, by which they may be held in higher esteem. Whence the Apostle says to Timothy: Do not, he says, neglect the grace which is in you, which was given to you through the imposition of the hands of the presbytery. It behooved, therefore, that it be done once, so that thenceforth this tradition might be believed not to be without the gift of the Holy Spirit. For just as in the Savior a form was given visibly, so that after baptism it might not be doubted that the Holy Spirit is thenceforth given invisibly to believers, so also in the aforesaid matter a form was given in the beginning, so that from it the Holy Spirit might be believed to be poured into the ecclesiastical tradition. But that the apostles might perform mighty works in the presence of the Lord, power was given, just as also earlier to the prophets.

.3. The forms, therefore, of three offices were shown by the gifts of the Holy Spirit in the apostles, of which the first is this, which pertains to ecclesiastical law in regenerating or in the other offices; the second, which was given at Pentecost, which is general — for the Holy Spirit fell not only upon the apostles, but indeed upon all believers —; the third form is that which was granted at the beginning to the apostles alone or to the ordained, in the working of signs and mighty works, until the seeds of faith might be cast for growth. For the seeds of faith are the mighty works performed through the apostles. For they themselves were set as overseers of this kind of truth, who through signs and prodigies might bear witness that our faith is not irrational. For there is no greater proof of truth than a mighty work. For this is what accuses all earthly philosophy, because it always applied itself to words alone, not having the testimony of a mighty work, by which it might demonstrate that what had been built up by words was stable. Therefore the Holy Spirit is given generally once to all believers, through whom they may be proved to be sons of God, while he remains in them. But in the working of signs and prodigies he does not remain in the man, but when called he comes or supplies what is necessary and withdraws; likewise also in the tradition or ordination he bestows grace from without, bringing also protection to those devoted to the faith.

XCIIII. IT MUST BE INQUIRED WHETHER JUDAS ISCARIOT, THE BETRAYER OF THE LORD, ENDED HIS LIFE BY THE NOOSE BEFORE THE PASSION OF THE SAVIOR.

When the Savior had been handed over, as the day of preparation was dawning, all the chief priests and scribes and elders gathered in the house of Caiaphas the high priest, to which they knew Jesus was to be led, that they might hear him. Both the evangelists Matthew and Mark bear witness to this, and that not one of them departed from the governor's house before the impious deed was completed, seeing that all their eagerness and devotion in celebrating the Passover was the death of the Savior. Since, therefore, they were occupied with the Lord's death from morning until the ninth hour, how is it proved that Judas returned to them the price of the blood which he had received, and said to them in the temple before the Lord's cross, "I have sinned, in that I have betrayed innocent blood," when it is quite certain that all the chief priests and elders were not in the temple before the Lord's passion, seeing that they were even mocking him as he hung upon the cross?

For it cannot be proved from this passage that the matter was reported before the passion, since there are many things which, though they are proved to have been done earlier, are set down last; and again there are times when the passage permits that what was done afterward be placed before. For it is surely evident that the fiftieth psalm is earlier than the third. So, then, it sometimes happens that things which are later are anticipated. Whence also, since Mary is shown to have anointed the Lord's feet with ointment at supper six days before the Passover, after the resurrection of her brother Lazarus, on account of its significance it was anticipated by the evangelist, who said among other things: "Now it was Mary," he says, "who anointed the Lord with ointment." He reports this before Lazarus died; and unless we found that it was done afterward, we would not know at what time it was done.

But perhaps it was done after the ninth hour, so that the impious and most cruel Judas, seeing the Savior slain, and on account of this the veil of the temple rent, the earth quaking, the rocks split, the elements terrified, was himself seized with fear and grieved, not unaware that he had betrayed a just man, at whose passion God the judge was so indignant. But even after the ninth hour the elders and chief priests were occupied, as I reckon. For in the evening of that same day they were to celebrate the Passover; and on the Sabbath it was not lawful to carry money according to the law: and for this reason it is not credible to me on what day, or rather at what time, Judas Iscariot ended his life by the noose.

XCV. FROM WHERE THE OBSERVANCE OF PENTECOST AROSE, OR FOR WHAT REASON.

There is no doubt that all things of our religion are strengthened by the testimony of the truth and marked out by the prerogative of the old law. For all things done in the past are known to have been done and appointed as a figure of our future faith, so that we might not doubt that we hold the truth, since we see that those things which we now both hear and believe were predestined before the ages, commended not only by words but also by the powers of signs. But now this is the point, whether they may be understood by us in the same way as they were appointed, so that we may fittingly discern from where and for what reason the day of Pentecost was derived. For the scriptures of the Lord open their meanings to the earnest and devout, and close them to the careless. For it was not fitting that things which have been reserved for the deserving and worthy should be seen everywhere by everyone.

Pentecost, therefore, which is reckoned the fiftieth day from Passover, has this rationale: that just as after the week the Lord's Day is the first, on which the mystery of the Passover was fulfilled unto the redemption of human salvation — because it is always necessary, after the courses of seven days, to return to the first, so that it may be shown that the age of the world is completed in the number seven —, so also after seven weeks the day of Pentecost is the first. And indeed Pentecost is never on any other day than the Lord's Day, so that everything which pertains to human salvation may be known both to have been begun and to have been fulfilled on the Lord's Day. For on the Lord's Day the world was made, and after the fall it was again reformed on the Lord's Day, the figure of which reformation was first given in circumcision, in which there was a sign of the faith to come. For after the week the day which was called the eighth is the first in mystery. For this is the day which the Lord has made. For He made one day, from which the rest received their courses. And through this He rose again on that very day which He made, and according to the number of the aforesaid reckoning He also on that very day gave the law on Mount Sinai through His servant Moses. So that, just as the lamb was a figure of the Lord's passion in the sacrament of the Passover, so also the giving of the law was a figure of the evangelical preaching, because on the same day, that is, at Pentecost, the law was given, on which also the Holy Spirit descended upon the disciples, so that they might receive authority and might know how to preach the evangelical law. This deed is confirmed by a twofold testimony, because it is both shown to have been predestined and is proved to be divine and supercelestial by the manifest infusion of the Holy Spirit; for unlearned men were speaking the mighty works of God in diverse tongues.—

To the children of Israel, therefore, on the third day of the third month the law was given through Moses, as is contained in the book of Exodus, which day is the fiftieth from the fourteenth of the first month, on which day the Passover was made in Egypt, that is, Pentecost. Whence it came about that, for the preaching of the law of faith, at Pentecost the Holy Spirit descended upon the apostles, so that the deeds of the ancients might be held as images of things to come for the security of our faith, because what has been foretold from the beginning cannot seem false. Therefore the time of remission and reformation is described in the fiftieth psalm, —so that the same providence of the fiftieth day, which is also that of the first, might be known. Hence it came about that also the manna was given from heaven to the people of the Jews on the first day, which is the Lord's Day, and that this is so is proved by the continuation of six days, on which they gathered it, and on the seventh day, that is, on the Sabbath, they rested. Now the manna is a type of the spiritual food, which by the resurrection of the Lord was made truth in the mystery of the Eucharist.

Now it remains for us to prove that the things we have said are to be understood in the way we have recalled above. The first month, in which the Passover was celebrated on the fourteenth day in Egypt—this day was the fourth day of the week (Wednesday); whence also the Law was given on the fourth day of the week. This is given to be understood from the fact that on the fifteenth day of the second month of the departure of the children of Israel a Sabbath appears to have fallen; and therefore the flock of quails was not sent to them by day, but in the evening. But in the morning there was manna; it rained upon them from heaven, that is, on the Lord's day, which is found to be the first after the week in the repetition. Then for six continuous days they gathered manna, but on the seventh day, which is the Sabbath, they rested. From this day, therefore, up to the third day of the third month, on which the Law was given, seek and you will find that it was the fourth day of the week when the Law was given. For from the fifteenth day of the second month, when there was a Sabbath, up to the third day of the third month, on which the Law was given, there are nineteen days. Hold, then, these nineteen and go back over the fifteenth day of the second month, on which there was a Sabbath, which counting from the first is the fourteenth, and arrive at the first day of the second month and you will have another fourteen; add these on top of the nineteen mentioned above and you will find that the first day of the second month was a Sabbath. Add yet further, to the head of the first month, seventeen days, because thirteen days of the first month must be drawn back, which were before the Passover; for on the fourteenth day of the first month the Passover was made. Therefore, with the thirteen drawn back and seventeen added, you will find that the fourteenth of the first month was the fourth day of the week.

And lest you suffer weariness of reading or of examining curiously the individual members of the question, I give a shortcut, so that you may both be certain of the number of days which are from the Passover up to the giving of the Law, and from that may more easily learn on what day of the week the Passover was made. On the third day after the second month the Law was given; there are therefore two months and three days. Draw back from these the thirteen days which were before the Passover, and the remaining will be fifty days. But that the Law was given on the fourth day of the week is easily seen from this. The manna was given on the first day of the week, because for six continuous days they gathered it, and that first day was the sixteenth day of the second month. And so from this first day of the week to the eighteenth day, on which the Law was given, you will find the fourth day of the week. And from the fourth day of the week, whether you count forward or backward to the fiftieth day, it is the fourth day of the week. Therefore, because the Jews kept the Passover on the fourth day of the week in Egypt and received the Law on the fourth day of the week, but set out from Egypt on the fifth day of the week, the dedication of the Law is therefore Pentecost.

XCVI. WHETHER IT MUST BE INQUIRED IF "PASSOVER" IS INTERPRETED AS "PASSAGE," AS IT SEEMS TO THE GREEKS.

The Apostle could not be deceived, who says: Christ our passover is sacrificed, which is assuredly not his own word but the Law's, since Moses says: and it shall be, when your sons say to you: what is this service? that you shall say: this is the sacrifice of the passover of the Lord. What more is necessary for testimony? The Law speaks, the Apostle proves it; it remains only that the gainsayer be cast out as obstinate. For it is manifest that the passing-over came after the passover. For from the blood of the sacrificed lamb they placed some upon the posts of the door and upon the lintel, so that the angel passing by in the night might not strike the house in which the sign of the blood was. Therefore the blood provided salvation, not the passing-over, because, in order that the passing-over might not harm them, the blood stood in the way.

XCVII. BY WHAT REASONING THE IMPIETY OF ARIUS CAN BE ANSWERED SIMPLY FROM THE LAW.

If faith be lent to reason, the very meaning of the names Father and Son would show their unity in a natural way. For he would not be called the true Son of God, unless he had come forth from the substance of God the Father; nor indeed would the truth and justice of God suffer him to be called the true Son, if he were not from him. For he is not true, if he is not from him. But scripture says: 'he is true': therefore he is from him. For whoever denies that Christ was born of God does violence to scripture, which for this reason calls him the true Son, that it may signify that he was born of God; for if he is not from God and yet is called the true Son of God, scripture is deceitful. But if scripture does not deceive, whoever does not confess that Christ came forth from God both makes scripture void and denies that Christ is the true Son of God. For it is not will that makes a true son, but birth. 'But,' they say, 'this lies within the power of God, that he should make false things true.' Nay, the power of God is this — whence also he is worthy of all praise — that with him the true is true and the false is false. For it belongs to a liar to call the false true, which does not befit God. For God can indeed do all things, but he does not do anything except what agrees with his truth and his justice.

It is therefore manifest that Christ is for this reason called the true Son of God, that he may be understood to be his own, that is, born of his substance. Whence also the Apostle: who spared not, he says, his own Son, and in another epistle: who, when he was in the form of God, thought it not robbery to be equal with God. If therefore he thought it not robbery in saying that he was equal with God, he demonstrated himself to be the true Son of God. For he could not be equal to God, unless he were from him. For those are equal who are either both true or both false. For that which has a beginning does not admit of equality with — or unity with — the eternal. For by saying that he was one with the Father, Christ made himself equal to God. For not only, it says, did he break the sabbath, but also said that God was his own Father, making himself equal to God, that is, equal. Is it not manifest that this equality descends from the property of his birth? For by saying that he was God's own, that is, true, Son, he made himself equal to God.

Hence it is that he says: he who has seen me has seen the Father also. For by the fact that they are consubstantial, he who sees the one sees them both. For just as the Father has life in himself, so he has given also to the Son to have life, that is, the Father begot the Son equal to himself; and all things that are the Father's are the Son's and the things that are the Son's are the Father's: and no one can seize anything from the hand of the Father nor from the hand of the Son. And he added: I and the Father are one, and: the words which I speak to you, I do not speak from myself, but the Father remaining in me, he himself speaks; and the works that I do, he himself does. Believe me, that I am in the Father and the Father in me; otherwise believe even on account of the works themselves. For because the generation is not from itself, but from him who begot it, therefore whatever it does, it calls the deed of the Father, so that through all these things that may be established, namely that Christ is for this reason called the true Son of God, so that he may be believed to be born from him, lest his essence too be reckoned to be outside the nature of the one God. His humility therefore is his greatest exaltation. For when he seems to subject himself, saying: the words which I speak to you, I do not speak from myself, but the Father remaining in me, he himself speaks; and the works that I do, he himself does, he proves that he has his origin from God and that he is his power, since he says that what he is, is not from himself, but from him. For by these things Christ is commended, not lowered. For he who speaks from himself is not the true Son.

You see therefore that the Arians misunderstand Christ from the very place where he ought to be rightly understood, and from the very place where he is proven to be the true Son of God they disapprove of him and call him false. For he asks to be glorified by the Father and promises to glorify the Father whom he entreats, so that where he seems to humble himself he may exalt. For the Father, when he gives testimony to the Son, attests his own praise; but the Son, when he proclaims the Father, manifests his own glory. For the magnificence and nobility of the Father is the high birth of the true Son. And therefore whatever is said to be the Father's, is without doubt also the Son's; and what is said to be the Son's, is also the Father's.
