For the Spirit is called God's, the same Spirit is called Christ's also; the Church is called God's, it is called Christ's also; God is adored, Christ also is adored; God is served, Christ also is served; the saints are called priests of God, they are called priests of Christ also; God is called the temple of the holy city, likewise Christ; the holy city is said to be illumined by the light of God, in the same way also by that of Christ; it is the seat of God, and no less of Christ also; God is judge, Christ also is, since the prophet Isaiah saw Christ, the Lord of Sabaoth, sitting on the throne of majesty, which the Arians do not deny. Of God the Father it is read that he is King of kings and Lord of lords, the same also of Christ the true Son of God. Thus everywhere the Father and the Son are made equal in divinity, so that the number of persons does not exceed the measure of the one God.

Now between the Father and the Son there is no difference, except this alone: that the Father is unbegotten, but the Son begotten; that is, the Father is from no one, but the Son is from the Father — which indeed is something sublime in the Son. For from this is learned the eternity of his nobility, and that he is the true Son of the true Father. For whence comes that which is read in Genesis, when God says: Let us make man to our image and likeness? Therefore, if their image and likeness is one, how are they not also one in nature? For in spiritual things, where indeed there are no figures, nothing will be alike except through substance, so that where the image of the two is one, how will their substance not also be one? For we read that their image and likeness is one for this reason, that we might believe that the one of them differs in nothing from the other — that is, that Christ is the true Son of God, because the nativity is not unlike the one begetting. In short, the Jews, understanding that he called God his own Father, say to Pilate: We have a law, and according to the law he ought to die, because he made himself the Son of God. The Jews understood, and do Christians say that they do not understand? Those who did not believe, understood; and do those who say they believe act so that they may not understand? Let them at least admit this, that the prophet Isaiah, in order to signify that Christ is God not indeed from himself, but God from God, among other things says: After thee they shall follow bound in chains, and they shall adore thee and shall make supplication to thee, because in thee is God, and there is no God besides thee. For thou art God, and we knew it not, the God of Israel, the Saviour.

As far as pertains to a good disposition, these things are manifest. For by them both the Father is proved to be in the Son, and the Son is signified to be God of the same substance as that of which the Father is. For in whom God is, and besides whom there is no God, and who himself is God — what else can he be said to be than this which the Father is, God, and to differ in nothing? For this is what it is for two to be one. For in him is God, because although he is God, yet he is not God from himself. For he is from the Father, because he is the Son. But besides him there is no God, because he is the only Son of the Father. But he himself is God, because there is one and the same substance of God and of Christ. Therefore no ambiguity is left, so that the substance of the Father and of the Son is believed to be one. For he is the true Son, and of the true Father.

Such also is that saying of the prophet Jeremiah; for among other things he says: This is our God: no other shall be reckoned in comparison with him. He found out all the way of knowledge, and gave it to Jacob his servant and to Israel his beloved. After this he was seen upon earth and conversed among men. Can he be called base-born, to whom no other can be compared? Or can he be called a god other than the Father, in such a way that it profits toward his being God unto the injury of the Father? For if no other god can be reckoned in comparison with him, and he is apart from God the Father, see whether what follows ought to be said; for it is dangerous. For he will be said to be greater than the Father. For, thinking that they render honor to God the Father if they separate the Son from him, they detract from him, so that the one whom they wish to make lesser—impiously blinded in mind—they set above that one to whose authority they think this one must be bowed. But scripture, in order to teach that the Son differs in nothing from the Father—yet he would differ, if he were not true—said that no other is to be reckoned in comparison with him, because no other substance could be found similar to the substance of God, which is also that of Christ. For that Christ is of God could not be taught otherwise than by saying that no other is to be reckoned as him. For, knowing that God the Father can in no way be called subject, in order to show that his Son is equal to him, he says: no other shall be reckoned in comparison with him, because he is equal to God the Father, who is God of all; for he is his true Son. Therefore the prophet says: this is our God, that is, the God of Israel. If this is he, he himself is the one God; for concerning this one scripture says: Hear, O Israel, the Lord your God is one God, and certainly this is not the Father, but is his Son.

You see, therefore, that it does not prejudge against the one God to attribute the person of the Son, if He too be called one God, seeing that Scripture bears witness to this. Whence comes this, unless because the Father and the Son are one, because the unity is signified not by persons but by nature, so that they are not two in substance but one, nor two in divinity but one God, so that, whether the Father speak or the Son speak, it is said that one God has spoken? For if the Lord Himself, among other things, says concerning male and female: they are now no longer two, but one flesh, how much more are the Father and the Son not two gods, but one substance! But that the Father and the Son are called two, the cause is the nativity; yet that there be not again two gods, the cause is the one divinity. Yet by what dullness do they proclaim that Christ was created, that is, made, when they read that God made nothing without Christ? If therefore nothing was made without Him, He Himself will not be a thing made. For if He is a thing made, something was made without Him. But away with the thought, since nothing was made without Him. For being born of God He did not begin, but existed; for nativity is nothing else than a going-forth; and a going-forth is a manifestation: and so He was not made, but brought forth. For that it might be signified that Christ existed or proceeded from the essence of God, His going-forth was called a nativity, so that, because the nativity is not unlike the begetter, it might not be doubted that Christ is consubstantial with God, and that mind which says: 'there was a time when He was not', might be condemned, because He cannot at any time not have been who, that He might be signified to have always been with God, is read to have proceeded from God. For no one has proceeded from Him where he was not.

The Arians are indeed wont to interpret thus, saying concerning the Son of God: 'Christ is therefore called the true Son of God, because He so made Him that He is true.' As if there were something that God makes false. And all those things which we have said above concerning the unity of the Father and the Son, what will they be, when they have been so strengthened and confirmed? If it be thought that here one must hold otherwise, many things established by divine oracles will be able to be torn away by the testimony of one degenerate. It must nevertheless be cut off, lest iniquity flatter itself even concerning this very point. Therefore 'He so made Him,' he says, 'that He is true,' so that will yields in place of nativity and a thing made in place of generation. 'For so much,' they say, 'must be granted to the power of God, that He can make things that are not as though they were.'

Since therefore they use this crafty cunning against the Son of God, out of these very things which they presume upon, because in the rest they have been refuted, they must be answered, so that they may appear refuted in the whole sum. For this proposition, as far as it pertains to the sense, is of no importance; but as far as it pertains to the words, it creates a fog, so that it does not quickly become apparent. For it is as if an Egyptian set in darkness, because a bad cause cannot be defended by good sense. Therefore, to defend impiety they use sacrilegious words. If, then, this is the power of God, that he made Christ to be a true Son to himself, although he was not born of him, then he made him such as he himself is, so that the making bestowed this which generation was going to give. If, therefore, you say that there is no difference between God and Christ, it could perhaps be thought that this is not maliciously asserted by you, so that what is said in word may also be so in sense, so that, because he is called true Son, he may be understood as true, through that which is of one substance with the Father—albeit not through generation, but through making, because God can do all things, as you say, so that the made thing is as if unmade.

But if, following the line of reason, you say that it cannot be that what is contained within a beginning should be equal and consubstantial with that which is eternal, you reveal the deceit of your malice, because, in order to evade the sense of the word, with a false utterance you say that Christ is the true Son of God, while denying to him that he is equal to God the Father. For he is not true, if he is unequal. Can he then be called a true Son, if the Father also is not called true? For if the Son is true, the Father also will be true; and this truth equality proves. But if they are unequal, neither of them is true. Yet since the testimony of the Scriptures cannot be made void, by which Christ is proved to be the true Son of God, he must be believed to have been born of God the Father, because, so that this truth might be confirmed, he is read to be both on a par with God and equal.

But you who proclaim the inestimable power of God say: 'God cannot generate, because he is a simple nature'; and by this you also do insult to God the Father himself. For you call him a liar, so that you may deny the Son, whom he asserts to be his own and true, and you expose your hypocrisy, because you falsely proclaim God to be powerful. For if you proclaim the power of God in that which you assert to be made true out of the false, so as to mock what is truly true, how much more ought you to embrace this—if you were of good mind—through which the true that is called true is proved, and the testimony that God the Father gave concerning his Son is confirmed as true! For the Lord says: 'But with God,' he says, 'all things are possible,' so that whatever seems impossible to men may be possible to God. For if the reasoning of the flesh is regarded, neither did a virgin give birth, nor did Jonah live in the belly of the whale, nor will the dead rise, nor did God beget a Son—because a simple nature cannot generate, since nothing is generated in the world without commixture. All these things must either be believed together or refuted together. For if the reasoning of the world is to be held, it ought to have authority in all these things; but if there is some reasoning of spiritual things, he must be called carnal who, infected by the reasoning of the world, denies that anything can come to be otherwise than as he knows.

Yet again they are accustomed to put forward another point with their evasiveness. For they say: 'Did the Father beget the Son by his will, or not?' — so that, when they have heard 'certainly by his will', at once, as enemies of the Son of God, they may reply: 'Therefore the will exists before the Son, so that from this he may appear not to be eternal, since he did not indeed receive a beginning through birth, but simply came into existence.' And yet, if they think this, not only will the will exist before the Son, but even before the Father, since the cause of generation is both the Father and the Son. Unbelief follows carnal perceptions in all things. For it introduces the cause of God as though of a man, as if he had first deliberated and pondered so as to beget, as though subject to weakness, whereas in God there was neither generation without will nor will without generation. For the will itself is generation. For, to be sure, it is a common statement: 'God made,' 'man made'; but God makes in one way, man in another. So too in the other causes; for one must speak of God in a way that is worthy of him.

They are also accustomed to raise another question about God the Father himself, saying: 'Did it come to him to beget, or is it of his nature that he should always beget?' — which they bring forward for this reason, that they may both assert God to be a liar, who testifies that he has begotten Christ, and make Christ not true but adoptive. 'For if he begot,' they say, 'he ought always to beget, so that, because he does not always beget, neither is he believed to have begotten Christ' — as though it should be denied that he made the world, because he does not make others. What irreverence, what indiscipline it is to lay down a law for God, that: 'if he begot, either he begot many or he was unable to beget one' — not even believing the Lord himself, who declared himself to be the only Son of God!

After these things which have been shown above to be equal in the Father and the Son, let us see whether those things which are written concerning the Holy Spirit accord with them, so that the Trinity, which we hold in our saving profession, may not admit a discrepancy of nature, just as it does not have a diversity of faith. For since salvation does not exist without any one of these, the power of the three appears to be one, which gives salvation to those who believe in it. Since we have brought forward the words of the Father as testimony to the Son, now let us use the words of the Son as testimony to the Holy Spirit, by which he declares that the Holy Spirit does not differ from himself, so that, when the Father is witness to the Son and the Son to the Holy Spirit, the Trinity may be understood to be of one nature. The Lord therefore says, among other things: I will ask the Father, and he will give you another Paraclete, that he may be with you forever, the Spirit of truth. When he says that another Paraclete is to be sent, he certainly proves himself to be a Paraclete. For it is also read in the epistle of John; for he says: We have an advocate with the Father, Jesus Christ, who intercedes for our sins, and again the Lord says: I am the truth. Behold, the open unity of the Holy Spirit and the Son of God; for the things which the Son speaks about himself, the same he speaks also about the Holy Spirit.

Now what the scripture arranges toward this meaning must be set forth. It is read in Isaiah that he saw the Lord of Sabaoth seated on the throne of majesty, whom John the evangelist understood to be Christ, saying among other things: "These things Isaiah said, when he saw his majesty and spoke concerning him." This one the apostle says is the Holy Spirit. For he says in the last part of the Acts of the Apostles: "Well did the Holy Spirit speak to your fathers, saying: With your ear you shall hear and shall not understand," and the rest. These words were spoken by the Lord of Sabaoth. This is the one God, who whether he be understood as Father or as Son or as Holy Spirit, there is no contradiction. For their divinity and power is one. For if in the edict of one prefect's court even the other prefects are said to command, on account of the authority of one power, how much more in the rule of one God, if one of the three has spoken, will the three not incongruously be said to have spoken! For their nature and will is one.

Again, they are wont to prejudge against the Holy Spirit, that he should therefore be understood as degenerate, because he is placed third in order, though there is such simplicity in the divine scriptures that sometimes you may find the third placed first. For as the matter has presented itself, so they are also placed. For it is the wrong of no one, because their divinity is one. In short, we read in Isaiah where the Lord says: "I am the first and I am unto eternity, and my hand founded the earth, my right hand made firm the heaven," and in what follows: "I have spoken, I have called, I have brought him and made his journey prosperous. Draw near to me and hear these things: not in secret from the beginning have I spoken; when they came to be, I was there, and now the Lord has sent me and his Spirit." Whom do you wish to be the one who founded the earth? For he himself says that he was sent. Surely not the Father? Far be it. It is therefore the Son who says he was sent by God and the Holy Spirit. Behold, these things are equal between the Son and the Holy Spirit. For just as the Holy Spirit is read to be sent by the Father and the Son, so also Christ was sent by God and the Holy Spirit. For this is peculiar to the Father alone, that he not be said to be sent.

Receive the sayings of the apostle, who embraces the Trinity in another order; for he says among other things in the second epistle to the Thessalonians: "May the Lord direct your hearts in the charity of God and the patience of Christ." Whom, do you think, does he call Lord except the Holy Spirit? And what wonder is it, since the one whom Isaiah saw seated on the throne of majesty, this same one he calls the Holy Spirit the Lord of Sabaoth, as we showed above? And John the apostle signifies that God is understood in the Holy Spirit, saying among other things: "By this we know that God abides in us, and from his Spirit which he has given us." If therefore through his Spirit he himself abides in us, it is not doubted that the Holy Spirit is of God. And there is no doubt that what is of God is God. Hence it is that the apostle says: "No one knows the things which are in God except the Spirit of God." How can he know the things which are in God, unless he be of the same divinity? For an inferior nature cannot know what is in a greater one; how much more was a creature unable to know what might be in its Creator!

And in the first letter to the Corinthians, having passed over the order that is in the tradition of the faith, he begins from the Holy Spirit to recount the workings of graces and ministries, and adds the Lord Jesus, and puts God third, whom he says works both in the Holy Spirit and in the Lord, just as the Lord also says: the Father remaining in me, he himself works. For through the fact that both the Holy Spirit and Christ are from God the Father, their working is the work of God. Finally, he calls the Holy Spirit also Lord, and the Lord he calls also God — this on account of the unity of nature; when therefore in this passage he says that the same God, who is also Lord, works all things in all, he concludes below thus, saying: but all these things one and the same Spirit works, dividing to each one as he wills. What is so plain as that the three are of one working, since, when one works, the three are said to work? Why, unless because they are of one divinity? For it is not doubtful that properly the Holy Spirit works the gift of graces. But in order to teach that God and the Lord and the Holy Spirit are one on account of the indifference of nature, he shows that the work of the Holy Spirit is the work of the three. And in order to make manifest that, as far as pertains to person, it is the work of the Holy Spirit alone: but all these things, he says, one and the same Spirit works, dividing to each one as he wills. Therefore 'as he wills,' because his will is the will of God. For he did not say 'as God wills,' but 'as the Spirit wills.' For a creature forces itself to do the will of God; but the Holy Spirit naturally wills this.

That the Holy Spirit works all things may shine forth plainly, the Lord says among other things: For if I cast out demons by the finger of God, and so forth. He calls the Holy Spirit "the finger of God," so as to prove that he is from God. Accordingly, he testified that the Jews, who said that he cast out demons by Beelzebub, had blasphemed against the Holy Spirit. Moses too, because he worked signs through the Holy Spirit, the magicians confess, saying: This is the finger of God. For in the Acts of the Apostles also we read: And as they were ministering to the Lord and fasting, the Holy Spirit said: Separate for me Barnabas and Saul for the work to which I have called them. Behold, the Lord is being ministered to, and this the Holy Spirit claims for himself — whence it manifestly appears that he is Lord just as also God the Father —; and Christ from heaven called Saul and sent him to preach, and this in like manner the Holy Spirit lays claim to for himself by saying: for the work to which I have called them. For no one is unaware that the Savior appointed bishops for the churches; for he himself, before he ascended into the heavens, laying his hand on the apostles ordained them bishops. This the Apostle assigns to the Holy Spirit, when he says: Take heed to yourselves and to all the flock, in which the Holy Spirit has placed you as bishops to rule the Church of the Lord Jesus. And among other things Peter says to Ananias: Ananias, why has Satan filled your heart, that you should lie to the Holy Spirit? and below: You have not, he says, lied to men, but to God. It is plain that the Holy Spirit is called God, since Ananias, who lied to him, is said to have lied to God. But if he had by no means wished the Holy Spirit to be understood as God, he would have said: "You have not lied to men, but to the Holy Spirit," so as to signify him neither God nor man. And what wonder? For the Apostle sometimes calls man the temple of God, but sometimes the temple of the Holy Spirit, for the reason that they are one in nature. For if the Holy Spirit were by no means understood to be God, with what audacity would he say below: For the temple of God is holy, which you are?

Since one God is proclaimed in all the scriptures, but his mystery is adored in the Trinity, scripture therefore proceeds in such a way that, under the profession of one God, those things which are of him — that is, his Son and his Spirit — are understood to be in the same honor in which God himself is; because the sacrament of God has been revealed for this reason, that his glory might be signified to abide in the Trinity. For this reason: that which is of God is not called God with full authority, lest it be believed to make another apart from him who is one, but he gives understanding, so that we may believe that on this account the salvation of the creature consists in the Trinity, because their divinity is one. For creator and creature, lord and servants, eternity and the things which are under a beginning, could not be joined together and set in the same rank, seeing that without one of them there is salvation for no one. For everything that is God, whether the Son or the Holy Spirit, is called to injury if it be reckoned outside the honor and glory of the one God. The scriptures therefore show Christ to be God, they show the Holy Spirit to be God, so that those who see may know what it is that is shown. It is as if someone should show a gem without crying out that it is a gem; will the thing that is seen not be a gem merely because he does not cry out? So also the scriptures show, through those things which have been treated above, what we ought to believe, enclosing our understanding within the faith of one God by the mystery of the Trinity.

XCVIII. ON THE GOSPEL OF JOHN.

You have heard what the most holy Gospel testifies, when the Lord says to the Jews: You are born of your father the devil, and you wish to do the desires of your father. He was a murderer from the beginning, and stood not in the truth, because the truth is not in him. When he speaks a lie, he speaks of his own, because he is a liar, and so also is his father. Let none of the faithful doubt that the devil has no author of his apostasy. For he himself is the prince of all error, who, seeing that he was made by God with no mean power, dared to presume deity, so that, because he saw the rest to be inferior, he might set himself forward as God. Finally, the names by which the devil and Satan are called are composed from his act, not from his nature. For every evil is proved by deed, so that it is not a subsistence in the crime, but a will conceived from causes. For while he saw himself mightier than the rest, he was puffed up to exercise domination.

How then will he be said to have a father, when he is held to be the author of his crime? or from what is it proven that he was a murderer from the beginning? But Scripture calls him who was his imitator by his name, so that, since it received the name from the deed, everyone who does an evil work is not undeservedly called a devil. Therefore in this passage the Savior called Cain a devil, because he was a rival of his works. While he envied his brother, he committed murder, leaving behind an example of the cruelty of parricide; just as also the devil, while he envied man made in the image of God, heaped up the malice of his work, offering a pattern for error. Hence therefore Cain also stood forth as a liar, when God said to him: where is Abel your brother? He, steeped in the malice of his father, bursting out into a lie, said: I do not know, so that, of the one from whom he had wrenched away life, he might pretend that he did not know where he was, thus blinded by fury, so that he answered God as though a man, whom he thought he could conceal from.

Therefore the Jews, having become imitators of this one, killed even the Lord himself, wanting rather to have Cain the fratricide as their father than God, so that they might become guilty of all the blood that had been shed. For by slaying the author of life they stood forth as the chiefs of all crime, so that they even bound their own sons to this evil, saying: his blood be upon us and upon our sons. For in order to persuade Pilate that there was nothing evil in what they were asking, they said that it would be turned back upon their own sons if it were unjust, because, in order to satisfy their fury, they were unwilling to spare even their own sons.

For since the devil is not by nature evil, from this it is more easily learned, because punishment would not be promised to one who had not done what he did not know. For it is unjust to punish him who does nothing other than what belongs to his nature; and this evil is greater, if you demand of someone that which you know he cannot do. For which reason he is justly to be punished, who, when it is agreed that he knows and is able to do good, does evil. This too can be observed from the words of the Savior; for he says, as was mentioned above: your father was a murderer from the beginning and did not stand in the truth. Therefore if he is a liar by nature, how is he said not to have stood in the truth? For he does not stand in the truth who does not remain in the truth. It is agreed, then, that the devil was in the truth, but did not persevere. Finally we read the Lord saying through the prophet: if they had stood, he says, in my substance, that is, 'in my law', and the apostle to the Galatians says: stand fast, and do not again be confined by the yoke of servitude. What is so plain? For he commands this, that for the rest they remain in his commandments. But if the devil had kept this, he would have stood in the substance and law of God. For the law is a foundation to those who keep it.

How then does it seem to some people that the devil, or evil, was either made by God or has its own origin—that is, arose from nothing—both of which seem contrary to God? For whoever says that he was made for this purpose, that he should be evil, thinks that God is of imperfect goodness, because one who is perfectly good does not make evil; but whoever does not believe that God is the author of his origin denies God's dominion. For by this, since all things are God's, they suppose that there is something which in no way pertains to God—men who must suffer punishments when they see that all things are judged by God through Christ.

XCVIIII. WHAT THE MEANING IS OF 'LET NO TEMPTATION TAKE HOLD OF YOU BUT SUCH AS IS HUMAN.'

Not without foresight, as I judge, does the teacher of the Gentiles, our apostle, wish that temptation may take hold of us, but only that which is human, because divine temptation brings forth destruction. For by this the Jews, seized by serpents, perished; for acting under the law of God, they doubted concerning His providence. And through this he exhorts us that not divine but human temptation may take hold of us. For here he is said to tempt God who, acting under His name, hopes for salvation from idols, because he does not hold God as proven. For everything that is not proven is tempted, and what is tempted comes into doubt. And through this divine temptation is deadly, but human temptation is salutary, because, just as to doubt concerning God is destructive, so to despair concerning man on account of the law of God is most wholesome. Wherefore the apostle providently says: Let no temptation take hold of you but such as is human, so that, tempted by men on account of the law of God, we may be found strong—while carnal men doubt concerning us—but may be proven before God, because those who are not proven before God are tempted that they may be amended.

Therefore temptation is twofold; for sometimes we are tempted as though faithful, sometimes as though distrustful, so that the temptation of the faithful is human. For on account of faith we are afflicted by men—for they tempt us, to see if they can lead us away from the faith—but on account of distrust they are tempted, so that, being straitened, they may repent and return into the way, as also happened to the Jews. For when they distrusted concerning the providence of God, they were tempted by serpents, so that, seized by pain, they might be amended. For God tempts in such a way that He may correct men of this kind. There is also another temptation, by which Abraham was tempted, and Job, and also Tobias, the servants of God: so that Abraham, through the tribulation of offering up his only and most beloved son, might have a more abundant fruit of righteousness unto eternal glory; and Job, through the loss of all his substance, might be increased, enriched with riches in heaven and on earth; and Tobias, having suffered the blindness of his eyes, thus stood forth the more advanced, so that both in the present he became glorious, his eyes being received back, and for the future a perpetual brightness was preserved for him—because the just are proven for this purpose, that they may make progress. And therefore in temptation we ought to be of a stout spirit, knowing that this avails not to our diminishment but to our increase, if only we endure it with an even spirit through Christ.

C. ON THE GOSPEL OF MATTHEW.

You have heard, most beloved brothers, what the gospel attests when the Lord says: I confess to you, Father, Lord of heaven and earth, because you have hidden these things from the wise and prudent and have revealed them to little ones. Yes, Father, since so it seemed pleasing before you. Therefore, if we examine the sense of these words, we shall see that the things which were spoken by the Lord for the exhortation of believers pertain to those who, because they spurn worldly wisdom, appear as little ones to the wise of the world—men who, commended by no testimony, wish to be called wise. For this reason it seemed unfitting to God to show the truth to these, but to the humble, who, presuming nothing about themselves, incline themselves to God's will, saving things are to be revealed, with fairness compelling it. For here the one prudent before God and mindful of his own salvation is he who sets God's law before his own knowledge—the law which he indeed sees is not propped up by the noise of words, but by the powers of things. And without doubt he is foolish and vain who commends himself to that thing which is fortified by no prerogative of signs.

Whence God promised the kingdom not to the lettered of this world, but to the faithful; nor does he pledge eternity to the searchers of the stars, but to those who act rightly; nor does he give glory to dialecticians, who by fraud and by certain minute niceties strive to shut out the truth, but to men of good will, who are more careful in life than in speech. For God rejects those who wish to make use of the speech of brilliant discourse rather than of good deeds. For he also wishes to turn God's glory upon himself who thinks to adorn God's matter with his own words. For the things themselves ought to please through themselves, so that not the speech but the sense may be praised. For if the sense is the author of the letter, and words were invented for the sake of signifying things, why are not the things themselves rather to be brought forth with pure sense, so that they may more easily incite the mind to salvation? And therefore the Lord chose fishermen as our apostles, simple men and without letters, who, without any cunning, by keeping the faith and by living well, might show forth in themselves the truth of God. Whence also the apostle Paul says: And I, coming to you, brothers, came not in loftiness of speech or of wisdom, among other things: because, he says, the kingdom of God is not in speech, but in power, so that, since words are subject to contradiction, power might stand as witness to God's law, to which all loftiness of discourse yields.

And so these things have therefore been set forth beforehand, most beloved brothers, that provision might be made for the simpler folk, lest perchance they should think themselves unworthy of the grace of God because they are by no means trained in forensic letters, since rather their simplicity is conducive to praise, when that which the prudent of the world do not see because of the pride of letters, these believe, being prudent not in words but in counsel. For they know that God rests in good works and seeks faith, not cultivated speech. For they read the Lord saying: On whom shall I rest, except on the humble and meek and one who trembles at my words? Wherefore, that we may be made worthy of the promised rewards, we ought to do the things which are commanded by the Lord, secure because the Lord loves those who keep his words. For thus he said to his disciples: If you love me, keep my commandments, so that, serving the Lord in these things, we may be able to be heirs of his kingdom through the lawgiver Christ our Lord.

CI. ON THE BOASTING OF THE ROMAN LEVITES.

While we wish to obey the commands of charity, we may perhaps fall into reproach, we who write about a matter which never at any time came into doubt, because, while we do not wish to offend friendship, we entangle the zeal which ought to have served for revealing some hidden thing—although, on account of the excessive foolishness of a certain man who asserts that which we shall lay open in what follows, the aforesaid charity has enjoined this too upon us, lest, since no one for a long time was answering these things, he should advance into what is worse and, long exercised in vain, be unable to be corrected. For this is the zeal of charity, which seeks not the things that are its own.

A certain man, therefore, who bears the name of a false god, with foolishness and the boasting of the city of Rome as his guide, strives to make the Levites equal to the priests and the deacons equal to the presbyters—I will not say to set them above. For that would be more foolish and might perhaps seem incredible, and we might be regarded not as correctors but as slanderers. By what law, then, or by what custom, by what precedent he dares to attempt this, I think no one knows: that he should make these men—to whom a seat in the church has by no means been granted—equal to those who, because they are the prelates of God, sit with dignity in the house of God and in the honor of Christ. What audacity it is to make their own ministers the equals of the presbyters! What vain presumption of rashness it is to set on a level with the priests the bearers of the tabernacle and of all its vessels, and the hewers of wood and the carriers of water! For this was the office of the Levites. It is as if subordinates were made equal to prefects, slaves to masters. For this is written, when the Lord says to Moses: Take the tribe of Levi from the midst of the children of Israel and set them before Aaron the priest, and they shall serve him. What is plainer than this example, which even now is kept in the church?

Yet although the deacons of the Roman church may seem somewhat more immodest, they nevertheless do not presume upon the dignity of sitting in the church. But that they do not perform all the ministries of service in turn, the multitude of clerics brings about. For otherwise they would both carry the altar and its vessels, and pour water upon the hands of the priest, just as we see throughout all the churches and just as was ordained by the Lord through Moses. Or are they perhaps better than Elisha, who used to pour water upon the hands of Elijah?

Thus he defends the deacons against the presbyters, as though deacons were ordained from presbyters and not presbyters from deacons. But because they are ministers of the Roman church, they are for that reason thought more honorable than among the other churches, on account of the magnificence of the city of Rome, which is seen to be the head of all cities. If this is so, they ought to claim this also for their own priests, since, if those who are inferior are exalted on account of the magnificence of the city, how much more ought those who are superior to be exalted! For whatever is bestowed on the officials becomes an increase to the power, just as the honor of a servant advances to the praise of his master—although before God, the just judge, this honor remains to each one, an honor which was created for the several offices of the churches, so that he who is a deacon may have the honor of the diaconate throughout all the churches—for it is no ordinary honor to serve the bishop of God, at least in the church—and that the priest may be held worthy of the whole ecclesiastical honor. For the greater order has within itself and with itself also the lesser; for the presbyter performs both the office of the deacon and of the exorcist and of the lector.

But that the presbyter is to be understood as a bishop the apostle Paul proves, when, at the time he instructs Timothy, whom he had ordained a presbyter, as to what kind of man he ought to appoint bishop. For what is a bishop, except the first presbyter, that is, the highest priest? In short, he calls them nothing other than fellow-presbyters and fellow-priests; does a bishop ever call his ministers fellow-deacons? Certainly not, for they are much inferior, and it would be disgraceful for a judge to call the chief clerk his colleague. For in Alexandria and throughout all Egypt, if a bishop is lacking, a presbyter consecrates.

But how great the dignity of the priestly order is, the case of Caiaphas shows, who, though he was a most wicked man, prophesied. Why? he says, "because he was the chief of the priests." And again, that there is a great distance between a deacon and a priest, the book which we call the Acts of the Apostles proves. For when the people of Samaria had believed Philip preaching, a deacon ordained by the apostles: they sent, it says, to Peter and John that they should come and give to those who had believed the Holy Spirit through the laying on of hands.

But it is out of ignorance, I believe, of the scriptures, or forgetfulness of the law, that they defend these things. He, therefore, who thinks these things are to be maintained ought to recall what befell the Levites when they held such notions. For when they presumed that there was no difference between priests and Levites, with God as judge Korah and those who agreed with him were swallowed up by the gaping of the earth, and a rushing fire consumed at once 250 men, and King Uzziah, when he presumed upon the priestly office, was covered with leprosy, so that by this example the rest, being terrified, might not dare to presume what has not been granted. For now we see deacons rashly doing what belongs to the priests, both at feasts and in prayer wishing that the response be made to them, though this is permitted to priests alone. For the order of the deacon is to receive from the priest and thus to give to the people.

Do you see what empty presumption gives birth to? For, forgetful through the elation of their minds—because they see that they are ministers of the Roman church—they do not consider what has been decreed for them by God and what they ought to observe, but constant domestic duties and officialdom take this out of their memory, which now has very great power through suggestions whether bad or good. For either they are feared, lest they make evil suggestions, or they are bought, that they may grant favors. These are the ones who cause them not to consider the principle of their own order. For while, through flattery, they illicitly comply with them, they make them headlong, so that they think more is permitted to them, seeing indeed that deference is not shown thus to the priests, and through this they think themselves to be given precedence.

'But', he says, 'by the testimony of a deacon a presbyter is made'—as though this pertained to the prerogative of greatness. For we read that the apostle Peter said to the people: choose, he says, from among you those whom we may appoint to serve the ministries of the church, I do not wish to say 'of the tables'. Behold, by the testimony of laymen deacons were created. And the apostle wishes that he who is said to be ordained bishop should have even the testimony of the gentiles; for thus he says: for it is fitting that this man have also a good testimony from those who are outside. It belongs therefore to all to give testimony, and yet not all are worthy of this honor. For all can judge, and yet not all can be what they judge. For a good painter can be judged by one who does not know how to paint, and one flute-player can be approved as better than another by one who is of another art; so too he who is of a lower order can be a witness of the presbyter to be created.

There is again something whereby they are puffed up and think much is owed to them: 'for by us', they say, 'those who are to be ordained are led forward'; so that, while they are hedged about at their side, they may seem worthy of honor. Grant it to be true; for he is sent by the bishop as it were an official, that he may render service to the one being ordained. For even an emperor, that he may appear an emperor, is set in order by military attendance; yet the army is not better than nor equal to the emperor. For Haman also, though he was illustrious in military honor, was sent by the king to go in attendance upon Mordecai, so that how worthy Mordecai was might be known from the attendances upon him; so too deacons are sent in attendance upon presbyters to be created, so that all may understand them to be made worthy priests. For excepting the priests, to whom they owe attendance, deacons are set before all.

CII. AGAINST NOVATIAN.

His disciples say to Jesus: "Lord, do you wish that we say for fire to come down from heaven and consume them, as Elias did?" And the Lord answered them saying: "You do not know of what spirit you are. For the Son of Man did not come to destroy souls, but to save them." Although it was certainly fitting to take vengeance on the Samaritans who did not receive him, the Lord nevertheless, because he came to bestow mercy, set aside the sentence, so that thereafter, correcting themselves, they might be amended. And the prophet Elisha, indignant at the boys who were doing him insult, with God as judge took vengeance upon them, so that they were devoured by bears in that place where there is said to be no forest. But the Lord spared the harlot brought before him — namely her whom the elders of the Jews said they had caught in adultery — so that, since the merciful preaching was beginning, he might teach that she was not to be condemned but forgiven. For the law given through Moses, because it held justice unto vengeance, exercised severity in its beginnings, since it both took deadly vengeance upon that man who went out on the sabbath to gather wood, and likewise gave punishment upon the son of the Egyptian woman who had blasphemed against God, and in the matter of the molten calf struck down the sons of Israel.

Each law, therefore, shows its full meaning amid its very beginnings, so that the old law, because it was readier to take vengeance, might appear harsher amid its beginnings, so that by instilling fear it might make its hearers diligent and attentive. But the new law, because it is merciful and inclined to mercy, displayed its beginnings most gentle and most clement, so that by this very delight it might call all to forgiveness. Afterwards, however, even that law which seemed harsher, on account of the frailty of the human race, not only softened its rigor but also showed itself human and merciful; and this law which seemed clement at times took vengeance. For Ananias, together with his wife Sapphira, were divinely struck down; and Elymas the magician, unbelieving and resisting the law of God, was blinded by the judgment and power of the Lord; and Herod the king, because he did not give honor to God, being struck by an angel of God, teeming with worms, expired. Lest, because mercy is preached, men should think that sin goes altogether unavenged and is committed with impunity, he established fear, showing that pardon of offenses is given for this purpose: that, as though brought to life from the dead, they might afterwards abstain from contrary things — so that this be worked at, that no one depart hence with guilt, because there will be a coming to judgment. For to each person that sentence is imputed in which he dies. Since, therefore, the new law has the fullness of mercy, lest it be held in contempt by the lovers of sins, it took vengeance upon the aforesaid persons. But the old law, although it was given for vengeance, nevertheless pardoned certain persons, lest it be considered wholly cruel, yet it took vengeance upon the many, because its purpose is prompt to punish.

Without doubt, therefore, the grace of the new law is greater than that of the old was. If, then, the grace is greater, how can it be denied that it is able to bestow this which that other law bestowed, which had the lesser grace? For it was more inclined toward severity. And so, if that law is proved to have pardoned the penitent, how can it be that this law, which is merciful, does not receive the penitent? For the Jews too were called sons of God, who, on the authority of the apostle Paul, are read to have been baptized through Moses and in the cloud and in the sea. For, being about to receive the law, they are without doubt known to have been purified, so that thereafter they might render an account of the law they had received and, if any should sin, might reform themselves through penance, the Lord saying through Isaiah the prophet: "Do penance, you who go astray, and be converted." But perhaps, on the contrary, it is said: "Penance indeed was preached, yet not remission." If, then, penance was preached by God, there is fruit for those who do penance; for the Lord would not admonish this in vain. Furthermore, the Lord, knowing that remission of sins ought to be given to the converted, commanded this, since indeed he said: "I do not will the death of the one dying, so much as that he turn back and live." And so there is a fruit of penance, as has become clear, for those converted to God. Excluded, therefore, is the impiously contrived assertion of Novatian, by which he inverts the sense of the Lord's words, saying: "The Lord says: whoever denies me, I too will deny him," so that if any Christian, for whatever cause he has denied, can now no longer return, or at any rate, on returning, is by no means received.

And where will penance be enjoined, when the Savior, after pronouncing this sentence, did not deny the apostle Peter, who was denying him? Why? Because, penance following after, he wept most bitterly over his error, [the Lord] knowing full well that the converted are pardoned and are reformed through penance. But Novatian pretends that penance is to be done. For because he does not dare openly to deny that penance is to be granted, being bound by the law he subtly strives to render it void. For when he says that "whoever denies will in no way have pardon, but will himself also be denied in the sight of the angels of God," how does he not with deceit profess that penance is to be done? And from this his fraud manifestly appears, when he adds, saying: "Whoever sins against the Holy Spirit, it will not be forgiven him, neither here nor in the future." Although the cause by which one sins against the Holy Spirit is one thing, and to deny the Lord Jesus is another, Novatian nevertheless, in order to deny pardon to this sin, wants there to be one and the same sense in denying the Lord and in sinning against the Holy Spirit, so that by this evasion he may cut off the fruit of penance. For if to deny the Lord is the same as to have sinned against the Holy Spirit, no pardon is to be hoped for by those who deny, nor any fruit of penance, since the Lord's oracles have promised that this sin is remitted neither here nor in the future.

Hence, therefore, it appears that Novatian speaks with guile when he says that penance is to be done; for why should one repent, if the offense is denied any abolishing? For the groaning, weeping, and lamentation of penance obtains this: that one may both merit pardon and reformation. Indeed David, repenting and confessing his sin, both merited pardon and was restored to his former state, since he both remained king and afterward prophesied. This is clearly proved; for in the fiftieth psalm he repented, and afterward, having suffered persecution from his son, he prophesied; for the third psalm, according to the history, comes after the fiftieth. But perhaps it had to be that the king be forgiven, whereas the lowly ought not to be forgiven — although rather it would seem that forgiveness is more difficult for the more powerful. For God does not accept the person of man. For the more exalted a person is, the more, however light his sin, grave is the crime. For just as humility in one who petitions is exalted and magnificent, so also his fault is to be held as a crime. For, apart from those sins which are agreed to be unlawful for all, not all things are permitted to the powerful that are conceded to the lowly. For it is unseemly for a man of dignity to engage in trade, and it is notable for him to enter a tavern; for senators, too, usury is a disgrace. If, then, they are to be marked when found in these light matters, how much more are they to be made defendants, if they have sinned! Through these things, therefore, it appears that David sinned most grievously, and indeed in a double manner: yet, having been converted, he obtained pardon for both homicide and adultery and — what is more — weeping, he was reformed. For when he was reproved by the prophet

Nathan, he did not dare to conceal his sin, but confessed, saying: I have sinned against the Lord. Then the prophet answered him and said: The Lord has taken away your sin and you shall not die, because you have repented; and since he is found to have prophesied afterward, there is no doubt that this man was reformed. For Ahab too, rebuked by the prophet on account of the death of Naboth, went weeping and tore his garments and girded himself with sackcloth, and the word of the Lord came into the hand of his servant Elijah concerning Ahab, and the Lord said: Have you seen how Ahab was moved before my face? I will not bring evils in his days. And for the Ninevites, weeping over their sins while death was imminent, life was restored. Behold how great is the profit of penance!

But perhaps it may be said, on the contrary: 'It is indeed conceded, but not the crime of idolatry; for although these are grave, yet greater is the crime of idolatry.' Yet rightly has Novatian's assertion been exploded, and in no small part, whereby two grave crimes are seen to be conceded to penance, because if fornication ought not to be forgiven, as it seems to Novatian, how much more homicide or adultery! But hence his vanity appears, when these things which are graver are proved to be conceded. Wherefore now it must be inquired whether God has promised that pardon can be granted to idolaters. For everything which shall not rightly have been conceded here, neither will it be able to be conceded in the future. For here, as the Lord said, sins are either bound or loosed; but in the future there will be nothing else except reward or condemnation. For everyone who is here rightly reproved will not be able to be chosen or judged worthy in the future.

Let us therefore hear what the Lord says concerning idolaters to the prophet Jeremiah: Go, he says, and say, Return to me, O dwelling of Israel, says the Lord, and I will not set my face against you, for I am merciful, says the Lord, nor am I angry with you for ever. Yet nevertheless acknowledge your offense; you have acted impiously and have set your ways toward strangers beneath every leafy tree. Behold what God promises to those who after their fall turn back to him, that to those who return after apostasy he may remit their sins. And again God says through Jeremiah: My dainty ones have walked rough ways, they have been led like a flock plundered by enemies. Be steadfast, children, and cry aloud to God; for there will be a remembrance of you in your carrying away. For as your mind was such that you strayed from God, so, being converted, you will redouble ten times as much in seeking him; for he who has brought evils upon you will bring upon you everlasting gladness together with your salvation. O the clemency of God! In what manner he provokes the fallen to raise themselves up! In what way he exhorts them that after the error of idols they may return to him! Like a kindly father he promises to his children the perennial gladness of salvation, if they correct themselves. Likewise in the same prophet, among other things: And I will restore them to this land and I will rebuild them and I will not cast them down; and I will replant them and I will not pluck them up — so that, because he had said by way of threat: He who sacrifices to gods shall be rooted out, lest those sinning against God should despair of themselves, as though now those who had sacrificed to idols, having been rooted out, could not be replanted, he exhorts them, since, if they repent, they will be able to be restored to their former condition. For the sentence stands then, if the offense is not amended.

Finally, it was proclaimed to the Ninevites that after three days their city would be overthrown. Why after three days, unless either that they might repent and the sentence be taken away, or that, remaining in it, they might more justly perish? This is what he said through the prophet: I do not desire the death of the dying, so much as that he should return and live. Thus it came about that pardon was granted to the Ninevites, who were to die for the cause of their sins, since repentance followed. For why do we find that under Noah the ark was built over a hundred years, unless that, seeing and hearing what was impending, they might correct themselves? For God wishes no one to perish. Hence it is that in Solomon we read: Because, he says, the Most High has had mercy on those who repent, and again: You have mercy on all, because you can do all things. And you overlook the sins of men for the sake of repentance. In this sense also the Lord, grieving over the faithlessness of the Jews, says: Jerusalem, Jerusalem, who kill the prophets and stone those sent to you, how often have I wished to gather your children, in the manner that a hen gathers her chicks under her wings, and you would not!

Therefore throughout the whole law the Lord always exhorts sinners to turn to him, not willing that his own work should be liable to perdition, since there is no other reason for the giving of the law than that men should be turned from error to the truth—yet in such a way that in the New Testament the author of the law appears more merciful, because at the time when he deigned to make manifest the mystery of his Son our Lord, he willed to be more generous and more abundant in mercy, so that there might be more grace when the Son preached than there had been when the servants preached. And not undeservedly: for thus it was fitting that he should bestow greater gifts through the Son than through the servants. Finally, to believers who have turned to him he grants all their sins at once, with no lamentation of penance intervening. Whence the apostle Paul says: For the gifts and the calling of God are without repentance. But afterward, if they have sinned, they will be able to earn pardon through penance, so that, because they sinned after receiving the freely-given mercy, they may be able to attain to forgiveness no longer freely, but with groaning and weeping intervening.

For it is impossible for a man not to sin. Whence the apostle John says: If we say that we have no sin, we deceive ourselves and the truth is not in us; if we confess our sins, he is faithful and just, to forgive us our sins and to cleanse us from all unrighteousness. Thus David earned pardon and restoration when he confesses his sin. Surely the apostle is speaking to Christians; for he says in what follows: My little children, these things I write to us, that you sin not; and if any man sin, we have an advocate with the Father, Jesus Christ. He says this, so that if we have sinned after baptism, we may as suppliants entreat the advocate, that he may plead with the Father on our behalf. For if among our forefathers penance had a place, how can it be that in this time it should not have a place, when the mercy of the preaching is greater? And will he who had mercy on enemies by calling them to grace not have mercy on those who have been made friends, when a price is paid? For the tribulation of the heart is the price of the penitent. But if this were true, men would grieve that from enemies they had been made friends. Or what sort of one would he have to be reckoned, if he gave mercy to enemies who did not repent, but did not give it to friends who even pray for it? Moreover, since the Lord knows that the devil exalts himself and rages more against the friends and servants of God—whence the apostle Peter says: Watch, be sober, because our adversary the devil, roaring like a lion, goes about seeking whom he may devour?

Since therefore the Lord knows that we have a dreadful and mighty adversary, if we have been conquered by him, he wishes us to flee to his help, grieving that we have been struck down, so that, restored by his power, we may fight back bravely. For just as, unless those days which the Lord speaks of be shortened, no flesh shall be saved, so also, if penance be denied to men, no one will be able to be saved. For if no one, he says, will boast that he has a clean heart. And elsewhere he says: No one is without filth, not even an infant of one day. Who will be able to escape, if penance is denied? And where is that which the Lord says, that there will be joy in heaven over a sinner doing penance?

But: 'the greater crimes,' he says, 'ought not to be pardoned — fornication and idolatry.' And where is the dominical saying, in which he promises that all sins and blasphemies shall be forgiven to men, but the blasphemy of the Holy Spirit shall not be forgiven to men? He says that absolutely all sins can be forgiven except one. Now choose, Novatian, concerning whom you wish it to have been said: concerning those not yet believing, or concerning those already believing? If concerning those not believing, how is the remission of sins given, since it is agreed that all, before they believe, sin against God and against the Holy Spirit? For some call the Lord's deeds acts of magic, assigning the work of the Holy Spirit to demons. But if you wish it to have been said concerning believers, your assertion is excluded, because all sins will be forgiven: and whoever, he says, shall speak a word against the Son of Man, it will be forgiven him. Therefore whether one sins against God or against Christ, it will be forgiven to men; for he has embraced all sins except that against the Holy Spirit. And so he does not wish this sin to be understood — that which is sinned against God — to be also a sin against the Holy Spirit; for if it were this, he would certainly not have excepted the latter. Since therefore he signifies another sin, when blasphemy is spoken against the Holy Spirit — for this saying pertains to the person, not to the nature of the Holy Spirit —, whether to those already believing through penance, or to those who will believe through baptism, all sins will be forgiven.

But that the Jews sinned against the Holy Spirit is another matter, and one which pertained to that time. Hence he shows that this is not forgiven to them, neither here nor in the future. For they did not sin against the Holy Spirit through error, but through malice. For, knowing and being fully aware that the works which they saw in the deeds of the Savior were of God, in order to turn the people away from faith in him they pretended that these were the works of the prince of demons. Hence it is that the Lord says to them: you have the key of knowledge, and neither do you enter yourselves nor allow others to enter. This sentence, therefore, was pronounced against the malicious, for whom no remedy can be found that they might be saved. For nothing is graver than this crime; for he feigns to be false what he knows to be true. For the Jews, who seemed to be under the law of God and to whom the promise of Christ had been made, seeing the mighty works and understanding this to be what God had promised, were blinded by envy, so that they denied this to be so, and not only denied it, but even persecuted him unto death. Therefore it is not fitting that this sin should ever have to be forgiven to these, who, with their own conscience as witness, dared to resist God, to whom they said they were devoted.

But that sins may be committed either against God or against Christ, the causes are various. For either someone, while he envies another's happiness, rises up against the Creator out of grief at his own lowliness; or, having suffered something sudden and adverse, stirred up by wrath he becomes insolent toward his parent. But in Christ's case, his incarnation provokes the injury. For while men are ignorant of the mystery of the incarnate Christ, they blaspheme, reckoning it unworthy that the Son of God, who is of an incorporeal and simple nature, should be said to have been born after the manner of men. They blaspheme, therefore, because they are ignorant of the mystery. For which reason a hand must be extended to such men when they are converted, because they stand against the truth in this way while they think they are acting on behalf of the truth. A man of this sort, when tortured and torn with hooks, says that this is true which he follows; but the Jews mentioned above thought one thing and did another, and, without judge or executioner, they are forever tortured with their own conscience as witness. For he who through error has become an enemy of the truth, when he has been converted, rejoices that he has come to know the truth. For the malevolent man has nothing to amend; for if he converts himself, he will begin to speak the truth which before he did not know to be false.

But that an injury should be done to the Holy Spirit, no cause exists; for his office gives rise to no offense, because he appears in the power of signs and wonders. For who would dare to leap up against him, seeing the dead raised? Or who would be angry at him, by whose working he sees paralytics running, the blind admit the light, the deaf hear? Or how will he not love him, whom he sees cast out unclean spirits, cleanse lepers, and at the same time heal all infirmities? To this one the Jews chose to do injury, saying that his work was the work of demons, provided only that they might satisfy the envy and hatred which they were exercising against the Savior. Therefore he shows that this sin will be remitted to them neither here nor in the world to come.

But Novatian says: "Let it be proved that the graver crimes are forgiven by name — that is, idolatry and fornication after the washing. For it is for this reason that in baptism," he says, "all sins are remitted together, so that thereafter, if they commit anything grave, they may in no way obtain pardon; because after the reception of the Holy Spirit, that which is against the Holy Spirit ought not to be admitted." Who would deny that one ought never to sin — if only it were possible? But moreover God, knowing the human race to be frail, has always attended them with saving remedies, so that after their sins they might have a means by which to restore themselves. But if the Spirit that has been given forbids that penance be granted to those who sin, then there is no providence in the New Testament, and it will seem that better provision was made for the men of old, to whom this medicine was by no means denied. And how is the grace greater in the New than in the Old? For this is the work of the enemy, who — knowing that men cannot be provided for otherwise, so as to attain to salvation, than by the intervention of penance — should give the Holy Spirit, in such a way that, if an injury be done to Him through some fear or by error, satisfaction should now have no place for reforming the man; this is to have sought an occasion for destroying the man, not for saving him. But far be it, for the Lord says: The Son of Man did not come to destroy souls, but to save them. For if God has commanded that the sins which we sin against one another mutually, if we are converted, be remitted by us to one another seventy times seven times, how much more does He Himself, if He be sinned against, pardon those who are converted! For He wills that every sinner be converted.

Whence the Apostle proves that remission was granted to fornicators and the unclean, saying: Lest perhaps when I come I should mourn many of those who sinned before and have not done penance over the uncleanness and fornication which they committed. He proves that certain ones have done penance, over whom he does not grieve but rejoices; but he signifies that some have not done penance for their fornication, on account of which he also says that he mourns them as not pertaining to life, because they have not done penance. For if penance for this crime were always to be done in this life, he would not say that he mourns them because they have not done penance, but because they do not do it. But knowing that penance is here both begun and completed, he says that "they have not done" it. For this is what he wished: that when he came to them he might have peace with their communion. Behold, it has been proved that fornicators are to be admitted to communion after penance has been done. For communion cannot be granted to them so long as they are doing penance; but then communion is to be granted, when they cease to do it by the judgment of the Church.

We now have to prove whether idolaters in particular can be granted pardon. For we read in the Apocalypse of the blessed apostle John that repentance over idolatry is proclaimed. For the bishops are admonished, whom he calls angels — that is, messengers, just as the blessed apostle Paul also says: you received me as an angel of God —, that they should grant repentance to those who sin and not suffer them to go without admonishment at all, those who cause scandals in the church, since they too are of the church. He says therefore: And to the angel of the church of Pergamum write: These things says he who has the sword sharp on both sides: I know where you dwell, where the seat of Satan is; and you hold my name and have not denied my faith in the days. In which Antipas my most faithful witness, who was slain among you, where Satan dwells. But I have a few things against you, because you have there those who hold the doctrine of Balaam, who taught Balak to cast a scandal before the eyes of the sons of Israel, to eat things offered and to fornicate; so also you have those who hold the doctrine of the Nicolaitans. Likewise do penance. This he says to be the doctrine of the Nicolaitans which was also that of Balaam, that is, idolatry and fornication, over which he commands penance to be done. And because he speaks to the men of the church, he shows it by saying: These things says the Spirit to the churches.

And because there is a fruit of repentance, he indicates it thus: To him who conquers, he says, I will give to eat of the manna which is hidden — that through repentance one may conquer sins. For what is it to repent, except to grieve over things past and henceforth to abstain from those things which one had committed? —■ And to the angel of the church of Thyatira write: These things says the Son of God, who has his eyes like a flame of fire, and his feet like fine brass: I know your works and your love and faith and ministry and patience, and your last works better than the former. But I have against you, that you allow the woman Jezebel, who calls herself a prophetess and teaches and seduces my servants to fornicate and to eat things offered to idols. And I gave her time to repent, and she would not do penance from her fornication. Behold, I will cast her into a bed, and her adulterers with her into great tribulation, unless they do penance of their deeds, and I will kill her children with death; and all the churches shall know that I am he who searches the reins and hearts, and I will give to each one of you according to his works. But to you I say, and to the rest who are at Thyatira, who do not have this doctrine and who do not know the depths of Satan, as they say: I will not put upon you another burden: but nevertheless hold what you have, until I come. And to him who conquers and keeps my works unto the end, I will give him power over the nations.

Under a single letter given to the church at Thyatira he confounds and refutes the twofold error of the Novatianist heresy; for he both promises fruit to those doing penance for the sake of idolatry, and shows that in one church the evil do not contaminate the good, since they were assuredly of one communion. For he was not writing to those placed outside the church when he says: He who has ears, let him hear, he says, what the Spirit says to the churches. For in like manner the apostle Paul says: But we know that whatever the law speaks, it speaks to those who are under the law; and in another place he says: For what have I to do with judging those who are outside? Therefore he partly summons the people of one church to penance, and partly commends them, saying: But I say to you, and to the rest who are at Thyatira, whoever do not have this teaching — that is, of idolatry — , hold fast what you have, until I come; that is, <persevere unto the end', because he who perseveres unto the end, he shall be saved. In like manner also he who perseveres in evil shall be damned. And through this, just as the righteousness of the righteous shall not deliver him in the day in which he goes astray, so also the iniquity of the unrighteous shall not harm him, when he turns himself. Therefore he said: He who sacrifices to gods shall be rooted out; but if he turns himself, he shall live with life. So too is that which the Lord says: He who denies me, I will deny him. But if he turns himself, he cannot be denied by the one whom he confesses.

Hence it is that he said in the psalm: No living man shall be justified in my sight. For this sentence is imputed to a man in that state in which he dies. For because in the church the evil were to be with the good until the judgment of God, the Lord himself also indicated this, saying: The kingdom of heaven is like a net cast into the sea, which gathers from every kind of fish. But when they had come to the shore, they separated the good into their vessels, but the bad they cast outside. So shall it be in the consummation of the age: the angels shall go forth and shall separate from the kingdom of God all scandals and those who work iniquity. This matter is signified also in that man who was found among those reclining at table not having a wedding garment, whom alone he shows cast out from the assembly of the church, because the offense of his unworthiness did not defile the rest; and because this unworthiness of his was marked out, in that he did not have a wedding garment, he was manifest to the rest. For they saw that he, among them, was clothed otherwise.

Hence comes that saying of the apostle Paul: "But in a great house," he says, "there are not only vessels of gold and silver, but also of wood and clay." He put this because of Hymenaeus and Philetus, who, although they were of the church, held perverse views concerning the resurrection, just as certain men of the church of the Corinthians were denying the resurrection. But Novatian holds that this great house is to be understood as the world, not the church, lest he be forced to assert that in one church there are the evil together with the good, the clean together with the unclean. Yet I think this is easily refuted. For in one house all are under one name; for although they may be diverse in their conduct, nevertheless they are reckoned in their profession under the one name of their lord. This world, however, contains men of diverse profession, so that they profess, being unlike one another, that they have both gods and lords. You see, therefore, that this could not have been said of the world, but of the church, in which those living under one name are diverse in deed. And indeed I observe that Novatian was not ignorant of this; but because he had no just grounds for complaint regarding his own secession, in order that he might not openly blush for shame, he forged for himself certain arguments through a wicked interpretation of the law, by which he might strive to excuse and to cloak the envious will of his malice.

Finally he replied to these things which we have said, saying: "I too do not refuse that penance should be performed for idolatry once committed, but I do not dare to remit it, because this crime must be remitted by the one against whom it was committed." But this he replied with guile. For he is exposed by his own interpretations, in which his fraud is made apparent. For when he says: "It is written: 'Whoever shall deny me, I too will deny him,' and he cannot lie whose words these are," he shows that the penance which he says must be performed is empty and unfruitful. For if the sentence has been given and he who gave it does not deceive, why do you compel a man to be tortured with lamentations and groanings, when you either deny the effect of this thing or confess that you scarcely know it? For everyone who gives counsel to anyone gives it assuredly to this end, that he may proclaim what benefit one may gain from it. But because you, Novatian, profess this deceitfully, you say that penance must be performed in order to mock the minds of men. For knowing openly that this cannot be denied, you act subtly, because you cut off penance not in word but in effect. Certainly you say that everyone who has sacrificed to idols sins against the Holy Spirit, for whom you contend, through the words of the law, that this sin is remitted neither here nor in the age to come. In what way, then, do you say that penance for this offense must be performed, unless it is because you deceive, as I have shown above?

But you are wont to want to cover this deceit with another trick, saying that in the same sense the Apostle Peter too said to Simon: do penance for this wickedness of yours, if perhaps it may be forgiven you. But the Apostle Peter, observing the venomous and most bitter malice of the man, therefore gave him a doubtful reply. For what mortal has so sinned as to wish to purchase the gift of God with money? This reply, then, was given to malevolence, not to error. For it is malevolence that has no remedy, since it is neither an error nor a necessity by which one might be excused, so as to be able to arrive at pardon through weeping. If, therefore, any be found in such a cause, let them hear what Simon also heard. For the Apostle Peter shows why he answered doubtfully when he says: for I see that you are in the gall of bitterness and in the bond of iniquity. If, then, in so grave a cause, and one that seems to be without remedy, the answer was given doubtfully, it is not doubtful that those who sin against God through error or some necessity can be forgiven, if they do fitting penance. For this has been granted to ecclesiastical law by its author, that it may both impose penance and after penance receive back.

There is also another thing that Novatian censures: 'why,' he says, 'do they hand over the body of the Lord to those whom they know to be sinners?' as if they themselves, who are judges, could be accusers. But if they have been accused and made manifest, they can be cast out. For what judge takes on the person of an accuser? For if the Lord himself endured Judas, whom he knew to be a thief and to carry off the things that were put into the purse, and did not cast out one who was not accused, one ought to use this example, so that it be not permitted to cast out one who has not been publicly exposed. For neither does it befit a just man to accuse anyone. Whence it says, Joseph, since he was a just man and was unwilling to make her a public example, wished to put her away secretly. Judas, therefore, though he was among the disciples, did not defile them by his contagion, they at any rate dissenting from his thefts, and receiving the Eucharist among them he did not pollute the innocent, nor did the Lord deny his body to him whom he knew to be a robber, because, according to the Apostle, he who takes it unworthily takes a sword unto himself — wherefore he did not even turn himself away from his kiss. For those defile sinners who consent to their evils. Whence it is said: you saw a thief and you ran along with him.

Hence it is that the Apostle reproves the people of the Corinthians, because they did not correct or avoid the one who publicly had his father's wife, so that he might amend himself, saying: do you not know that a little leaven corrupts the whole lump? For since there was no leader or overseer of the church, it belonged to the people to correct the one whom they saw conducting himself so basely and obscenely among them. And through this the Apostle reproves them as, in a manner, consenting to his crime.
