For in this matter there was no need of an accuser or of witnesses; for he publicly had his stepmother in the place of a wife.

And so, as far as it pertains to our case, we have set forth our argument, not accusing others, but defending ourselves. But I call to witness the conscience of the Novatian, who is forever accusing us, whether he knows that all whom he keeps in his company are holy. Yet he is so angry with us that he even denies that we are Christians, though he knows that we have been dyed with that same faith on which he himself relies. Surely it is profession and life that make a Christian. If, then, you see a man professing the same things as you, and not unlike you in his life, why do you deny that he is what you are? Surely he who says "the Lord Jesus" says it in the Holy Spirit; for he speaks the truth. For all truth is from God. Why do you not assent to one who speaks true things, but deny him? You contradict one who confesses God, not by reason, but by fury. Do you not seem to be an enemy of God, you who resist one professing true things about him? Does a pagan, hearing someone say that idols are gods, deny that he is a pagan? Why do you deny to be a Christian one whom you hear professing the words of the entire faith, when you read: with the heart one believes unto righteousness, but with the mouth confession is made unto salvation? But you, like an enemy — a thing which through your wrath you do not see — fight against God, denying that one who confesses these things has salvation, though the Lord says: Whoever confesses me, him also will I confess. Does he say: "If a Novatian confesses me"? But you so build up this claim, that you seem to yourself to be defending this: that he is saved who is not only a Christian, but who has also been a Novatian. Yet if confession made in Christ gives salvation, then wherever, among whomever this confession is whole, it has salvation. And so, if the tradition of this confession is corrupted among us, or its sense altered, you accuse rightly. For if this remains in us just as also in you, then by denying us you seem to deny yourself as well.

But you accuse the ministers of the faith; grant that it is true, though you cannot prove it. Yet those who have heard from these men—is what they heard true or false? For even if a vicious man has spoken the truth, it will not thereby fail to be true simply because it was spoken by a vicious man, since the matter of one's manner of life is one thing and that of one's profession another. Does not the persecutor, out of hatred for the Christian name, persecute the profession, not the manner of life? In the first place, knowing that this profits religion, when we confess that we are Christians, the sacrilegious man, when we confess, believes what he does not wish to hear, because he hates it. But you, since you are of the same profession and the same seal, do not believe us when we confess the very things that you yourself confess. If you knew us to have been reborn otherwise than you were reborn, you would rightly not believe that we are what you are. Why do I suffer persecution from a sacrilegious man, if I am not what you are? For if I professed of myself what you say of me, I would not suffer persecution. In the end, I am to be believed concerning myself, not you. For the judge seeks my profession, not yours concerning me. Why then, when I confess, do you deny, when in this cause it is true that not another's testimony but my own profession is required, whether for me or against me? I wished you to be tortured for the name against me, so that it might appear whether, when I confessed the truth, you would deny it against me, or which of us should be believed. For you propose this by a trick, so that you may seem to have a just cause for schism. For you are not unaware that in this matter my testimony concerning myself is required, not another's, and that that is held to be true which I shall have confessed, not what another shall have denied.

It is therefore manifest that those are free who, out of love for Christ, not scrutinizing the deeds of men, run together to attain faith in him, approving in these men that which they know to be useful to themselves—that is, that they may attain the tradition of the faith which is concerning Christ. For whoever thinks that, besides the ministry, anything else of the man is necessary in this matter, does injury to the tradition; for he thinks too little of the grace of God. For when he denies that the gift of God can profit one devoted to Him unless the merit of the man is present, he makes the man either superior or equal to the tradition itself, whereas the merit of the man is far removed from this office, just as the apostle Paul says concerning this same matter: I planted, Apollos watered, but God gave the increase. And so neither he who plants is anything nor he who waters, but God who gives the increase.

How, in this matter, is anything to be hoped for from man, when the words that are handed on have been given [by God]? Let us therefore see what benefit it will bring if he be of good merit, or what harm if he be of bad merit. The man who approaches desires to hear the words of God, and by lending them his faith he believes that he receives salvation. What difference does it make in this matter whether the one who delivers the solemn words is of a good or a bad life, when what is required is the devout faith of the one who receives, so that he may believe with his whole soul the things which he hears? Surely, if the one who delivers them is of a bad life, will he place an impediment before the one who receives, so that he should not believe the things he hears, which are to be believed — so that that man's sin should enter into his heart and keep him from believing? Or does God, who is just, have to despise one who is devoted to Him on account of the sin of a man whom He Himself allows to be in [holy] office? Nor does even a man judge thus — a man who, as is agreed, is frequently deceived or is partial. If it were so, and the man were to plead his own cause, would he not say to God: 'Who this man is on whose account You despise me, I do not know. I came to him for Your sake, because he was called Your priest. You I sought, You I desired, You I believed; from man I hoped for nothing: why, then, should his evils harm me — a man whom I would not know had I not sought You? If his good deeds could profit me, it would perhaps seem just that his evils too should harm me. But just as his good deeds, had I believed doubtingly, would have profited me nothing, so too his evils ought to do no harm to me who believe well. For I gave my faith to his words, which are said to be given by You and which breathe You, speak You, and promise concerning You; to this man I believed nothing concerning himself nor his deeds, but I joined myself to the faith which is from You.'

According to these things, the cause of the believer is intact. For take the priests after the manner of advocates. Surely, if the advocate is of a bad life, will judgment be pronounced against his client? This is the office of an advocate, that according to the order of the law he plead the cause of his client: surely his life, if it is base, cannot harm his client's cause? For the person of the advocate can neither harm nor profit; for of whatever sort the cause has been, so too will he be answered. So too are those who wish to become Christians. They approach the priest, they tell him their desires, he speaks the words of ecclesiastical law. If the desires are true, they are received by the judge. In what way can that man either harm or profit, seeing that he does not even know the cause of his client? For to God the judge alone is it known who approaches with what mind. It belongs, therefore, to the priest to discharge the office delegated to him, but to the judge either to accept or to refuse the cause of the client. For He Himself is found to have said: Daughter, thy faith hath made thee whole.

You see, therefore, that by no one's help, but each one is saved by his own faith. For God has decreed this to faith, that, if it does not doubt, it may obtain everything which it wills. You, however, not as a friend but as an enemy of Christ, have gathered a people under your own name, so that they should not be Christians unless they be Novatians — whereas the apostle condemns it if anyone should say that he is of Paul or of some other. But you, judging yourself the more powerful, have set your own name above the name of Christ, as though He were not sufficient to protect His own Church.

CIII. SINCE IT IS ESTABLISHED THAT IN LEVITICUS THE LORD COMMANDED CONCERNING THE SACRIFICES TO BE OFFERED, HOW IS IT THAT, ANGERED AT THE PEOPLE, HE SAYS THAT HE DID NOT COMMAND THEIR FATHERS TO OFFER LIBATIONS OR SACRIFICES OR BURNT OFFERINGS? FOR THUS HE SAYS THROUGH THE PROPHET JEREMIAH: 'THUS SAYS THE LORD: GATHER TOGETHER YOUR BURNT OFFERINGS WITH YOUR SACRIFICES AND EAT THE FLESH, FOR I DID NOT SPEAK TO YOUR FATHERS NOR COMMAND THEM, ON THE DAY WHEN I BROUGHT THEM OUT OF THE LAND OF EGYPT, CONCERNING BURNT OFFERINGS AND SACRIFICES.'

If you attend closely, there is no reason to raise a question about this matter; and do not suppose that the Lord lied or changed, so as to command something and afterward deny it. For concerning sacrifices and libations or burnt offerings of this kind nothing is found to have been commanded, concerning which He testifies that He said nothing. For take note that there are two kinds of sacrifices: one which was commanded to be offered according to each individual kind, and another which was said to be voluntary. That one, therefore, which was commanded to be offered through its various kinds, whether for sins or for firstborn, it was commanded what and of what sort and in what manner it was to be offered. But the voluntary sacrifice was assuredly not commanded, because it was left to one's own discretion, so that, if anyone wished to offer, he might offer what seemed good to him; for these were votive sacrifices. And because they offered these carelessly and heedlessly, without deliberating that whoever offered ought to offer choice gifts to one more powerful, therefore it is said to them: 'I did not command you concerning these; but if you wish, you ought to offer things that are worthy'.

For thus it is found that Cain also sinned, when he did not consider that those things ought to be offered to God which are the better ones. For when they said that they offered these to propitiate God, in that very thing they provoked God to indignation, so that in the very matter in which they said they were doing well they sinned by offering to God unworthy burnt offerings. Hence He says in the prophet Malachi: if you bring to the sacrifice a blind or a lame or a sick animal, it is not good. But offer it to your leader or your superior, whether he will accept you, says the Lord almighty. Hence it is that in Isaiah God complains about the same matter, saying: what to me are the sheep of your burnt offering? nor in your sacrifices have you honored me. For I did not make you for this, that you should toil over incense or that you should buy me frankincense with silver, nor did I desire the fat of your sacrifices; but in your sins and iniquities you have stood before me. It is therefore clear that the complaints arose because, when they offered voluntary sacrifices, they did not honor God in them, offering unworthy things, since this was assuredly not a matter of necessity. For if they had been commanded, they seemed to offer of whatever sort out of necessity: but when they offered these of their own discretion, they ought to have offered such things as would show forth in themselves obedience to God. But because their mind wandered in desire for idols, they did these things without consideration. Therefore they are reproached with a double kind of fault, because both, acting wickedly, they dared to offer to God, and they offered things that were unworthy, since wicked deeds are not redeemed by sacrifices, but by lamentation and mercy.

Whence David says when he had sinned: "If you had wished it, I would surely have given sacrifices; in burnt offerings you will not delight. A sacrifice to God is a spirit crushed with sorrow, and a heart contrite and humbled God does not despise." Then Samuel says to Saul, who, when he had despised the commandment of the Lord, thought that God could be appeased by sacrifice: "For God does not," he says, "desire sacrifice more than that his voice be heard." And Scripture says: "I desire mercy rather than sacrifice," because, since they were unjust and unmerciful, they supposed that God was made favorable by offerings, just as even now certain people who spurn justice think themselves praised on account of their offerings. Good therefore is an offering, if justice be preserved and mercy not neglected, because what we seek for ourselves we ought also to bestow on others.

CIIII. ON THE GOSPEL OF LUKE.

The Lord, knowing that the Jewish war against him was to come, told his disciples to prepare swords for themselves. And when this had been done, when the war had been begun under the leadership of Simon the Iscariot, the apostle Peter, seeing the enemies rising up, drew a sword and cut off the ear of the servant of the priests. Then the Lord told Peter to put back the sword into its sheath: "For everyone," he says, "who takes the sword shall perish by the sword." Why did he order them prepared? He who forbade the striking, designating even as guilty of death him who should strike with the sword, when the apostle does not indeed seem to have struck unjustly? For as far as concerns the matter, he struck him whom he saw armed against him. For he ought to have resisted the ungrateful servants who were armed to slay the Lord.

If you examine the individual members of the question, whose reason you wish to be laid open, you will find also other things which you may inquire into from these things said. For what need was there that this one, who was relying on spiritual power, should order carnal swords to be prepared, and that he who earlier on the way had ordered that neither a bag nor money nor a staff be carried, should now order to be had those things which he earlier forbade?

Let us begin therefore to explain why he ordered swords to be prepared and forbade the striking. For not only for the sake of the enemies, but also for the sake of his own disciples, did he command arms to be procured for the war that was to come upon them. For a new and marvelous thing was to be, that this one, who was doing new and unheard-of signs, should so suddenly be humbled that he should be subjected both to blows and to death: lest therefore he should seem to suffer these things as if unforeseeing, and as one from whom perhaps power had departed, he foretold all these things thus, so that he should not be in doubt about them, but foreknowing should prepare himself against these things, and, because he was humbled by his own will, should command that the enemies not be resisted more fiercely—showing to such a degree that he himself was the one who he had been, that, already having been seized, he restored the ear which had been cut off, (and) that he gave back what had fallen away by the working of the sword. Not indeed as a carnal physician, but as the maker of the body, he refashioned his own work that had been maimed. For physicians cannot repair cut-off members of the body. But so that the power of God might be seen not to be diminished in him, but to be present just as it had been, and that he might confirm that which he had said: "I have power to lay down my life and I have power to take it up again," for this cause therefore he ordered swords to be prepared, yet did not order to kill.

Now it remains to address why, in considering the deed of the apostle Peter, he said that the one who had used the sword would perish by the sword, since it does not seem that he struck unjustly. For, as is indicated in Luke, he struck with the Lord himself permitting it, and thus, being forbidden, he heard these words. It was permitted for this reason, namely, that they might be seen both to be able to avenge themselves and yet to be unwilling, and that the power might be shown to remain in him in whom it had been, so that in both respects it would not be doubted that they could avenge themselves, while the enemies would hold it for certain that the power had been given to them by him to do these things. For he did not appear as one conquered, but as one handing himself over to their will. Why, then, was the sentence given, that whoever takes the sword should perish by the sword, except because it is lawful for no one save the judge to kill anyone with the sword? But to the apostle Peter it was permitted only up to this point, that he should cause pain, not that he should kill. For hearing this, that he should not strike again, he learned besides that, to those who have now become Christians, it is not lawful to kill; for to those who are set in mercy it is not lawful to make use harshly of the law of the justice entrusted to the world. But that which he seems to have commanded to the disciples going ahead on the way, that they should take nothing on the way, is for the sake of peace and by the grace of virtues and the affability of teaching. For what need was there to take anything on the way for those to whom, for the sake of signs, all things were to be offered? But when the time came in which he permitted himself to suffer, on account of which a strife—rather a war—was threatening, he orders the disciples to be armed, not that he who wished to suffer might resist, but, as I said, that he might show himself both foreknowing of this matter and having his passion in his power, yet that he might humble himself for the sake of human salvation, which will be told in its own place.

CV. THAT THE GOSPEL AGREES WITH PROPHECY.

Since at the first coming of the Lord it was foretold that the sun would be darkened in his passion at the very hour in which it is proved to have happened—that is, at midday; for thus it is written in the prophet Amos: and it shall come to pass in that day, says the Lord, the sun shall set at midday and the day of light shall grow dark upon the earth—, on that day therefore the sun was darkened from the sixth hour until the ninth hour, and thus the prophecy of the aforesaid prophet was fulfilled. For the image of this thing, which is to be in the second coming of the Lord, was shown in the passion of the Lord, because in the end, when the Lord shall have begun to come in order to judge the world, the luminaries shall be released from their office, the Lord saying: in those days the sun shall be darkened and the moon shall not give its brightness. Which indeed the prophet Joel also did not omit, so that, since it is signified by Joel, it may without doubt be believed to be fulfilled. For thus he said: the sun shall be turned into darkness and the moon into blood, before the great and manifest day of the Lord comes. This, as far as it appears, agrees rather with the second coming; for then it will be openly manifested to all, as the scripture says: then shall every eye see him, and all the tribes of the earth shall wail over him, even they who crucified him.

Therefore, if in both comings this is contained—that the sun will be darkened in those days—what is that day on which Scripture sang that the sun and the moon would be enlarged? For Isaiah says, among other things: And there shall be upon every high mountain, and in every high place, water running through in that day, when many shall perish, when the towers shall fall. And the light of the moon shall be as the light of the sun, and the light of the sun shall be sevenfold, as the light of one day, in the day when he shall heal the breaking of his people, and so forth. What day can be understood, on which the Lord promised that he would heal the breaking of his people? This, I think, is the day which John's father Zechariah pointed out, saying: Blessed is the God of Israel, because he has visited and wrought the redemption of his people, and has raised up a horn of salvation for us in the house of David his servant, as he spoke through the mouth of his holy prophets, who are from the beginning. And he has delivered us from our enemies, and from the hands of all who hated us, to show mercy toward our fathers. And Simeon, among other things, says: This one is set for the ruin and for the resurrection of many in Israel. This <ruin> is that of the scribes and Pharisees and the leaders of the Jews, whom the prophet signified in the word 'towers.'

Therefore, when these had fallen through unbelief, others rose up by believing—those who before seemed weak and sick through negligence. Whence the Savior says: For judgment I have come into this world, that those who do not see may see, and those who see may become blind—so that those who through the cultivation and skill of the law seemed to be lights might be blinded through unbelief, while the blind, that is, the unskilled or the publicans, might see, once they believed. Their infirmities the prophet foretold that the Savior would bear. And so this was done in the time of the Savior, as is read in the Gospel, that he himself took our infirmities and healed our wounds.

Which things indeed were all accomplished and fulfilled in the Savior's passion; for thus the salvation of the human race was both redeemed and made secure, both among those above and among those below, because the prophecy of the aforesaid Zechariah embraces both. For both here among those above they were freed from the crushing of their enemies by the intervention of mercy, and there among those below, when they were snatched out of Tartarus. For all who had hoped in the promised Christ were awaiting his coming, that, death having been conquered, they might be freed from the underworld. Whence he said: to show mercy to our fathers. But if anyone should understand this salvation to be contained also in the second coming, yet in each coming, and most of all in the second, the sun shall be turned into darkness and the moon shall not give her brightness. How then shall we perceive that the sun and moon are increased on the day when the Lord shall visit his people, when in both times, in which the liberation of the people was begun and fulfilled, we read that the sun and moon are not diminished but darkened? Unless plainly we understand the sun and moon to be compared to the saints, just as in another place we read that the saints are made equal to the lights, as the apostle Paul preaches, because among the rest of men the saints in this world are seen as it were as lights; and in the Gospel: then, he says, the just shall shine as the sun. For the Lord calls just works light; for thus he says: let your light shine before men, that seeing your good works they may glorify your Father, who is in heaven.

These therefore, who having forsaken all their own follow the Lord for the sake of justice and sanctity, are compared to the sun and moon, honored sevenfold, as the Lord says: there is no one who leaves house or wife or children or fields, who shall not receive sevenfold as much in this world. Those therefore who at present are honored sevenfold, it can be understood that in the future also they are believed to be about to be sevenfold brighter than the rest, so that their resurrection may be compared to the sun and the moon; but that of the rest is as the stars. The brightness therefore of the saints, which here is honored sevenfold, in the resurrection attains another sevenfold; so that he who here has the brightness of the moon in the future may receive the brightness of the sun, and he who here is compared to the sun is believed to receive sevenfold as much in the future.

The prophet Isaiah therefore foretold that by the comparison of the sun and moon the merits of the saints are increased, and not without reason. For if the Lord himself is called (the sun of justice), and the saints shall be like him, as the apostle John says: for when he shall appear, he says, we shall be like him, it is fitting that the saints are signified in the sun. Yet the aforesaid prophet shows the sun to be greater by seven parts than the moon. For when he shows the moon to be as the light of the sun in the future, but the sun sevenfold as much, he signifies that the moon is increased indeed to the measure of the sun sevenfold, so that it may be such as the sun is. Although the account of the saints is here treated, yet under this pretext he has set forth in part the quantity of the lights, so that after the increase of the sun and moon it may again be as much greater as the sun was, so that, as much as in the present a saint differs from a saint, so much may he also differ in the future after the reward.

CVI. ON THE BOOK OF GENESIS.

In the beginning God made heaven and earth. If in the beginning he made heaven and also earth, and heaven is the firmament, as appears in what follows, this is not true, because light was made first and then the firmament; for it could not signify another heaven, since it describes the making of the world as accomplished by God's working. For because men were being deceived by diverse opinions concerning the cause of the world, Moses, under God's inspiration, showed what the true account of the world was. For to some it seemed to have no beginning; to others indeed to have a beginning, but to have been fashioned by certain angels; to some, however, to have been made likewise, but by a certain Sacla, who was a god of the opposing power; but a few, following the transmission of the truth about the origin, asserted God to be its maker. Therefore on account of this diversity among those who were led astray by false opinion, Moses was compelled to show what was the true cause of this, lest the sons of Israel, rescued from the power of the Egyptians, should nevertheless not be freed from a false understanding of these things. For they above the rest were rather pursuing the zeal of error; for these in ancient times, before the rest, gave their effort to a vain philosophy. In short, Moses, through the fact that he seemed by adoption to be the son of Pharaoh's daughter, was instructed in all the wisdom of the Egyptians.

What then is it that he says: In the beginning God made heaven and earth? For if firmament does not signify it according to what we have shown above, let us see what he means. We judge that Moses, in order to remove every error concerning the lower creation and concerning the materials of the world's making, signified that in the beginning the very substances of which the world consists were made, because, if he had passed this over in silence, the substances might perhaps be thought to be eternal, reckoned as co-yoked with God, so that whoever receives the testimony of this matter cannot doubt that the world was made, since he confesses that at the beginning the substances, which went toward the material of the world, were subjected. Therefore in the beginning God made heaven and earth, that is, the upper and the lower substance. The upper is that of which heaven consists; but the lower is a certain dreggy concretion, which went forth into the dry land, which is called earth, and into darkness. Whence he says in the prophet Isaiah: I am God, who made light and created darkness, that is, water, darkness, and earth.

Therefore, when these substances had been created simultaneously before the ages — for the ages are under the firmament; for the firmament, as it seems to some, is a solidification of the waters, and it is called "firmament" for this reason, because it has been made firm by the waters congealed through divine action, and it is called "heaven" (*caelum*) from hiding or concealing. And so, because water is the greater element, it is signified first, and because a part of it advanced into the vault of the world; but the earth is the pavement. And these things indeed David also confesses to have been created through the Holy Spirit; for he says: and let the water which is above the heavens praise the name of the Lord, for he spoke and it was made, he commanded and it was created — after these elements, therefore, before the joining-together of the world, God made light, so that his works might proceed in the light; and this light, in a fixed measure, completed the course of the day. And night was made according to the measure of the day, and it likewise, when the measure of its span had been filled, was illuminated by the coming of the morning light; and thus the first day was completed, when the second began; for the night was reckoned to the day, so that together they are called one day. For because the darkness is made subject to the light, they lack authority, so that they cannot make up the number. For thirty days are called one moon (*luna*), although they have the nights along with them. And so the ministry of the light, by which it completes the span decreed for it, is called day; but the substance of the darkness, before it is illuminated, is called night. And that span which is between the setting and the rising is night. For darkness does not make the night, as light makes the day, because darkness remains in its own nature. But the approach of light makes the day, and its departure the night. Not without reason, therefore, is the night made subject to the day. -

Therefore, when the light had been made and the course of twelve hours completed, and an equal span of night likewise carried through, there was an equinox on the first day. And thereafter the day began to increase over the night, namely for three months, which are the season of spring. Then, from that increase, the day is diminished for another three months, which are the season of summer. And again an equinox occurs, so that from it the night increases over the day for another three months, which are the season of autumn. And the night begins again to be diminished from the magnitude by which it exceeds the day, until the end of the fourth season, which is winter. And the equinox of the first season occurs, when God created the world, which day is understood to be the 11th day before the Kalends of April, when the first day of the Passover is, that is, the first day of the first month. For in the Law it was commanded that on the tenth day of the first month, in the evening, they should prepare themselves to keep the Passover on the fourteenth day of the month, that is, from the eleventh. For already the evening of the tenth day shines into the eleventh, so that the beginning of the first month was from the 11th day before the Kalends of April. For in the beginning the moon was made on the fourteenth, because all things were made full; add also the three days from the evening of the tenth, and these are the three days before the luminaries which existed, so that the world was made on the 11th day before the Kalends of April, because the number of the moon comes from the eleventh to the fourteenth, to which the whole first month of the Passover is assigned, so that on the Passover, the solemn day, it may be neither outside the 11th day before the Kalends of April nor outside the fourteenth moon, because on the eleventh the world was made. And on the fourteenth moon it was made brighter with a greater light.

Between the beginning, therefore, and the brightness by which the world was illuminated, our Savior both suffered and rose again. For he suffered on the fourteenth day of the moon and rose on the first day, which is for that reason called "the Lord's" (dominicus), because he made it—he who, multiplied, makes the months and the seasons and the years. But that it might by no means be celebrated in one and the same manner, the course of the moon produces this by its waning and waxing.

Now the earth was invisible and unformed. It is clear that the earth, after it was made, was invisible and unformed. For it was covered by the waters; therefore it was invisible. It was unformed, moreover, for this reason: because, since it was fluid, it was not fit for cultivation.

And there was darkness over the deep. He says that there was darkness over the deep, that is, over the immense water on every side.

And the Spirit of God was borne over the water. This water he signifies as, so to speak, the surface, over which he shows the Spirit of God to have been, because it was borne over it—so that he may signify that there was darkness over the boundless depth, but the Spirit of God over the waters, because, where the Spirit of God was, there was no obscurity. For as far as it could, it illuminated the water; but the deep, that is, the immense depth, it could not illuminate; that is, it illuminated the upper waters; beneath were also the darknesses over the deep.

For this reason, moreover, he calls this "the Spirit of God," not because he wishes this to be understood as the Holy Spirit, but because over the material (hylic) creation he wishes spiritual things to be understood; and, signifying that the whole belongs to God, he calls this—which was borne over the waters—the Spirit of God, so that he may overthrow the perverse assertion which certain men employ, saying that there is something which does not belong to God. For it is neither of order nor of reason that the Holy Spirit should be said to have been over the waters, since he is over all creation. For that which is of God is above all powers and principalities and at the same time above all spiritual powers. But let it not disturb anyone's mind that it is called "the Spirit of God," since it is established that all things are God's. For God says, among other things, when about to make the flood: My Spirit shall not remain in these men, because they are flesh; and in Ezekiel, among other matters: Thus says the Lord to these bones: I will stretch skin over you and will put my Spirit in you, and you shall live. Do these things pertain to the office of the Holy Spirit and not to the soul? This Spirit, therefore, is indeed God's, like the rest, yet it is the governor of the material substances; its order is to preside over these.

Therefore, once light had been made and the course of the day regulated and completed, on the second day He made the firmament, that is, the sky, so that the light within the firmament might regulate the day, so that it might be as it were a lamp in a house. And so this firmament was made from the above-mentioned substance of water. For now the world is being fitted together into a distinct form, so that, from the confusion of things, once the substances had been separated out, the individual parts might be arranged, which would serve toward the members of the world. For the substances which we said were made first were the raw materials of the world, and it is for this reason that it is called "world" (mundus), because things separated from one another, which had been in confusion and brought forth into distinct form, caused it to be called a world, toward the fashioning of which they served. The firmament therefore, that is, the sky, was made in the midst of the waters, so that it might divide between the water which was above the firmament and the water which was below the firmament, so that by the power of God the firmament might stand fast in the midst of the waters, having water beneath it and within it and above and around it, so that it might be hollow on the outside and fortified within, lest the waters, whether the outer or the inner, should penetrate its hollow, so that it might be as it were a house fortified and made firm, so that those dwelling in it might be secure.

On the third day He commanded the water which was under the sky, that is, within the capacity of the firmament, to be gathered together into one gathering, so that the dry land might appear, which is the earth, and it was done so. For once the breadth of the earth had been hollowed out, there came to be as it were a channel, into which all the water which was within the firmament might sink down, and then the dry land appeared. For as the water receded it was necessary that the earth should appear, made visible, which before had been invisible. Then the dry land was called earth and the gatherings of the waters seas. And He commanded the earth to bring forth the herb of fodder and fruit-bearing trees for seed upon the earth.

On the fourth day again God commanded that lights be made in the firmament of the sky, that they might shine upon the earth: a greater light unto the beginning of the day and a lesser light unto the beginning of the night, and the stars. These pertain to the conception and birth and nourishment of the things which are begotten in the world, and to the signs of the seasons necessary for fixed reckonings, and also to the adornment of the whole world. For just as it pertains to the adornment of a house if its vaulted ceiling has panels picked out with gold, so too the stars, shining with varied light, furnish adornment to the world. And so to the light which was made first the splendor of the sun was added, in such a way that these are joined together and undivided; but to the night, which was dark, a light was given. And because the moon was in its fourteenth day when it was made, He speaks thus: a lesser light unto the beginning of the night; for it shone when the whole night had come to be. It was therefore said from the part with respect to the whole: unto the beginning of the night, because it does not always shine in the evening. But the greater light, which we call the sun, remains at the beginning of the day, because it was formed together with the earlier light.

For three days, then, the world was without these luminaries; for there did not yet exist the things for which their services would be of use. But when the earth brought forth the herb of fodder and fruit-bearing trees, the things which would nourish or preserve what had been produced became necessary. Therefore, since the generation of all these things is accomplished by their intervention, it was not fitting that this should be made first, lest the things which were produced from the earth should be thought to have arisen by their effect; but rather to make those first and thus these, so that it may not be doubted that those were made without these.

Then on the fifth day he commanded the waters to bring forth the creeping things of living souls and the flying birds that fly above the earth according to the firmament of heaven; and it was done so. And among other things: and God saw, it says, that they are good, and he said: increase and multiply. And he added this, "and God saw that they are good," because by certain people they are said to be evil, so that at least in this way they may blush for shame. For when they please God and their own Creator, they surely ought to displease no one. For fools are accustomed to say: "for this reason," they say, "he saw them and they pleased him, because previously he did not know." O dull-witted ones, how did he not know, he by whom what he willed was produced? Does anyone will what he does not know? And can it be said of one who is able to make something that he does not know what he makes? And what is more, he did not himself make it, but commanded that it be made; and she whom he commanded—is it not of his power that she should make it? But because all things are possible to this one, at his command she who was not able began to be able, so that it might redound to his praise; for it is a twofold power, that he should command the insensible to bring forth things sensible.

But on the sixth day he ordered that the earth should bring forth the living soul, the quadrupeds and the beasts of the earth; and it was done so, and they were produced. And God saw that they are good, and very good, because all things are necessary.

On the aforesaid day, then, God said: let us make man to our image and likeness, and let him have, he says, power over all things that have been made upon the earth. First indeed God made the substances out of which he might frame together the world; and when it had been framed together and adorned with all ornaments and honored with the necessary things, after these he made man, who might enjoy it and all the things that are in it. For although he had commanded all things upon the earth to be produced from the waters and the earth, yet, in order to make man, he is described as having taken up the slime of the earth with his hands, so that he might teach that man differs greatly from those things which he had made. For he wills it to pertain to the honor of man, when he describes him as made by the hands of God and made to the image and likeness of God, that is, to the image and likeness of the Father and of the Son.

Now what "in the image and likeness" is—though we have discussed it elsewhere, yet this passage and occasion demand that we speak of it again. For these are the words of the Father to the Son, saying: Let us make man to our image and likeness, although in every work the person of each is to be understood, of the one speaking and of the one obeying. This, then, is the image of God in man: that one was made as it were the lord, from whom the rest would arise, holding the sovereignty of God as it were His vicar, because every king has the image of God. And therefore woman was not made to the image of God. For thus it says: And God made man; to the image of God He made him. Hence it is that the Apostle says: A man indeed ought not to veil his head, because he is the image and glory of God; but woman veils herself for this reason, because she is not the glory or image of God. But the likeness of God is this in man: that, just as woman from man, likewise from the Father the Son—in this plainly unlike, that she was made, but He was born.

To some, however, it seems that the Lord made all things at once. "For if He made by a word," they say, "why should He not be said to have made all things at once? For this is the manner of men, if He be believed to have made in parts." They say, therefore, that it tends rather to the power of God, if all things are believed to have been made by Him in one day. But furthermore in this work and order we behold a great providence. For He could indeed have made all things at once, but a manifold reason prevented it; and setting all these things aside, faith must be placed in divine Scripture, which says, and often says, that in six days the Lord your God made heaven and earth and the sea and all things that are in them. For in the first place they had to be made in such a way that they might perceive and know that they were made. For when they did not see all things at once, but one today, another tomorrow, they recognized that they were contained in a beginning and could not suppose eternity for themselves, where they recognize one another in parts. For if all things had existed at once, there would be no sense of weakness in them, but they would think that they had not begun. For the things that suddenly exist together by the word of God do not perceive that they are made, unless by seeing one thing before themselves and another after themselves. And lest perchance that which was made first should think itself unmade by discerning greater things made after it, it believes concerning itself what it sees in another more excellent. Finally, the light, which is called day, has before itself heaven and earth, that is, water and dry land. But when the water itself and the dry land and the darkness had been made in confusion, they could not claim eternity for themselves, because none of these dared to claim for itself a settled state and property. Again the sun and the moon, though they are most brilliant, have many things before themselves. Thus therefore it came about that, since the more excellent things are later, and the earlier things are inferior when they see themselves, none of these might claim eternity for itself.

Furthermore, since the work was completed in six days, it contains within itself the age of the whole world, so that the works of the six days would bear the figure of six thousand years, because what was to come to pass in each several age is contained in order in the works of the six days: so that — I suppose because such great knowledge of God was not to be on earth in the earlier ages — the earlier days appeared without splendor; and that the cattle were made before man, because our ancestors, uncultivated and rustic, were going to live after the manner of cattle; but that man was made on the sixth day, this fact brought about, because in the six-thousandth year He made man at the coming of Christ, that he might not be liable to death; and that man was the image of God, because by the gift of God he was to be made a co-image with the Son of God, since John the apostle says: we know, he says, that when He shall appear, we shall be like Him. And the vessel of election says: made co-images with the Son of God. But the tree of life, which was placed in paradise, was an image of the future grace of God, which is the body of the Lord, and whoever eats it shall live forever. For the tree of the knowledge of good and evil is the law given through Moses, which, when it had made known the sin that previously lay hidden, gave the knowledge of good and evil. For it showed what was good and what was evil. But that fact, that on the seventh day He rested from His works, signified this: that when the six-thousandth year was fulfilled, in the seventh millennium He would rest, the world now ceasing from every worldly work. 20. These few things out of many we have said, in order to open a way of understanding according to those things which we have shown above.

CVII. ON THE ORDER OF DAY AND NIGHT.

As far as pertains to the account of the world, we read of darkness before light. For those things which contributed to the fabric of the world and which seem to have been made together are considered to have been deprived of light, that is, water, earth, darkness, out of which the world was framed together, as is contained in the Wisdom which is called Solomon's: out of unseen matter, that is, dark matter. For the sacred volumes attest that both darkness and water were made, by the voice of the Lord Himself through Isaiah the prophet: for I, He says, am the God who made light and created darkness. Likewise also through David the Holy Spirit shows that both the waters were made and the earth was established upon the waters. This agrees both with the evangelical authority and with the apostolic tradition, in which all things are read to have been made, and, that it might make no exception, whether those which are in the heavens, he says, or those which are on earth.

In this part, then, which pertains to the earth, we read of darkness before light. This is in respect of the order of the reading, not in respect of dignity; for light is assuredly far better than darkness. Nevertheless, in the ordering of the world light received a name, so that it was called day and the darkness night, but by this reasoning, namely, that, while the darkness is illumined and the light passes on, that period—until the light returns—is called night, and that period, in which the darkness is illumined, is called day. When therefore night is spoken of, the day to come is awaited, and when day is spoken of, the night that is to be is awaited. But when these functions have ceased, as the world passes away, there will be spoken only of darkness and of light. For it cannot be called night, when the darkness is perpetual, nor day, when the light has ceased to set. For where there is eternity, these names cease, because they began with the world. In short, before the light came to be, which was called day, we do not read that there was night, but darkness; and this darkness, after it was illumined and the light had passed on, was called evening and night.

Therefore, so far as appears, night is made subject to day. For it ought not to be thought that, because darkness is read of before light, on that account it must be set before it, since both the heaven is before the sun and yet the sun is greater in brightness, and the earth is before man and the cattle and the other living creatures and yet these are made subject to man. Not therefore on this account ought night to be preferred to day, because darkness is read of first, since it is plain that light is set before darkness in dignity, and day before night in time. For as was shown above, when there was darkness, light was made.

And God called the light day, and the darkness he called night; and it was evening and it was morning, one day. For unless there had been day, there would have been no night either; for there was darkness, which, when it was illumined and the day had passed, its course having been accomplished, evening came to be. And when after the evening it had grown light, the first day came to be, the night having been spent, so that night followed day. This both is fitting and is commended by reason, that the lower nature should in all things be made subject to the more excellent.

How then can night be said to come before day, whose name you read after "day"? For neither night nor evening would have been named unless light, once made, had produced day; and when this had passed, evening came to be, which is night: day indeed became evening, so that you may know that night is a part of day. For a day is completed when it passes; and do we not, in short, speak thus: <a year has 365 days'—certainly not without nights. For when we say "days," in that very phrase we count the nights as well. For under the authority of day we signify the night also; for if night preceded day, day too would be reckoned under the name of night. For what man would say, 'after five nights I will see you,' and not, 'after five days'? Nor do we recall having read anywhere that night is indicated before day. For, it says, Moses was on the mountain forty days and forty nights; and in the psalm: the sun, it says, shall not burn you by day, nor the moon by night; and, to give an example from the very beginnings themselves: let there be, it says, lights in the firmament of heaven so that they may shine upon the earth; the greater light for the beginning of the day and the lesser light for the beginning of the night. If the moon can be set before the sun, then so too night before day; but, just as the moon is beneath the sun, so also night is beneath day. And in the Gospel we have the nights signified under the authority and number of days. For the Lord says, among other things: there are some standing here who shall not taste death until they see the Son of Man in his kingdom. And he added: and it came to pass after eight days, and so on. Are not the nights also contained in this number? And in another place: these things, it says, were done in Bethany beyond the Jordan, where John was baptizing. The next day he saw Jesus coming. Did he say, 'the next night'? Again it follows: on the following day he wished to set out into Galilee; and in what comes after: and on the third day a marriage took place in Cana of Galilee.

Behold, everywhere the day is shown to hold the authority, having the night made subject to it. For if the reckoning were contained under the name of night, night would go before day. For because the Romans hold the empire, the Spaniards and Gauls and Africans and the rest who are subject to them are called Romans under their name; so too the night, because it is subject to the day, is contained under its name. But some are deceived by this, because the Jews are commanded to enter into the sabbath in the evening, not considering why it was so ordained. For assuredly on the day before they must buy food for themselves and make preparation for the sabbath and go washed. Could they do these things by night, so as to enter into the sabbath when the day had already risen? The Lord certainly rose again in the evening, and it is reckoned to the day. For the resurrection is celebrated on the day, which day is called the Lord's day — for so you read: This is the day which the Lord has made; yet there is no "Lord's night," because the day holds the primacy. For whether it be to the past day or to the future one, the night is always subject, because it is both lower in order and in nature, as the apostle says: You are sons of the day and of the light, not of the night and of darkness. And if we should reason from the consuls, we shall see that he is named first who is chosen first. Finally, it is commonly said: "With whom will so-and-so be consul?" If, then, the night too were before the day, it too would be named first. For the reckoning begins from the day to such a degree that, suppose we say, "Tomorrow is the sixth day before the Kalends," it is counted from the morning right up until another morning is reached, so that it is computed when the day and the night have passed. For the moon too was surely made at the beginning, in the morning, as a fourteenth-day moon; for once made, it had to shine the whole night through, and at the next morning it began as a fifteenth-day moon. Why, then, is it doubted, that beginnings are not to be reckoned from the day?

Because of the disputes of certain people about a matter that is plain, we have been compelled to speak at greater length, although the passage itself explains itself, in which day is signified before night by an evident reasoning. All these things have been mentioned above. We have spoken according to the origin of the world. But if we wish to speak more deeply, raising our understanding with spiritual vigor, we shall find it unfitting to assert that darkness was created before light. For if the nature of light is heavenly and that of darkness earthly, it is surely absurd to think that light was made after darkness, because Moses says that light was indeed made, but in that part in which the world now is, not that it was created then, since all spiritual things were created before carnal ones, so that the light which was in the things above might come to be in the things below, like a lamp in a house. But by what reasoning is darkness said to be before light, since it is always seen after light? Indeed, heavenly and spiritual things are believed by nature to be light, earthly and carnal things to be of darkness, so that the nature of darkness seems to subsist by the power of light, because everything lowest belongs to what is highest. For if we inquire, we find darkness in the sun itself; for when, if you are close beside it, it appears so very bright that it cannot be perceived; but if at a distance, bright indeed, yet such that it can be looked upon; but if far off, less bright, and the farther it is, a certain kind of failing appears, which announces darkness, and so one arrives at it. So that darkness seems to take its origin from light, and thus he who made light in this manner also created darkness, in the same manner in which, making water, he at the same time created earth; so that just as earth is the solid part of water, so darkness is a failing of light.

But God alone is he who suffers no failing, who, though he is everywhere, indeed has all things within himself, is so exceedingly bright that he is seen by no one, unless he should be willing to temper himself. Finally the Savior, though he was in a body and was not enclosed, nevertheless was by no means seen unless he willed it. For surely, the fact that he was transfigured on the mountain and appeared in great brightness, this was as it were hidden in his body, and he manifested it when he willed. How then was he hidden who was not enclosed? For if with his body he entered to the disciples while the doors were shut, in what way does his divinity not penetrate all things? That is, it cannot be shut in, because it suffers no failing. Since therefore God is nowhere absent, he is nevertheless said to be there where he appears and wills to be seen. Since therefore all things are in God, because he surpasses all things, as the apostle says: for in him we live and move and are, he himself nevertheless is in no one except in whom he shall have willed. For by a certain mystery he is in all things, and by a certain providence he appears in whom he wills.

CVIII. ON THE HEBREW LANGUAGE, FROM WHAT IT RECEIVED ITS NAME.

Everything that is from God is rational, and it gives to each individual thing a beginning-cause that is its own origin, so that it may seem to be named or to subsist not absurdly, but so that the naming might display the thing which is understood to be the head of the name itself. Therefore the rationale of "man" is the cause by which he consists. Now he consists of four elements—earth, air, water, fire—with God alone as author; the fifth is the mind, like a charioteer, so that it may drive the compacted and shaped body like its commander. From this we all took our beginning, and by its name we are named. For we are called "men" (homines) on account of the soil (humus), from which our father has his origin on the outward side. Therefore cause and rationale bring it about that from the name of some one person the rest, subsisting under the same name, are called, signifying that person in themselves.

For which reason let us see whether it fits, as some suppose, that the Hebrews were so called from Heber. For it seems, as far as pertains to the name, that the origin of this name descends from there; for Heber is earlier than Abraham. But if this were true, those who are of the tribe of Heber ought to have been named after him by the same name; for Abraham was born in the seventh generation from Heber. If, then, above Abraham—or Abraham himself—was called a Hebrew, without doubt the Hebrews are named from Heber; but if it is not read that those before Abraham were called Hebrews, but rather after Abraham, then they are not called Hebrews from Heber at all, but from Abraham, seeing that the sons of Abraham are known to have first been called by this name. For in Genesis [Joseph and his brothers were called Hebrews. For knowing that there had been an Abraham who had descended from Syria into Canaan and had been multiplied, being on account of his faith filled with all abundance, called a king and a prophet and the father of many nations, who with three hundred and eighteen of his home-born men had struck down five kings—because these things were done and spoken not in a corner, so as to lie hidden—they named those who were of his seed Hebrews. It was therefore of Abraham's rank and merit that he should become the head of those who have their descent from him. For by God's judgment he was set as prince and origin in the land of Canaan, so that those begotten from him should be of another profession and manner of life than he himself had been before he descended into the land of Canaan; for he himself, renewed, was to establish a new people in devotion to God. Hence it is that the apostle Paul confesses himself a Hebrew of the Hebrews; for this seemed to be a surname of dignity. For it was a testimony and nobility of lineage that they should be surnamed from the name of him who had pleased God in many ways and who was their origin.

Let it be rejected, if it does not accord with reason; let it be spurned, if it is unequal; let it be torn down, if it is foreign to the merits of Abraham, and let us be refuted as though we were his flatterers. What is this enmity against Abraham, this rivalry, by which his enemies disparage him, and who are these, unless they are the very ones who are called his sons? What is there in which Abraham gives displeasure, that this glory should be thought foreign to him? And what is there that is pleasing in Heber, that he should be said to hold this primacy? If his manner of life could be investigated, he would perhaps be found to have worshipped idols like Terah and Nahor and the father of Nahor, who, it is agreed, were not far from him, as Joshua the son of Nun testifies, namely that these worshipped idols.

Or at any rate let those who are of the opposing view show by what reasoning this must be claimed for Heber, lest perchance his merit be suppressed by us; but there is none, because the scriptures are common to us and what is owed to each single one is in plain view. For if they think this must be defended from the name alone, it is weak and improbable. For then it would rightly be claimed for his name, if there existed some witnesses of the matter which confirmed this; for some persons of the same name could claim for themselves a thing not their own, and therefore this is true, which is established not by the name alone, but also by clear reasoning. For that it was by God's gracious favor brought about that in Abraham the human race should be visited and a saving pattern be made for men, is doubtful to no one. Therefore in him was restored what had been lost by the fault of human frailty. For he was set as a certain image, so that after his likeness the race of men might return to God, so that it might recover for men both the worship of God and the word, which had earlier been given for the use of men, because, just as in the beginning in Adam there was the image of God, so that the knowledge of Him might be on earth, so after the ruins of the human race and the forgetfulness of the truth it was reformed in Abraham, so that from him faith in God, begun again, might germinate into fruit. Whence it is not absurd that the people begun from him should obtain their appellation from his name.

But lest perchance on account of the name Heber they suffer a scruple, because the name Heber sounds more like the Hebrews, since they are called Hebrews (Hebrei), not Habraeans, let them recognize again that they are called Hebrews (Hebrei), not Heberites (Heberei); for he was called Heber, not Hebrer. If therefore they think an attack must be made from this quarter, they suffer what they do, and neither of us will have the victory from this side, except him who, having rendered the reasoning in order, shows that what he asserts is true. For he who is equal in one matter must be said to have lost, if in the other he is found unequal; for on account of the sound the letter was changed, so that they should be called Hebrei instead of Habraei, because it sounds better, since also from Judah they are not called Judai, but Judaei. For wherever it seemed absurd, the letter was changed, so that the utterance might have a well-composed sound. For instead of 'medidie' we say meridie (midday), and there are many such cases. Therefore it is fitting, as has been shown, that both the language and the people should be named with the name of Abraham.

This, then, is the language which we say was first given to Adam and to the rest, which, on account of the presumption of the tower that was built, we believe was scattered into many and confounded, so that this one no longer existed, but many arose out of it through the alteration made in certain of its words, in such a way that it did not retain its own form and yet did not perish, but was wholly confounded with the other languages. For thus was it then brought about, lest men should understand one another and dare greater things. So great a number of languages, then, is understood to have arisen at that time as there were men, who, scattered in the places in which they dwelt, each one established his own language.

But if it be judged that the language which is written to have been then one is by no means that same one, this will remain: that it was gathered piecemeal out of the other languages and pieced together into the form of a single language which Abraham used, so that, because he was the father of many nations, he might have a speech composed out of many languages and be in all respects renewed. But since those men, whom the book of Genesis records as being of one voice and speech, are read to have migrated from the east, and from there again to have been scattered by God over the face of the whole earth, assuredly the one who would remain in the language first given did not remain there. Finally, it has neither any land among men, as the other languages do, nor a nation, the Jews excepted, because it was given to the first man in paradise. And after the languages were confounded, this one which is now called Hebrew has nowhere been found. If therefore it has nowhere a place or a nation — but Abraham was by nation a Syrian —, whence this language for Abraham or for the rest of his seed, unless because either that one is the earlier or else is certainly fitted together out of many? For we see that a good many languages have things similar to it.

But it does not so agree with reason in the way that the other view does, if this one be said to be the first; for reason proves that this was not done idly. For Abraham ought to have had that same language which the first man had, so that Moses, coming and describing both the creation and man made by God, might use this speech, with which God spoke and called the man Adam, and Adam's woman was called Eve, so that this speech might be in the book of Moses which had also been in those whom he describes, that he might teach the first cause truly reformed in all things and the divine benefaction in Abraham.

CVIIII. ON MELCHIZEDEK.

We read in the book of Genesis concerning Melchizedek, and in like manner also in the epistle written to the Hebrews, that "Melchizedek," it says, "the priest of the most high God, met Abraham as he returned from the slaughter of the kings, and brought forth bread and wine, and offered it to him and blessed him, saying: Blessed art thou, Abraham, of the most high God, who made heaven and earth, who delivered thine enemies under thy hands"; and, in order to signify how this same Melchizedek was to be understood, he added: "for without doubt," he says, "that which is least is blessed by the better." Which indeed he by no means referred to the tradition of the ecclesiastical office. For who would doubt that the rule of the Lord's tradition, by which those subject to the Lord are blessed, is better than those who are blessed by it? For the mystery of the Lord works in these words, which embrace and record him. But he wished to demonstrate the proper merit and power of his person.

Who then and how great is this man, in comparison with whom Abraham is called least—Abraham who, it is agreed, holds among all the faithful the mark of the testimony of nobility and faith! Therefore this Melchizedek is not to be understood to have blessed Abraham in the way that priests among men do, so as to give a blessing by delegation with solemn words, but as something proper to himself, which he received substantially not through words but through nature, so that it is his own. But the priests, whom we call prelates, have a rule ordained with solemn words and handed down to them, which they place upon men and bless—not those whom they wish, but sometimes even those whom they do not wish, because the giver of the rule himself knows to whom the most sacred blessing ought to be given or poured out.

But he who has the blessing substantially in his nature, whom he calls a priest of the most high God, gives the blessing through his will. For the words of the blessing and his nature accord with his will. Nor does he err, so as to will where he ought not, nor to be unwilling where he ought, but on whomever he has bestowed the words, he has bestowed the effect. But our priests daily impose the name of the Lord and the words of blessing upon many, yet in few is there the effect; and there are times, again, when they bless those better than themselves. For although one may be holy, he nevertheless bends his head to receive the blessing, because it is not the priest's own but God's device. For Eli the priest blessed Hannah, and the effect followed—not indeed by the priest's merit, but by that of Hannah herself, whose pure heart God beheld.

Therefore Melchizedek is said to be better than Abraham not on account of the office of the priesthood alone, but he is also set before him by nature, so that he is understood to be beyond men. For it can in no way come about that one who is set above the friend of God, and one so faithful that out of love for him he did not hesitate to slay even his most beloved son, should be thought a man. For by what justice, by what works could he merit more than Abraham merited? For there was nothing more that he could do to commend his merits than Abraham did. For first, when he did not know God nor had seen any sign by which he might be persuaded, when God said to him: Go out from your land and from your kindred and from the house of your father, he did not delay, but at once obeyed, fulfilling the will not only of the God present but also of the Lord to come. For the Lord says: Whoever has made more of his house or parents or brothers or kinsmen than of me, is not worthy of me. What kind of man then is Abraham, and of what merit, who fulfilled the commands of the Savior before they were preached!

The Apostle teaches that hospitality is greatly to be pursued: this

Abraham cultivated [it] so devoutly and so greatly, that the apostle seems to have learned from him that this [hospitality] was to be preached. Then, advanced in years, he believed that his seed would be multiplied — a thing which seems foolish to the prudent of the world — even as the stars of heaven are, so that, before the sentence was pronounced against the prudent of this age, they seem to have been condemned by him. For afterward it was said: I will destroy the prudence of the prudent. By God's command he is circumcised — which assuredly is not done without pain — and he does not bear it grievously, that he might give to posterity a pattern of patience. From Sarah his wife, now an old woman, a son is promised to him, and he does not doubt, so that he might show to those to come that one must yield to the authority of God to such a degree that, even if he command or promise things that seem irrational, there be no wavering, because his person is more to be regarded than the words, so that what in the words seems weak or impossible, his person may confirm and make to seem possible. For it is God who promises, concerning whom it must be believed that he can do what seems impossible to men. For what offense can be judged, when one supposes more concerning God than humanity feels concerning itself?

On account of which Abraham does not hesitate to kill the son given to him by God, when that same [God] so commands, nor does he put off the time nor marvel at God's will — that God should order this son to be killed, whom he had given as a special grace of favor and of wonder — being certain that God's will ought not to be reconsidered by man, nor that the command and deed of God are without providence. And that he might fulfill this hastily with all devotion, he does not disclose it to the boy's mother, his own wife, not being unaware that women are more frail concerning their children and could bring an impediment to this devotion by the compassion of tears; and likewise he keeps it hidden from the boy himself up to the hour of his slaying, that he might obey God's command with the whole compliance of his mind, reckoning this not a parricide but a holocaust, which was being done with the just Judge as its author.

What greater things, therefore, than these could Melchizedek perform, whereby he might surpass Abraham, who, as we see, is established to have been disobedient in nothing? For Moses, to whom the Lord is read to have spoken face to face, when he was sent, was unwilling to go to the nation and to his brothers. Jonah likewise, a prophet, broke through the command given to him to preach to the Ninevites, so as to go off to another place, to which he had not been sent. Job, plainly outstanding in all things, nevertheless, moved by the loss of his children, tore his garments and shore the hair of his head; but Abraham is not read to have been saddened over the death of his dearest son — and one whom he himself had been commanded to kill — nor is he anywhere read to have trembled when God commanded him._

Therefore by these things it appears that Melchizedek is beyond man, because there was no reason why he should be better than Abraham, unless nature alone should give him precedence. For an impassible nature has beatitude through its substance, whereas human birth acquires beatitude through deeds. For because it does not possess the perfection of deity, it advances through exercise and struggle so as to become better, when it conquers more than it is conquered. For if it should so act — which is impossible — that it should never sin, it would become better than God — which be far from it! —, because a nature which is able to sin, just as also not to sin, if it should always conquer, would have to be preferred to that nature which for this reason does not sin, because it is impassible. For it would not seem a great thing not to sin, because it is not able to; but it would be magnificent if, when it were able to sin, it should not sin.

This, therefore, is the difference between the substance of God and of man: that God's substance is always blessed in the security of its invincible eternity; but man's substance becomes blessed in another way, through toil. And it is necessary that the beatitude of the impassible be one thing, and that of him who is worn down by tribulations another, so that he may at some time be able to rejoice. For the impassible beatitude is in this, that it is always blessed, whereas the passible rejoices, after great exertions, that it has escaped death. It is saddened, therefore, in order that it may be able to rejoice, nor is it perpetually secure because of the passibility of its nature; but the impassible is always blessed, because it neither knows sadness nor suspects that it can be saddened. For the nature of men also, although it attains beatitude through toil, will nevertheless not be untouched, nor will scars be lacking to it. For how can it come about that a nature which has often engaged in combat and been sometimes conquered should not be wounded? But the impassible remains always untouched, unharmed, inviolable.

Therefore Abraham would not be called least in comparison to Melchizedek, unless the nature of Melchizedek were more powerful than Abraham. For these things which we have said may perhaps seem to belong to native wit; but if we look to Scripture, still more and weightier things will be said. For this Melchizedek, king of Salem, priest of the most high God, who met Abraham returning from the slaughter of the kings, who also blessed him, is first indeed interpreted as king of righteousness, then king of Salem, which is king of peace, without father, without mother, and without genealogy, having neither beginning of days nor end of life; but, made like to the Son of God, he remains a priest for ever. And that he might commend these things by the passages in which he had signified them, he added concerning him: You see, he says, how great this man is, to whom Abraham the prince of the fathers gave tithes of the first-fruits. For in order to show with what power and how great Melchizedek is, he praises Abraham, saying that he is prince of the fathers, that is, Abraham better than all the rest, but least in comparison to Melchizedek.

Is it not clear that this one is not a man, but something greater? For what is it that it says about him, that he is king of peace and king of justice? Draw nearer and see. For seeing the sun from afar you take it for a lamp, and looking upon silver you reckon it tin. Can any of men be called king of peace and justice? For peace is preached to men, and justice likewise; but this one is called king of peace and justice for this reason, that justice and peace may be known to have their origin from him. For that which is ruled by him cannot be said to be above him. For men are instructed, with justice as their teacher and with peace, to earn God. This therefore, which is the teacher of men, has Melchizedek as its king. How much better than a man, then, is Melchizedek, when the governess of men is beneath him! This is to be a king of kings.

And so when he is called king of justice and peace, he is signified to be their author. So that, just as the Lord Jesus is the author of life, so also Melchizedek is the author of justice and peace, because those who live through Christ are ruled by the dispensing of justice and peace. For into the hearts of the servants of God he pours justice and peace, to adorn the Lord's discipline. We read it written in the eighty-fourth psalm, that justice and peace have embraced each other and truth has arisen from the earth; and, in order to show of which justice it was speaking, it says that justice has looked down from heaven. This the scripture shows concerning the time of the savior, in which the justice of God was given to the world through Christ in the knowledge of the mystery of the one God, which he had promised beforehand — for it is just that the creature should know the truth of its creator —, which justice, manifested on earth, with discord taken away — discord which had been begotten from the injustice of the unknown God — made the discordant peaceful, when it established in one faith those who a little before were diverse. Thus "justice and peace embraced each other and the truth of the one God arose from the earth," with justice as teacher. For the incarnation of Christ showed what truth was to be known concerning God himself. Therefore this certainly is the justice of God. Likewise also peace, as the apostle says: "But the God of peace shall crush Satan." :':

I cannot see how this can be distinguished: since God, the Father of our Lord Jesus Christ, is the God of peace, and Christ himself is likewise so — for he says, My peace I give to you — how it is said of Melchizedek that he is king of righteousness and of peace; on what grounds this is to be set apart, I do not understand. For I think that, as regards the matter, there is no difference between a king of peace and a God of peace. For because in the world no one ought to be called God — so that this name might be reserved for the one alone from whom are all things — he nevertheless established a king who should bear his image, one who, apart from the name of God, should possess all his power. But because he is earthly, he is a king of men, yet not of peace and righteousness, because he too has righteousness as his leader, which it is not permitted him to despise. For righteousness is God to him. For righteousness is a thing belonging to God, and whoever transgresses it becomes guilty before the judgment of God. Melchizedek, however, as it is given to be understood, is not called king after the manner of men, because he has under himself righteousness, which rules over kings. For no one can have righteousness under himself unless he is of such a nature that he cannot sin. And he is said to be under him for this reason, because by him it was devised in the manner of a law, by which those who can sin may be governed. Therefore the king of righteousness is the ordainer of the words devised by him, by which one may both live and believe well and blessedly.
