Long and much do we labor, and still we say too little about Melchizedek, unless we return to Scripture, which itself also long knocks and cries out, that it may rouse those weighed down by heavy sleep to an understanding not of night, but of day. It says therefore that Melchizedek is without father, without mother, without genealogy, and, lest it should be understood otherwise than is fitting, it added: having neither beginning of days nor end of life. By this it shows that Melchizedek was neither born nor died. What does human subtlety argue against things so manifest? What is that cunning craftiness of wit which dares to resist things manifest, so that it does not take a meaning from the reading, but assigns a meaning to the reading? Violence is done to the Lord's Scriptures, so that it feels as enemies even those who appear to bow themselves to its authority. For some say that Melchizedek is not to be believed of in the way that has been reported in the Scriptures, and through this they turn the Scriptures to their own meaning; for whom it would be better openly not to receive the Scriptures than to act deceitfully against them, so that under the name of peace war is prepared, and under the covering of friendship the enemy lies hidden.

They say, therefore, that Melchizedek belongs not to nobility of birth, but to the lowliness of that same lineage, because he is read to have been without father and mother. For they say it was written thus for this reason, that it might be shown that he was of foreign stock, not of the tribe from which Abraham arose, because his lineage is not found in the law. "Whence he also added," they say, "'and without genealogy,' so that it might be understood that his origin was in no way at all described in the law, in order that he might be believed to have been made a good man by his own self, from parents of whatever sort." But furthermore, is Scripture so incautious that it would set down all things, arranged in order, as necessary to the matter! For first it says: without father, without mother. Let us see whether the mothers of the others are indicated in the law: who was the wife of Nahor, the grandmother of Abraham, or the mother of Terah, is nowhere read; nor who was the mother of Abraham—to say nothing of the rest. Are these, then, to be said to have been without mothers? If it had said "without father," would they not have been †corrupt†? Because the fathers of all who existed are recorded in the law, it adds: and without genealogy. If it said these things for this reason, to show that his generation is not in the law, it would have sufficed to say "without father," because of one whose father no one knows, much more is his grandfather unknown.

For the sake of clarity it says: having neither beginning of days nor end of life. Tell me, whoever you are, you violent one, how do you interpret this? What is it to have no beginning of days nor end of life? It would certainly have sufficed to say that his generation was not written in the law, and through this his origin would have to be believed to be from foreigners. It can be said that he was "translated," like Enoch, and for that reason did not die. So be it. What is it to have no beginning of the days of life? Perhaps you might say, because the day of his birth is not written. For whose day of birth is written in the law? And is this the reasoning, that one whose day of birth is not written is denied to have a beginning of the days of life? Therefore the same could be said of the others as well. And even if he had been translated as a man, he was nevertheless not without an end of life, because whatever is under the expectation of death has an end.

Cease, whoever you are that are bent on contention. For it is more profitable to be conquered by what is true than to conquer the truth by falsehoods. For it is to lose, not to conquer; because, even if in a man the truth should seem to lose, yet with God it conquers, since its reasoning is invincible. And so the mind ought to be conquered by the law, so that it may perceive what is signified by the law, and not itself give a meaning to the law by its own judgment, so as violently to set itself up as a law over the law. And hear Zerubbabel, who says: above all things truth conquers.

Now already Melchizedek showed the future mystery of the incarnation and passion of the Lord, when he first handed over to Abraham, as it were to the father of the faithful, the eucharist of both the body and the blood of the Lord — so that there might be prefigured in the father the truth which was to be in the sons. But if he is thought to be a priest as a man, after the manner in which Aaron was or as they now are, let the place of his dwelling be shown or read, or whether there was some temple or synagogue in which he gathered the people and offered their sacrifices, or what people assembled to him. For if he was a priest on earth, without doubt he had a people. And before Abraham, certainly, or in his time, there was a people who would serve God. And how was Abraham made, as it were, the head of the faith, so that from him the people might have knowledge of God? For likewise, if there was a Melchizedek who taught the fear of the one God on earth, Abraham was chosen in vain, that from him the people of God should be named. For it could have been among those who assembled to Melchizedek.

And where is that which is read in the canticle of Deuteronomy, that "when the Most High divided the nations," he says, "in the manner in which he scattered the sons of Adam, he set the bounds of the nations according to the number of the angels of God, and the portion of the Lord became his people Jacob"? Therefore, if in the world there was no people of God apart from the sons of Abraham, by what reasoning is it thought that there was another people which had descended from the teaching of Melchizedek, when the prophet denies it? For when he names all the nations which are in the world, and says that there is no people of God except the sons of Abraham, what remains except that, apart from these, it should be denied that the rest had knowledge of God, since "God is known in Judea"? But Melchizedek, "the priest of the most high God," appeared to bless Abraham as a foretoken of the saints to come, because a blessing was to be given in the future to the people of God through him who is almost the high priest of God, whom we call the priest. This Melchizedek, therefore, appeared as the forerunner of the sacrament of the coming Son of God, because he goes before in service who is following in order. Therefore the things we have said about Melchizedek are not to be wondered at, since Scripture, for the confounding of those who clamor against it, adds still more and speaks. For after all things worthy of praise, so that it may confound the malevolent: "but being made like," he says, "to the Son of God, he remains a priest forever."

Now consider whom you are striving to think ill of, and, if you do not fear him, at least dread Christ, to whom he is reported by the authority of Scripture to be like. For step by step he gradually arrives at his natural worth, so that, if the earlier statements should disturb any — by which he was signified as king of justice and of peace, and because, having been seen in the flesh, he had neither father nor mother, that is, he appeared unbegotten, having neither rising nor setting — he might, from these last things which he subjoined, prove the earlier things credible. For who would dare to say, save a madman, that all these sublime things are unfitting to him who was designated like the Son of God, remaining a priest forever? But he cannot be like the Son of God unless he be of the same nature. And what seems incredible, if Melchizedek appeared as a man, when he is understood to be the third person? For if Christ, who is the second person, was frequently seen in the guise of a man, what doubt is there concerning the things that have been said? For we read in the psalm, Thou art, he says, a priest forever according to the order of Melchizedek. None of us doubts that this pertains to the person of Christ, because Christ is a priest forever in the same manner as Melchizedek also is; but Christ is the high priest, Melchizedek the second. For if Melchizedek is a man, how does it fit that Christ should be a priest forever according to his order? For both are therefore read to be alike and to be of one dispensation, because they are also of one nature.

But since in every way the authority of the one God must be preserved, therefore the second and third person are made subject to the paternal name. Now Christ is the vicar of the Father and his representative, and for this reason he is also called a priest. Likewise also the Holy Spirit, being sent as it were as a representative, was called a priest of the Most High God, not the highest, as our people presume in the oblation, because, although Christ and the Holy Spirit are of one substance, nevertheless the order of each one must be preserved. Priests therefore, or legates, are so called because they display in themselves him whose legates they are; for they are his image. And for this reason Christ and the Holy Spirit, naturally having the image of God, are called his priests. In them God is seen, as the Lord says: He who has seen me has seen the Father also; and if in the deeds of the Lord God was seen, and the deeds of the Holy Spirit are works — the Lord signifying this, that I cast out demons in the Spirit of God — then in the Holy Spirit also God was seen.

CX. ON THE FIRST PSALM.

Since the prophet David, signifying spiritual reasoning through the appearance of carnal things, and pronouncing with an eager mind psalms of diverse kind and merit unto the praise of God and unto the mystery, veiled the first psalm with no covering and assigned it to no one, but established it as general, so that it might belong to those who, having been separated from evils, have fortified themselves with the fellowship of the good. For neither ought the beginning, and especially the beginning of this grace, to be sung in obscurity — since light comes before darkness, and the head of each thing is in the open — nor does the question arise from that point, but from what follows; whence he thus began, saying: Blessed is the man who has (not) gone away in the counsel of the ungodly. The counsel of the ungodly is the conspiracy of the malignant, to be understood in manifold ways. For this reason the ungodly differ from sinners — for ungodliness is the gravest sin — because all ungodliness is sin, yet not all sin is ungodliness. In what way, then, does someone go away into the counsel of the ungodly? Surely when he is called forth from himself. For in saying this he shows that, so long as one does not depart from nature, he does not incur sin. On account of this he says, 'Blessed is he who has not gone away in the counsel of the ungodly.' For ungodliness, begun by the devil with his attendants assenting, entices men, so that it may make them sharers of its own ungodliness. For he himself, sinning first against God, while he wishes to usurp for himself the primacy through tyranny, having been cast down from the sacred seats, reckoned this a consolation, if he should acquire very many companions for his own perdition. And therefore, he says, 'Blessed is the man who has not gone away in the counsel of the ungodly.' For those entice men always, who, acting under this prince, are hostile to us, as the apostle says: For your struggle is not, he says, against flesh and blood, but against principalities and powers; and in another epistle he says of their tyrant, among other things: so that he sits in the temple of God, showing himself as though he were God. For because to sin seems sweet and the evil is not perceived unless there be loss — and through this those who deceive do not appear so long as they are ensnaring, but when they have deceived, then they are recognized —, therefore he calls blessed that man who has not been enticed into the counsel of the ungodly, so as to go into the fellowship of their ungodliness.

Therefore the first cause of impiety is this, whereby they began to be rebels against God in a malicious conspiracy, by the imitation of which idolatry took its beginning. For the transgression, begun in the regions above, descended to the earth. For while they are not content to be subject to the one God and creator, they showed themselves impious by parceling out his majesty to others, so that, having spurned him, they might choose others for themselves for worship. For this is the first cause of the human race's offense, from which, being neglected by God, they are handed over to be deluded by various enticements and passions, according to the teaching of the apostle Paul. For what work remains inviolate that has not acknowledged its author? From that point the evil, now sown, rejects restraint and, like a vine its shoots, stretches out into many directions, so that those who had not spared God would leap forth all the more easily into the insult and murder of their parents; because — to be silent about the rest, since the apostle says: For what is it to me to judge those who are outside? — Reuben, to his father's dishonor, committed defilement upon his father's concubine, and Absalom against divine law usurped the kingdom, so as to deprive his own father of rule and of life. -

Impiety has still other aspects as well, since both to despise one who begs [for help] in danger, when one could be of profit, is impiety, and, in a harsh and malicious affair, to give counsel that it may be carried out ... of this matter, nor did he escape for the present, and such things as could be found bearing on this meaning. Not without reason, therefore, is that man blessed who has not gone into the counsel of the impious. For it is a great destruction for a man, and an incurable wound, to be joined to these errors after the knowledge of God. For he must needs be dealt with more harshly who after the truth turns to what is false; and blessed is the man who, having received the knowledge of the truth, flees the counsels of the impious, because impiety is the head of error.

And it follows: and has not stood in the way of sinners, because it is impossible not to sin, just as it is possible not to be impious, since the gravest sin can be avoided, but the rest have no way by which they may creep in. For human life itself frequently, while it is not thinking of sinning, unexpectedly falls into sinning. And through this he is blessed who does not go into the counsel of the impious; that is, not even to begin evil. But since sin cannot but be begun, as I have said, he called him blessed who does not remain in it; that is, not to stand in the way of sinners. For the way of sinners is a way of life spent in sins. For he is said to walk wickedly who proceeds to this, that he seeks to sin. For while his mind does not stand in the law of God but wanders about, he is said to walk in the way of sinners, because this wandering seeks to sin. And therefore blessed is he who does not stand in the way of sinners, that is, who, with repentance following after, grieves that he has been ensnared and withdraws from the conspiracy of sinful men. Therefore just as he is blessed who has not gone into the counsel of the impious, so he will be incorrigible if he has gone, since Solomon says that «the foot of the impious will perish» and: blessed is the man who has not stood in the way of sinners. But if he has stood, he is no longer blessed, but guilty and deserving of blows. For he seems to have some hope for amendment, because he is not impious but a sinner. But if there be one who has not gone into the counsel of the impious and does not stand in the way of sinners, he is blessed in a twofold manner. For he cannot be blessed if he does not go into the counsel of the impious yet stands in the way of sinners, because, if not to perdition, he is nonetheless liable to punishment.

Then he added: and has not sat in the seat of pestilence. He says this is blessedness, which consists of these three degrees and is fortified by a threefold reasoning, that is, that one neither go into the counsel of the impious, nor stand in the way of sinners, nor sit in the seat of pestilence. But since only two kinds are held, of the impious and of sinners, in reproach, which have been mentioned above, to which of these did he wish this third to be ascribed, which he added, saying: and has not sat in the seat of pestilence? To the impious or to sinners? But let us see what it is to sit in the seat of pestilence, and then we shall understand to which kind he ascribes this. We call this the seat of pestilence, which is outside the ordination of God, which assuredly was devised for this purpose, that unjust judgments may go forth from it; for this reason it is called the seat of pestilence, which is a corruption that gives birth to death, just as iniquity also gives birth to damnation. It is therefore not from God, this seat of death; for Moses received the seat of life. For it was given for this, that in it there should be the authority of a just judge or of God the creator. Whence the Lord says: upon the seat of Moses have sat the scribes and the Pharisees; and the apostle says: there is no power except from God; for those which are, are ordained by God—

Whence he says to the ruler of the people: You indeed sit judging according to the law, and contrary to the law you order me to be struck? In that he said 'according to the law,' he signified the just and salutary authority of the chair. But that which he said, contrary to the law you order me to be struck, showed that very man to be an unjust judge, in that, sitting in the chair of God, he judged unjustly. Hence it is that Daniel too says: The kingdom is God's, and to whom he wills he will give it. Therefore, just as the authority of an earthly emperor runs through all, so that reverence for him may be in all, so God has ordained that the authority of God should begin from the king himself and run through all. Although frequently the world does not understand this, and one set in power subjects himself to another than he ought, nevertheless it is the ordinance that there be one who is feared. Where, therefore, this ordinance is not, there the chair of pestilence is found. Nowhere is the authority of the one God denied, except among those who preach the fear and reverence of many gods. These are they who sit in the chair of pestilence, because what their pontiffs preach is death. For they teach this: that the worshippers of the one God, as though foolish and enemies, should either be held in reproach or be taken from this life. This very thing is the material of impiety, which extends also into other areas, through which it may set forth the laws of its impiety. For by the command of Jezebel the prelates of iniquity sat in the chair of pestilence, who, against the innocent Naboth, though they were not judges, plotting wicked things, with false witnesses suborned, passed a sentence of death. Whence in another psalm he says: And I will not sit with the impious. For this is the judgment of him whose also is the chair of pestilence. And so, if those sitting in the chair of God oppress the innocent, it will be the judgment that is unjust, not the chair. For where the chair of pestilence is, the judgment cannot but be iniquitous, although they apply to themselves certain coverings, as it were, with which to conceal their impiety, so that those who see may be deceived. For impiety cannot be set forth by itself alone, because it finds no buyer, and, where the law of the devil is, if he himself be named, he is dreaded.

Just as poison is mixed in with something through which it may lie hidden, so also they mix certain just things into the law of the devil and attach the name of truth, but the sense of each individual thing is to be judged, since sometimes it displays something true for this reason: that it may deceive, so that, when it proclaims malicious things, they may be thought good. Since we have asserted that the seat of pestilence is not from God's ordinance, we say that the seat of those too who have set up seats for themselves outside the Church or against the Church is a seat of pestilence. For whoever presumes upon things not permitted is guilty: how much more if he also corrupts the tradition of the one whose seat he usurps! For they disturb also the order begun by the apostle Peter and preserved down to this present time through the propagation of succeeding bishops, claiming for themselves an order without origin, that is, professing a body without a head; whence it is fitting to call their seat too a seat of pestilence. For it will not be unpunished on this account, that they do these things under the name of God. For it is established that they conduct their own causes under the name of God. For they do not do these things out of zeal for God, but wishing to defend a place for themselves, since we know that Korah and the 250 men, offering to God through presumption, were swallowed up by the gaping of the earth, and that King Uzziah, when he had offered to God unlawfully, was struck with leprosy on his forehead.

Since it has become clear that the seat of pestilence pertains to the impiety spoken of above, let us pursue the rest. For he added: but his will is in the law of the Lord. He says that the will of that man is in the law of the Lord, who, having spurned the impious and abandoned the sinners, refusing the seat of pestilence, has established his own will in the law of God. On which account he called this man 'blessed.'

And there follows: and on his law he will meditate day and night. It is manifest that a man free from all enticements and immune from those things which are contrary to the law of God does nothing other than perform exercise in the law of God. Confirmed by the sacraments of the Son of God, in the day he applies himself to good works and in the night to prayers. For this is he of whom he says in the Song of Songs, that he sleeps and his heart keeps watch.

Whence he says: and he will be like a tree which is planted beside the courses of the waters. Who doubts that he who meditates on the law of God has hope of receiving a reward? For, just as a tree planted beside water cannot be barren, so also he who by frequent practice devotes himself to the law of God cannot fail to have the effect of that work.

And he added: which will give its fruit in its time. Every plantation well tended gives fruit at the fitting time: so also this man, who obeys the law of God, will have fruit at the predetermined time. For then he will receive the reward of this devotion, when the Lord Jesus Christ shall have begun to sit to judge the living and the dead.

Then he added: and its leaf shall not fall away. It is manifest that by a tree whose leaves do not drop off he signified life. For the wood, kept vigorous by the moisture of running water, retains its leaves. For the Lord Jesus also says: If they do these things in the green wood, what shall be done in the dry? By the green wood he signified life and fruit. Just as the wood, then, by the aid of running water does not suffer dryness, so also this man, who is zealous for the law of God, when temptation comes upon him, being safe under God's protection does not lose but acquires salvation, as the Apostle says: that through tribulations we must enter into the kingdom of God.

And there follows: and all things whatsoever he does shall prosper. What he says is plain, that whoever keeps faith with the law of God, desirous of his commandments, becomes happy in all his acts; just as Joseph too, because he set the fear of God before the present life, God made all things that he did prosper in his hands.

And he added: not so the impious, not so. The repetition of the phrase signifies that it will altogether be no otherwise than he promises, because for the impious nothing will be prosperous in the future, but all things adverse even unto their destruction.

Whence he says: but like the dust which the wind casts forth from the face of the earth. He says the impious perish in the same way as the dust which the wind casts forth from the face of the earth that it may perish. For, lifted up from its own substance and scattered through the air, it gradually perishes: so also the impious, cast forth by the ministering angels from the face of their author God, without whom one cannot live, shall perish.

And there follows: therefore the impious do not rise again in the judgment. What he says is fitting: for to what end should they rise in the judgment, who preferred that no business of God's be entrusted to them, of which they should render an account? For those who spurn and deny the law of God have nothing whereby they might render an account, so as, when questioned in the judgment, to render their reasons according to the law entrusted to them. Therefore, having been found arrogant and rebellious against God, they shall rise again for this purpose, that they may see that what they refused to believe is true, that they may perish, because they judged God a liar.

And he added: nor sinners in the counsel of the just. In this psalm he signifies three kinds of men: of the impious and of sinners and of the just, in the order in which the error began. But the impious are estranged from these, because, as I said, they refused to live under God's decree; sinners, however, acting under the law of God, are to be set before the tribunal of Christ, separated from the just, that they may render an account of the law they received, being amended in those things in which they gave no effort, so that they might have more of praise than of blame; but the just, indeed, found much more inclined in the exercise of fulfilling the law, are to be rewarded.

And he added: because the Lord knows the way of the just. God is said to know those who, mindful of his precepts, persist in good works, so that, held to be just, they may be crowned. But to those who, unmindful of him, are negligent concerning the care of the soul, he says thus: Depart from me; I know you not, workers of iniquity.

And it follows: and the way of the ungodly shall perish. Whence the psalm was begun and ended, so that, the case of the ungodly having been treated, they might be shown liable to nothing other than destruction. For as I have said, God is therefore said to know the way of the righteous because they walk in his law; but the way of the ungodly, because it is outside the law of God, shall perish, because all the ways of the ungodly are dark, says Solomon, but those of the godly are most bright, with Christ our Lord as guide.

CXI. ON PSALM XXIII.

The title of the psalm intimates its meaning. For, about to speak concerning the mystery of Christ our Lord, he signified the Lord's day, saying: To this David, the first of the sabbath. For when it is said To this David, it pertains to him whose image this David holds, of whom the prophet says: and my servant David shall feed them. Now the first of the sabbath is the first day. For after the sabbath the first day is the Lord's day, the Gospel affirming and saying: And on the first of the sabbath Mary Magdalene and the other Mary came to the sepulchre, and the rest. On the Lord's day, therefore, he signified the mystery of the Saviour. But that the psalm might be expounded through the order of reasoning, he made a beginning from the person of God the Father, so that the mystery of truth might be made manifest to men not without order. For he who wishes to intimate some matter, unless he grasps its head and origin, will not be able to show its proper character nor to teach what its truth is.

And so the prophet began thus and says: The earth is the Lord's, and the fulness thereof. He held fast to the faith of things, saying: The earth is the Lord's, and the fulness thereof. For he followed the authority of the ancient saints, who said that the earth and the things that are in it pertain to the dominion of the Lord God.

And he added: the world, and all who dwell in it. Lest perchance he should be thought to have signified something less, because he had said that the earth and the fulness thereof are the Lord's, he opened his meaning, saying: the world, and all who dwell in it, that he might teach that absolutely all the earth is the Lord's and the things that are in it, so that there should be nothing excepted which is not his, as certain heretics think, who, according to the saying of the apostle Peter, deny to God his dominion, to whose condemnation the prophetic oracles taught that all things are God's.

And it follows: he hath founded it upon the seas, that is, what he signified in another psalm, saying: thou hast established the earth upon the waters. That the testimony might follow the faith, he showed in what manner he made it. For while there was a confusion of things and neither the earth nor the water had its proper appearance, at God's command the earth was gathered together and made firm upon the waters, so that both the earth and the water might have the property of their origin. Whence in Kingdoms there is the voice of God saying: have I not made the waters?

And he subjoined: and hath prepared it upon the rivers. Thus it was prepared upon the rivers, since it holds within itself waters established there, which, flowing as if through the channels of veins, furnish it density, lest, being utterly dry, it should be brittle and useless for cultivation. For they furnish a vital effect to the roots of growing things, and at the same time are a help to arid places in their thirst.

And that he might show that profession without a good life does not greatly profit, he says: who shall ascend into the mountain of the Lord? or who shall stand in his holy place? He sets forth what sort of person he ought to be who professes God as Creator, lest — because he showed that those are to be punished who, as I have said, deny God's lordship — these who confess Him should think themselves exempt from punishment, if they should conduct themselves in no upright manner. By saying, therefore, the mountain of the Lord, he signified heaven. Whence in another psalm he says: I have lifted up my eyes to the mountains, from whence help shall come to me. But the place of the Lord is where He appears. And it is said to Joshua son of Nun, when the Lord had appeared to him: and the place in which you stand is holy ground, and to Jacob, where he saw the Lord: this, he says, is the house of God.

And there follows: the innocent in hands and clean of heart. Then he says that he is worthy to ascend into the mountain of the Lord, that is, into heaven, or to stand in His holy place, if anyone shall be innocent in works and have a clean heart in the cause of God, that is, of faith. For a person of this kind can ascend into heaven and stand in His holy place. For when this life is ended he ascends to heaven. Then, together with the city of Jerusalem, at the coming of the Lord he shall descend from heaven, worthy to stand where the Lord is about to judge the living and the dead, as an innocent man; but the impious and sinners will be unworthy to stand in the holy place of God's judgment — they who shall rise again, the impious to destruction, sinners to punishment. For if the children of Israel, by reason of sin, could not look upon the glorified face of Moses as he came down from the mountain, how much more will a conscience liable to offenses dread to gaze upon the very Lord of glory Himself seated on the throne of majesty! For they shall hear: I have not known you, workers of iniquity.

And he added: who has not received his soul in vain. Now he touches upon the very kinds of sins. For in the first place this man is innocent and has a clean heart, who is exempt from the fornication of idolatry. For this is the vanity to which whoever inclines himself subjugates his soul to corruption.

And there follows: nor has sworn to his neighbor in deceit. He mentioned two causes, which are the principal ones, that of God and that of neighbor, because whoever shall have been fit in these will without doubt be held approved in the rest also, the Lord saying: you shall love the Lord your God with your whole heart and with all your strength. This is the first commandment. And the second is like it: you shall love your neighbor as yourself. On these two commandments the whole law hangs, and the prophets. Who therefore, loving the Lord with his whole heart, would not keep His law? Or who, having love for his neighbor, would be zealous for sins, since indeed for the sake of the fear of God he loves his neighbor?

And He added: he shall receive a blessing from the Lord, and mercy from God his Saviour. Now he shows what the merit is of the one who, in the cause of God and man, of the Creator and of his neighbor, has held faith and love, since he shall both be blessed by the Lord — so that he may appear as one commended — and in these matters he shall obtain mercy, in the things which he was not able to fulfill. For no one can be so circumspect that he never errs anywhere; but because he has been found solicitous and faithful in great things, in the least things he shall receive mercy from his God, who is for this reason called the Saviour, because he does not will the death of the dying man, but that he should turn back and live.

And there follows: this is the generation of them that seek him, of them that seek the face of the God of Jacob. He said that the generation of those who seek God and of those who seek the face of the God of Jacob is one, which is not idle. For although each is good, yet by these words he signified two grades. It is good to seek God, but it is a full good to seek the face of the God of Jacob. For those who are before the face of the judge are more worthy. Of these merits, therefore, this is the generation. For, in order that one may come to this, innocence and purity of heart bring it about, just as again, in order that one may be able to come to evil, injustice and iniquity run ahead, because there are two generations, one on the side of iniquity, which is the mother of Cain, the other on the side of justice, which is the mother of Abel. The latter consists in faith and good conduct, the former in faithlessness and crooked works. These are the two laws, of the good and of the evil, of God and of the devil, and whichever one a man has followed, of that one shall he be called the son. And through this, the generation of those who seek God is this: faith in God and love toward one's neighbor, as we said above. For he who has faith in God will always seek his help; nor let him show himself unjust to men, who wishes to have God favorable, of which this is the sign, when it is said: the God of Jacob, because God is known in Judea. And why are they said to seek the face of God, when Moses heard: my face, he says, shall not be seen by thee? If therefore even Moses sought the face of God and could not see it, in vain are these said to seek it, since they are not better than Moses. But no one is to be said to seek the face of God in vain, because they are for this reason said to seek the face of God, that it may be known whom they seek, since they are worthy also to see. For everyone who is sought is then recognized, if his face is seen. But since the face of God cannot be seen by mortals, this signification stands in place of the face, when it is said: the God of Jacob.

DIAPSALMA. The diapsalma in this place signifies a change of person. For after those things which pertain to the person of God the Father, it appends those things which pertain to the mystery of the Son of God, so that the complete order of Father and Son may be preserved. For the law was given through Moses, so that it might be as it were a schoolmaster to men, instructing them and preparing them, with justice as their teacher, that they might become worthy to receive the year of the Lord and the day of retribution, so that those who had been faithful in the hope of the Christ who was to come might receive him and be able to escape the sin of Adam, being secure on their own account, because his coming, which they had hoped for, would give them mercy concerning those things in which it was stealthily brought about that they should transgress. This is what he mentioned above when he said: he shall receive mercy from God his savior.

He says therefore: lift up the gates of your prince. This is the voice of the holy angels, of whom he says in the Gospel that the angels, he says, drew near and ministered to him. These therefore say to the principalities and powers, against whom the Apostle says we have our struggle, that they should lift up the gates of their prince, that is, of the devil, who is the prince of the princes established in error, through which one goes into hell. These gates are the perfidy and deceit of idolatry.

And there follows: and be lifted up, O eternal gates. Faith and hope together with charity are the eternal gates, because what is preached through the truth cannot be abolished. But those gates, which I said are the perfidy and deceit of idols, are temporal, because every lie does not endure; for when the truth has been made manifest it will perish. They are taught therefore to lift up the gates of their prince, that is, that with the error of many gods removed, the faith of one God may be introduced in Christ. This is what the Apostle testifies that he was sent to teach, so that he might show not only to men, but also to the principalities and powers in the heavenly places, the mystery of the one God in Christ.

And he added: and the King of glory shall enter in. He says that the deceitful gates of the devil, composed by subtlety, must be lifted up—which are presumption, through which he esteemed himself to be God—so that the King of glory may enter in, who is Christ Jesus our Lord. For the faith of God enters no one's mind unless it has removed from itself the error of the devil devised by transgression; and the aforementioned princes cannot receive Christ the King into the faith of the one God unless they have cast from themselves the tradition through which calumny is brought against the one God—not only the earthly tradition, but also that which was usurped in the heavenly places by Satan, the prince of princes, because there they arranged the error which they conspired among themselves—that, with the devil as author, they should call themselves gods—here on earth also, so that this error might be the image of that one.

And there follows: who is this King of glory? Lest perhaps he be thought to call Christ the King of glory needlessly, he sets forth the person of one asking and saying: who is this King of glory?—one who as it were marvels or wishes to be taught whether it is true and fitting to call Christ the King of glory.

And so the answer is given to him: the Lord strong and mighty, the Lord mighty in battle, so that from this proposition he might learn, not by bare words but with testimony, that Christ is the King of glory. For the strength and power by which he subdued and crushed the devil together with his henchmen — these show that he is the King of glory.

And it adds: lift up the gates of your prince, and be lifted up, O eternal gates. That is: since by the testimony of that strength, which it is wicked to contradict, you have learned that Christ is worthy to be called the King of glory, remove the error from your minds.

And it says: and the King of glory shall enter in. For it is shown that they cannot share in the faith of Christ unless they have cleansed their hearts from all the uncleanness of idolatry. For unless a man has been renewed, the Holy Spirit will not enter into him.

And there follows: who is this King of glory? This repetition produces confirmation. For everything that is heard as new is not grasped by the heart unless it is repeated. For wonder produces astonishment, when something is said that has never been heard.

And it adds: the Lord of hosts, he is the King of glory. By repeating the same thing it teaches that this one is rightly called the King of glory, being the Lord of hosts. For not without reason is he called the King of glory, who is Lord over the hosts. Whence the apostle: for if they had understood, he says, they would never have crucified the Lord of majesty.

CXII. ON PSALM L.

This fiftieth psalm has been placed here not according to its position in time, but by reason. For its heading is subject not to number, but to reason. For as far as pertains to the history, it is earlier than the third. For David was first rebuked by the prophet Nathan on account of his sin, before his son Absalom rose up against him, wishing to deprive him of both life and kingdom. This, then, is the reason why it was placed as the fiftieth, which it is agreed derives from the law. For there it is provided that the number fifty holds remission. For it is, after seven weeks, the first, bearing the type of the Lord's day, because, just as after seven days the first day is that very one which was made in the beginning, which, when the week is finished, is always first, so also after seven weeks the fiftieth, being counted, is first in mystery, which by a twofold character is called the Lord's day: first, because it was made in the beginning by the Lord, which, always turning back upon itself through the completed cycles of the week, is first; then, because on it He rose again, death being triumphed over, it is named the Lord's day. Not undeservedly, then, in the number fifty, which we have observed to be signified as first in mystery, is remission contained. Finally, the law was given on the fiftieth day. For from the departure of the children of Israel out of Egypt, on the forty-eighth day they are commanded to be washed, so that, purified for two days, on the third day they might be prepared to receive the law. And what else does this washing indicate than the remission made on the fiftieth day, so that, being purified, they might receive the law, from which, henceforth, with past things blotted out, they might render an account of their own conduct according to the law entrusted to them? For this reason also the law commanded that a purchased possession ought to be remitted in the fiftieth year. Hence it is, then, that this psalm too was entitled the fiftieth.

For in it King David asks for remission according to the sense stated above; whence he says: have mercy on me, O God, according to thy great mercy. King David, knowing that he had sinned grievously, thus makes his petition, saying: have mercy on me, O God, according to thy great mercy. For great mercy is that which then exists, when it pardons great sins.

And he added: and according to the multitude of thy tender mercies blot out my iniquity. That he might make manifest that he had transgressed bitterly, he calls his sin iniquity, because iniquity is not a light sin, and he who blots out iniquity, that is, does not impute it, abounds in compassion. He therefore strives to excuse nothing in his own cause, so that he may not only soften the mind of the judge toward himself, but also make him suffer along with him. For the mind is moved to mercy, when it sees the guilty one thus confess his sins, so as to show that he grieves because he sinned. For to whom he does not grieve, he seems to mock the judge, because he prays for this reason, that he may escape for the time, being about afterward to do the same things.

And there follows: thoroughly, he says, wash me from my injustice and cleanse me from my sin. Since he proclaims the great compassion of God, he prays to be cleansed to the very depths, so that there may remain in him nothing of sin or injustice, whereby he might be held suspect.

And that he might be able to obtain or secure this very thing, he added: for I acknowledge my iniquity, and my sin is always against me. Because he knows he has sinned grievously, he is anxious lest not everything be forgiven him. For considering the magnitude of his sins, he is afraid, knowing that grave sins are not easily pardoned; and therefore he confesses his sin with weeping, keeping it before his eyes, so that in every respect he may make the judge propitious to himself. For he knows that it is written: Declare thou first thy iniquities, that thou mayest be justified.

And there follows: Against thee only have I sinned, and done evil before thee. Now from another side of his case he looks out for a remedy, so that he may bring his petition to its effect. For by this line of argument he declares himself innocent in the first charge, because whoever sins against another god is an enemy of God the Author. For in his place he chooses another, to whom he ascribes His authority. Whence here, showing himself a stranger to this iniquity, he says: Against thee only have I sinned, and done evil before thee, that is: 'because I have sinned against thee and against no other, whom by error I might call god, my case is lesser than that of the rest, who are liable to perdition. For I am not impious, but a sinner, because I have sinned not against thee, but against thy law, nor have I denied thee, but confessing thee as God and Lord I have sinned in a man. Therefore, since in that which is thy own proper cause I am not guilty, pardon me for having sinned against a fellow-servant. For I have so sinned that I neither denied the honor of thy name nor gave it to another.' And so there was something whereby the judge might be moved to mercy. For since many sin against Him, whom He nevertheless exhorts to be converted to Him, while these, who do not sin against Him, yet from other causes ask for pardon, He gladly admits them. For the Lord always wills that His servants flee to His aid.

Whence he added: that thou mayest be justified in thy words, and mayest overcome when thou art judged. Asking for mercy, he prays that God may not change His justice, since justice is wont to be in the middle, so that it may be equal to those who do not sin and those who sin, that is, that He may render to each one according to his works. But he is not ignorant of what an innocent man asks for in the mystery of God. For knowing that the Lord had frequently said in the law, for I am merciful, the Lord your God, from this he asks that this faithfulness may remain and that God may be justified in His words, while He abides in that which He has spoken, and is not overcome by the sins of men, so as not to have mercy. For those who sin, knowing what God has promised to those who live well, and because they persist in it, judge Him a liar. For by living badly they do not believe that the things He promised are true. Therefore King David asks that God may overcome this thing which men suppose, and may give what He promised, so that they may be put to shame who, while they do not keep the commandments, judge that He will not give.

And it continues: for behold, I was conceived in iniquities. Now by adding this he calls to mind the cause and the weakness of the human race — because it is easy for a man to sin —, so that he may soften the Judge's scrutiny concerning himself. For he makes mention of the first cause, from which, by transgressing, Adam made the race of men liable to sin. For by the persuasion and deceit of the enemy, in conceiving iniquity, he subjected all his own seed to iniquity, so that from him all who are begotten by propagation should be liable to sin.

For this reason he added: and in offenses did my mother conceive me, so that the derivation of sin made from Adam should be an impediment to those conceived and born for the leading of a good life; and on account of this, mercy is not to be denied to the suppliant, because he has an enemy grafted into him, who persuades him to sin. For sin, which is the devil, when the first man had been subjected to him, received the power to graft itself into his flesh and, by a certain subtlety of offenses, to suggest allurements, through which, once the man is captured, it despoils him of life. Recalling this matter, the teacher of the nations says: but I see another law in my members, warring against the law of my mind, so that the Judge, beholding this, may stretch out His hand to the one grieving that he has been beset.

And it continues: for behold, Thou hast loved truth. ('Because, therefore, Thou lovest truth,' he says, 'come to the aid of the one who asks, and him whom Thou knowest to set forth the true impediments of his heart — for every guilty one, once moved by mercy, Thou hast decreed to come to the aid — so that heavenly providence might amend on earth the error that has been committed.')

On account of which he subjoined: the uncertain and hidden things of my heart Thou hast made manifest to me. 'The uncertain and hidden things of the heart' — these signify those things which God has provided for the protection of the human race, just as the apostle also says: that which the eye hath not seen nor the ear heard nor hath it ascended into the heart of man, which things God hath prepared for them that love Him. Who are they that love God, except those who consent to His law by not concealing nor excusing their sins? These hidden things of the heart, therefore, have been made manifest to those who love God; and because they grieved that they were beset by offenses, nor, when rebuked, did they deny it — imputing it to themselves and to their origin, transferring to God as author the things they sinned — their hearts were illumined by a spiritual flash, so that they might see God's providence in Christ, because He was to come to condemn the sin which, from Adam's transgression, held sway over the human race. This, therefore, King David prays: that what God had decreed for the future, seeing the race of men to labor, He would first come to the aid of him bound in the same necessity in which He had decreed to come to the aid of all, so that the gift, which was to be gratuitous and could not be given before its time, might be granted to him in return for his prayer and tears.

In what way this may come about, he does not keep silent, saying: Thou shalt sprinkle me with hyssop, and I shall be cleansed. Rightly does he ask to be cleansed by the sprinkling of hyssop. For with this the children of Israel smeared their doorposts with blood, so that they might be rendered safe from death; so also here he prays to be cleansed from his sins by the sprinkling of hyssop, lest he be touched by death.

And it continues: you will wash me, and I shall be made whiter than snow. It is manifest that what God makes differs from what man makes. Therefore I shall be made whiter than snow, he says, because what God has made is far better than what man has made. For this reason he asks to be cleansed by the sprinkling of hyssop, so that, just as the fleshly body is washed with water, so also through the signification of the hyssop the stains of souls may be washed away by a spiritual principle. For the sprinkling of hyssop is a kind of illumination, so that through that which is visible he may be invisibly cleansed.

And it continues: to my hearing you will give joy and gladness. There is no doubt that the voice of one proclaiming remission furnishes joy to sinners. For who would not rejoice when he hears that pardon is granted? For the one to whom God forgives is secure both in the present and in the future.

Therefore the humbled bones shall exult. The bones are humbled in a twofold manner, because he was humbled both that he might sin — for no one who sins is exalted — and that afterward, as a suppliant, he might implore forgiveness. For indeed, rolling in sackcloth and ashes, he was entreating for mercy, and, obtaining it, he was raised up by exultation of mind and refreshment of body.

And he added: turn away your face from my sins, and blot out all my iniquities. In this manner he requests remission, just as it had been established for the future through faith in Christ, so that the Savior, who was specially promised to him, should begin from him himself to grant pardon for all sins to those who are converted. For the one whose sins are not regarded and whose iniquities are all blotted out has no ground on which to be held guilty, and the mouths of his enemies are shut. For the one who does not regard the sins does not impute them. For this is what it is to turn away one's face from someone's sins: not to accept an accusation against him. And what is this, that, although he was rebuked concerning two sins, here he adds by accusing himself concerning many? And when he had heard from the prophet Nathan that it was unknown to him, why does he thus request with earnest prayer, that he may be able to attain to pardon, unless because he is pleading not his own cause alone, but under the occasion of his own sin he narrates the miseries of the whole people? For he prays for restoration for himself, because the one who is forgiven is not without shame, unless he be restored to his own place, so that the dignity of the place may excuse the shame; but for the rest he signified the benefit of God, which was to come in Christ for the washing away of all sins.

And there follows: Create in me a clean heart, O God, and dedicate a just spirit within my bowels; that is, to beg for mercy with a good disposition. But he prays not only concerning past things; he is also anxious about future ones, that he may not sin—so that, showing that he hates to sin, he may be helped by God through the spirit of righteousness, which he prays to be dedicated within his bowels: that is, that a new spirit be placed in him for this purpose, that it may guard him from sins, lest he be forced to do what he is known to be unwilling to do. And he can have a clean heart in this way, if he offends neither within nor without, that is, neither in deed nor in thought. But since it is nearly impossible that this be preserved in all things, it must at least be preserved in the cause of God, which is the principal one, so that the heart may be kept clean in the sacrament of God and of Christ, so long as faith in them is kept inviolate—because in another psalm he says: Who shall boast that he has a clean heart, or is free from sins? Finally, King David had a clean heart—in the cause of God, because he never sought help from vanity, which is idolatry, nor did he fail to fulfill anything commanded him by God.

You may see for yourself concerning the rest. And he added: Cast me not away from thy face, and take not thy Holy Spirit from me. This is the sense which I mentioned above, that, secure concerning pardon, he is anxious about his restoration. For he to whom something is merely forgiven is wont to be separated from the face of the Lord. For although vengeance is not taken upon him, nevertheless in the mind of the Lord or of the judge his crime is not blotted out. Therefore he prays that his sin may be utterly blotted out, and that he may be worthy to approach the face of the Lord and to prophesy again—which also came to pass. For this is what he says: and take not thy Holy Spirit from me. This is the spirit of the prophets.

And there follows: Restore to me the joy of thy salvation, and strengthen me with a principal spirit. He asks that the joy be given to him which had been promised in Christ, so that, washed of all stains and restored, he might be joyful in the salvation he had sought, and not removed but strengthened in the kingdom by the Holy Spirit, whom he therefore called principal, because he is above every creature, through whom kings reign.

And he added: I will teach, he says, the unjust thy ways, and the impious shall be converted to thee. He says this, that the unjust may learn in him the piety of God, by which he receives to himself those who confess their sins and advances them, and does not neglect the impious who are converted, but exhorts them, while he shows mercy to others.

And there follows: Deliver me from blood, O God, thou God of my salvation. By saying this he shows that his sin was worthy of death, so that he may proclaim how great God's mercy is. For by this he also provokes others to take refuge in the clemency of God.

Whence he added: My tongue shall exult thy righteousness, O Lord. He says that he rejoices in the righteousness of the Lord, because he abides in what he promised, saying: But my mercy I will not remove from them. Therefore King David, joyful and secure in this regard, proclaims the righteousness of his God.

And it continues: my lips you shall open, and my mouth shall proclaim your praise. Thus he opens his lips, so long as the cause of sin does not permit him to be subjugated. For the mouth of the freed man lies open, whereas the mouth of the sinner is shut; for, seized by shame and fear, he does not dare to speak; but the man who has been absolved or set free, dancing for joy, proclaims the praise of the Judge. For the apostle too, as he himself testifies, because he was free from all men: our mouth, he says, lies open to you, O Corinthians.

And he added: for if you had wished for sacrifice, I would surely have given it. He speaks this from what is well established, knowing that holy Samuel said to King Saul, when the latter thought that his sin could be blotted out not by grief of mind nor by the compassion of tears, but by the offering of sacrifice: God does not will, he says, sacrifice more than that his voice be obeyed, because sacrifices of this kind can cause loss, yet cannot appease God. For God is appeased in this way, if the soul, mindful of its sin, weeps with tribulation and grief of heart over what it has sinned.

And it continues: with burnt offerings you will not be delighted. God is not delighted with burnt offerings, but in causes of this kind. For the mind itself ought to make satisfaction on its own behalf, not to redeem itself from outside. For these things can furnish assistance, but the mind, in the same manner as it took delight in sins, so ought it to be afflicted in penitence, and then it will delight God to wipe away its sins.

Whence he subjoined: a sacrifice to God is an afflicted spirit; a contrite and humbled heart God does not despise. He shows what kind of sacrifice for sins God accepts, because no one will be able to appease God except the man who, having rejoiced against his own salvation, has been afflicted in heart, that he may be restored to life. In short, everyone restless or undisciplined frequently meets with harm, so that he injures the members of his body, which surely cannot receive the medicine of health without pain: so too sinners cannot redeem their sins unless the soul which has sinned has felt pain. For this is just, that, because it rejoiced against reason, according to reason it should suffer grief.

And it continues: deal kindly, Lord, in your good will with Zion. By saying Zion he signifies the Church, so that our assertion may not seem improbable — to which, that God may fulfill his promise, he suggests, knowing that it will then receive full remission, when God shall have fulfilled in Christ what he decreed, that is, that those hoping in the promised salvation may be freed from all sin, because they will be freed through Christ not only from their own sins, but also from Adam's sin, which through propagation has come upon all.

And he added: and let the walls of Jerusalem be built. What does it mean that he says: 'and let the walls of Jerusalem be built'? Had they perchance been destroyed? But by saying the walls of Jerusalem he signifies the Church, to be constructed through the faith which had been appointed in Christ, whose walls are understood to be holy men, as the Apocalypse of John the apostle teaches us, because both Jerusalem the city and its walls are to be understood as the servants of God. Knowing, therefore, the promise through Christ, which was to be for the liberation of the servants of God, King David prays that it may be fulfilled, that is, that through faith the Church may be built and those hoping from this faith for salvation may be freed.

And it continues: then you will accept the sacrifice of righteousness, offerings and whole burnt offerings. He says this because offerings and whole burnt offerings would come to be acceptable then, when the Church had been built, and not indiscriminately or according to custom, but, he says, you will accept the sacrifice of righteousness. A sacrifice is righteous when a worthy gift is offered; but furthermore nothing is so righteous and worthy as that we offer our very selves to God. For since God is spirit, spiritual sacrifices are to be offered to him, so that a living victim may be offered to the living one. For because he had said that in the matter of sin God is by no means delighted by carnal sacrifices, he showed that it would come to pass that offering was to be made to God spiritually and that he would accept it, because these are sacrifices worthy of God — that is, in the past they were lesser, being as it were carnal, now greater, because spiritual.

And he added: then they will lay calves upon your altar. If, with the former sacrifices ceasing, he signified that spiritual sacrifices would succeed them, why is it that he should say: then they will lay calves upon your altar, unless because through these bodily things he signifies spiritual things, seeing that he has said that he is not delighted by carnal ones? Therefore in saying "calves" he signified the new people reborn through faith in Christ, whose devotion is daily offered as firstfruits upon the altar of the Lord. For he who is understood to be speaking about the Church must be understood to have signified also her sacrifices as spiritual.

CXIII. WHY THE SON OF GOD WAS SENT AND NOT ANOTHER.

Although everything that God does must be believed to have been done reasonably and providently, I nevertheless think it better if, after faith, the cause of the deed can also be perceived, so that what is believed may not be unknown. Whence the Lord says: and this is eternal life, that they know you the only and true God and Jesus Christ whom you have sent. For first it must be believed, as was said by the prophet, then what is believed must be known, because now the Holy Spirit helps one who is faithful and diligent in this matter, so that in knowing he may understand the reason of the faith. For then he has full joy in his belief, if he understands what he believes. For this is the strength of faith, this the immovable foundation: to learn the mystery of one's belief. For where there is faith alone, the devotion is not so pure, and stealthy deception can find an entrance.

Whence the vessel of election labors for this, that believers may learn thoroughly the reason of their faith. For when they have come to know how great is he in whom they have believed and what is his power, by no compact, by no reasoning will they suffer themselves to be drawn away from hope in him. Whence we read it said: taste and see how sweet is the Lord, so that by deliberate effort they may come to know the savor of his divinity, understanding that nothing is more wise than to believe in Christ. On which account the very teacher of the nations says among other things: in whom are hidden all the treasures of wisdom and knowledge, and again: for I know, he says, whom I have believed, because he is able to guard my deposit. Therefore this man knows the reason of his faith and does not doubt about the power of him who understands that all the treasures of wisdom and knowledge are hidden in the mystery of the nativity and passion and resurrection of Christ.

Therefore, with my faith remaining firm, I long pondered within myself, that I might know why our Lord had come from his holy and heavenly seats to the earth to be made flesh, and not another of those whom the Lord himself calls holy angels. For it seems unbefitting to his person to have undertaken this work, unless the reason for the deed be known. This, in short, is what troubles many. For the foresight of God the Father could have carried out this business through another person, so as both to show the truth to men established in error, and to crush the devil — that presumptuous one and enemy of salvation — together with his satellites, and to strip him once vanquished, since the obstinacy and irreverence of most wicked servants had to be restrained by attendant servants, and the Lord of all should not undergo a matter unworthy of himself.

For the strength and power of Satan is not so great that he could not be overcome by another power of the same condition, since it is agreed that with God's help he is vanquished even by men. For although he be unequal — if we take examples from earthly things — nevertheless in some part they will accord with reason. For to pursue and slaughter the servant [Sheba] rebelling against King David, Joab his fellow-servant was sent; and against Absalom, the impious one against his father, the task was enjoined upon servants: how much more, to vanquish the tyranny of the devil, would the efficacy of one of the many holy angels have sufficed, seeing that we read that, warring against the angel Michael, he could not endure but was cast down to the earth!

Reflecting therefore often on these things with myself, this alone I knew: that he was for this reason sent by the Father, lest it should pertain to his injury, if he were to restore through another the work made through him. But to know this was a very small thing; for it was not enough for so great and admirable a cause, unless something else also were comprehended, which might avail toward the hidden understanding. So it came about that I perceived the cause to have demanded this: that he who came should come here. For reason itself touched upon this: that he should come to crush him who had usurped his dignity and merit. For after God the Father the devil wished to be called god, which he even now still contends for, although indeed the matter did not befit him, but this belonged to the Son of God, who after the Father is second as god, not by nature, but by order.

For carrying out this cause, therefore, it behooved none other to come than Christ our Lord, because he came not only to crush the devil, but also to manifest himself to every creature, so that, the person of the devil being reprobated, they might recognize this to be the one whose princedom and majesty Satan had presumed upon, and thenceforth, the truth being known, might abandon their error, holding for certain that Christ is the Son of God, who alone is God from God and the head or beginning of all those things which are in heaven or on earth, because by the virtue and power with which he overcame the tyrant Satan, he was manifested to be the very one whose dominion the impious devil, who is Satan, had presumed upon.

For while he wishes to be called god unjustly, so that he might have dominion over the spiritual powers — whence he is rebuked by the prophet who says: for you, he says, have said in your heart: I will set my temple in the clouds, and I will be like the Most High —, he deceived many, by whose conspiracy he was made the prince of error, who in the psalm are admonished, that, rejecting the lie, they may receive the truth. For it is said to them: lift up the gates of your prince, and be lifted up, O everlasting gates, and the King of glory shall enter in. For unless the error be taken away from the minds of unbelievers, they will not be able to receive the faith of the one God in Christ. For they are taught this, that, the gates being lifted away — through which one goes to the second death, which are the treachery begun by the devil — they may receive the sacraments of the faith, through which one goes to life. For these are the things which he signifies by the everlasting gates, because the truth of the faith is eternity, but treachery is temporal, because the lie is the devil's invention. For the devil, having begun his transgression in the heavens, sowed it upon the earth by urging the worship of many gods, among whom he might hold the primacy. For he wished to be the head and origin of the rest, which was not owed to him, but to the Savior. Whence he pursues him even to the earth, so that, pressing him down, he might show the transgression of his error, the truth being made manifest in himself. Whence it is said: the truth has arisen from the earth. For, cast down from the heavens, he fled to the earth, so that he might complete in lower things the deity he had contrived in the heights. Whence the apostle says: so that he sits in the temple of God, showing himself as though he were God. For in whatever way he wishes to fulfill his desire, even to be condemned; for being full of fury he reckons it a lesser thing to be condemned than not to fulfill the greed of his error.

CXIIII. AGAINST THE PAGANS.

On what ground the pagans should dare to contend against us, or should suppose that they attack our faith, I do not see to be settled, seeing that they have no proofs of their assertions — of their superstition, I should say, rather than of their religion. For they put forward things that have no safeguard, so that they themselves are rather held to be the authors and defenders of novelties. For in the first place they assert that they worship gods, of whom they give no tokens, no testimonies. For they call those gods who did not dare to call themselves by this name, so that men are held to be the authors of the gods, whereas God ought to be the author of men; and through this the assertion is empty and void. For whatever is without God cannot be stable. For their books prove that the things I say are manifest, in which nothing divine, nothing established at God's command is read, but they assert that individual men, for certain reasons, instituted the diverse sacrifices of the deities, and these men such as would seem of no merit, in order that the things they had established might be shown to have been worthily established. When therefore those whom they call gods are read to have commanded none of these things, by what authority do these men act, or suppose it to be credited to them, what they do not prove was commanded — since, even if it were proved to be commanded, it would first have had to be examined whether it were worthy or fitting to obey those of whom no signs or prodigies existed in their deeds to commend their divinity?

But when they are shut out by these arguments they are wont to take refuge in the elements, saying that they worship those things by whose governance human life is ruled. Of them, as above, we ask: has it been commanded or enjoined by God, whom even they themselves confess to be great and supreme — and yet they neglect him? For if it ought to be done, it had to be commanded by him who is said to be their author. But if it has not been commanded by him, it is presumption and will lead to punishment, not to reward, because it pertains to the dishonor of the Creator that, the Lord being despised, servants should be worshiped, and, the emperor being scorned, his retinue should be adored. How will this go unpunished, when we may see it avenged even in this life, and indeed rather bitterly?

But perhaps they may say that it was commanded by these very elements. Let them show a precept somewhere, let them at some point read something that these ever spoke. But if they do not show it, of what punishment are they worthy, who are exposed as the authors of a presumptuous and fabricated religion? The elements are free from this impiety. For those very luminaries of the world will accuse them before God the judge, because in this vanity of counsel they show themselves in no way to be the authors. Likewise also those whom — though it is agreed that they were men — they call gods, when they have begun to be tormented for their sins, will in this respect defend themselves as being in no way guilty, throwing this charge back upon those who, when they had given no command, they nonetheless began to worship as gods.

Therefore with what shame do they think our faith is to be reprehended or laughed at, they who see our law supported by the testimonies of mighty works, and who moreover read the very voice of our Lord and God saying: I am God and there is no other besides me? But if he said this in bare words, he was not to be believed, lest we should seem like the pagans, who, seeing no signs of the majesty of their gods, began to worship them, and, what is worse, dishonorably. For shameful things are done there, which it is a matter of decency to reveal, and they call themselves prudent, because by their own law they are mocked as by a charlatan.

Furthermore, our God commanded that he be worshiped with mighty works as witness, and, so that he might leave no room for suspicion, he handed down the law itself as honorable and holy and, as is worthy of God. But we, who are called fools by the pagans, would not have believed our God unless he had satisfied us by the testimonies of mighty works, nor would we have received his law, had we not known it to be pure and fully worthy of that very profession.

Hence it is that among us nothing is done in darkness, nothing in secret. For everything that is known to be honorable is not afraid to be made public; but that which is base and dishonorable cannot, since shame forbids it, be made public. For which reason the pagans celebrate their mysteries in darkness — prudent even in this. For they blush to be openly mocked; for the shameful things which are done there in place of a law they do not want made manifest, lest those who call themselves prudent should seem dull-witted to those whom they call fools.

But perhaps in this we seem foolish to them, because our law preaches chastity, mercy, and self-restraint. For among them these things are foolish, since to every good man the wicked man is bad, and to the prudent the fool is senseless. For they so abominate chastity that they even take delight in catamites, being subject to the tutelage of those men who, unless they were of this sort, would not themselves be fit — if it must be said — for their religion. For this is why they are castrated and change their dress, so that from men they may become, as it were, women, and, made subject contrary to nature, may suffer the things women suffer, so that then at last they may be fit and worthy ministers of that superstition of theirs. Can a man of this kind be accused, whom the law makes such? For it is for this reason that, having lost both the act and the dress of a man, he is transformed into a woman, so that he may with license suffer the things women suffer.

What sort of mysteries, then, are those thought to be, where from the honorable comes the dishonorable and from the incorrupt the corrupt, whereas this is truly the worship of religion: that it should make honorable men out of the base and modest men out of the undisciplined? How can the worshippers of it be zealous for a good life, when the mysteries of their law cannot be celebrated without catamites? If therefore their law delights in these things, whoever has not been such will be a sinner and a fool. And for this reason they call us fools. For those who cultivate these shameful things are judged prudent by them, while those who avoid and flee them are judged fools. And this indeed seems to have been fitted together by them cleverly and cunningly. For they attach the name of prudence to their law in order to cover over its reprehensible features, because where there is prudence there is no reproach. But our law they call foolish, in order to keep people away from it. Yet if the names of prudence and folly be removed, and all the ill-will be set aside which the defense of both parties breeds, let the laws themselves clash with one another, so that it may appear where prudence lies and where folly. But in the presence of the sign of the cross paganism falls silent, and, if that prudence of theirs — which they call foolish — is present, their sacred rites dare not respond; for their entrails are checked and reverence is concealed before the majesty of Christ. A great thing, that what they call prudence should fear what they call folly.

Let us now compare the tenor of the laws. The pagans profess even in their writings that they worship gods and goddesses; and what they say is true, that they worship both males and females. For Janus and Saturn, Jove and Mercury and Apollo and the rest, likewise Minerva and Isis, and that Fruit-goddess and Venus and Flora the harlot, together with the rest, are gods and goddesses, as the histories both of the Greeks and of the Romans testify. But the Christians, being poor, as it were — whom they call fools — worship one God in mystery, from whom are all things, and they venerate nothing that has been created by him. For they know that he alone suffices for them and abounds unto salvation, being not ignorant that, if they should assign his glory and name to others, they would offend him, because no emperor permits that tribunes and counts be adored in his name.

Let the laws now be compared, so that it may be seen where prudence lies: with the one who worships the Creator or the one who worships the creature, with the one who serves the Lord or the one who serves slaves? Can it happen that in some household anyone besides one man be called master — as the pagans, trained in the literature of the courts, who seem to themselves prudent, in one world founded by one God venerate many gods and goddesses? For they have called the managers and agents of the world by the name of "lord" and "god," while they are exposed as improvident. But to assert that which begets ruin belongs to the improvident and foolish man, since God must needs avenge the wrong done to him upon those who have shared out to their fellow-slaves the name and glory of the Lord God.

Next let the precepts be considered. Our law, whose adherents they call fools, commands that priests and ministers be ordained holy, without crime, and blameless; but on the contrary the tradition of the pagans holds that its priests and ministers cannot be fit for it unless . . . men be transformed into women, so that they may licentiously and publicly suffer womanish things, and, with their hair shaken out unseemlily in water, may send forth a soft and quavering and shameful voice. And if they did this publicly, they would be stoned by the whole people. And that Cynocephalus, who wandering unsteadily carries himself about through all places seeking the members of the adulterer Osiris, husband of Isis: behold with what ministers, and without any masters, the pagans arrogate to themselves the name of prudence, when it is a matter not only of imprudence but even of crime! For to love things obscene and shameful is deadly. But of what sort is that, that in a cave they are mocked with veiled eyes? For lest they shudder at being basely dishonored, their eyes are veiled. Others, moreover, beat their wings like a bird, imitating the voice of the raven; others truly roar after the manner of lions; others, with their hands bound, are thrown down upon pits full of water with the entrails of chickens, while a certain one approaches with a sword and bursts through the aforesaid entrails, one who would call himself a liberator.

There are also other yet more shameful things. Behold by how many modes those who call themselves wise are basely mocked! But because they suffer these things in darkness, they think it can remain unknown.
