For all those things which were devised and ordained in secret by evil and shameful men, the holy Christian faith has disclosed and uncovered. For when the faith had been preached, those who heard, considering what good and holiness was publicly promised, betook themselves to the faith, leaving behind those hidden, dishonorable, and shameful things, and confessing in what manner they had been mocked through ignorance. For then already for a long time nearly all were being turned about in this error, and for that reason they seemed to themselves prudent, because there was none to rebuke them. For although all erred under one name of deceit, that of idolatry, yet each one arranged his worship according to his own customs and manner of life, so that the one error had diversity arising from the baseness and manner of life of its authors. Finally the rites which they call those of Liber are dishonorable and most vain and full of frenzy; for it is difficult for an impure man not to be wrathful. Finally he is everywhere seen painted together with Priapus, with whom he lived dishonorably.

In this manner are the rest of their sacred rites as well; but it was through Satan's subtlety and cunning that these things were brought about, so that later generations might be ensnared by these inventions. And although these things were not devised apart from his design, yet upon each of the inventions he sprinkled certain sleights, by which he might lure men into error, and thus it came about that through the transmission of antiquity a deceit was commended and a base invention was excused. For through custom what was base began not to seem base; for when certain persons are dishonored, at first they blush; but afterward, as custom soothes them, shame withdraws and the countenance is changed, especially if one should see many such persons. For the traffic of baseness is at its height when those who are called noble are seen to be dishonored; for a dishonored nobility readily finds imitators.

If therefore for this reason they long ago already seemed wise to themselves — because there was no one to whom this foul invention was displeasing — now, since by God's mercy the human race has been enlightened and it has been made plain that what passed for law was a crime, and that what was thought to have been prudently invented was foolish, they ought at the very least to cease from this title, if they wish to remain in a crime now uncovered, acknowledging that the name of wisdom is foreign to them.

But so that they may add to the heap of their own confession, being caught in folly they call those men fools by whom they are refuted. But they accuse our faith and our posterity, while in the precepts they do not deny that nothing can be found blameworthy. Those who accuse our faith take up their charge not so much against us as against our Author; for they pronounce us fools, as having given our faith as it were to a foolish matter, but him a liar and a deceiver, and moreover malicious, as one who would hand on a faith through which believers would be deceived. Therefore let us first prove that we did not believe foolishly, and then go on to plead the cause of our Author.

When we were passing our life in error — in which the pagans now remain — drawn by no signs of powers, but receiving the bare words which they call sacred rites, thinking them to be of profit, not because divinity had commended them, but because old custom had handed them down — in which, as is not hidden, mocked by various vanities, we recognized no hope of salvation; for what could a thing invented by men profit? But that we should approach the faith of God and believe his Son to have been incarnate and crucified, we were persuaded not by words, but by facts. For we saw the dead raised, lepers cleansed, sight restored to one born blind, demons cast out, and at the same time all infirmities healed.

Now let it be judged when we were fools: when we believed bare words, or when we believed facts? For without doubt facts come before words, since words were invented to signify facts. If therefore we gave our faith to a human tradition with no power to persuade — a tradition invented to mock the minds of men — how much more to this one, to which all the signs that invoke God bear witness that it is divine and deifying! Should we not rightly be judged fools, if we did not believe powers, we who had believed mere words? Should we not reasonably be marked as improvident and inconsiderate, if we did not follow a hope that calls us to it, we who had been subject to despair?

'But', they say on the contrary, 'what is believed is foolish; for it does not stand to reason that God has a son, nor that dead and dissolved bodies should be restored again to life'. All the philosophers and founders of sects have pierced one another through with diverse disputations, none passing over to another, because each one remained in that with which he had been imbued; for through the fact that words are liable to contradiction, none overcame another; for there was no ground on which anyone might show himself the victor, but wearying one another with mutual contradictions, neither in the least persuaded the other.

Hence it came about that the providence of God, whose purpose cannot be searched out, joined power to its own preaching, so that the truth of the preaching might be proved by the testimony of power, so that those who were prepared to contradict with words, seeing the power, might not dare to contradict. For what greater attestation of truth could there be than the working of power?

But if anyone should deny the works of powers, accusing the scriptures, how will he be able to call our faith foolish while denying the scriptures? For where it is written concerning Christ that he is to be believed the Son of God, in that same place the witness of power is found. And if to anyone reading that Christ the Son of God was fixed to the cross it seems absurd, let him read on and he will find that he rose again from the dead, and let him understand that he did not die idly nor unwillingly, he who was able to rise again, but that it is a mystery. Whoever, then, that man may be, either he will be silent about the cross — because, if he calls the cross foolish, he will not be able to call the resurrection foolish; for the resurrection excuses the cross — or, if he speaks about the cross, he will not be able to deny that there is providence in it, which he sees confirmed by the testimony of the resurrection; for either both are accepted or both are refuted, so that he who will not defend may not dare to accuse either.

But why he permitted himself to be fixed to the cross by his kinsmen, is a mystery to be reserved. Yet this death he so commended that he testified that he was glorified through it. For he also says: I have power to lay down my soul, and: I have power to take it up again. He then was not compelled to die, who had power both to die and to rise again. He who denies this will not be able to say that death was inflicted on him by violence. For if he denies that, neither will he be able to affirm that he died, because these things are contained in that place where he is read to have been handed over to death. For no one condemns the last things from the first or the first things from the last, because that which belongs to one body is not contrary.

But concerning the dead, that they rise again, they say craftily that it is foolish to believe it. For they see examples by which it may be judged credible. For all seeds necessary for our uses, unless they have been dissolved, will not be able to be born again. If he has decreed these things in the world for the sake of men, why, if he be believed about to grant these same things to their very bodies, do they think it foolish — unless because they themselves are foolish, who grant no more to God than the world is able to do? Yet a shadow of the mighty works once performed appears even to this day. For because it is not the time for performing mighty works — for they had to be done in the beginning, so that the seed of faith might through this make its growth — nevertheless even now the demons are terrified when the cross of Christ is named, and, if it be done more earnestly, are put to flight; and the gods of the pagans, from the dread and fear of the named cross, cannot give responses.

If the death of Christ is a reproach, why is it a cause of terror? For a thing which comes from a crime ought not to be feared, but despised. For who would fear a man slain for his own crime? And if he was innocent, still he cannot be feared, but (only) mourned. Therefore, unless the demons or the gods of the pagans perceived that the cross of Christ is a sacred mystery, they would not be terrified when it is named; and, to speak more precisely, unless they were guilty, they would not fear. For all these, who are on the devil's side, consented to the death of Christ. Whence all the demons or the gods of the nations are shaken with terror when the cross of Christ is named. For everyone who is guilty in the death of some innocent person, when that man's name has been mentioned, fear seizes his heart. For, once recollection of the misdeed is had, he sees himself guilty: how much more the demons or the gods of the nations, who are guilty of the slaying of the Lord and Savior of the world!

But the pagans contend that they hold the truth on account of antiquity, because, they say, "what is earlier cannot be false" — as if antiquity or old custom could prejudge the truth. For murderers or catamites or adulterers or the rest of the criminal could by this defend their unlawful deeds, because they are ancient and began from the very beginning of the world; whereas from this they ought rather to understand their error, because what is reprehensible and base is thereby proved to have been badly begun, while what is honorable and holy is worthily cultivated; and (because) the base and reprehensible cannot be prior to the holy and irreprehensible.

And finally, it cannot be denied that the tradition of the pagans was devised by man; but that our law was given by God is an evident matter. For on the mountain the majesty of God appeared to give the law to men; and, that credit might be given to this, he sent beforehand many signs and prodigies performed in Egypt — which even today the books testify, the books stored away by Ptolemy in the library of Alexandria. Thus, then, he deigned to give the law in such a way that no one might doubt that it was God who delivered it. For openly and manifestly the majesty of God was seen by all in such great splendor and terror, that both no other god besides him was believed (to exist), and what he commanded was held worthy of all observance.

And that all things may accord with the divine plan, let the transmitted law be examined, to see whether there is anything unjust in it, anything dishonorable, anything ridiculous. Thus it was fitting for God to make himself known -- not that he who cannot be confined should appear in a corner through imposture, nor in a cave, where the thing is other than what is seen, an enticing deceit being at work; nor that he should hand down as things to be observed those which, because they were dishonorable, would be carried out in the dark.

For when the knowledge of the one God had been blotted out in the world by the habit of transgressing, because falsehood had furnished a veil for the truth, the clement and merciful God, unwilling that his own work should become liable to perdition, deigned to visit the human race -- not appearing as those did who wished to be falsely called gods, but, as the truth of God is, so he manifested himself, in order to take away the error which had corrupted the world's understanding by the proclamation of false gods. And so, above six hundred thousand men who had gone out of Egypt, seeing and recognizing that this was the Lord above all, on account of the appearance of his ineffable glory, feared to draw near to him, but adored him with knee bent from afar; and afterwards, as other signs followed -- greater than those which he had done in Egypt among all his marvels -- he was manifested to be the one God.

These things became known to nearly all tongues; these things were a terror to the neighboring nations. What such thing is there among the gods of the nations? To what nation, to what people did they show their glory? What signs or mighty works did they perform as a testimony to their divinity? Where or when are they proven to have spoken?

But the irrational common crowd began to worship either appearing shades or demons or images of the dead as gods; and this matter, having been drawn into the habit of long antiquity, supposes that the truth of reason can be defended on its own behalf -- although the truth of reason is not from habit nor from antiquity, but from God, who is proven God not by antiquity but by eternity. For which reason faith is not a thing begun, but is without beginning. For to believe in God is our beginning; for that which is believed is eternal. In what way, then, do the pagans think themselves prior, when that which they worship is after God? Is not the work after the maker? The pagans worship works, we the maker; they the creature, we the creator. Certainly God made the world: come now, having made it, did he not show forth knowledge of himself? Or did he make man and not wish to be worshiped by him whom he made? But that is absurd.

Man, therefore, having been made, began to venerate his founder, because it is both fitting and the cause demands it. And when this had fallen into disuse through the sloth of men, God restored it in Abraham, so that the knowledge of God, which had been in Adam, might begin again in Abraham, so that those begotten of him might be brought up under this knowledge, and through the line of descent there might not fail nor be lacking one who would worship God -- men of a foreign nation also being called to this knowledge of God. He, therefore, who knows is under a beginning; but he who is known is above a beginning. By what reasoning, then, do the pagans say that their law existed before ours? If the world is before God -- which God forbid! -- then paganism too can be set before Christianity.

Let no one wonder at the name of Christianity. For it is to worship one God in the mystery of the Trinity, because, though the name of Christ is thought to be from "chrism" (anointing), nevertheless the meaning of the name is prior to creation. For among our forefathers, those who were anointed into kings were called christs, bearing the image of the Christ to come, who, born of God the Father as king, is not undeservedly called Christ, because what for those men was the chrism, for him his birth conferred. And him, as long as the pagans do not know him, they reject; but when they have come to know him, they worship him more diligently, rejoicing that they have passed from evil to good. For those who were obstinate in a thing so frivolous and foolish, how much stronger and more vehement will they be once the truth is known! What sort, then, is our Christ, who when he is not known is hated, when he is known is loved! For every evil man, when he is not known, is loved, and when known is hated. How many formerly hated Christ who now love him, and who grieve that they hated him because they had not known him! For it is the mark of an imprudent man to hate one whom he does not know, because one must first test whether the thing is worthy of hatred. In short, those who today oppose him will tomorrow defend him, doing penance that they came so late to know what is true. But if it were a thing worthy of hatred, or had anything of falsehood in it, then daily pagans would be made out of Christians. But, on the contrary, since this is the truth, daily, every hour, without ceasing, men abandon Jupiter — among them sophists and nobles of the world, who had fashioned him into a god — and take refuge in Christ, to whom is honor and glory unto the ages of ages.

CXV. ON FATE.

Nothing is so contrary to a Christian as if he should devote care to the art of astrology. For this is recognized as an enemy of the law of God. For if those who are evil are born so, and on the contrary those who are good are born so, then the law will be said to have been given in vain — and not only this, but the lawgiver too will be held unjust. For he who, having given a law, compels men to act contrary to that which they were born, knowing that nature cannot be changed, seems assuredly to have given the law for this purpose: that he might have an occasion by which to sate his cruelty on the slaughter of man. For why does he forbid what he knows cannot be averted? Or how does he condemn a man for what he did not do, since he could not do it?

But if he seems just — as he is, because to think that concerning God is wicked — then he gave the law rightly and by a saving purpose, knowing that a man is able to restrain himself from that which the law forbids. Therefore, since he gave the law justly, he is by no means unjust when he takes vengeance.

And so, if faith is to be lent to the law of God, since it is proved to have been given justly, the art of astrology is to be avoided and fled. For the cunning and subtlety of the devil devised it. For because he does not dare to oppose the author openly, he accomplishes it by subterfuge, so that he may both do injury to God and, setting man at enmity with the law, punish him with the second death.

Whoever, therefore, yields to God's authority and considers it wicked that His work should be called into question, obeys the law given by Him, pressing on day and night in eager pursuit of His pursuits, and looks to nothing opposed to Him, not doubting that a human being is capable of keeping His precepts. For those who long to sin seek occasions by which they may make their sins alien to themselves, so that, while the sins are as it were to be charged to nature, the sinner may seem to be exempt from punishment. This is the aim of those who, in despair of the promised life, desire to enjoy present pleasure.

But those who, relying on the confidence of faith, await the hope of things to come, strive to fulfill what they promised at the beginning of their rebirth: that they would renounce the pomps and pleasures of Satan. With what great subtlety, then, did Satan make use, that he might make man readier to sin, whereby he might satisfy the wickedness of his envy! For when man hears that he was born in such a way that he sins, and is not ignorant that God is most just, he believes that he sins with impunity, because He who is a just judge does not condemn the man who has sinned at nature's command; nor indeed is that to be called his sin which another performed through him, if the man was deceived; for weighed down by his sins he was made subject to death. Therefore he who takes away evil also takes away every good of man. For if the sinner has no punishment, neither has the just man a crown; and why are the good praised and the wicked blamed? For if it belongs to this nature that it cannot sin, why do you praise in him what is not his own? And why do you condemn the offense in a sinner, when you know that what he sins is not his own — unless it be that he will be unjust who praises the good and accuses the evil?

But perhaps it may be said on the contrary: 'I praise the good nature in man and condemn the bad, because good wine too is praised, since it is good by nature, and bad wine is refused, since nature equally furnishes this.' If it were so, there would be nothing at issue for blame, so that the just man would be praised in such a way that he would not be judged worthy of a crown, nor would the unjust be accused in such a way that he would be said to be worthy of punishment. But furthermore, both in the present and in the future we know and read that the just are rewarded and that the unjust, subjected to punishments, are burned up — which would surely seem unjust, if this man, who had carried out the business of his own nature, should suffer punishments by the avenging fire, or if another, equally carrying out the business of his own nature, should be endowed with rewards.

But if the good man is to be praised because he did what belonged to his nature, the bad man too ought to be praised, because he likewise did what belonged to his nature, so that each one, remaining in that which he was made, should be praised; for if a man remaining in that which he was made is held blameworthy, the blame touches the author, who caused such a one to be born as would be displeasing.

But if the just man is rightly to be rewarded and the unjust man condemned, then the response is not to nature but to the will, because nature is free, while the will is the responsible cause — a will that admits of the senses' approaches and withdrawals — so that the unjust man, seeing the just man crowned but himself condemned, may lay the blame upon himself, since, trusting the senses against the law, he did evil when he could have done good. Can the sun be accused because it burns, or water because it chills? Or is water to be rewarded because it revives men afflicted with thirst, or shall the sun be endowed with prizes because it restores men with its heat after cold? For they do what belongs to their own nature, and hence are neither condemned nor praised; for they know not how to do anything else.

But since it is not as the star-gazers reckon, the account of men's acts proves it. For no one can be found who always does well or always ill. For then a man would seem to have been born so as to act well, if he remained beyond reproach; and likewise he would be fittingly reckoned to be evil, if he persisted in it so as always to do ill. But since you may sometimes see even the man you think born good acting ill, and may now and then discern the man you call born evil doing good, how do you prove this to be true, that the evil man is evil and the good man good, unless because this rather is most certain, that, wherever a man has given his mind, in that he makes progress? For there are also causes that engender sins, so that a man either envies or quarrels or turns enemy.

But those things which are forbidden by the law they commit not openly nor manifestly, but in secret. Why, unless because they reverence the law? But if, as they assert, they had been born for this, that they should do ill, they would not seek hiding-places nor show any reverence to the law, but would do everywhere those things which nature compelled. But since you see men taking pains over their sins and contriving where they may commit them secretly — for they choose places and persons and times, that they may fulfill the will of their evil purpose —, in like manner also good men by a certain judgment determine their good works, so as to dispense their good according to the time and the case of someone; which surely is now to be imputed not to nature, but to the will, which is in nature. But if it were natural, judgment would cease.

For if we discuss the matter more fully, those who are now just, we shall show to have been unjust before, but by deliberate effort they have been changed. For Abraham himself is read to have been made just through faith — before, therefore, he was not, since he is understood to have worshipped idols with his father as his leader — and Zacchaeus assuredly, to whom, after his common life, the Lord bears witness, and the vessel of election, who confesses this very thing, saying: we too were by nature children of wrath, even as the rest. Since the will yields in place of nature — for a man is judged from his will, not from his nature —, without doubt all the martyrs too and the justified are good, because they were not born faithful, but reborn. And how many do we know to have been of base life and prodigal devotees of luxury, who now are continent and holy! For when the fear of God comes in, the vices are conquered which before held dominion.

But how much the fear of the law can accomplish, let us observe from this. Before Julian's edict women were not able to dismiss their husbands, but once they received the power they began to do what previously they could not do; for they began daily and without restraint to dismiss their husbands. Where did fate lie hidden for such long ages? By fear of the law, I believe, it was concealing itself.

But that which Moses handed down—how did it slip in? While the fates were sleeping, I believe. For such great things were handed over to them to be kept, and such precepts as no nation possesses. And lest anyone suppose that this should be referred to the region of the sky, scattered through nearly the whole world they keep the commandments of their law. These precepts are against the fates; for they did not exist, perhaps, for three thousand years and more from the foundation of the world; but afterward, once given, they remain until the end. That which therefore for so many thousands of years did not exist, how did it begin? For the course of the stars is from the foundation of the world.

And what is this, that, although there is so great a multitude of Jews throughout the whole world, none of them is changed so as to become a gentile, whereas we see—though rarely—that from pagans some become Jews? Behold, as far as pertains to the rite of the Jews, fate is excluded, because they both began to be outside fate and remain in that condition.

What is to be thought of Sodom and Gomorrah, whose end—of what sort it was—lies before the eyes of vengeance? It was such that all, besmirched with the infamy of filthiness, deserved to be consumed by divine fire. In the flood all were of one fate, so that by one death and at the same time they all perished, except the house of Noah. And shall the whole army of Pharaoh be said to have been born under one decree, so that the foolish error of astrology may be vindicated, because they were drowned in the Red Sea together with their king? And how many other examples there are, which we pass over, because two examples suffice for proof—how much more three!

For all nations too have diverse institutions, and what is permitted elsewhere is not permitted here, and again, what is permitted here is not permitted elsewhere. For here in the city of Rome and its territories, which is called most sacred, it is permitted to women to dismiss their husbands, although it is provided in the divine law that not even men should make use of this power, except for the cause of fornication. Behold, in this matter the barbarians are better! And it is not impossible that this could be observed here too, because it is shown to have been observed formerly. Fate therefore has been changed, because that began to be permitted which before had been forbidden.

And it is not permitted for eunuchs to be made in the Roman realm, though among others it is permitted; but that here too they could be made is beyond question; for it is proved to be done, albeit secretly. But if fear were to cease, it would be done publicly. Fear therefore hinders fate; for fate is overcome if it does not do what it wishes to do.

And the Persians, in the manner of women, wear earrings, which here is dishonorable and unlawful; but that this too could happen here is attested by the priests of the Mother who is called Great — and indeed she was great, but a harlot; yet there is this difference, that these men are catamites, those were men — and what shall we say? In all the world are these alone born under such a fate, that, being castrated, they are transformed into women, so that in the whole world only these few are found, who, it is agreed, are wretchedly deceived and, by promises of rewards, compelled to this suffering and disgrace? But if they had been born for this, they would not be compelled.

The Persians hold it lawful to have intercourse with their own daughters, which is shown to be able to happen here also, by the fact that it is proven to have been done by certain persons. But because punishment intervenes, they are seized with fear, lest they do what can be done but is not lawful. For everything that is forbidden is forbidden for this reason: lest, because it can be done, it be done; but if it were a matter of fate, it could not be done nor would it be forbidden. For if it is not of fate and yet is forbidden, it is signified that what is not of fate can be done. And what prudence is there in forbidding what cannot be done? Would anyone forbid that a man fly across from the city of Rome into Spain, or that anyone carry a mountain on his shoulders? But those things which can be done, though they ought not, are forbidden.

It is the custom of the Moors that their women wear earrings even in their noses. Is this then a matter of fate? If it were of fate, women brought here would so remain, because, if it is fate, each one carries his own fate with him; but because it is not of fate, all who come from there are changed here, seeing that to be reckoned base here which there is thought comely.

Surely among all nations number is number, and earth is earth, and water is water, and air is air, and fire is fire. Can there anywhere be found earth that is not dry, or air that is palpable, or water that is not fluid or cold, or fire that is cold? So everywhere Jupiter is Jupiter, and Saturn is Saturn, and Venus is Venus; and in the same way the other signs. For this art is treated everywhere by one and the same principle.

But because among all nations the established customs remain not by the principle of the fates, which is everywhere one, as they maintain, but by the contrivance of men — for whatever seemed worthy of reason and fitting for propriety to someone who was reckoned the first among his own people, he ordained for those over whom he presided —, hence each single region or nation has its own customs which it observes, just as Solon and Lycurgus had ordained many things which were observed by the Greeks after the manner of law, which little by little, as their kingdom declined, fell into oblivion.

For to the kings of the Jews mules were pleasing for riding; but to the Romans not mules, but horses; to the kings of the Garamantes, however, who are beyond Tripoli of the Africans, bulls are pleasing for riding; but the kings of the Persians are carried on couches — for the ancient kings of the Persians were not even seen by their peoples —; and the kings of Media delight in camels for riding, so that they adorn their necks with golden collars; and throughout Africa they prefer to sit on asses rather than on horses. -.

Since all things exist everywhere, yet not all things are pleasing to all people, because each person chooses what has greater favor in his eyes. For we ourselves too have differing preferences, both in clothing and in food and in the rest of our use of things and in our very opinion. And lest this be said to be a matter of fate — since many people assert differing fates — this very fact forbids it, because our preference is frequently changed in us. For what we love for a long time, we afterward despise; and we begin to love what we previously did not gladly possess, and once a habit has been formed we remain changed.

But they say that the order of the fates is such that there is something they bring about at certain times through intervals that return to their former state, as, for instance, what they do today, they do again after a time. For they are not said to remain in one condition; for they are overcome through training and habit.

Indeed, for many ages the use of wine was unknown to the women of the Romans. For a discipline, once made into a habit, confirmed its own perception, so that it retained what it had learned to be beneficial. Were these fates present in those times and afterward died out, so that a well-devised practice should cease? But it was not fate that had discovered this, but discipline. Yet when there was a departure from the habit, the discipline perished.

And the ancient Romans were of such great restraint that they even refused gifts offered to them, reckoning a good reputation to be their riches and preferring virtue to pleasures. And either these were followed by fates, or they died out; but it was not of fate, but of discipline, which is even the tutor of fate itself. For how could that be of fate which it neither had before that time nor has now?

And what it is, I do not know, that they cast such odium upon fate, so that in all things done they call it the author, though it both is and is not? For where there is discipline, it is not; and where there is habit, it is not; and where there is chance, it is not. But where it seems to be, it is overcome by fear of the law; for lust too is restrained by fear, and the profit of money is repudiated for the sake of earning God's favor. In this part, therefore, it cannot be denied, because that which is overcome cannot fail to exist, and yet it can rightly be said that in that very thing it does not exist, because that which is overcome lacks authority.

And so, for whoever follows the law of God and holds to the habit of a good life, all those things that are called fates yield to him. For habit has such great power that it even tames wild beasts. For those who wish to live badly establish fates for themselves. For through extravagance and pleasure there arises intemperance and restlessness, which gives birth to incontinence and irascibility. And knowing this to be worthy of punishment, in order to make themselves free of it, having suffered shame, they ascribe this to the fates, while it is the demons who furnish assistance to those thinking about this matter: those who stand ready for this, that they may grant effect to those thinking evil — these they call fates, not understanding that they are the enemies of man.

For if sins did not steal into the soul through the senses, but were born from within, then rightly everything that is done amiss would be said to belong to the ... . But since they do see that the lust for sins arises through sight and hearing and through the rest of the senses, with the aid of the enemies who suggest that it be done — which is harmful and is not thought to be so as long as it is being done (for it seems sweet, when it is being done or before it is done; but once accomplished it appears to be bitter) — why do they impute it to the fates?

That it can be overcome, however, we perceive from this. For when we are thinking and are anxious about some matter, the enemies fall silent and lust grows quiet; for they cannot make their suggestions to us while we are occupied. Does anyone make some other suggestion to a person who is devoted to a given task? He does not do so, knowing either that he is not listened to, or that he is rebuffed as one who suggests importunely. So too the enemies, who are agreed to be demons; for these are the gods of the pagans, who, if they see us occupied with divine matters, do not thrust themselves forward to suggest contrary things, but lie in ambush, seeking an occasion by which to insinuate themselves.

The defenses of souls, therefore, are the constant meditation on the law of God, and action; because, if they see us idle from divine matters, they are roused up to tempt us. For they are the ones who through flesh and blood sow lusts. For although the flesh has its own motion belonging to its origin, since carnal birth is not idle, nevertheless in this matter the aid of the demons is added toward the destruction of men.

For the demons are diverse and have differing wills. There are some which, when they disturb souls, dash the bodies; but some, mingling themselves with the blood, engender desires in souls; others, joining themselves to the heart of man, suggest contrary things with flatteries; and there are some which bind the bodies alone with infirmities, as also that daughter of Abraham whom the Lord healed.

Being ignorant of all these things, the pagans call it fate, though the causes are diverse and these things are procured by the enemies, so that a man, ensnared from whatever cause, is made subject to the passions. But they call it good fate when bodies are well-tempered, or are commended by affability, having a certain charm; and that it is bad fate, if one has more heat or moisture, or if he is not sprinkled with charm, but rather disposed toward harms — those whom they call unfortunate.

Grant that these things are true. For we see these things also in animals, that they have more heat; but animals are not rational as man is, so that they might be able, by applying care, to temper their own body. For it is for this reason that the soul is the commander of the body, that it may govern it with the reins of the divine law. For those things which are suggested either by the impulse of the flesh or by the enemy, the divine law shows to be not useful — which law God therefore gave as a help, so that, when the enemy dictates evils as though they were good, we, looking to the precepts of the law, may understand that he is a seducer, because he urges something other than what the law teaches.

Thus the body is bridled, so that, because it grows hot, less is given to it and it is held back from indulgences. For just as a feverish body, if it takes food or drink, its heat increases, so too bodies of this kind, which are burned by the fervor of the blood, will blaze up more violently unless they are governed. For if you give no food to a fire, it dies down.

It belongs to the soul to guide the body; but if it lets it go, so that it goes wherever it wishes, it hurls the soul headlong, like a bridle-champing horse its heedless rider. And because the body's fervor blazes up more when enemies are at work to deceive the soul — since it is not the body that desires, but by its heat generates the soul's desires — the protection of our God must be implored for defense, that he may restrain them — because our struggle is not against flesh and blood, but against princes and powers — and when these have been removed, it will be easy to check the motion of flesh and blood. For a fire is extinguished if the material by which it lives is lacking.

But to those people happen the things that are foretold by the astrologers, who believe that what they say will come to pass. For this is brought about by the subtlety of the demons, so that they may effect those things which their priests have said would come to pass, lest, being detected as false, they be exposed. But regarding those who do not lend faith to their responses, the effect of these things does not prevail, because neither are they themselves so intent as to satisfy those who do not believe them, nor does God permit them to do that through which those who trust in God and do not believe the devil might be seduced. For God has ordained all things in such a way that it may happen to each one according to his own faith, so that the free choice of the will may remain. And through this, whoever believes God, taking refuge in his aid, nothing will be able to prevail against him; for he is certain that the things which He made are in His power, and that they do not dare against Him whom they see to have the common God favorable to him.

Surely God made the world and created the sun and the moon and the stars, appointing for them their courses, by which the human race is governed. Does He not indeed have the power to command them, so that at some time they do something other than what they have as decreed? For He so established them that they are subject to His will and only then carry out their decreed courses, if nothing else has been commanded: so that, if He is asked by those to whom they minister, on the grounds that either the rains are unseasonable or that through excessive drought the crops appear barren, He may order the weather to be moderated; or if perchance, as is wont, God being angered, men live in such a way that, wandering astray through luxury and vanity, they do not do what pleases God, and the rain having been suspended for a very long time — as we read was done under Elijah and Elisha — He may stir up famine upon the earth, then, appeased by satisfaction, He may grant that showers water the dry ground.

For in the same manner is the ordering of the world, as is secular government. For just as certain men, oppressed by the governors of the provinces, run to the aid of the emperors that they may be raised up, so too men, if they see the times adverse to themselves, present themselves as suppliants to God, in whose power all things are.

But this does not seem so to the astrologers. For they say that nothing else can happen than that the stars fulfill the causes decreed for them; and they can neither be recalled nor driven to anything else, but keep the duties once established. This is the assertion of the astrologers, so that they say the fates cannot be changed nor anything obtained by prayers, because they say that God once established the plan of the whole world and handed it over to the stars as ministers, and these can be carried neither backward nor forward.

This is the error of the astrologers, by which they even assail God. For through this assertion they deny that anything can happen outside the order of the world, so that all the things which are read to have been done by God in the world, if they are at variance with the law of the world, they deny are to be believed, but they assert those things which are said to have been done in accordance with the established order of the world, so that neither is a virgin said to have given birth, nor Aaron's rod to have blossomed and borne fruit, nor Sarah as an old woman to have given birth. For the plan of the world does not admit these things, since they are read to have been done by God outside the order of the world. And what wonder is it, if the author of the world did what the world cannot do?

'But he seems to have transgressed', they say, 'the plan which he established, so that he himself is said to have acted otherwise than he decreed for the world'. Come now, he established a law of making or generating for the world: did he do so for himself? And it would be a lowliness in him, if he made things in the same way as the world was founded by him. And from what would it be understood to have been made by him, if he had made what he made by the law of the world? But in order that it might be understood to have been made by God, not by the world, it was made by another order.

Surely he decreed for the sun that it should unceasingly complete its appointed courses and not have leave to stand still: is it, because he established this law for it, that he too will not be permitted to establish anything for it?

Surely it is established for judges that they are not permitted to recall a sentence given against a defendant: will the emperor himself also be under this law? For to him alone is it permitted to recall a sentence and to pardon one condemned to death: how much more ought it to be permitted to God, which was not permitted to be granted to the world, since the world is the servant of its Creator! For the world, by the law handed over to it, is able to kill, but not able to give life. Through intemperance it is able to create a blind man, but not able to restore his eyes; it is able to make one weak and sick, but not able to cure him; for this God reserved to himself, whence it might appear that he is the Lord of all. For it pertains to the overthrowing of fate that the dead have risen again, that eyes have been restored to one born blind, that the sick and the paralyzed have been made strong, and the other such things which have been done, so that to those fleeing to God, if there can be anything of fate, it can bring no hindrance. For John the Apostle says: greater, he says, is he who is in you, than he who is in this world.

For when God is entreated, he even removes the things which he has decreed. For he has assuredly decreed that vengeance must be taken upon sinners, and yet he pardons those who entreat him, because to him alone is it permitted to bind and to loose. Hence, although standing still has not been granted to the sun, under Joshua son of Nun it was ordered to stand still and obeyed his command, contrary to that which had been prescribed to it in the order of the world. But under Hezekiah something greater is recorded to have been done, because, so that Hezekiah might not doubt that what had been promised to him was true, he received such a sign as was new and unheard-of to human ears. For the sun was not, as before, ordered to stand still, but to turn back backward, seeing which Hezekiah might be certain that he was able to add fifteen years to his life — he who had made the sixth hour out of the ninth. And because what was granted to Hezekiah is against the astrologers — for they deny that anything can be added to a life —, he again received in this matter a sign which was against the astrologers. For they assert that it is impossible for the stars to do anything other than what they have fixed.

Certainly they say that it belongs to fate to have a slave or not to have one. So be it. From the beginning of the world, where were these fates? For up to the flood, and for a long series of years afterward that passed, this condition of slavery did not exist. Or did these fates perhaps begin from the times of Ninus, who is said to have been the first to begin waging wars on his neighbors and to make the captives subject to slavery? You see, therefore, that this was devised afterward and does not belong to fate, but to the working of demons, because, if it had been of fate, it would have existed from the beginning of the world. For the stars themselves are the very ones that were made in the beginning, and their course remains the same in it.

And formerly the life of men was rustic, nor was wealth pursued: how is it that now the astrologers say through the stars that men become rich and poor? Have the stars been changed, or is it the cunning and trickery of Satan, that he should say those things come about through the stars which he works through his angels, his ministers? For as the time approaches at which his destruction draws near, he devises greater things by which sin may be committed.

Behold, we know that Italy and Africa, Sicily and Sardinia have suffered from famine: let the astrologers say whether all these had one fate, since among a hundred not even the fates of two are said to be able to agree with one another. Let them blush and be silent and stretch out suppliant hands to God, in whose power are all things. But perhaps in this case fate was outwitted: what shall we say about Pannonia, which was so wiped out that it cannot have a remedy?

0 the weakness of those who profess themselves Christians with a wavering faith! For while they doubt about the promises, they also give no thought to the things that have been done. For if they were to consider the things that were accomplished by the prophets and apostles, they would never admit the fables of the astrologers. For they would know that all things are in the power of God, and they would not be terrified when evils befall the good, because in the judgment to come they will be recompensed, mindful of the apostle who says: "For it is through tribulations," he says, "that we must enter into the kingdom of God." For he who is content and awaits the judgment of his God will, being recompensed, also be vindicated. But if he desires to be vindicated in the present, or with every greediness to enjoy present resources, he doubts about the promised life, and through this, being counted among the faithless in the time to come, he will be held deserving of punishment. For God made the world for the sake of training, and honored it with all resources, setting forth two conditions, the present and the future, the heavenly and the earthly, so that they might serve for the testing of men: that he who, having spurned the future life, chose the present, might be assigned to the flesh; but he who set the future before the present might be held worthy of things spiritual; and he who, unjustly oppressed by the power of the unjust, laid his complaints before God or reserved them for the judgment to come, might, relieved, be honored with spiritual glory. Hence no one of the saints appeared glorious in the world, nor loved present delights. Hence the apostle: "Having," he says, "food and clothing, let us be content with these." But the Christian complains and grieves if anyone seems fortunate in the world, whereas the dignity and felicity of Christians has been promised not on earth, but in heaven: how then does he call himself a Christian, who wishes to glory not there, where it has been promised, but here, where it has been forbidden? For if it were expedient to enjoy present things, heavenly things would surely not be promised to those who despise these.

With what hope, therefore, do Christians lend their ears to astrologers, when they hear John the apostle crying out and saying: "Do not love this world, nor the things that are in the world"? And so, if these are the precepts of our faith or law, that the world be not loved nor the things that are in the world, how is it that certain of us complain, saddened over the felicity of others which is in the world—which before God is perhaps unhappiness—when they ought rather to rejoice, because the world does not furnish them those things by which they might be held bound, subject to human affairs?

For this felicity, once the means of security has been received, lacks concern for divine things. For he must needs be negligent concerning the divine oracles who, stuffed with all necessities, has nothing to be anxious about. For men of this sort are wont to begin to labor under another anxiety, namely that they may be enlarged in the world, which cannot happen without sin. But if it be looked at more closely, the world will be seen to be rather the enemy of those whom it is said to make blessed, for scarcely any of them thinks of heavenly things.

Christians, therefore, ought to rejoice when, though living rightly, they are pressed down in the world. For here the one who has conquered the world is he who, content with however things have turned out for him in the world, gives thanks to his God, from whom, if any temporal goods seem to have been denied him here, he hopes that eternal ones will be repaid. For to what end did God appoint a future judgment, except that those who are unjustly cast down for a time might (be exalted) in eternal life, while those are put down who, through their power despising, or not believing in, the coming judgment of God, supposed that there was impunity for their crimes?

But the champions of the fates deny that there is a future judgment. For if some are born to be good and, on the contrary, some to be bad, why do you praise or accuse, seeing that neither did the good man strive when he engaged, so as to conquer, nor was the bad man negligent, so as to be lost? These tales, indeed, they peddle among private persons. For sinners brought before tribunals have never dared to excuse their crimes by the fates, but, confessing themselves guilty, they do not deny that they rightly receive the sentence. And so the authority of the law tramples down the reasoning of the fates in such a way that it cannot even be named once the law has set itself in motion.

But how do they believe that they will escape forever, who, having conducted themselves wickedly, by a certain effort escape the severity of the law for a time, not considering that the author of the law cannot on this account overlook it? For here is the image of the law, there is the truth; and he who mocks the image cannot mock the truth. Is there any place without God, or can anyone escape his hand? But they think, flattering themselves, that whoever escapes here is now secure. And how do they judge this to be just, that while others are condemned in such cases, these should escape?

Surely God established the world and showed by what law it should be governed. For, careful of his own work, he showed it the way by which one goes to life, having appointed what guides they should use, since there is also a way that leads to death. Ought he not, then, to inquire whether those whom he set as ministers of his kingdom have governed his people by that law which he established, or whether the people accepted his edict, so that both the ministers found faithful might be worthy of praise or, being unfaithful, subjected to punishments, and likewise the people, having kept the law, might be praised, or, having despised it, condemned? This is just; this the case demands.

How, then, does it displease certain people to believe in a future judgment? But this the madness of the astrologers has devised. For how could they accept a future judgment, who resist the present one, since they contend that judgment lies in one's nativity? And because the truth, when it is thought to be pressed down, rises again, they themselves, who assert that the good and the bad are not made but born by a certain judgment of nature, nevertheless requite those who sin and do not allow sins to go unpunished. For with rods, with the mill, with chains they lay hold within of those whom outwardly they assert to have been born incorrigible. Does any prudent man punish one whom he knows cannot be corrected? That indeed is the mark either of a fool or of an unjust man.

But lest they should say that this very thing happens by fate — because he who is under fate does not do what he wills — so that even they themselves have it as natural to take vengeance upon those whom they know to remain naturally unconvertible; and truly, since it must be put thus, so that whatever is said to belong to fate is reckoned to folly. For how foolish it is that,* when they call someone born evil, they do not prove him good whom they assert to have been born good! For how can he be judged good who is caught doing an unjust deed? For what is so unjust as that he should be beaten who is said to have been able to do nothing other than what he did?

And if all things happen by fate, how did the law creep in, which does not permit that to be done which belongs to fate? For if the law be taken away, unlawful things will everywhere be done openly, and the powerful cannot be endured, nor will there be liberty, because even he who now does not contemplate evil, once fear is removed, will begin to be evil. In short, we read that the law was given for the sake of the wicked.

But this very thing too, perhaps, belonged to fate, that the law should be given. And by what reasoning do the fates subsist, if they generate that by which they are destroyed, since the law declared that what belongs to fate ought to be done contrariwise? Therefore they lack reason, if they generate things contrary to themselves, and they are unjust, because they cause men to be born unto damnation; and if they are unjust, they ought not to have authority, because everything unjust is liable to punishment. ,

Or perhaps they say that the law is of no profit, because he who has been born in such a way that he is evil cannot be changed through the law? But if this is so, the law was given superfluously; and how did the fates cause a superfluous thing to be born, since 'nothing is without fate'? And so the fates act both for themselves and against themselves. But if this is so, that cannot stand which through inconstancy of reason does not hold firmness.

But perhaps for this reason they held the law to be of fate, that those whom the fates cause to be born evil should be condemned by the law, so that fate and the law might agree unto the punishment of these? What will be said to this, except that both the laws and the fates deserve to be condemned by their own judgment, if with equal injustice they contrive this, that those should be slain who did evils not of their own accord, but with the fates driving them? To these things the law speaks contrariwise, because the fates are to be condemned by their own profession. For they do not deny that those who are evil belong to fate in that they are evil. But the law is so far opposed to the fates that it denies that the evil belong to fate in that they are evil, but rather to their own will; wherefore it will duly establish them as guilty. Nothing therefore is common to the law and the fates, since that is denied by the law which is asserted by the fates.

But by what reasoning will they be said to have been born who prohibited the astrologers from the city of Rome — which right, as is not unknown, was observed, and they were certainly pagans? How do things happen by fate which are against fate? But if there is fate, it does not act against itself: these men do not have fate, who act against fate; and if these do not have fate, there is no fate.

(But,' they say, 'there is a fate assigned to each individual determining by what death he shall die.' But if this is so, the murderer is not to be accused. For he will defend himself on a twofold ground: both that he himself did this under the compulsion of the fates, and that the other was slain under the compulsion of the fates. Yet amid these things the law is struck at, because it punishes the murderer brought before it. In like manner too there is born one who is to be an adulterer, and she who is to be an adulteress, and the one against whom the adultery is committed. But if this too is so, then even now the law is impugned, which punishes adulterers; for if it were truly a matter of fate, then that man too ought to be punished who has such a fate that he suffers adultery. For he himself is found to be a participant, because his fate allowed the adultery to take place. It would therefore be just that they should either be condemned together or acquitted together; amid these things the law appears unjust.

And therefore, if the fates are from God, the law will not be God's, because to condemn what one has done befits not only not God, but not even a prudent man. But since no one ever dared to speak against the law or to deny that it is from God, it will not be possible to say that the fates are from God, because these things God punishes through the law. For when what is done under the impulse of the fates is condemned, without doubt the fates are condemned.

'But evil souls,' they say, 'are born for this purpose, that they may do evil.' So be it. But the innocent ought not to be born with such a fate that they can be killed by them. For if those souls were evil, they ought not to be born, but to be punished, lest they kill the innocent. But it is not the souls, which they imagine to be evil, that kill the innocent, but the fates, which cause men to be born in such a way that they are killed. And through this it is not the souls, but the fates that are unjust; and if they are unjust, they are not from God: therefore they are from the devil, and are to be condemned together with him.

And since they are said to decree for each individual in what manner they shall die — whether by the sword or by the noose or by a fall or by shipwreck or otherwise — they assign the same to many. For no one can alone diverge from the fates of all. But if anyone should be found to diverge, then it is not a matter of fate, but of chance; and if in the death of one it is chance, it will be so in the death of many also, and then it will not be a matter of fate. Anaxagoras, cast into a ball by the king's command, expired crushed, which befell no other man. And a woman fixed a stake into the temples of Sisera, king of the Philistines, and thus he gave up his spirit. In this manner no one has died. And formerly men were fixed to the cross, which afterwards remains prohibited by edict. If it was a matter of fate, how did it cease? For when what is a matter of fate is prohibited and does not happen, fate is overcome. Or if it was not a matter of fate, then men were not fixed to the cross by fate, but by the judgment of the law. And so it is that men are not condemned by fate, but convicted by their own evil deeds. :..

Among the Etruscans, it is said, there was born a certain man so beautiful of face as none ever was; and because he was a good man, lest he fall under anyone's suspicion — since he allured even the eyes of respectable women — he wounded his own face with seven blows, that he might condemn the beauty which is desired by all. Behold in what way, if there are fates, they were overcome! But perhaps it may be said: 'It belonged to nature that he was born beautiful, but to fate that his appearance was wounded.' Yet if this is so, then fate is no longer something born [with one], but an accident is the cause; and if it is a matter of accident, it will not belong to fate, but to deliberation, or at any rate to outcome, so far as concerns the remaining parts.

For what else is it to conquer fate, except to change nature? But if it were assuredly from God, it could neither be changed nor conquered. For it is read, as recorded in a certain legal document, that a woman once bore five children [at a birth]: how was it filched from the fates, that nature should decree for her alone this thing which did not belong to fate? Had it been of her fate, a good many women would have been burdened with this lot. And when a certain man was setting out for foreign parts, he entrusted his wife to a friend whom he knew to be faithful — not indeed a boy, but of mature age — who, that he might be a more careful guardian of her, cut himself [i.e. castrated himself], so that he might sleep beside her cautiously, without anyone's suspicion. What do we think: did this belong to fate or to deliberation? But how did that belong to fate which is contrary to it? For he who conquers what is inborn in him — this man empties fate of its force.

And in studies, too, the cause is certainly assigned to the fates, so that one may know something or else be unable to grasp it. Therefore all senators have one fate — for none of the senators is without letters — and the nations which are without letters have one fate, and all slaves are of one fate; for no one of the slaves is a senator.

But if you call fate that which is born [with a man], whatever is added on will be outside of fate. Or if what is added comes from fate, why does it come through labor? For then it would be called a matter of fate, if it befell a man against his will. For that which impends without forethought is said to belong to the fates; but that which is thought out and long pondered and acquired through the exercises that come between — why should it be said to belong to fate?

Some one man is born a eunuch, others are made eunuchs: where will fate be? In that one, I believe, who was born so; but as for those who are made so, what is to be said about them? For it is a different matter. Or if these have been made eunuchs by fate, then that other one was born a eunuch contrary to fate. And why is a eunuch so rarely born, so that it seems incredible? There was a certain woman in the city of Rome who is known to have had eleven husbands, and another man who had twelve wives: what are the fates which decreed this for these people? For in the time of the emperor Constantius it is well known that a girl in a part of Campania was changed into a male and brought to Rome: what fates brought this about? Others, I suppose, which you do not know.

O, if only you would keep silent about this name [of fate] and refer some things to outcome and to chance, you would grant certain things to providence! For so much can the virtue and diligence of minds accomplish, that it not only resists nature, but even amends itself or leaves itself unamended, because it has judgment over itself.

For it is related that the women of the Scythians, who were called Amazons, because—when their husbands, occupied in a distant war, were long absent—for the sake of offspring mingled with slaves and killed every male that was born, keeping the females, in such a way that none of them devoted her labor to woolworking, but from the earliest age they were trained in wrestling-ground and in arms. So great was their valor and their industry in their exploits, that reigning through a long series of years they subdued not a few nations and put all Asia under tribute. What could be said to these things? Were these events matters of fate—things that were carried on thus through so many years, so that they made void all that are thought to be of fate? First, that against nature women should reign, having spurned husbands; then, that they mingled with their own slaves; thirdly, that all the males born were killed, while the females were kept alive, with such prosperity attending them that a kingdom begun without husbands they extended with great forces and over long periods. Certainly this was never done since the foundation of the world, except in Scythia alone—lest perchance they should say that, when the world is renewed after one thousand four hundred and sixty years, such things come to pass, since indeed the world is now being carried on in its six-thousandth year.

And [it holds] so long as one does what he has imitated. For M. Crassus was called (Agelastus), because he is reported to have laughed once in his life; and C. Junius was surnamed (Brutus), because by a shrewd design he feigned himself a fool, lest he should be killed by the proud king on account of the greatness of his money; and in the times of Constantine one who was called Samsucius pretended to be a fool for thirty years, in order, as he himself afterward said, to divert the emperor from his weariness. Where is the principle of the fates, when each one does what he wishes? It is excluded.

But of what sort is this, that the assertors of the fates are given over to supplications, though these are contrary to them? For they both commend their years and inquire about marriages and about a journey and about purchases and about honors. But if it is a matter of fate, why do you pray, why do you supplicate for that which is going to happen even against your will? "But I ask," he says, "whether I ought to buy." Therefore that which you say you ought then to do, if you have learned that it is advantageous, does not rest in fate. For if it is a matter of fate, you ask in vain, since—whether you will or will not, whether you ask or do not ask—you are going to do it.

But because this asseveration is not fixed even among those men themselves, therefore, being uncertain, they make trial, lest they should find something else to be true than what they have handled. But the more prudent among them, who seem [wiser], say: "It is not on account of the fates that we supplicate, since we know these can by no means be changed, but for the sake of those things which are alongside the fates, lest they do us anything adverse." Come now, if some person or other has a good fate and alongside it there is something that seems contrary, what will it be able to be, when a good fate is denied to be changeable, just as an evil one also is? Surely supplication is made about this thing which is alongside the fate, that it may not do harm—for it will do harm unless supplication has been made—: how then is that fate said to be unchangeable, when this thing, which is neighboring [to it], is feared? For this would not be feared, unless that [fate] were believed able to be changed. But because the cause consists rather in this—that you may not say fate is changed—without doubt you supplicate without cause.

But see whether this too—your supplicating—may be a matter of fate, for whatever you assign to fate is wont to be without salt. Surely supplication is not a bad thing: why then should it not be said to belong to fate? If, then, that which one supplicates for shall be of fate, whose part will it be to supplicate and not obtain? For if it was of fate, he ought to obtain what he supplicates for. But if it is of fate to supplicate and not to obtain, fate is foolish, because it makes a man supplicate whom it knows will not obtain. But if that which makes him supplicate does not know whether he can obtain, fate will be improvident and devoid of reason, because all ignorance is want of understanding, and want of understanding is indeed folly. Into this quarter falls the whole discourse of the pagans. Let the pagans see to it, who, wrapped about with a fog, do not see the light. For, ensnared by an ancient error, they refuse to lend their ears to the truth, which formerly lay hidden.

What shall we say of certain Christians, who, changed in name alone, lay claim to the vanity of their former error, so dulled that they contend that the Lord himself acted under fate, saying: "he himself said: my hour has not yet come"—as though this hour were not a matter of will, but the necessity of a fated condition, whereas he was signifying the hour of his own will, in which he permitted himself to suffer; for at the last he says: I have power to lay down my soul, and I have power to take it up again. Fatal necessity therefore ceases where there is the power of the will.

And in what way is he called Savior by us, if the things we do or that befall us are of fate? For he is called Savior for this reason, because he showed us the way of salvation. And so, if it was shown by him, it was not of fate, because it both formerly lay hidden and—what is said to be of fate—they contend comes about unforeseen. For here lies the merit of faith, which cannot be said to be from fate, because what is believed was not heard in other ages; for they say that the fates furnish this, which is bound up in the daily converse of men.

Now by what reasoning are they said to be able to grant this, which they are caught not knowing? This is why the apostle says: whom none of the princes of this world knew. These are the principalities and powers having under them the evil angels, who under the name of the fates carry out their own pleasures, according as it has been agreeable to them, just as the workers of evil are wont to confess. For there are some whom they love, others they do not love, certain ones they hate. And each one suggests to each person that which he loves, because they too have diverse pleasures: to some the lust of one kind of debauchery, to others of another, to some wantonness of pleasures, to some wrath, to certain ones avarice; for some they procure gains, for others losses, for some a charism, for others hatreds and also hindrances. And sometimes at fixed seasons, being sated, they rest in idleness; but sometimes indeed they do not thrust themselves upon a mind occupied with some reasoning, because they do not rule, but they creep in seeking an opportunity by which to catch. Therefore the judgment of the will is free, but while through a certain sloth we are made negligent concerning our own selves, they, soliciting us, persuade us to adverse things; especially if they find us thinking something that is not of the law, they will come, in that very thing helping, that we may fulfill what the law forbids. For if the fear of God be in the soul, neither flesh nor blood nor principalities and powers prevail, because they are checked by the supports of God, so that they are overcome without toil.

But if we were not of our own power, how would the Lord say: ask and it shall be given you; for everyone who asks shall receive? If therefore he who wishes to ask shall receive, then it is no longer that which is wont to be said: 'not what we will, but what has been given,' because the will is incited by the Savior to ask and to receive what it has asked. But they say that this belongs to fate, that whether one asks or does not ask, one nevertheless obtains what fate holds, whether it be evil or good. But the Lord, excluding this, says: daughter, thy faith hath made thee whole, so that it is no longer of fate, when faith receives the benefit. And again: if thou believest, he says, all things are possible to him that believeth: assuredly he placed the merit in the will, because it is the good of faith, not of nature. But faith is a thing that is added, which rouses the will to exercise, so that, since it is not in nature, the will, which is in nature, having received faith may establish merit for it before God. For the nature of men is like a stone, which, although it does not have fire in its substance, nevertheless has it in potency, so that by work it may create what it does not seem to have, because it does not exist, but as it were in seed. So it is in potency, so that through exercise it may be generated, and, if the exercise be lacking, it may not exist, because the seed too, unless it be worked, does not generate, but remains itself alone. The accession therefore causes it to generate. So too man does not have faith in nature, but the seed of faith, which, unless it be provoked and exercised, will not give fruit, that is, will not have faith. For those believing in the fables of the astrologers exterminate the seed of faith, which bears witness both to the Creator and to his justice. For the work recognizes its maker and testifies that it loves with equal right the things it has made.

Therefore we advise fleeing from this art by every means. For those curious about it are also enemies of God and are never without anxiety; for they are always in suspense, awaiting what they know to be uncertain. But we, who believe that all prosperous things come from God and that, if any adversities arise, they are restrained by his command, let us live in simplicity, secure in his protection, yet not unaware that, even if something adverse should befall us who act well, once endured it will profit toward the crown.

CXVI. THE ACCOUNT OF THE PASSOVER.

The Passover, most beloved brothers, is named from the passion, as the prefiguration of this tradition teaches us, which was accomplished in Egypt through God's servant Moses, Scripture saying: this is the immolation of the Passover of the Lord. What then is the reason that the sacred mystery was celebrated through blood, and the restoration of life through death, so that, when death thought it was making increase, having received the blood of the Savior it should flow away diminished, and, when it reckoned it had accomplished something by its power, weakened it should perish? For death was found for death through its own work, by divine provision, so that, because it always wills evil, it might be yielded to for a time, in order that, destroyed in justice by its own work, it could not complain that a kingdom had been taken from it. For although God can do all things, yet he does nothing that would be absurd to reason. For the justice which he demands, this he also practices, not presuming upon his power.

Great therefore is the providence toward the human race, that, keeping justice, he thus loosed those things which had been justly decreed and rescued man from death, although he was held not undeservedly. But because he had been deceived by the envy of the devil, it was just that this one be helped. For he was held by the sentence of God, but Satan had wrought this. Whence, just as it seemed just to rescue this one, so it was unjust to do this through power with justice neglected. For man, conquered by the persuasion of the devil, so that he might receive mercy, the devil as it were as accuser spoke against it. Whence this was done, that this one, who gloried in the sin of man, should be caught sinning, so that then at last, having been made guilty, he would not dare to speak against it. Thus it came about that the Son of God was born a man and, preaching justice, provoked the devil, because he forbade men from his vices, so that he even killed him who knew no sin.

Then he was found to have sinned, and indeed more grievously than the man whom he was accusing. For when, by God's decree on account of sin, he claimed the man for himself, since whoever sins belongs to the party of the devil, he was found to have sinned the more, when he slew the one who had not sinned, so as to usurp him for himself. Therefore through death destruction crept in upon death, and, so that blood might be recovered, blood was poured out; because, as I have said, death, which is the devil, since he was found to have sinned unto the death of the Savior, lost the Savior's blood together with that of us all — because, just as through the one Adam who sinned he held all in death, so through the one who did not sin he lost all. Therefore the blood of the Savior, poured out unjustly, obtained this by way of reward: that, recovering with triumph the blood of its own origin, it might recall it to its former state with its substance made better. For death, once conquered, could not gainsay the one who had conquered it. Blessed therefore is the mystery of the Passover, which redeemed by our blood, death being vanquished through death, just as poison is wont to be overcome by poison.

CXVII. ON ABRAHAM.

This patriarch's faith was so preeminent and admirable that all the other righteous ones acknowledge him, by God's own judgment, as the father of belief, and that no one is worthy of God and dear to Him unless he has been called Abraham's son. For the prerogative of his honor and merit is his faith, which, since he does not hesitate to give it even in incredible matters, among other things he became worthy even to see the Savior in the likeness of the incarnation then to come, as the Lord says and proves to the Jews: Abraham your father desired to see my day, and he saw it and rejoiced. For he who deservedly became the father of faith, it was fitting that he should foresee the hope of his future sons, which, with God propitious and provident, by the grace of inheritance should overflow from the human and dutiful father into his obedient sons.

Let us now see what this faith believed, so that it attained to such great honor and glory by divine judgment. For we have praised the faith, but what it believed we have not yet said. For the Lord, leading him out, showed him the stars of heaven and said: Canst thou number them? So shall thy seed be. And Abraham believed God, and it was reckoned unto him for righteousness. It would not be a great thing to have believed God, were it not that what he believed the world judges not to be believed and foolish, because that lies not within the hope of the things of nature. Whence Abraham, one man out of the world, because he believed this, being set apart from the worldly, was justified.

That he believed this, therefore, is made a great thing by the faithlessness of worldly wisdom, against whose hope to have believed this is of the greatest merit. For the despair of the worldly is the merit and hope of Christians; for the cause of the wicked produces the reward of the good, since faith is stronger and fuller when it commits itself the more to things incredible. For a man already withered believed that from an aged wife he would have seed, which on account of its abundance could not be numbered, not so much regarding this which was shown as reckoning that he who showed it could not lie. And therefore it was reckoned to him unto righteousness. For to believe a thing that is absurd and known to be impossible is the mark of a dullard, unless there be a person who counsels that this be believed. Hence Abraham is both of admirable faith and of a skilled heart, since he both believes that which is incredible and commits himself to him whom not to believe is both foolish and dangerous.
