For this proves our faith to be reasonable, because it believes that none other than God is able to do what he promises. This, then, is the bulwark of faith, this its triumph. Which indeed the wise men of the world, perceiving the nature of things, because this cannot be, say it is foolish to believe, not knowing that, according to the apostle, that which is foolish of God is wiser than men. For they would justly judge us fools, if we believed this of the nature of things, which is proven unable to be. But further, those things which the nature of things cannot do, we believe that God is able to do, if he has promised. What foolishness can be detected in this matter? But if the work which is believed possible is unworthy of God, then faith is rightly compared to dullness; but if it is worthy, through that which is impossible for a creature, why is faith not to be praised, which gives so much to the Creator as the creature is denied the power to do?

Great, therefore, and admirable is Abraham, who against the world's judgment did not hesitate to believe God, because he is able to do what he promises. By birth a Chaldean, he appeared a master of belief: although skilled in astrology, he nevertheless set God before human counsel, esteeming it worthy that this be believed of God, namely that whose manner of coming to be cannot be investigated. For the power of God confirmed his weakness, because, in order to attest the singular greatness of his majesty, he would arrange to do things which are impossible and unheard-of to the world, and by that deed would show himself Lord of creation, and the creature would subject itself to him whose work would seem impossible to all others—by whose unbelief the most faithful Abraham is the more exalted. For his faith is the punishment of the unbelieving, and the faithlessness of the unbelieving is his glory; for he is the father of the faithful and the judge of the unfaithful. For by his example both the good are granted eternal life and the evil will pay eternal punishments. For at that time, when the world was still a little child in knowledge and not yet illumined by signs and wonders which might draw it to faith, he did not deny faith to God against his own knowledge.

Therefore holy Abraham, most faithful, was given as an example to the human race. For in him all the nations were prefigured as coming to salvation through faith; and so that the superabundant increase of his faith might be known, he is tested and commanded to sacrifice his son to God — a thing which had never been done — and he is neither disturbed by the novelty of the matter nor does he dispute whether, at God's command, a parricide ought to be committed by him who had threatened that no homicide should occur. Thus, fearless and untroubled concerning the will of God, he does not hesitate to be provident in what God commands, and this son, whom two old people had received from a promise — the reward of faith, the token of merits, and in whom all hope from the promise of the future seed was held. And that he might fulfill it with all devotion, he did not disclose it to the boy's mother, lest he bring some impediment to his dedication, knowing that mothers are more inclined to love in their affection toward their sons; and therefore he concealed it from her, not doubting that what God commands ought to be fulfilled, so that by this example we might be taught to carry out God's commandments with all care.

For if the most faithful Abraham is found obedient in a matter so grave and harsh, how much more we, to whom those things are enjoined which can be borne! O faith dedicated to God and hope firm in the Lord, which is so dear and sweet that it is set before the affections of parents and children, as Scripture says: Taste and see how sweet the Lord is!

Now our patriarch, the most faithful Abraham, because he was a prophet, knew what he ought to follow. Therefore, according to what the Lord has now said, he then did, so that he set God in love before a son, however dearest, as the Lord says: If anyone, because he loves father or mother or children above me, is not worthy of me. Whence the patriarch, that he might prove himself worthy before the Lord above all others, did not even refuse to kill his son.

CXVIII. ON JOB.

Great is the love, most beloved brethren, of almighty God toward the human race, which, when understood, indeed leads to the heavenly kingdoms, but, when not understood, leads down to hell. For God wills His benefit to be pleasing and fruitful, that it may both profit us and delight Him to show mercy.

Therefore, since He is good and wills all men to be saved, He has shown us an example of righteousness in Job His servant, as the present reading testifies, so that by following it we may be able both to be free from evil and to come to good, being not only free from punishment but also rewarded.

How admirable, then, is most holy Job, who before the law was set down in writing displayed by his works the exemplar of the law, learning in this matter no teachings from anyone, but with nature herself as guide — nature in which God decreed that the seeds of righteousness should naturally reside — preserved reverence for the Creator with pious devotion!

How great a praise, then, is he worthy of, and with what words ought his deeds to be extolled—he for whom we can find no equal either before the law or after the law? And yet, if anyone could marvel at his deeds, he would no longer be one to be compared with him, nor would he rightly be called similar to him. For it would not have been his own that he had proved himself such, because he would have followed another's example. But holy Job, since he had seen nothing of the kind nor read of it repeatedly in books, showed himself such that he had nothing bestowed upon him from anyone, but he himself bestowed upon the rest, offering a pattern of wondrous deeds for earning favor with the Lord. Wherefore he is commended also by the testimony of the Lord, who says: Have you considered my servant Job? For there is no one like him on earth, a true worshipper of God.

Who could merit so much, one to whom the Lord bears such testimony, except this man, who is found to be not an imitator, but the author of the things he did? Whence also he is declared a true worshipper of God. For no pretense of fabricated truth can be seen in him who has no one such before him. For everyone who feigns feigns that which he is seen to imitate.

And yet greater still than all these are the things that may be said. For among these praises there were no proofs from tribulations, but rather a purpose dedicated to God, established in practice and in observances; and because to the tempter this did not seem full for a crown of merits, he asks that it be permitted him to test his righteousness by various temptations, so that, being constrained in many things, he might somewhere become stuck and be able to exceed the bounds—because for this reason various punishments are wont to be inflicted upon sinners, that, since no one can bear all torments, some one of them may elicit from him a confession of the truth.

And so it was permitted to the tempter to exterminate and destroy all that was his, even to the death of his children, so that, if he should bear the loss of the oxen, he might not bear that of the sheep; or if he should bear that of the sheep as well, he might not bear the loss of the camels, nor of the servants, nor of his whole substance; or if, because he was of great spirit and of sufficiently pure devotion, he should not be overcome by all these, then at least, with the affection of love compelling him, he might be broken by the death of all his children at once. But because, before the law was set forth, he had the law written in his heart, no losses nor bereavements made him less in his devotion to God, so that he might set a pattern that God is to be loved with the whole heart above all things. How great a glory, then, is he worthy of, who kept the law before the law, so that he might demonstrate the law which was to come—in what manner it was to be kept—not in words, but as a doer in deeds!-

And because the tempter is irreverent, and this glory did not seem to him sufficient for the testing of a just man, unless he should contrive something else against him which he knew could not be borne by a man, he asks again that he might strike that very man with a savage wound from head to toenails. And when this had been permitted — because no trust ought to be placed in him — it was commanded to him that he should preserve his life, on account of free will, lest he should venture anything violent against the one whom he could not overcome by the right of reason. Having therefore received power, with the ferocity of his cruelty breaking out afresh within him, he struck the just man with an exceedingly great plague, so that there was nothing in his body that was free from a wound — which no man could endure, save Job alone, who was able to conquer Satan. And when not even thus did the wickedness of the devil draw forth from the just man anything against God, he was mindful of his former craftiness, by which he deceived Adam, to see whether he could deceive this man too through a woman, since one is easily deceived through a member of his own household.

And in all these things gaining nothing, nay rather suffering loss, the most shameless Satan found the servant of God not only immovable but even a master. For, set in that very necessity, he not only endured in the fear of God, but even, rebuking his wife who was suggesting the contrary, taught that all things which happen by God's permission must be bravely endured. This pertains to the twofold punishment of the improvident Satan, because he neither persuaded this man of what he wished and, while he envies this man, he made others learn what he did not wish even this man to know.

How much, therefore, temptation profits the servants of God and harms the devil, we are more fully taught by this reading. For when he thinks he is harming them, he advances them, and when he persecutes one, he provokes many. For many, seeing this man protected by the helps of God, become his imitators. Wishing therefore to make gain, he lost; for his fury is always harmful to himself. For while he does not allow the just to be just lest they be rewarded, he makes them the more worthy, and when he is jealous of holy Job, he doubled his merit in heaven and on earth, because both here he was increased and into heaven he was received with the Savior, so that all these things profit the glory of the saints, but the punishment of the devil.

CXVIIII. ON TOBIT.

So great is the providence of the Lord God concerning us that, not wishing us to err, he gave both a law and examples of good works, by which a modest and tranquil life may be led with the fear of God. For he who is the author of life does not at all wish his work to be subject to death. Although nature itself is not without a law, yet because the Lord is good and merciful, in order that there might be a greater knowledge of those things which are to be followed and of those which are to be avoided, he showed it by writings and by examples, as the present reading testifies.

The servant of God, therefore, holy Tobit, was given to us as an example after the law, so that we might know in what manner the things we read are done and, if temptations should come, that we might not withdraw from the fear of God nor hope for help from any other source than from him, mindful that it is written: I have been young and have grown old, and I have never seen the just man forsaken, nor his seed seeking bread.

Therefore he who hopes in God with his whole mind can never be deceived. Although for a time tribulations may arise through the snares of Satan, the examples of holy men teach us that these are not permitted by God except for the increase of our merits, because, if they are borne with an even mind, consolation will be given us here with real effect, and in the future eternal life with glory. For our God, since he is just, and there is no respect of persons with him, permits us to be tempted because he loves us, so that after our labor he may be able to give us the most abundant rewards. Finally, to the apostle, who was praying that his temptations might cease, the Lord says: My grace is sufficient for thee; for strength is made perfect in weakness. Hence he goes on to say: When I am weak, then am I stronger, and: I rejoice, he says, in my tribulations, that the strength of Christ may dwell in me.

Scripture therefore teaches us how praiseworthy holy Tobias was, whose devotion neither captivity diminished, nor did the loss of his eyes persuade him to bless God any the less, nor did his exhausted substance turn him away from the way of justice and truth. For necessity proves the just man, and to preserve equity in want is true and perfect justice. For from that very thing whereby the devotion of some is diminished, from that Tobias, worthy of praise, made increase. For want, it says, humbles a man; and he who is humbled cannot preserve justice. But the mind of holy Tobias, lifted up to God, was neither broken by captivity nor humbled by want, because even against the prohibition he committed the bodies of the slain to burial, and, secure concerning God's bounty, he was merciful out of his very own scanty means, knowing that this mercy which is done out of little pleases God more, just as that widow also did, whom the Lord commended in the Gospel. For this man is truly faithful, this man does not doubt concerning the promises of God, who bestows out of little.

Therefore, consoling his mind with the hope of things to come, Tobias, dedicated to God, was found robust and strong in temptation, showing that in necessity one must be all the more vigilant in the fear of God, because if necessity does not drive one to God's help, how much more does security! Certain therefore that God had reserved to himself the judgment of all those things which are done in this world, the holy men did not bear grievously their tribulations and losses and the rest of their ruinous afflictions or insults, but even received them gladly, just as our apostles also did, who, when they were beaten, rejoiced because they were counted worthy to suffer insult for the name of God. For the ruinous afflictions which are unjustly inflicted upon the faithful, whether in tribulations or in insults, are the signs of future merits.

And so the just Tobias pleased God to such a degree that he obtained the reward of his merits in a twofold kind. For both in the present he recovered, through the ministering angel, the sight which he had lost, being enriched also with abundance of the things that pertain to the present life, and in the future he was made an heir of the kingdom of heaven, so that in this we might be taught that he who obeys the law of God with his whole heart and does not doubt concerning the promises—God both takes care of him in this world and in the future grants him eternal life.

There is also another thing that invites us to divine service. For holy Tobias not only obtained the merits of his own righteousness, but there also accrues to the sum of his glory even from the good deeds of those who become his imitators. For in imitators examples are praised, which will be able to accrue to us as well, if we live in such a way that it is fitting that we too have imitators.

CXX. ON FASTING.

It is fitting, dearest brothers, that the most devoted priest of God and overseer of the people of Christ exhort the people placed under his care in sound doctrine, as the apostle commands, so that we may perform the work of faith, in accordance with the observance of the season, with all care and diligence and with an eager and devoted spirit. For although it is not hidden what the fasts profit—those which are now to be celebrated as the feast day of Easter draws near—yet it ought not to be passed over in silence. For devotion is stirred up when those things which, though they are not unknown, are nevertheless reviewed again. For such is the character of our nature that it grows sluggish if the practice of reading ceases, because just as iron, unless it is put to use, breeds rust, so also the soul, unless it more frequently exercises itself in the divine readings, sins will be born in it. Hence it is that in the psalm he calls that man blessed who day and night meditates on the law of the Lord.

Finally, although the divine sayings are known, yet when they have been recalled from the sacred volumes, the devout heart receives them as if they were new. For the desire of the soul toward the works of salvation is aroused. Whence the apostle to Timothy, among other things, says: that you may revive the grace of God which is in you. Therefore fasts are necessary for us, just as medicine for wounds. For they confer a remedy of eternal life, yet in such a way that they are commended by the testimony of two things, that is, by prayer and by mercy.

Fasts therefore mitigate the intemperance of the body, restrain adverse impulses, take away the pressure upon the soul, as the Lord says: do not be given over to food and to drunkenness, lest your hearts be weighed down. For when the soul has been freed from food and drink, then it recognizes itself better. For just as in a dirty mirror a man does not see himself as he is, so also, if he has been weighed down with food and drunkenness, he perceives himself to be other than he is. Then lust is stirred up, anger is kindled, pride is inflamed, dissoluteness is engendered. Whence the apostle says: do not be drunk with wine, in which is dissoluteness. But if the body has been made temperate by the interposition of fasting, the soul, having recovered knowledge of itself, understands with what devotion it ought to serve its Redeemer.

For the most part, therefore, fasts are necessary. For under Queen Esther, when the Jewish people had been placed in peril, they were delivered by the support of fasts. And the Ninevites, when they had heard from the prophet the appointed day of their destruction, by a fast being proclaimed deserved to escape. And our righteous men and prophets, when they wished to obtain something from God, humbled themselves by fasting, as David says: in fasting I humbled my soul. For the Savior also, who had no need of it, fasted in order to give us an example.

And so there is no doubt that fasting is beneficial. For a person shows in this way that he desires to obtain what he asks, when he afflicts himself with fasting. Whence it is said: prayer is good with fasting. Therefore, in order that prayer may be acceptable, it wants to have the solace of fasting; and because none of these things is firm without piety, he added: and almsgiving, he says, with righteousness, so that almsgiving, with righteousness preserved, may commend prayer with fasting. Finally, Cornelius' mercy commended his prayer and fasting. For he himself, though abounding in resources, fasted, yet he also fed those who had nothing, so that the fullness of the latter made his fasting acceptable. Therefore fasting has need of certain things and ought to lack certain things, so that it may be able to merit what it asks

CXXI. THE PRAISE AND GLORY OF EASTER.

O holy and salutary day of Easter, to be proclaimed with all praise, on which death was vanquished, the kingdom taken away from the devil; the sacrament of God made manifest, the decree that was against us made void; the gates of hell shattered, the bound loosed, the imprisoned released, the blind enlightened, the ignorant endowed with knowledge, the impious made merciful, the unjust made just; forgiveness of sins granted, reconciliation of enemies, correction of error, declaration of truth; sons acquired for God out of the lost; pride cast down, humility exalted; the poor enriched, the rich emptied; the mountains levelled, the valleys filled, the hills laid low; shamelessness trampled, modesty cherished; to souls the means to reach heaven granted, liberty restored, captivity broken asunder and dissolved; the darkness grew numb, malice was confounded, filth was purified; Satan cast down, hell emptied, our Lord Christ Jesus proven to be the true Son of God, the flesh, to the confounding of the prudent of this world, raised into the heavens, and the things heavenly, earthly, and infernal shown to be of the one God and Lord!.

And so, most beloved brethren, we ought to keep and venerate this feast day, devoted to God, with moderation of life and gladness of soul, avoiding what is base and dishonorable, that we may be worthy to come to the fruit of Easter through Christ our Lord, to whom is honor and glory unto the ages of ages. Amen.

CXXII. ON THE BEGINNING.

In the beginning was the Word. What is 'in the beginning'? For we read also in the old books: In the beginning God made heaven and earth, and in the epistle of him whose gospel it is, about whose beginning we are attempting to declare something, it is signified in the same sense; for he says: That which was from the beginning. Therefore the epistle and the gospel have one sense. For it is one thing that he says in the old (scripture): In the beginning God made heaven and earth, and another when he says: In the beginning was the Word, and: That which was from the beginning. For to be 'in the beginning' and to be 'from the beginning' signifies one thing, because that which was from the beginning did not begin to be. For that which begins to be was not from the beginning, and therefore it is made subject to a 'beginning', so that it is said: it began to be in the beginning, that is, so that among the rest it may be first in order, because it, being itself made in the beginning, began to have others after itself, who were made after it, as we read, that in the beginning God made heaven and earth. He did not say: 'in the beginning were heaven and earth', because, since they did not exist and God was disposing to make the world, 'in the beginning', that is, among the rest of the things that served for the fabric of the world, he first made heaven and earth, because a beginning is the starting-point of something commencing, which is first in order.

But that which is said, that in the beginning it was, that is the Word, is signified to have existed before the commencement of the creation above and below, so that it should by no means be understood to have been made first among these things which were created, because it was in the beginning, when God was disposing to make the creation. Therefore if it was in the beginning, that is, it was before all things, it always was, and therefore the Word also was.

It was with God, he says, so that what always was with God might by no means be undeservedly reckoned to be made subject to a beginning. For it is fitting that what was with God before all things should be subject to no beginning.

Whence he also added: And the Word was God. Now he shows openly that the things said above are congruent with the Word, because the Word, he says, is God, and concerning God one ought not to think otherwise than that he is said always to have existed. For if he began, he is a creature; if he is a creature, he is not God; for whatever exists is either God or a creature, and through this the name of God does not belong to a creature. But because he did not begin to be — for he was —, he is worthily called God.

But he is said always to have been with God because he is God not from himself, but from God. For which reason his name also is called 'the Word of God', as the faith of the same evangelist demonstrates in his Revelation; for he says among other things: And his name is the Word of God. For so that not he from whom are all things should be signified, but he through whom are all things, that is, not the Father but the Son, he was called 'the Word of God', so that, because God cannot be without a word, this one, who is called 'the Word of God', may be believed always to have been with God, and, because a word is not outside him whose word it is, this Word, which was with God, may be understood to be signified as being not from elsewhere, but from God, and, because it is from God, it is not absurdly called God.

And so the fact that God was with God does not make two gods. For if there were two, they would be diverse in nature and not of one will. For if we humans, though we are of one nature, are nevertheless of diverse will, how much more where the nature too is diverse! And He would not even be said to be God with God. For God, who was and is with God, does not have from Himself that which He is as God; otherwise, He would neither be said to be God with God, nor would the Word of God' be called God the Word. But because that which is of God signifies its being to be nothing other than what God is, the Word of God* has been named God—so that, because He is called 'the Word of God,' it might not be referred to another God, while that He is called God serves lest it redound to the injury of God, if that which was of God were by no means called God. Therefore both the unity of God has been preserved and the due honor rendered, since this One, who is God of God, has been received not into His own glory, but into the glory of Him of whom He is God.

For this reason the Gospel begins to speak of the Word before it speaks of God the Father, because the questions are raised not about God, but about the Word of God. For no one doubts concerning God, but concerning Him who is God of God. For every tongue of things heavenly and earthly confesses one God, but is troubled in the mystery of the one God. For it trembles on hearing that the Word of God is called God'); for it is battered by bodily notions, because the word of a man cannot be called a man—whereas God is assuredly simple in nature, not composed of members, nor one who has an inside and an outside, nor before and after, nor top and bottom, but, if it may be said, wholly the same throughout all things, never varying or unlike—and His one brightness is immense; for if fire has no front and back, or inside and outside, how much more its Creator!—; therefore, because all that God is is wholly one, there is no discrepancy when that which is of God is called God.

Therefore in part by a human example, and in part by one not human, the Son of God, who is the Word of God, is God. For by that same principle by which the sons of men are men, the Son of God is God. But because the sons of men are men from intermingling, it is not by this example that the Son of God is God, because He was born simply from the simple God. And just as our word is from us, by this example it is understood that the Word of God is of God; but, because our word is not this which we ourselves are, it is not by this principle that the Word of God is of God, because the Word of God is a substance, not a sound that perishes.

For not because we and God have terms in common will there be one and the same manner for us and for God in the effecting of things. For we read: In the beginning God made heaven and earth. In what way did He make them? Was it perhaps, as we make things, by the labor of the hand? And in what follows: And God said, it says, Let there be light; and light was made. In what way do we suppose God spoke, since assuredly He did not speak to a man, but to Him who could make—who was also going to make man, as indeed He did? Was it as we speak, with a bodily organ? Far be it. You see, therefore, that terms are indeed common to us and to God, but they differ in effect; for God makes otherwise than we make, and He is said to speak, but not after our manner.

So too the Word of God is not a word such as ours is, which, after it comes to be, is not; but that Word abides, because it is such a word as both hears and speaks and works. And it is not only the "Word of God," but also the "power and wisdom of God"; this is the Son of God, who, as regards effect, is called the Son of God; but as regards the address by which God addresses us through him, he is called the Word of God; as regards wisdom, moreover, by which God teaches us his mystery through him, he is called the wisdom of God; but as regards operation, because through him God made and makes all things, he is called the power of God.

These things could belong to no other than the Son of God. For because he is God from God, he is called all the things of God. For by being born he obtained all the things of God. For it would not have been fitting that the Word of God and the power and wisdom of God should be called degenerate; for this is what God is: Word, power, wisdom. But Christ, because he is wholly God from wholly God, is called both the wisdom of God and the power and the Word of God — this is to be God from God and with God.

For this reason he says: this was in the beginning with God; for in order to overcome the minds of the unbelieving, Scripture, being constrained, burst forth, so as to show that Christ is God; whereas in every place, on account of the unity of God, it wishes Christ to be understood to be God, but now with open voice it has said that he is God and has always been with God. Disclosing the mystery of God, lest he be thought altogether solitary, it declared that God is not called one in such a way that he should be reckoned solitary, but that, although there be two or three, yet, being from one, they do not prejudice the unity, because that which is from one is referred to that very one, since he is one.

Although all things are said to be from God, yet it is one thing that properly came to exist from God and another that came to be by a nod: from without is that which is created by God; that is, it is one thing that proceeded from his substance and another that, whereas it in no way existed, was created by his will, because that which proceeded from the substance of God never came to be through substance, but that which was created then began into substance when it was created; and through this the Trinity alone has no beginning.

The disclosure, therefore, of the mystery diminished the merits of faith, because, the more hidden is that which is believed, the more abundant is the reward of the believer and the less the penalty of the doubter. But the disclosure of the mystery, just as it made believers merit less, so it has aroused greater penalties for the doubters. For a more manifest law makes one the more guilty.

For the testimony of the Savior would have sufficed, by which he had called God his own Father. For what believer would doubt that the Son differs in nothing from the Father in substance? But because the perversity of the heretics had begun, by the fabrications of infidelity, to assail the laws of faith by taking the Son of God otherwise than he is proclaimed, it was added, to make manifest, that he was before all things with God and is God, so as to declare what the true meaning of "Son" mystically contained; whereby the divine clemency is seen to have provided for human weakness, so as to open what, being closed, it had reserved for the merits of a stronger faith.

Hence he added, saying: all things were made through him. So that if anyone, from the foregoing, were to be in doubt about anything — since the understanding of men is too narrow to grasp divine matters — he might from these words become capable, when he hears: all things were made through him, so that he would by no means suppose him to be a created thing, because he hears that all things were made by God through him. But if he too were a created thing, he would not have said that all things were made by God through him; for neither did he himself come to be through himself.

And in order to convey this still more absolutely, he added: and without him, he says, was nothing made. In saying this he shut out all controversy and earthly arguments. Although there are some who lack faith, nevertheless, when he shows that nothing was made without him, he taught that one ought in no way to suspect him to be a created thing. For how can he be said to be a created thing, when it is said that God made nothing without him? For if he did make it, Scripture lies. But far be it, for Scripture is faithful, which, in order to cut away error, employs as much manifestation as it can for redeeming the salvation of men.

Finally he added: what was made, he says, in him is life. "In him," that is, in the Word, he signifies that what was made is life. Just as the Lord himself also says: as the Father has life in himself, so has he given also to the Son to have life in himself, not because the Word was without life and afterward life was either given or made in him, but he wishes the Word itself to be understood to be life. If of God the Father it can be said that he himself is one thing and what he has in himself another, so also of his Son, since as the Father has life in himself, so has he given also to the Son to have life in himself.

For human words are not adequate for explaining divine matters; therefore what is said seems faulty, that, although the Son of God is not a created thing, what is made is said to be in him. And if he had said, "what is begotten is in him," even so it seemed faulty. But in order to show his substantive generation, he demonstrated the work made upon it — with such language as he was able to use — through which he came to exist, since his generation has life in itself.

For we indeed live, but we cannot give life to others, since not even the same life is within our power; but he is said to have life in himself for this reason, that he is able to give life to and to create whatever he wills, that they may be and may live. For he is said to have been born for this, that he might be able to do all things which the Father does, because he thus has life in himself as the Father also has it — that is, to have made all things through him and in him, to have begotten him thus, so that he might have in himself the life through which he might be able to do all things. Not because he himself is anything other than life, but since his life has this power, that it both lives and can create other things that live, he is said to have life in himself. For we live, but we do not so have life in ourselves as to give life also to others. This same meaning the apostle Paul also recalls, saying among other things: who is the image of the invisible God, the firstborn before every creature, for in him were all things founded in the heavens and on the earth, the visible and the invisible, whether thrones or dominions or principalities or powers; all things were created through him and unto him.

The gospels of the two apostles John and Paul agree; for they say the same things, that the Son of God was begotten before all creation, in order that he might create the spiritual powers and the world and the visible things that are in it. For what John said, that what was made is life in him, this same thing Paul also signified, saying that in him all things were founded in the heavens and on earth.

And in what follows: all things were created through him and unto him. "Through him" he created, because in substance he is the same God, that is, the Father in the Son; but "unto him," because he so begot the Son that he might have the power of making all things visible and invisible. This was to have made life in him, that he might both live and furnish to others the life of living, acting, understanding, and to the other living creatures as well, according to what he willed, as it were the image of God.

For while he is called the image of God, it refers to both, because through his expressed birth he both has in himself the full likeness of the Father and can do whatever the Father can, so that it is true that the Father is seen in the Son, who is the image of the invisible God.

Did he then, because he called the Father invisible, make the Son visible, since assuredly the Son is by nature that very thing which the Father also is? And since the heavenly creature is invisible, how much more its creator! But he signified this—what is in the heavens, not what is on earth—because, although the Son too is invisible, yet there, that is, in the heavenly places, he is seen by the apostles—or by others of the like sort, of whom he said: Father, I will that where I am these also may be with me and may see my glory; and in another place: Blessed, he says, are the pure in heart, for they shall see God, that is, the Father in the Son, God in the image, which is God in God.

Just as the body is the image of a bodily being, so too God is the image of God, because the Father is the exemplar, but the Son the exemplar from the exemplar, which he shares with the Holy Spirit: for "of mine," he says, "he shall receive." And therefore God is seen in the Son as in his own image. For just as no one was found worthy to open the book and its seals except the Word of God, so also no one is worthy to see God the Father, neither by nature nor by merits, except his true Son. For there is nothing intermediate that stands in the way between the Father and the Son, with himself as our witness, who says: for no one has seen the Father, except him who is from God, this one has seen God.

But if the Father is seen in the Son, why is no one said to be worthy to see God, since he is seen in the Son, because the Son differs in nothing from the Father? He plainly differs in nothing in substance, because he is the true Son; but he differs in cause or degree, because all power is in the Son from the Father, and, if the Son is not less in substance, yet the Father is greater in authority, with the Lord himself testifying and saying: if you loved me, you would rejoice, because I go to the Father, for the Father is greater than I.

Observing this measure, the apostle Paul says: one God the Father, from whom are all things and we in him, and one Lord Jesus, through whom are all things and we through him, so that the first grade is that from which are all things, the second that through which are all things, the third that in which are all things. And because none of these is degenerate, they are signified in the unity of God, as the apostle says: for from him and through him and in him are all things; to him be glory.

CXXIII. WHETHER ADAM POSSESSED THE HOLY SPIRIT?

I have found that certain of our brethren have not fully learned the scriptures, but out of simplicity of mind assert that Adam, when made, received the Holy Spirit, which by sinning he lost, just as it is now given to believers—being led by this reasoning, by which it is usually asserted by very many that man was restored through faith in such a way that, being returned to his former state, he received all that which Adam had attained in the beginning; and that he ought to be called a man made perfect, who—"if he did not have," they say, "the Holy Spirit, was imperfect."

But I say that not only man, but also all the things that God made, which cannot be said to have received the Holy Spirit, are perfect. For all the kinds of animals are perfect in their own kind, so as to fulfill that for which they were made; so also man is perfect in his own kind, so as to be able to discern evils from goods, the crooked from the right. For he is an intelligent animal, perfect for that for which he was made; for he is capable of learning, of devising, of doing, so that what he cannot fulfill by power he may fulfill by understanding.

And how have they dared to say that man was born perfect and knowing everything, whom they see to know nothing beyond what is learned here? For what he does not learn here, he does not know. Finally, since he does not learn here what he himself is, he does not know it. He therefore who does not know what he himself is, how does he know all things, since it is less to know oneself than the rest? But because he learns about the rest, yet does not learn about himself, he knows about others, but does not know about himself—neither what he is, nor whether he exists before the body or after the body.

For if he had been made so perfect that he needed nothing, he would not have been a man, but God, nor, having been deceived, would he have transgressed. But to have the Holy Spirit is beyond the nature and perfection of man, so that he may be able to do the things which are of God. Was it not, because the ass spoke to Balaam, beyond its perfection? For it received the power to do what belonged not to its own nature, but to ours.

And so, because man is more excellent than the other animals, are those therefore to be called imperfect? Or because the holy angels are not what God is, are they imperfect? Or because the luminaries and stars are inferior to the higher angels, or because the clouds obscure the sun and moon with their veil, are they less perfect? And because the members need one another, because the feet cannot do what the hands can, are they to be called imperfect? God forbid. For all things are firm and perfect according to their own places and grades, so as to fulfill that for which they were made

Therefore all things are perfect, because their creator too is perfect, but in comparison with him they are imperfect. For God is perfect through all things, as the fount and origin of all. For the things that have been made are indeed perfect, but with respect to that for which they were made, so that in another respect they are not perfect, because the one has need of the other, so that it is perfect in that which it does not lack, not in that which it lacks; and therefore all things are both perfect and imperfect. The hands have need of the feet, because, unless they walk, the hands will be idle. Again the feet have need of the hands; for they cannot shoe themselves nor tend to themselves.

Since, then, the body is perfect in its members, yet it cannot fly nor carry as much as a mule can. Although they are perfect in these respects, yet they do not know how to govern themselves nor can they apply medicine to themselves. Whence it has been said: Do not become like the horse and the mule, which have no understanding. And both water and fire, though they are opposed to one another, are perfect in their own kind; for they fulfill that for which they were made. For fire cooks and consumes, purifies and warms; but water washes, cools, irrigates, refreshes the thirsty. -

And so, although all things are perfect in their own kind, yet without man they can do nothing, nor can man, however more perfect he may be, do anything without them. Hence the apostle calls us both imperfect and perfect. For in comparison with unbelievers we are perfect, because we have come to know God; but, because we know the things that have been promised less than we ought, since in this life we cannot grasp as much as is the thing that is believed, we are imperfect. Therefore, since they suppose that the perfection of Adam is restored to believers, let us see whether this restoration has anything beyond of the divine gift more than what Adam had obtained.

For Adam, (having been made,) 'was placed in paradise, that he might work there and keep it'. That is, that he might till the earth and keep the precepts of God, through which he might know that he had thus received dominion over all things, yet in such a way that he himself might live under the law of the creator, lest dominion should exalt him and, puffed up with pride, he should become forgetful of his maker. He was placed, therefore, that his life might be sustained by food; but through Christ this has been granted, that those who rise again should not need food or drink, because what is mortal in man will be converted into life. Adam was made that he might dwell on the earth; but faith has deigned to bestow that our habitation should be in the heavens.

Scripture itself bears witness, for it says, the first man Adam was made into a living soul, but the second man into a life-giving spirit. The first man, it says, from the earth, earthly; the second man from heaven, heavenly: as is the earthly, such also are the earthly; as is the heavenly, such also are the heavenly. What is so plain as that Adam did not have the Holy Spirit? For he was made into a living soul, but through Christ into a life-giving spirit, so that man might be in some respect like the creator and be what he believes. For because the mystery of faith, which has been given for salvation, is threefold, he becomes threefold in body, soul, and the Holy Spirit, through whom we are called sons of God, which Adam is by no means proved to have been called; for he was made earthly. But the sons of God, born not carnally but spiritually, are called heavenly.

Behold, it is settled that the gift of God bestowed far more grace in the time of the Savior than Adam had received, because he was not only restored but also improved: restored in this, that he was washed clean of sins, improved in the rest. For this both justice and reason demanded, that the mercy of God should then be more abundant in bestowing benefits, when he willed the mystery of his divinity to become known to the creature, so that, coming to know what was unknown from ages and generations — that the sacrament of the one God consists in a Trinity — they might, on account of this dedication, as it were, of the new state, having been washed clean of their sins, be moreover justified, and, adopted by God, receive the Holy Spirit, through whom they may be seen to have the mark of adoption. For those adopted by God ought to have the mark of God the Father, so that not undeservedly they may be called sons of God.

This gift was promised through the prophets, that it should be given at the time when the mystery of God was to be declared in triumph, death having been vanquished, so that the creature might know that the Father and the Son and the Holy Spirit are one God. Whence also the evangelist says: the Spirit, he says, had not been given, because Jesus had not yet been glorified. For this is the glorification, when through his own power it is recognized to be what he testified concerning himself; and this glorification gave this gift, which had been promised through the prophet Joel. For God says: because in the last days I will pour out of my Spirit upon all flesh, and so forth. And the Apostle, among other things: but when the kindness, he says, and love toward man of God our Savior appeared, not by works of righteousness which we have done, but according to his mercy he saved us by the washing of regeneration and of renewal, through the Holy Spirit, whom he poured out upon us abundantly through Christ Jesus our Savior, that, being justified by his grace, we might be made heirs according to the hope of eternal life. The Apostle confirmed what had been promised through the prophet Joel, namely that God fulfilled this after Jesus was glorified, so as to pour out the Holy Spirit upon those who believe in Christ; and that this is 'to become an heir of eternal life' — to receive the Holy Spirit, so that, since the Spirit is eternal, he who has received him may have eternal life; for it is the pledge of immortality.

For he who has received him and remains in his love, once this life has been pierced through, passes into the heavens to him whose Spirit he has. For it is incongruous that one who departs hence having the Holy Spirit should be held among those below. For the sign in a man of the victory of Christ, by which he conquered death, is his Spirit, so that in whomever his Spirit shall have been, he cannot be held by those below. And on account of this, in the saints of old the Holy Spirit was not present as he now is in the faithful, because upon departing from the world they were among those below; and it cannot be said that the Holy Spirit, by reason of Adam's sin — which through the propagation of the race made all his seed subject to those below — was at the same time held together with them by the sentence given to Adam.

That there was, then, a holy spirit with the prophets and with just men is not in doubt: with the prophets on account of dispensation, but with the just for the sake of holiness, as one reads concerning holy Simeon, because the Holy Spirit, it says, was with him — not that it might be a sign of adoption in him, but by the grace of his merits; for men began to be sons of God through believing at that time when the Son of God was made manifest, once death had been conquered for the whole of creation.

But if it be said that the Holy Spirit was in Adam or the rest in the same way as it now is in the faithful, what new gifts did God give when he dedicated the kingdom of his Son within us? And in what way is the coming of the Savior called a time happy and blessed above all others, if he bestowed those things which had already been bestowed? And where is that which the Savior says to the disciples: many, he says, prophets and just men desired to see the things which you see and to hear the things which you hear, and did not hear them?

By what reasoning, therefore, will it be possible to say that the blessedness of this time conferred nothing more of a gift than what was conferred on the ancients? This indeed works to the injury of the Savior, that he should have had nothing new which he might give at the rise of his empire to those receiving him, since the rich are wont to take pains at this, that on the festal day of their birth they may give sought-out party-favors to those invited. How great, then, is the injury to the Lord, that to those whom he has invited to a new and unheard-of feast day, worthy of all praise, he should be said not to have had untried things which he might give! And where is that saying of John the evangelist, which says: he came unto his own, and his own received him not; but as many as received him, to them he gave power to become sons of God, to those who believe in his name, who were born not of blood nor of the will of the flesh nor of the will of man, but of God? Therefore how is it not unwonted, that which God gave to those believing in Christ, when he gave them power to become sons of God — that is, brothers of his own Son — not of the will of flesh and blood or of man, but of God, that they might be born spiritually? Or if that grace is proved to have been granted before, then let it be said that the coming of Christ conferred nothing new.

Adam's making, certainly, was fleshly and earthly, not spiritual. For he was not born of God without flesh and blood, but was made from the earth by God. And therefore he could not receive the Holy Spirit, he who was not spiritual and to whom it was not granted to call God Father in prayer. For those who receive the Holy Spirit, to them is given the power that, through the fact that they have the Spirit of God, they may call that One the Father of Christians in prayer. And because this was not granted before, the former men cannot be said to have had the Holy Spirit. These, therefore, who suppose that Adam or the rest had the Holy Spirit do not know what gift of God they have, nor can they render fitting thanks to him who, receiving more than the rest, say that they have obtained nothing further.

CXXIIII. THAT ONE AND THE SAME WORK DIFFERS ACCORDING TO PERSONS, WHETHER UNTO PRAISE OR UNTO CONDEMNATION.

There is one and the same mercy in the rich man and in the poor man, but it is reckoned one way to the rich man and another way to the poor man, because it is more to be praised in the poor man than in the rich. For the poor man did not fear to give freely out of his scanty means, hoping that it would be repaid to him by God both in the present and in the future. For he believes the Scripture which says that he who gives to the poor shall not want. Whence also that poor widow, when the rich were casting in much, alone deserved to be commended by the Lord, because she did not fear to give freely out of her want. But the rich give freely being secure in their riches, whereas the poor man is secure in the Lord. The rich, then, do well when they give freely to the needy, but the poor do much better, and for this reason the reward of the poor man is one thing and that of the rich man another. For the rich man, if he does not do this, shall be beaten; but from the poor man not so much is required, and therefore the poor man who is merciful is praiseworthy.

Theft in the poor man and in the rich is one sin, but it makes the rich man more guilty, because the poor man commits theft through want, whereas the rich man, though he abounds, not content with his own takes what belongs to others. And, what is worse, they are wont to despoil the poor: and for this reason the punishment of the two differs.

The justice of the poor man and of the rich is also one, but it is more praiseworthy in the poor man. For to keep justice in destitution is a magnificent thing; but the rich man seems to keep justice for this reason, that he is a stranger to want. Therefore each is just, but it is a greater thing to keep justice in necessity.

Pride is one, but it is more to be condemned in the poor man than in the rich, because the rich man is lifted up by his abundance, whereas the poor man is proud in destitution, which belongs to madness, and for this reason the poor man is the more guilty.

Humility is one, but it is more to be praised in the rich man. For what great thing is it if the poor man appears humble, whom his very want makes humble? But it is a magnificent thing if he who is distinguished by rank and wealth bows himself down, not claiming for himself what he knows he deserves. Therefore in all humility is good, but much more so in the rich man.

The cause of learning and study is one, but it is more praiseworthy in the rich man. For the poor man, since he was commended by no prerogative, gave his effort so that he might have something by which he could be sought after; but the rich man, since there was no lack of that by which he might be commended, by the labor he applied increased himself, so that he might be needed by a twofold kind; for he was not, being enticed away by his wealth, drawn back in mind, so as to flourish the less through himself, and therefore this man is more worthy of praise than the poor man, who, if he had given no effort to studies, would have remained worthless in all respects. The former, then, was made eager for study by will, the latter by necessity.

Libido is the same in the poor man and the rich, but it is more damnable in the poor man than in the rich. For his very want ought to call the poor man back from the desire of luxury; for he ought to consider that he does not have the means to satisfy this, and that, while he hastens to satisfy it, he must of necessity commit many other evils, from which perhaps he may not even escape for the present, or at any rate, by spending this very thing which he has, he will be left a beggar with a mark of disgrace. But the rich man is provoked by an abundance of delights to the pleasure of lust, especially because such men are free from care about impunity, knowing that judgments are for sale and that they are not even called to account by anyone. For who would dare to accuse a man upheld by rank and wealth? Whence they are the more inflamed, so that they even do this violently. For not only are they in no way censured, but they are even held in great honor, so that they can even boast, because they are such men.

. 8. But if both, that is, the poor man and the rich, be chaste, the chastity of the rich man differs greatly from the chastity of the poor. For lowliness can call the poor man back, lest he be able to satisfy what he wishes, or the fear of the laws; but the rich man, since he can be enticed to pleasure by many favoring circumstances, is praiseworthy if he turns his mind away from this. Therefore the chaste rich man is worthy of one reward and the chaste poor man of another. But if a king be chaste, it is a thing of great glory, that, having all things in his power, he does not touch what he knows can be done by him with impunity. This man truly fears God, this man truly keeps His precepts, who, having the laws in his power, contemplates the future judgment of God. And so it is a great thing that, when in the present he has no one whom he fears, he should conquer what delights him, since the delight can be so strong that he fears not only future things but even present things not at all. Whence they merit much who overcome this, but more so this man, who in the present rules over laws and men. For the rest fear both men and laws, and therefore they keep their resolve. For, lest they blush before men, they preserve what they have long held, and, lest they be condemned, they abstain from forbidden things; but this man, who by his dominion neither fears the laws nor blushes before men, is of great glory if he abstains.

CXXV. AGAINST EUSEBIUS.

I remember that I read in a certain little book of Eusebius, once a man distinguished in other respects, that not even the Holy Spirit knows the mystery of the nativity of the Lord Jesus Christ; and I marvel that a man of such great learning has inflicted this blemish upon the Holy Spirit. For in saying this he has signified that He is degenerate. For it cannot be said that He is of God, if He does not know the things that are God's, because a nature that is inferior does not know what is in one that is superior. But if indeed He is of the same substance and divinity, how can He not know the things that are His own? Finally the Son of God, because, He says, I and the Father are one on account of the unity of substance, not without reason added, saying: all things that are the Father's are mine, and the things that are mine are the Father's.

Wherefore, if the Holy Spirit is of the same divinity, on what ground is he set apart, so as not to be of the same knowledge, when it is not denied that he is of the same substance? You concede what is greater, and you withhold what is less. For a creature learns and begins to know something about its author, and there were things it had learned that were shared in common with its author; but substance or divinity cannot be common to God and to a creature. For God's substance has nothing to learn, because there is nothing it is ignorant of; but the substance of human beings does not have it in their nature to know, but to learn. And so knowledge accrues to the nature of human beings, yet the substance is not changed thereby, because man's nature lacks knowledge; for the substance was so founded that through exercise it might acquire knowledge.

Therefore substance is greater than knowledge, because knowledge does not acquire substance, but substance acquires knowledge; and substance exists without knowledge, yet knowledge cannot exist without substance. But in God, just as there is substance, so also there is knowledge; for the substance, which has nothing to learn, is itself substance-as-its-own-knowledge. Wherefore all things in God are said to be substantial. For substance, to which nothing is lacking, has all things substantially, because nothing has accrued to it through study.

How then can it be said of the Holy Spirit that he does not know the nativity of the Son of God, if he is consubstantial with him? Can one and the same substance both know and not know? Surely the Son of God did not learn the mystery of his own nativity; for he knows it substantially, not through instruction. On what ground, then, is the Holy Spirit said not to know, when likewise he too has nothing to learn, because he knows all things through substance?

For just as the Son of God, so also the Holy Spirit is the substance of God, since the Lord says: all things that are the Father's are mine, by which he signified that the substance of God the Father is his own substance; and he says that the Holy Spirit proceeds from the Father and receives of his own: and therefore, he says, I said: he shall receive of mine, because all things that are the Father's are mine. If he proceeds from the Father and receives of the Son, how does he not know the nativity of the Son, when his substance is the substance of the Son? For all things that are the Father's are the Son's. Without doubt, then, the substance of the Father is in the Holy Spirit. For the Son added, saying: and all things that are mine are the Father's.

What then is doubted concerning the Holy Spirit, whether he is of the same divinity, when he is of the same substance? For just as the Lord said of himself that he proceeded from God, so also he testifies concerning the Holy Spirit. And lest this could be interpreted otherwise: he proceeds from the Father, he says, and: he shall receive of mine, so that to proceed from the Father is the same as to receive of Christ. For when he is said to receive of the Son of God, it is not doubted that he too is of God, so that in the Holy Spirit the substance and divinity of the Father is not doubted.

And so the one who says that this Spirit does not know the birth of the Son of God detracts from him from whom he is said to have received; for he received nothing other than that very One from whom he received, since to have received from Christ signifies to have proceeded from God, and therefore he cannot be ignorant of what this One knows from whom he received, nor is he to be said to be ignorant of God, since he proceeded from God.

Certainly the Son of God, because he is from God the Father, knows all the things of God: why then should the Holy Spirit be denied to know the things that are Christ's, since he has received from him? And so, if the Son of God knows the things that are in God, there is no doubt that the Holy Spirit also is not ignorant of the things that are in the Son of God, and, through that by which he knows the things that are in the Son, he is not to be said to be ignorant of the things that are in God. For from him, from whom he received, he has knowledge of God.

And why do we labor, when the apostle is a witness in this matter, saying: no one knows the things that are in God except the Spirit of God? Behold, that over which we long sweated, with the apostle's help we have accomplished. And did he in any way prejudge the Son of God, because he said that no one knows the things that are in God except the Spirit of God? Far from it. So too the Son did not prejudge the Holy Spirit, when he said: no one knows the Father except the Son. For he said that no one knows the things that are in God except him who is from God, because everything that is from God knows God and the things that are in God. For the nature of God knows itself, and when you understand that this same nature is also in the Holy Spirit, through that by which he proceeded from the Father and received from the Son, it is not safe to say that he is ignorant of God the Father, whose nature you recognize in him.

And yet the Savior spoke thus, that no one knows God except the Son, so as to reserve to another person the knowledge of this One. For he immediately added, saying: and to whomever the Son shall wish to reveal him. To whom, do you suppose, does the Son wish to reveal him? For no one can be dearer to him than his own Spirit, because, he says, if anyone does not have the Spirit of Christ, this one is not his. Therefore, in what way do you suppose God the Father is revealed by the Son to the Holy Spirit, except in the way the Lord himself says: he shall receive of mine? For God the Father cannot be revealed to a creature, because it cannot bear the nature of God, unless it be of the same substance; for he dwells in light inaccessible—inaccessible, that is, to a creature.

But to some it seems to be rightly said that the Holy Spirit does not know the mystery of the birth of the Son of God, because he is said to search all things and <he who searches), it says, 'assuredly does not know'. This ought to be understood from another passage; for they are the words of God, saying among other things: I am the God who searches the reins and the hearts, and in the psalm: God, it says, searching the hearts and the reins, and the apostle: he who searches, he says, the hearts, knows what the Spirit desires, because he makes petition for the saints according to the will of God.

His examples show that when the Spirit of God is said to search, it does not mean that He is ignorant. He is said to search "even the deep things of God": and what are the deep things of God, which He is said to see through? For to search means to penetrate hidden things, so that through this nothing may be believed to be hidden from Him. For the deep things of God are the inner mysteries of God, which cannot be searched out by a creature. For He is also read to make intercession for the saints according to the will of God, which the saints certainly do not know, as the Apostle says: for we know not what we should pray for as we ought. Therefore to know the deep things of God and not to be ignorant of the will of God signify one and the same thing. For what is so deep a mystery as the will of God, which is known to the Holy Spirit but unknown to all the rest?

For it must be considered in what manner it was said, that no man knows the things that are in a man except the spirit of the man which is in him. By "spirit" he signified the mind, because no man knows what is in the mind of a man except his mind, which is the spirit. So too in God no one knows the things that are in Him except the Spirit of God, that is, God Himself.

For He who is of God is understood to be nothing other than what God is. Therefore the Spirit of God knows what is in God, because He is of God. For if no one knows the thoughts of men except God, how much more can no one know the secrets and the will of God unless he be of God, that is, the same God Himself! For He is known to Himself alone.

For assuredly in God there are no thoughts. For He does not deliberate within Himself what He should do or what He should not do, weighing whether it is expedient, because all things that are in God are without hesitation not by accident but by substance, and not by pursuit but by nature, because He does not change. And on account of this the Holy Spirit knows the mysteries of God in no other way than by substance; for one substance has one perception and will.

He speaks of God, who is ever one and unchangeable, whether in the Son or in the Holy Spirit. For what the Father wills, this the Son also wills, and what the Son wills, the same the Holy Spirit also wills. For this reason He is sometimes called the Spirit of God, sometimes the Spirit of Christ, because, He says, He proceeds from God, and: He shall receive of mine.

Hence it is that John the Apostle says: hereby we know that God abides in us, by His Spirit, which He has given us. Therefore if, when the Spirit of God abides in us, God is said to abide in us, it is signified that the Spirit of God is God.

Finally, this is the sign of believers, that they are the sons of God, so that, when they receive Him, they may dare to call themselves the sons of God. For unless they have the sign of the Godhead, they cannot be called the sons of God; for since anyone who is not a true son of God, so as to be whole from whole, as Christ also is, but they become sons of God through adoption, so that, receiving the Spirit of Christ, they may through Him be called sons of God, through that which is of God, namely the Spirit whom they have in themselves. For in sons, although they be adoptive, nevertheless the substance of the father ought to be seen in some part.

For in the world, because things are imperfect, sons adopted by human beings receive no pledge but only the name; but God, because he is perfect, does more, in that he gives his Spirit to those he has adopted, through whom the adopted are seen to possess some truth from him, since names without substance are empty.

Our Apostle supports this assertion of ours, saying: O the depth of the riches both of the wisdom and knowledge of God! How unsearchable are his judgments, and his ways past finding out! How is it that, although elsewhere he says that the deep things of God, that is, his secrets, are searchable, in this place he denies it, saying that they are unsearchable? But in this place he spoke of the creature, because the secrets or judgments of God are unsearchable to it; yet to the Holy Spirit, in that he knows all the things of God, he said that all things which are in God are searchable, so that what is unknown to the creature he may affirm the Spirit of God does not fail to know. Therefore the nature of God cannot be denied to exist, which holds known that which cannot be comprehended by the creature.

Those who say the Holy Spirit is of a lower kind assail God's purpose, who declared that the salvation of the whole creation consists in the divinity of the Trinity; for if he is not consubstantial with God and Christ, it is absurd to place him in the number of the Father and the Son, such that without him there is neither salvation nor dignity for anyone. For if he is not of the same divinity, it was done either foolishly or gratuitously — which be far from it. For who among the prudent would deny that the Creator cannot be numbered together with the creature, nor the eternal be compared with him who exists under a beginning, nor the Lord be made equal with the servant, nor the powerful be set alongside the powerless, nor the knowing be reckoned with the ignorant?

But now let the slander cease. For he is third in order, not in nature; in degree, not in divinity; in person, not in ignorance. For just as the Son of God is second from the Father and is not less in divinity, so too the Holy Spirit follows from the Son, not unequal, but equal in the divinity of substance.

Finally, the things we read concerning the Son of God, these same things we read also concerning the Holy Spirit, with himself as our witness. For he says: I will ask the Father, and he shall give you another Paraclete, the Spirit of truth. When he said 'another,' he signified that he himself is a Paraclete. This the apostle John declares, saying that the Son of God intercedes for our sins, and the apostle Paul: the Spirit of God himself, he says, intercedes for us. Behold, Scripture shows that both are advocates. And when he called him the Spirit of truth, he showed that he in no way differs from himself, since he said: I am the truth. He said that he himself was sent by the Father, and he himself promised that he would send the Holy Spirit. If he himself is not unequal to the one who sends him, neither is the Holy Spirit unequal to him. Nor, because they are three, is each one member of the other, such that they could be diverse. For the power of the three is one, so that nothing is lacking to the individuals.

Let this be the end; for now, in the little book composed against the Arian impiety, the remaining matters have been fully treated, which embrace the undivided unity of the Trinity.

CXXVI. ON HIM WHO RECEIVES.

It is written that "the righteous person's righteousness will profit him nothing on the day he goes astray," and that "his own injustice will not harm the unjust person, when he has converted"; and the Lord in the Gospel says: "whoever has not believed is already judged," so that by this he might mark out the one who has believed as not being subject to judgment for receiving a sentence, but as destined to be worthy of praise by the merit of his faith.

Therefore the knowledge of God has this prerogative, that it obtains remission of sins. For the one who believes Jesus to be the Son of God stands forth worthy to be freed from all sins. For having long wandered off course through error, when he was being tossed about through diverse waves of ignorance, once illumined by the flashing of truth he has gained an anchorage, in which, after excessive storms, he may rest secure — a person whom it befits, after knowledge, to abstain from those things which, once placed in ignorance, he had for a long time been doing. For what does knowledge profit, if the former manner of life remains? For from this a person is seen to have come to know God: if he changes his life and his manner of living.

For God, once known, ought to be feared, because he is proclaimed as one who will judge, so that both the faithful may receive the fruit of their righteousness and the impious, that is, the unbelievers, may pay penalties suited to their faithlessness. For it is fitting that the righteous rejoice in the future, where Christ will reign with his own, so that those who in this world are subject to reproach and injuries — in which the devil is prince — may appear glorious in the kingdom of Christ, on whose account they were judged contemptible by the worldly; but the unrighteous, because they falsely seem to flourish, resisting the truth through falsehood, may be afflicted in punishment, so that those who through deceit were seen as glorious may through the truth appear despised and exceedingly humbled. For then the righteous rejoice that they have believed, when they see the faithless tormented; and it will not repent the faithless of not having believed, unless they see the glory of those men and their own punishment — those men whom, because they believed, they reckoned foolish and contemptible.

Therefore, from the fact that a person professes himself to be a Christian, he ought to strive for this: not to sin. For he ought to arm himself against the vices and — because it is impossible always to conquer — if he has been conquered, to grieve, lest he be conquered again, because the one who grieves that he has been conquered restores himself. Therefore the one who resists has grounds on which to excuse himself, if he has been conquered. For he shows that he had the will to conquer, but that he simply did not prevail. Concerning such a one there is hope, because he can restore himself, so that by constant practice he may be found more skilled and stronger in the contest.

For the one who rises up for this purpose, that he may sin, is both inexcusable and has a most grievous case. For it is worse to live under the profession of God and to have the will to sin, than if a person were ignorant of God and conducted himself basely and defilingly. For the latter does not know whom he ought to fear, whereas the former, knowing, holds him in contempt.

Believers, therefore, receive the remission of sins; yet they will not be glorious unless, after receiving the faith, they have lived under the fear of God, so that, having struggled against the enemies of the Christian name, they may win the victory, so that they may be able to merit the reward, so long as they conquer more than they are conquered.

For whoever understands God and yet does not come to faith in him, so as not to live as a Christian, but wishes to receive the faith at the end, so that he may die a Christian—either he is ashamed to live as a Christian, or he devotes himself to the sins which he supposes can be remitted to him once he has come to believe—this man, I think, does not have a good case, because, in order that he may go on sinning as long as he is in the world, he does not wish to become and live as a Christian, or is embarrassed to be called a Christian. What sort of God does he hope for, from whom he wishes to receive pay, though he is ashamed to serve him as a soldier? A man of this kind is a mocker. For, knowing that he no longer has time to sin, with death imminent, then he wishes to become a Christian, so that, because he can no longer sin, he may say that he believes in Christ—Christ, who abhors that sin be committed—so that it is not now that he is unwilling to sin, but that he is unable, since death forestalls him. To such men this ought to be read: that God is not mocked.

For men of this kind are also accustomed to give such instructions, that faith be given to them when they are already failing and beside themselves, merely gasping, fearing lest they escape and be put to shame because they have become Christians. To these the words of the Gospel ought to be recited, which say: "For whoever is ashamed of me," he says, "and of my words in this generation, of him also will the Son of Man be ashamed, when he comes in the kingdom of his Father."

But perhaps they may say: "Knowing that the human race is frail, for this reason we wish to receive the faith at the end of life, so that we may no longer sin." But faith ought to be received in such a way that what is received is understood. For he will have the benefit of it then, if he knows it and professes it—for with the heart it is believed unto righteousness, but with the mouth confession is made unto salvation—and through this, one who no longer knows where he is, how can he be said to receive? For that man has received who professes that he has received, knowing that he has received; for if they believe that remission of sins is given to believers, they ought to know that he believes who believes with his own heart as witness, since God, who has promised, is the inspector of the heart; for he receives that very one whom he sees believing with the heart. And so, one whose heart does not attend to the words of faith, how will he have the benefit of it?

Therefore this man—who is discerned to act in this way so that he may not sin easily—can be seen [rather] to fear to become a Christian by receiving the faith. For the one who hastens to sin, for this reason does not wish to receive the faith, lest he be compelled to live well, which he seems unwilling to do; but at death he wishes to obtain impunity, hoping also for the remission of past evils, so that he may be secure both about the past and about the future, since, being about to die, he assuredly will no longer be able to sin.

But this is done neither with good counsel nor with a clean conscience, that he should wish to receive the faith then, when he no longer can sin, not when he is unwilling to sin. To these applies that saying: "Let us do evil, that good may come"—that is, "Let us sin now, and at the last let us believe, and it will be remitted to us." And therefore he added: "Whose condemnation," he says, "is just." For it is not fitting that remission of sins be given to those who, already knowing God—God who wills that no sin be committed and holds sinners in hatred—yet crave to sin, being unwilling to notice that the gift of God profits those who, not knowing God, sin in such a way that they do not know that God will judge. But one who has heard and pondered it within himself and has the deliberate wish to sin—not so that he is overcome by some compelling cause, but so that he may sin willingly—it is hard for him to be pardoned.

For there are two grounds which secure the remission of sins: one of the gift, the other of penance, so that whoever has sinned after the gift may reform himself through penance. For because he did not remain in the grace he had received, so as to persevere in freedom, but again subjected himself to sin, by lamentation and groaning he obtains that his sin be blotted out. For he sinned knowingly and deliberately — not that anyone is ignorant of sin, but he who does not know that God will judge thinks that what he sins will go unpunished.
