And on this account a gentile, if he begins to fall ill and, in the very despair of life, ponders within himself concerning the faith and the promised hope, has a complete case, so that to him, thus believing, his sins are remitted and he is not held fast in the nether world, bearing with him the sign of death conquered and despoiled — although he may not have dignity or reward, because there ought to be some difference between him who, provoked by love of his Lord, did not fear to fight against His enemies, arming himself with continence and with watchfulness against all vices, that he might be able to resist evil, ever anxious how he might defend his Lord's dominion, and him who, consorting with his Lord's enemies and taking his stand with them, returned to his Lord at the end of life, not that he might champion his Lord's kingdom, but that he might profit himself alone — a discharged veteran before being a soldier.

For that man, who prefers to live as a Christian so that after death he may be rewarded, knows that the sufferings of this present time are not worthy of the glory to come which shall be revealed in us; and on this account, having taken up the arms of faith, he does not hesitate to engage against the principalities and powers, so that, proving victor in the world, he may be crowned after the world. For it is fitting that he who, amid the faithless and the rebellious, presently bears witness to his Lord's kingdom should in the future obtain the reward for this thing, so that the enemies of God, seeing the witness of Christ appear exalted in the kingdom of God because of the truth of his confession, may grieve, confounded that they had reckoned false what has been declared to be true; and likewise also those who believed late, confessing God not in life but in death, seeing how great is the glory of those who, soldiering under the arms of Christ, wage their emperor's wars devotedly and faithfully, may repent that they did not live as Christians, but died as Christians.

For he may seem by no means fit to die a Christian, who had no wish to live a Christian. For perhaps, being now about to die, he wished for this reason to be called a Christian, because now there was no longer time for sinning. (Lest perchance, he says, what is believed be true, I wish to become a Christian, losing nothing if what is believed is false.) So that, because now in death not the will to sin but the committing of sin ceases, he makes trial whether faith can profit after death — whereas this indeed would have been useful, that here the vices and sins should be amended, where they had been committed.

Then indeed someone proves that he has condemned his past deeds when, following the line of the faith, he is seen to change his conduct and life. For if he previously sinned because he did not know God, then when he comes to know him he ought to sin no longer, lest it be in vain that he has known him; for a person ought to have made progress from the knowledge of God. For a Christian ought to be recognized before his profession: they are proved more by their works than by their name. For this reason commands were given, purer than the other laws and pertaining to holiness, so that from these the worship of God might appear; otherwise, they will hear: Why do you call me, Lord, Lord, and do not do what I say? By this he then signified that it profits to call the Lord "Lord" only if his commands are kept; for one who calls him Lord whose precepts he despises seems to be a mocker. To such a person the words of the law must be recited, which say that God is not mocked. And so he will be condemned by his own words who calls him Lord, whom by his works he shows that he in no way fears.

CXXVII. ON THE SIN OF ADAM AND EVE.

I judge it doubtful to no one that this world was fashioned for the sake of man, which, although it consists of diverse substances, is nevertheless one, fitted together out of many members, so that from these, by mutual works reciprocal to one another, the things that would be necessary for man might be brought forth. For a house was made for man together with provision, in such a way that each of them might come to be upon the earth according to its kind — that is, so that, once made, they might have this power implanted in them, that the offspring of each of their kinds might be created upon the earth. For origins were established, whose seed might advance toward the procreation of the kinds to be multiplied upon the earth.

Whence Scripture bears witness, saying: and God blessed them, saying: increase and multiply upon the earth. In a like manner also the race of men was blessed, which meaning we behold in the law; for we see it written: the people grew and was multiplied in Egypt. For by the same blessing with which those things were blessed that were created for the use of man, by that blessing man too was blessed, so that likewise from male and female the offspring of the human race might grow and be multiplied upon the earth, and so that, just as the seeds were to be improved through cultivation, so also the race of men, with care applied, might be zealous for this: that, having received the knowledge of the Creator, it might bridle its own life so as to earn him, that at the same time all things might advance to the praise and glory of the Creator.

And since these words signify nothing other than this, the things themselves bear witness. For all things that were made have, by God's nod, been multiplied and improved upon the earth. Nor indeed could anything grow otherwise than as God's will and blessing decreed for the seeds. In what way, then, can it be said that something is done wrongly or is not permitted, which by God's blessing and with him favoring it produces increase? The tradition of this matter both remained in the synagogue and is now celebrated in the church, that the creature of God should be joined under God's blessing — not indeed through presumption, since by the Author himself the form was thus given.

But if it is thought that it ought to cease, then it will have to cease when those things too are going to cease which were likewise blessed so that they might multiply; for, if the generation of human beings ceases, to what purpose are those things born which have been blessed upon the earth? For the world cannot in part act and in part cease. For either the whole of it works or the whole of it rests in idleness. Is a body of any use, some of whose members are vigorous while some grow torpid?

Why then is that which has been blessed by God asserted by certain people to be a filthy and defiled work, unless it is that they are in a manner laying hands upon God himself? For they would not censure this unless they thought ill of God, the author of this work. For because they openly fear to disparage God, they nevertheless accuse him through the work devised by him. For when the work is displeasing, the author is censured.

But if men of this sort were to read, or rather to accept, the Scriptures, they would be mindful of Balaam, who says: How, he says, shall I curse whom God blesses? For in vain is he accused who is praised by the judge; rather, he is his own accuser who calls him guilty whom the laws defend. Who, then, are you, who judge that you must condemn what you read to have been blessed by God, unless either you deny that this is God or accuse the Scripture of being false?

For there are those who, receiving as it were new things, think the old ones must be repudiated. But the new precepts made known to the peoples through Christ do not disagree with these. For he himself, being invited, did not disdain to go to a wedding, and not only did he honor it with his presence, but he even supplied what was lacking for its joy. For it is written that wine gladdens the heart of man. And that he might show that he had done this according to the will of God his Father, when the Jews asked whether it was permitted for a man to dismiss his wife, he answered, saying among other things: Because from the beginning God made male and female, and said: For this reason a man shall leave father and mother and shall cleave to his wife, and the two shall be in one flesh. And so they are now not two, but one flesh. What therefore God has joined together, let no man separate. This is why, being invited, he went willingly to the wedding, lest he should seem to weaken the deed of God his Father; but rather, showing that the teaching of the old and the new law agree, he not only did not forbid marriage, but even deigned to be present, granting them his testimony, since God is the author of them, and by a salutary precept he showed that what has been joined by God ought neither to be forbidden nor separated.

And because birth is beneficial, when he was now leaving the world, he entrusted his mother to John his disciple. For which reason we too are taught, through all the commandments of the old and the new law, to honor our parents; and unless we do this, it has been provided by the law that we shall incur a curse.

And so what presumption is it, or from what law does it proceed, to block off access to marriage, when both the new and the old law seem to favor these things in every respect? But this is what we read, that "what is beyond this is from evil." Hence the apostle says that men of this kind have a seared conscience, who forbid marrying and teach that one must abstain from foods which God created to be partaken of. For they are marked out as doing this in the hypocrisy of enmity, that they may accuse the law handed down by God.

Others, being zealous in this way, stretch out hands opposed to the saving precepts, so as to commend the injunctions of perverse doctrine, and through this they are said to have a seared conscience. For with a corrupted mind they know one thing and profess another, just as the Jews also, who, not being ignorant that the deeds of the Savior were the works of the Holy Spirit, out of envy said that he cast out demons by Beelzebub, in order to turn the people away from faith in him. Such also is the cunning of those mentioned above, since, in order that they may feign themselves to be lovers of chastity and holiness, they even say that marriages are to be condemned, so that by this they may be commended and may turn the people away from the truth, and, that they may pretend to be zealous for abstinence, they teach that one must be temperate in foods, so that by these things they may show that they, as strangers to the world, are hastening to the heavenly kingdoms. By which, enticing the minds of men, they thereafter teach that unlawful things are lawful and condemn lawful things as if they were forbidden.

These are the deceptions of Satan, that he may invert the causes, so that, as if insinuating something new, he may exclude the truth, in which there is nothing new, because the whole is eternal. Why does he not perceive that this notion has been contrived by the adversary? For who would dare to reject God's device, and that which never harmed anyone except the adversary of the truth, who, in order to cloak his own impurity, proclaims a holiness which he does not love, and, while by this he shows himself to be as it were of good will, urges unlawful things as if lawful? For he commends himself, that he may deceive more readily, and through this, when the imprudent have been captured, he suggests things by which he may make them greater sinners, because, in order to bring some remedy to his own cause, he greatly urges men to sin, seeking from it a great consolation, while he shows that many are the associates of his crime, reckoning the punishment light, as though it would be grievous, if he should see many with him in Gehenna. Let men look to it amid their other sins, while they are overcome by vices, or through ignorance they will defend as not to be punished here what is not.

What contrary concupiscence deceives, or what darkness obstructs, that what is true may not be seen! For letters are wont to seem to signify something else, when they are either not well pronounced or improperly distinguished. But when it is read: "God made," and "he blessed what he made," who would dispute this? Who would doubt? For who would think that what he hears blessed is cursed, unless he were animated by another spirit? And indeed if it were said to be the voice of a man, perhaps some deception might be believed: God is said to speak, and you doubt? God blesses, and is he reprobate?

But perhaps under God's name Moses introduced an error! Let the signs and wonders done through Moses in Egypt satisfy you; let the marvels that were accomplished at the Red Sea for the liberation of the sons of Israel persuade you; hear the magicians confessing that "this is the finger of God"; agree with the apostle when he says: "For I would not," he says, "have you ignorant, brethren, that our fathers were all under the cloud and all passed through the sea, and all were baptized unto Moses in the cloud and in the sea, and all ate the same spiritual food, and all drank the same spiritual drink; for they drank of the spiritual rock that followed them, and that rock was Christ." And from what source did the apostle speak these things? Scripture is at hand, in which we read that Christ our Lord said to the Jews: "If you believed Moses, you would believe me also, for Moses wrote of me." Who would deny faith to this concord? Who would dare to say that what is one disagrees with itself? Who is of so evil a mind as to contend that an undivided love should appear as enmity? Behold, you have both the testimonies of words and the examples of mighty deeds, which may subject your mind to the truth, so that you may think nothing true except what the books of the catholic Church contain.

Since therefore it has been proved that the same God existed in the old covenant who is now ours, and that he is true by many proofs of mighty deeds, his authority ought to be so far preferred that even that which is judged by us to be harsh and perhaps absurd ought to be accepted and understood otherwise than we suppose, if it is commended by the judgment of God, because we ought rather to believe God than ourselves. For our weakness and inexperience is wont to judge as useful the things that are harmful, and to reckon false things as true — which it is impious even to suspect concerning God, for he is not a nature that can be deceived —: how much more ought there to be no doubt about marriages when God speaks! For it is a matter open and simple.

Certainly every man rejoices when he has attained the esteem of God, and believes himself made better while he learns the mystery of the Creator, which assuredly he would not attain unless he were born. Why then does he bewail that in which he rejoices, and condemn what he glories in having learned? For if he rejoices because he has learned, but would not have learned unless he had been born, without doubt it is good to be born, because the fruit of birth is the knowledge of truth. But if it is evil to be born, the knowledge will not be good; for to what purpose will knowledge be of use, if birth is condemned? — because, if it is neither expedient nor right to be born, it is empty that one who is to be condemned should learn. But since no one is so dull as to deny that the knowledge of God is profitable to men, it is both good and useful. For through it birth is made better, so that it may merit more than had been conferred on Adam, because believers shall reign in heaven, not on earth, in the paradise of God the Father, not in that in which Adam had been bidden to labor bodily.

For if the feast of dedication was celebrated at Jerusalem—that is, the festival of the dedication of the Lord's temple was observed—how much more must the birth of the man himself be celebrated, who is more truly the temple of God, and for whom, moreover, a temple has been built by hands for the rendering of thanks to God! For our body is the better temple, because this one was constructed by the work of God, but that one by human labor, and this one exists under the hope of eternity, that one under the hope of destruction. And so whoever knows that he was born by God's ordaining ought, in order to give thanks to Him while holding the knowledge of His mystery, to rejoice on his birthday, seeing that his nativity has advanced him. But truly those ought not to have been born who, forsaking their Creator, assign His glory to others. For the nativity of such men advances toward punishment; yet it is not the nativity that incurs the guilt, but the will.

But who are you, who forbid marriages? Perhaps Marcion, since you suppose the body to have been fashioned not by God but by the devil, and you contend that the soul, by a certain error, suffered a fall, so that it might come into the regions of darkness, in which the world now is. How then could it be freed from here, if it were forbidden to be born? For once born you recognized your fall and by deliberate effort brought this about, that you might return to your ancestral seat, restored to your source. And indeed you say you give thanks to Christ, through whom you rejoice to have attained this knowledge. But come now, if you had not been born, the knowledge would have ceased, nor would liberation have followed. And so, if you rejoice that the soul is freed, favor the nativity; for if you resist the nativity, you are an enemy of souls.

Or if you are a Manichaean, who reject marriages as though contrary, I ask of you: since, if bodies were not at all begotten, how would the soul be rescued from here—the soul which you assert has been poured out into the region of darkness and clings to material things? For you have it written in your books that it is freed by being born, so that the souls, taken up by the moon as they depart from bodies, are handed over to the sun, which you assert to be the god of your souls. For you rejoice when you are called Manichaeans. For through this name you demand your liberation—which surely you would not know, had you not been born. And so it plainly appears that you condemn marriages through hypocrisy. For while professing sanctity, you secretly pursue uncleanness, which has been exposed not only privately but even by the edicts of emperors.

Hear now, O Catholic, that by the testimony of the Gospel a person's birth is beneficial. For when Symeon, a righteous man, wished to depart from this world, thinking that knowledge of the Creator without acquaintance with His mystery was enough for him, it was not permitted to him unless he should make an increase in his perception of God, so that he might have the full reward of his faith. And so, receiving the newborn Savior in his hands, he blessed God and said: Lord, now dismiss Your servant according to Your word in peace, for my eyes have seen Your salvation. It was plainly shown that there is a benefit of God in a person's birth, when to a righteous man pondering death it was answered that he would not die before he had seen the Christ of the Lord. For he was making such progress in life that he became worthy even in the present to see the one whom he hoped for as his deliverer after death. He, therefore, who was kept back in life and not permitted to die unless he had seen the fruit of his hope, so that he might be secure because he would pass from life to life — how can it be said that birth did not benefit him?

For if it is an evil that we are born, then paradise would not be promised, nor eternal life, nor the kingdom of heaven, but rather the punishment of Gehenna and perdition, so that he who knew himself born unto perdition would fear to beget another, so that it might not be lawful to beget one unlawfully born.

But perhaps it may be said: 'The kingdom of heaven is indeed promised, but to the faithful and to those who act well.' Rightly so. You see, then, that men do not become guilty because they were born, but because they have conducted themselves badly. For the kingdom of heaven was not promised to the unborn, so that those to whom it will not be given should charge it to their birth; rather it was promised to those who are born and act well, so that neither may birth profit the one who acts badly, nor be able to harm the one who acts well.

For by the faithful and by those who act well birth is made better, but by the unfaithful and by those who act badly it is made worse. For birth is like a tree that is grafted, because, if it is grafted with a good shoot, it will become better and will be called good, but if it is grafted with a bad shoot, it will both be worse and will be called not good, but bad. So too, if good teaching is added to birth, it will produce good fruits; but if bad teaching, it will produce bad fruits. Thus, just as a tree is necessary, so that there may be something in which the graft is set, so too birth is necessary, so that there may be something in which progress is made.

But it is answered on the contrary: 'If birth is useful, why is there rebirth?' We would not be reborn unless birth were useful. For to be reborn is to be renewed, and he who is renewed is restored. Therefore the capacity for rebirth does not accuse birth, but reforms it, and that which is reformed is proved to have been well established from the beginning. Therefore, in that we are reborn, we are changed by design, having received the purification of the body, so that we may be restored to the pristine state of Adam. But the body was stained by the soul's sin, and, once the design has been repaired and made better through faith, it

is washed clean, so that, just as it had been polluted through contempt of God, so through obedience it may be washed clean, that it may escape the sentence given to Adam and be able to rise again.

Therefore, if sin began from the soul, why is the nature of the body accused, since in the cause of Adam's sin there was no bodily desire, but the soul, enticed by the hope of divinity, «transgressed God's command, so that it might also subject its own body to sin and human beings might be born under sin»? And this indeed in no way harms a human being—provided that he obeys the law of God—except that he dies; for which matter the benevolence of God has promised a reward, so that, since they are found faithful in devotion to their Creator, but through the transgression of Adam have been made subject to corruption and to death, on account of this they may receive from God the judge more than had been granted to Adam: namely, that in the future they may both be glorious and have eternal life and be called adopted sons of God, so that it may be a gain that they were born.

From this point I will now return to the remaining part of the reading. When man had been placed in paradise, he received these commands: «Of every tree,» he says, «that is in paradise for food, you shall eat; but of the tree whereby good and evil are discerned, you shall not eat of it.» All the trees which he mentions are fruit-trees created for eating. Accordingly they are called by the one name "trees," but they are understood as various in their fruits; yet they are all trees, and the manner of eating is one, although the things that are eaten are diverse. For when he had created many fruit-trees for man to eat, as I have said, he forbade that one tree be eaten from, so that they might have reverence for the Creator in some part—they who had received all things in their power—while one tree was reserved, over which they would not have license, so that they might be mindful of the law of their condition.

In what way was the kind of sin to be understood from the sentence? For this sentence fits also the murderer and the evildoer and the adulterer and the disreputable. Achar too, the son of Charmi, when he had sinned, was adjudged to be destroyed with all his own. In what way can the kind of sin be understood from the sentence? That the offense was great can be recognized, yet the kind of the offense cannot be understood. For we know that others too were condemned by this sentence, though they are known to have offended otherwise. For both Aman and the Sodomites are known to have perished with all their own. For so it comes about that both those guilty of one sin are struck by a diverse sentence, and sinners of a diverse crime are punished by one and the same kind. On which account, in the case of Adam and Eve, the kind of sin cannot be understood from the sentence given.

For although their sin was one, yet according to their own person the man and the woman received the sentence, and likewise also the serpent, so that not only did they not remain in that which they had been made, but also labor was added to them for punishment. For when all cattle and living creatures had been made subject to man, as we read, the serpent indeed raised itself up against this ordinance and by guile, through deceit, had subjected to itself the man whom it had encircled. For without doubt he who captures someone brings him within himself. On which account the serpent, lest it should have the effect of its cunning, is by the sentence of God called back and repressed beyond what it had been made, so that it should not be above man, so that by its craftiness it might grieve not only to have accomplished nothing, but even to have made itself worse. For although it is read to have been more prudent than the other beasts, after it deceived man, it was made cursed by all the beasts of the earth.

After the sentence pronounced against the serpent, it follows that the woman too, whom he had allied to himself for the despising of God's law, should receive her sentence. For it says: Filling I will fill up thy sorrows and thy groaning: in sadness shalt thou bring forth sons, and to thy husband shall be thy turning, and he shall have dominion over thee. Let no one confirm the very thing that he censures. If sons were granted for the reason that she had usurped intercourse, then the usurpation gained more. If, because he said, in sadness shalt thou bring forth sons, you thereupon suppose that generation took its beginning, where then will be that which was said, increase and multiply? Understand therefore that this was added only for punishment, so that, since it had before been granted through gladness, sadness was placed underneath by way of punishment. And so that, being harder and multiplied, it might ever press upon the woman, there is added the turning to the husband, that there might be a source from which it might again be renewed unto her.

If the Lord was condemning intercourse in his servants, why did he add, to thy husband shall be thy turning? Let no one set up as a punishment the very thing that he condemns, since a punishment is assuredly the contrary of the condemned thing and never proceeds from one and the same source, but on the contrary. But if they were one and the same, no one would fear condemnation. Again, would anyone be sentenced to that from which he saw that a transgression against the law had been committed? This would be to confirm sin, not to punish it. If therefore the woman's turning to the husband is both from the fact that she had intercourse, and not from what is true, let the turning that was simply granted before be believed to have been imposed upon her, together with the utmost servitude, on account of punishment. For since through the woman the man had been made subject — he who without doubt seemed the superior, by whose counsel she had acted and thought that what she urged was profitable, so that the deed of God seemed to be undone by the subtlety of the serpent — the ordinance is restored through the sentence of God, so that the woman may return to the subjection of her condition, humbled to the man, as it had been established; and there is added to her what is added for punishment, when she hears: Filling I will fill up thy sorrows and thy groaning: in sadness shalt thou bring forth sons, and to thy husband shall be thy turning, and he shall have dominion over thee.

Had it been decreed in any other way than that the woman should be subject to the dominion of the man? Hence therefore it appears that the woman was recalled to that which she had been made, with an addition. Whence it says: Filling I will fill up thy sorrows and thy groaning. What does he fill up? He adds in that which is less; he is not seen to work in that which is not. Here therefore the sentence which had gone before, increase and multiply, does not begin unto creating, but unto the ruin of the creature which had been granted. To the woman for the bearing of sons sorrow is added, difficulty is imposed; no new form of procreation is composed. For if from this generation is rightly hoped for, it can be understood that generation came into being rather by the choice of the serpent than of the Lord, and truly it is, as he says, a brood of vipers. But if anyone considers himself born in this way, let him consider what he deserves, seeing that on account of sin the pain from birth was amplified for her, so that she who was to have but a little of the pain of sons, the cause of her offense grew for her, because they are brought forth both with groaning and sorrow, and once born are not held without sadness.

Now for a third time such a sentence is pronounced against the man: "Because," he says, "you have listened to the voice of your wife and have eaten of the tree of which I had commanded you not to eat, of that you have eaten; cursed is the earth in your works, and in your sorrows you shall eat of it all the days of your life: thorns and thistles shall it bring forth for you," and the rest. Here too, that is, Adam, is recalled to that for which he had been made, but with loss. For before he had been set to work the ground simply, so that the result of labor might follow him; but when he scorned the commandment of God, believing that better provision was made for himself by the serpent's counsel than God had made, he was recalled to his former appointment with the cost of labor, so that the earth should answer him but little according to his labor — cursed not for his own sake, but for his works' sake — and so that it might be shown that God's purpose cannot be overturned, nor can anyone provide better than God. For no one can love another's work more than the very one who made it, since the apostle says, "For no one," he says, "hates his own flesh, but nourishes and cherishes it, as Christ also does the Church."

Let us now look at what follows in the law, whether it agrees with its beginnings. Abraham, when he had pleased God, among the other things that pertain to the reward of his faith, when he was already an old man, was even judged worthy to beget a son. How then can it be blamed that this seems to have been granted as a reward — that, because he himself had done God's will, God should fulfill his will? Which will would by no means have been fulfilled had it not been harmless, nor would God fulfill it. For he saw that what was asked wrongly or ignorantly, especially by one who was pleasing to himself — this is not fitting even for a man. Anna too, when she was barren and loved God, asked that she might have a son, and she received one. But if it had been something contrary, she could have been warned by the one whom she loved not to demand a harmful thing. And the most holy Samuel, born of her, begot sons and yet did not diminish the merits of his righteousness. For in his early age he was ever increased, so that in his old age he might be commended by a fuller testimony of God. And Zacharias the priest, a just man, in his old age by God's nod begot a son, and when that son was born he was granted to prophesy. On what grounds, then, is that blamed which is proved to be no hindrance at all? And who would deny that a thing ought to be called good which harms no one?

And that something may be said in this place about the apostles, it bears upon the strength of the case. Certainly holy John was a guardian of holiness; but his fellow disciple, equally holy Peter, is known to have had a wife and children, and, so that he might receive the primacy among the apostles, the begetting of children did not stand in his way. How then is that thought worthy of condemnation which does not hinder one's merits?

Hence the Apostle shows that a man who has a wife, if in other respects he keeps the commandments, can and ought to be made a priest. But if this were unlawful, he certainly could not say that a sinner ought to be made a priest. And what could be so plain? For it is the utterance of the same Apostle, who says: concerning virgins I have no commandment from the Lord. For when the Corinthians were being harassed by heretics, who in hypocrisy were teaching that marriage was to be condemned, they consulted the Apostle by letter as to whether it was permitted to marry or to send away a wife. Then the Apostle instructed that a wife ought not to depart from her husband, though he had had an occasion for saying, if he had known that this was what should be taught, that it was not permitted for a man to marry in any manner whatsoever. What he knew had not been delivered to him, he shows that he cannot teach. Who then among the disciples would dare to teach what has not been delivered by the master, and whom he hears preaching: I wish young women to marry, to bear children?

But perhaps it may be said: 'if it is permitted and good to marry, why is it not permitted for priests to have wives, that is, that those already ordained are not permitted to come together with them?' Who does not know that each single person has a law according to his own status and dignity? For there is something that is altogether not permitted to all generally; there is again something that is permitted to some and not permitted to others; and there is something that at one time is permitted, but at another time is not permitted. To fornicate is never permitted to anyone; but to engage in trade is at one time permitted and at another time not permitted. For before someone is a churchman, it is permitted to him to engage in trade; once he has been made one, it is no longer permitted. And for a Christian to come together with his own wife is at one time permitted, but at another time not permitted. For on account of the days of procession it is at times not permitted to come together, because one must abstain even from what is lawful, so that what is asked for may more easily be obtained. Whence the Apostle: by consent, he says, one must abstain for a time, that you may have leisure for prayer. For also according to the law, on a fast day it is not permitted to strike or to quarrel, though afterward it is permitted, because a greater reverence is owed to the causes of God.

Is everything that is permitted among ordinary people permitted in the presence of the emperor? How much more so in the affairs of God! And for this reason his prelate ought to be purer than the rest, for he is seen to bear the very person of God. For he is his vicar, so that what is permitted to the rest is not permitted to him, because he must daily perform the office of Christ, whether praying for the people, or offering, or baptizing. And this is not permitted to him alone, but also to his minister, because he too ought to be cleaner, since the things he ministers are holy. For just as in comparison with a lamp shadows are not only dark but also foul, while in comparison with the stars a lamp is a murky gloom, and in comparison with the sun the stars are cloudy, while in comparison with the brightness of God the sun is night, so too the things that are permitted and clean for us are, in comparison with the dignity of God, as it were unlawful and unclean; for although they are good, yet they are not fitting for the person of God. Is not the tunic of an ordinary man, however clean, nonetheless foul and unlawful for the emperor? Likewise also, is not a Saxon garment foul and unlawful for a senator? And for this reason the prelates of God ought to be purer than the rest, because they bear the person of Christ; and the ministers of God ought to be cleaner. For no one ministers to the emperor unkempt; therefore they minister clothed in bright, clean garments: but since God is by nature most bright, his ministers ought to be clean by nature rather than by garments.

QUESTIONS [OF SAINT AUGUSTINE] ON THE OLD AND NEW TESTAMENT.

I.

I huius recensionis = I recensionis quaestionum numero CXXVII.

II. AGAINST THOSE WHO DENY THAT ANYTHING PERTAINS TO GOD.

To many, whom this worldly wisdom has blinded, we are exceedingly displeasing, if we anxiously keep God's precepts, if we flee occasions of sinning, if we strive to fulfill all good things with a saving zeal, if we disregard the seductive enticements of allurements and vices, if we allow ourselves to be captured by no worldly sweetness, if of our own accord we undergo tortures and punishments for the name of God, if we despise death itself by the strength of the spirit — displeasing, no doubt, to those whom this mortal sense has persuaded that everything which is done is worthy of neither praise nor blame. For they are unwilling that any of these things should pertain to God.

Some people, indeed, in their eagerness to defend their own sins and crimes, say: "It profits nothing whether you live well or badly. For God has no concern now about this—so as to be anxiously in a hurry to watch you as you conduct yourself in various ways according to your own bent and sickness, and at times to defile his eyes with your foul deeds. All things are empty, which are performed however it has pleased one. He who belongs to God is set apart and removed from these human affairs, and therefore he is neither angered nor moved. He has no contemplation of human affairs, none of our conduct; the whole course of the whirling age and a certain inexplicable rolling of the worldly sphere so seizes, snatches, and casts him about, that whoever supposes he can be recalled to God's care is thought a fool. Nor indeed is the disposition of the martyrs to be approved, who reckon that their God—whom they proclaim to be merciful—takes delight in the shedding of their own blood. Nothing in us is dear to God, nothing hateful; he rejoices in no condition of our death, in no soundness of our health, nor does he desire to know what is done or where, not because it is impossible for the divine majesty to have knowledge of nearly all things that have been done or are being done, but because he is unwilling to know the miseries of men and the vanities of the world. And so, in short, it is written: Vanity of vanities, all is vanity."

Just as their opinion, buttressed by despair, is dissolved by the authority of the divine sense, so it is refuted and overthrown by their own common speech, which is held to be in greatest use. For when, amid dealings and the contracts of various parties, good faith and treachery contend, the divinity is called as witness—the very divinity that is denied to know at times what we do. "He is witness," they say, (God: may God see, may God judge, may God act, may God repay.) And when it comes to the necessity of an oath, and the mind of the one swearing fears lest something befall him if he perjures himself, and he exacts an oath, he trusts that recompense can be made to him by the one who has sworn falsely to him. And when longed-for health and good soundness has been obtained, thanks are rendered to the divinity; and when, upon some material falling, one nearby escapes unharmed by chance, God is said to have granted it as good; and when, roused by the adverse blast of the winds, the waves of the sea rise up and the ship, battered on every side, is shaken, the crowds of those sailing stretch out their hands to heaven, beseech God with suppliant voice, and trust that they can be heard and freed. Whence come these things, if God has no concern for us and is ignorant of what we do?

Empty, therefore, is that persuasion: all things are known to God. Let no one flatter himself concerning the impunity of the crimes he has committed. The greatest fruit of a good life, of a bad one perpetual punishment and eternal torment. Therefore to us, who believe that God knows all things, who devoutly hasten to martyrdom, inestimable fruit—perpetual life—will be granted; but to the impious, who have taught that God does not know their crimes and the secrets of their misdeeds, a consuming flame of fire and a burning ever unfailing will be furnished.

III.

III—XII = II-XI.

XIII.

XIII = XXXV

XIIII.

XIIII-XXXVI = XII-XXXIIII

XXXVII. WHY DID GOD SAY: 'MY SPIRIT SHALL NOT REMAIN IN THESE MEN, BECAUSE THEY ARE FLESH, AND THEIR YEARS SHALL BE A HUNDRED AND TWENTY'?

Since in those days there were giants upon the earth, pestilent and proud, puffed up by the loftiness of their stature, walking after the desires of the flesh, born as apostates, then, since it displeased God, He promised that He would destroy the human race by a flood, and He appointed a time, so that, if perchance anyone, having heard of the wrath of God and His indignation over this matter, should wish to correct himself, he might have space to amend himself. Accordingly, on this reckoning the ark is seen to have been built over a hundred years. But before the beginning of the ark, twenty years are reported to have passed.

But the narrative seems to obscure this, in what it adds after speaking of the number of Noah's years. For this sentence is recognized to have been pronounced before Noah was five hundred years old. For what does it say? My spirit shall not remain in these men. And therefore it also added, because malice increased upon the earth: I will destroy man from the face of the earth, even to the beast — which assuredly was fulfilled when the flood took place. For we recall that even after the flood men lived many years, and Aaron the brother of Moses is recorded to have lived a hundred and twenty-three years. But Moses himself brought it about that he could not pass beyond a hundred and twenty years, because at the contradiction of the water he did not glorify God. For he was about to enter the land of promise, had not sin stood in the way. For it seems to some that this saying pertains to the time of Moses, although Jehoiada the priest is found to have lived a hundred and thirty years.

XXXVIII. WHAT THE BLESSING OF JACOB CONTAINS, WHICH HE GAVE.

Not only from present circumstances did he foretell the future of each single tribe, but he also signified, in the Jewish people, future things concerning things to come, of which as yet there was not even a shadow. For he showed what each single tribe would be in morals, faith, discipline, luxury, wantonness, contempt of the faith, so that, though they were of one stock, they would nevertheless not preserve one character. For some advanced toward the better, others grew worse, others remained as they were, so that through this they might not claim for themselves the prerogative of their descent, since it had been foretold by that very man that certain of these, who assuredly would be of his origin, would be reprobate, indeed that many would perish, so that others were signified as entering in, who to the disgrace and condemnation of those men would be grafted into the nature from which those had fallen away. Therefore he declared that in all the tribes there would be a mixed people, by which variety were to be understood both the good, but few, and the bad, but many. And although he gave the scepter to Joseph his son for a time, he nevertheless set Judah over all, not because all who were of Judah would be worthy, but because the Savior, who is truly king, was to arise from that tribe according to the flesh.

XXXVIIII.

XXXVIIII = XLII

XL. WAS THERE NO OTHER SIGN THAT WAS PERFORMED BY MOSES OPENLY BEFORE PHARAOH EXCEPT THE SERPENT?

Whoever thinks this reprehensible, let him say what else ought to have been done; for he surely made a serpent, one that would both strike terror yet do no harm, in that by a certain sluggishness it is rather slow to injure. For if he had made a lion or a bear or some other such thing, how would those who were present escape? For he did not wish to kill anyone, but only to instill fear and to display power, so that, because the first transgression began through the serpent, likewise also the recollection of the knowledge of God and the amendment of morals might come about by the sign of the serpent, so that, in the manner in which sin was committed, in that same manner it might also be amended; just as also the sin committed through a woman was taken away through a woman, namely Mary the mother of the Lord, from whom Christ, being born, condemned sin. Eve, still a virgin, transgressed, just as Mary too was a virgin. In this way, then, the things that had perished were recalled to their former state, because the kingdom had both been lost through the tasting of the tree and was again sought through the tree. For the Lord, it says, has reigned from the tree. In like manner the world too was reformed in that same [season] in which it was made, that is, at the beginning of the first month, which is the Passover. There will therefore be no evil nature except transgression alone, since sin was amended through that by which it was also committed.

XLI. WHAT IS THE MEANING OF THE FACT THAT IN THE PSALM IT SAYS, 'MEN AND BEASTS YOU WILL SAVE,' AND TO THE PROPHET JONAH HE SAYS, 'I WILL NOT SPARE THE CITY IN WHICH DWELL 120 THOUSAND MEN AND MUCH CATTLE,' BUT ON THE CONTRARY THE APOSTLE SAYS, 'IS IT ABOUT OXEN THAT GOD IS CONCERNED?'

As far as the words are concerned, it seems contrary; but if the case of man and of the cattle be examined, it will be made clear that it is not at all divergent. God takes care of all things as the maker of all, but he does not preserve the cattle for their own sake; rather, for the sake of men he preserves the cattle, not men for the sake of the cattle. And so he who takes care of men guards the cattle, so that his care is not for the oxen but for men. For what hope is there in cattle?

XLII. WHETHER ADAM, WHEN MADE BY GOD AND GIVEN LIFE, RECEIVED THE HOLY SPIRIT, SINCE IT IS WRITTEN: 'GOD BREATHED INTO HIS FACE THE SPIRIT OF LIFE'?

It was not in keeping with order that he should receive the Holy Spirit, because this was reserved by God for later, so that in the last days, when the mystery of the one God was made manifest, it might be given to believers. From the beginning, indeed, the Trinity was proclaimed, but the understanding of it was, as it were, under a veil. For the person of God the Father was first proclaimed and made manifest without any hesitation or veiling, because from him are all things. But the persons of his Son, our Lord Jesus Christ, and of the Holy Spirit were not passed over in silence, yet neither were they made manifest. In short, a question is raised by the faithless concerning the Lord and concerning the Holy Spirit. For certain madmen say that the Holy Spirit is the Father himself, just as Sabellius too says that the very one whom he calls the Son is the Father. But concerning the person of God the Father no one is in doubt.

When, therefore, the manifestation of the Trinity takes place, then at last the Holy Spirit is given to believers, so that both his person may be made manifest and those who receive him may have a sign that they are sons of God, through the fact that they have within themselves the Spirit of God, who is assuredly from God. For this is the signification of perfection, when it is known that there is both a Father and a Son and a Holy Spirit. This perfection bestows this gift. For the Son of God, coming and disclosing and manifesting these things, was bound to give something more, and it was fitting that he bestow this perfection upon believers, because perfection or salvation is not in the Son alone nor in the Father alone, but in the name of the Father and of the Son and of the Holy Spirit. Whence John the Evangelist says that of his fullness we have all received, grace for grace, because the law was given through Moses, but grace and truth came through Christ, the law's fulfillment.

It is made plain that, with the coming of the Savior, the fullness of truth was accomplished; for the things which lay hidden and that which was promised, when it is revealed and made manifest, become the fullness of truth. Then believers, having received the Holy Spirit, become sons of God. For the inspiration, which is read to have been made by God into Adam, is the handing over of a soul. For "spirit" is frequently put for "soul," as the Gospel says among other things: "and," it says, "her spirit returned to her"; and in the psalm: "a spirit," it says, "afflicted, and a contrite heart, God does not spurn." Frequently, however, some manuscripts do not read thus: "and God inspired," but: "God breathed into his face the spirit of life, and man was made into a living soul." Not because he said "the spirit of life" did he thereby signify the Holy Spirit; for he also says thus concerning the animals: "in which was the spirit of life." It is called the spirit of life because it animates bodies unto life.

XLIII.

XLIII = XXXVI

XLIIII.

XLIIII = XXXVII

XLV. "HE HAS SET," IT SAYS, "HIS TABERNACLE IN THE SUN," THAT IS, HIS BODY, IN WHICH CHRIST DWELT, BECAUSE HE WAS BEATEN WITH SCOURGES BY PILATE: HOW THEN, IN THE PSALM, CONCERNING CHRIST: "THE SCOURGE SHALL NOT DRAW NEAR TO YOUR TABERNACLE," SEEING THAT HE CALLED HIS BODY A TEMPLE?

Both the son of man and the Son of God is understood to have a body as a temple. God, however, that is, the Son of God, although he came in the flesh, nevertheless dwelt in the soul; therefore both the soul and the body are the tabernacle of the Son of God, even though the Son of God and Son of man is one God and man. Therefore in this respect the scourge is said not to have drawn near to his tabernacle, that is, to his soul, in which his divinity dwells: it was immune from the punishment of hell or of its princes. Lest, because here he was crucified as though a sinner, though he is innocent, and the flesh suffered something of sorrow, his soul should be thought to be among those below, Scripture shows that his soul could not be troubled among those below, since he descended for this purpose, that is, permitted himself to suffer, in order to confound the darkness and to send back the princes of those regions dulled. Whence the Lord himself also says: My soul is sorrowful even unto death. For it was permitted, as belonged to his nature, that he should be sorrowful, forthwith bringing back the trophies and the spoils of those by whom it had been made sorrowful even unto death, after death now established in eternal joy. It can also be understood of the whole man, since he who knew no sin could not be scourged by the law.

XLVI. WHY, WHEN ADAM WAS PLACED IN THE WORLD, DID HE RECEIVE A COMMANDMENT OR LAW, THOUGH HE HIMSELF HELD DOMINION OVER THE REST?

Although Adam was placed as lord in the world, nevertheless, because he assuredly did not have it from himself but received it from God, he ought to have received a sign of this fact from a law, so that he himself, who is seen to be lord, might through the law be made subject to him who had given him this power — that the authority of the Creator might beget reverence in him through the law, lest so great a dominion should exalt him and he should become forgetful of God his maker.

XLVII. THE OLD LAW ALLEGES THAT GOD SWORE — FOR THUS IT SAYS: 'THREE TIMES HAVE I SWORN BY MY OWN SELF, SAITH THE LORD' —, BUT THE SAVIOR FORBADE SWEARING: HOW DID HE NOT DESTROY THE OLD THINGS?

Before the knowledge of God existed on earth, it was fitting to summon men to an unheard-of hope in no other way than that a promise should be made to them by an oath, made assuredly by him whom they did not know as was fitting. But when he began to be known, it was assuredly not fitting that he should swear, whom it would not be right to suppose could lie. Therefore the Lord commanded that not even his servants should any longer swear, but that they should so establish themselves that trust might be placed in their words. For the sacrament of an oath demands either the treachery or the deceit of an inconstant person, so that, because they are wont to deceive men, they may perhaps out of fear reverence God; or else it will be enough even for him who is deceived, since from this he acquires the offense.

XLVIII. WHY, IN THE LAW, WAS IT SAID EVEN TO AARON HIMSELF THAT HE SHOULD OFFER HOLOCAUSTS FOR HIS OWN SINS, WHEN DAVID SAYS: 'FOR OFFENSES THOU HAST NOT REQUIRED A HOLOCAUST' AND IN WHAT FOLLOWS: 'THOU WILT NOT BE DELIGHTED WITH HOLOCAUSTS.' HOW DID HE COMMAND THAT TO BE OFFERED IN WHICH HE IS NOT DELIGHTED?

It is manifest that God does not take delight in holocausts, but that pardon can be earned by the groaning and grief of the heart in satisfaction for sin. He willed, moreover, the sacrifice to be a testimony of sin, so that through the offering of the sacrifice the sinner might confess that he had sinned. But in what way remission was to be obtained, he himself shows in another place, saying: break your bread to the one who is hungry; if you see one naked, clothe him, and you shall not despise the members of your own household, or certainly: a contrite and humbled heart God does not spurn. First, therefore, he admonished that the offense must be confessed, then he showed in what way it can be pardoned. For he never commanded a sacrifice for a manifest offense, but for things done in ignorance, because a man sins even when he does not know it and, when he thinks he is acting rightly, he transgresses through a certain intention. Such a sin he said could be purged through sacrifice.

XLVIIII. WHY DID SOLOMON HAVE THE SPIRIT OF WISDOM, WHEN HE DID NOT HAVE A PURE LIFE? FOR HE TOOK EXCEEDING DELIGHT IN WOMEN AND THROUGH THIS SINNED.

First, by his own merit he received the spirit of prudence, but afterward, once he began to make excessive use of women and through this to sin, if he had the spirit of wisdom, he had it by the merit of his kingship, just as Nebuchadnezzar too, by the merit of his kingship, saw Christ in the furnace.

L. IN TOBIT: 'IT IS HONORABLE,' HE SAYS, 'TO REVEAL AND CONFESS THE WORKS OF GOD'; BUT THE SAVIOR, WHILE DOING A WORK OF GOD: 'TELL NO ONE,' HE SAYS.

The Savior did not say that the works of God ought to be concealed — for in another place he says: go, and tell how much God has done for you — but he sometimes refused their testimony and repressed their boasting, lest he should seem, what is absurd to prudent men, himself to bear testimony to himself like a seducer.

LI. WHY WERE CIRCUMCISION AND THE PRECEPTS GIVEN TO THE PEOPLE, WHICH FORMERLY DID NOT EXIST NOR ARE NOW HELD IN AUTHORITY?

Circumcision is a sign of the faith of Abraham, so that, when this sign was seen in his sons, his sons might be known to be his who, believing in God, had received this sign; but the precepts of the sabbaths were given both as a testimony of things past and of things to come, of which they would hold the figure. For this sabbath, which is fulfilled in the completed week, is a figure of the future sabbath, which will give rest in the eternal things; but the law of foods was not given at the beginning of the law, but when, with faithlessness as their leader, they were unwilling to give faith to the words and promises of God, they received, according to the hardness of their heart, precepts that were not good, as the prophet Jeremiah says. For this was done so that their neck might be pressed down for their advancement — for the whole of creation ought not to have been subjected to proud and stubborn men — but afterward, when the mercy of God came, the liberty of eating was restored, whence the apostle Peter says: why do you impose a yoke upon the neck of the brethren, which neither we nor our fathers were able to bear?

LII.

LII = XXXVIII

LIII.

LIII = XXXVIIII

LIIII. IN WISDOM: 'WHO CREATED,' HE SAYS, 'THE WORLD OUT OF UNSEEN MATTER' AND ON THE CONTRARY: 'BECAUSE WE WERE MADE OUT OF NOTHING.'

The sense of the scriptures declares that God created the confused substances simultaneously. With the darkness indeed thoroughly mingled in, he called this confusion of things — that is, air, water, and dark earth — an unseen matter, as is read in Genesis: "But the earth was," he says, "invisible and unformed." From this confusion, therefore, he created the world by establishing the firmament, so that within it, with the waters gathered together into one, a dwelling place might be made for the human race. For with the substances distinguished and separated, he made a habitable house in the hollow.

LV.

LV. LVI = XL. XLI

QUESTIONS ON THE NEW TESTAMENT.

I.

I = XLVIII.

II.

II = LXXXVII

III. WHY WERE THE LORD'S DEEDS AND WORDS ARRANGED INTO SCRIPTURE IN FOUR VOLUMES AND BY FOUR WRITERS?

It was fitting that the acceptable year of the Lord, as the prophet says, be contained by four volumes, as though by four turns of season. For just as the year unfolds through four seasons in such a way that they have need of one another, so also the deeds and words of the Lord have been set down in four books, in such a way that one has need of the other; yet together they are made perfect in their fullness. And that they should be arranged by four writers, this was the reason: that, since the names of the seasons are diverse, the names of the gospels too should be diverse. And if any things seem contrary in their words, yet they do not disagree in sense, through an interpretation fitted to the matter — just as the seasons, though they seem indeed diverse in name, in air, and in the course of the stars, yet do not disagree in the effect of the things that are brought forth.

IIII. SINCE IT IS ESTABLISHED THAT FOUR BOOKS OF THE LORD'S DEEDS AND WORDS WERE DULY COMPOSED, IT MUST BE KNOWN WHAT THEIR ORDER IS.

The gospel is gathered by arrangement rather than by time. Matthew, therefore, must be placed first, because he took his beginning from the promise itself, that is, from Abraham, to whom the promise of the incarnation of our Lord Jesus Christ was made. After him Luke, because he narrates in what manner this incarnation came about. Third, Mark, who testifies that the gospel, which was preached by Christ, was promised in the law. But fourth, John, because he shows with open voice that this one — who was promised to Abraham as about to be incarnated, and of whom Luke told in what manner he was incarnated, and of whom Mark showed that his gospel was preached according to the prophecy of Isaiah — is God, saying: "In the beginning was the Word, and the Word was with God, and the Word was God."

V. WHY, IN DESCRIBING THE GOSPEL OF CHRIST, DOES MATTHEW SAY: 'THE BOOK OF THE GENERATION OF JESUS CHRIST, SON OF DAVID,' WHEN ABRAHAM IS PRIOR?

For this reason he thus began, so as to speak first about him, describing the genealogy from the promise of his incarnation, just as the apostle says: whose are the fathers, and from whom Christ came according to the flesh. Therefore he said: the book of generation, because the incarnation of Christ consists of many and diverse elements, so that — since from one man diverse people began to be — he might draw his bodily substance from all: from Jews and gentiles, and from the worthy and the unworthy, since Ruth was a Moabitess and Bathsheba was made a wife after being an adulteress, so that, drawing the flesh of all to himself, he might recall them to their native unity. And for this reason he says: of Jesus Christ, the son of David, although Abraham is earlier, because Christ is called son of David in a special way on account of the kingdom, so that, just as God from God, so also he might take his origin as king from a king according to the flesh. For thus it was said to David: of the fruit of thy womb I will set upon thy throne.

VI. WHY IS IT THAT MATTHEW THE APOSTLE, MENTIONED ABOVE, DIVIDED ALL THE GENERATIONS INTO THREE PARTS?

Causes and times produce the divisions; therefore he marked off from Abraham up to David. For the first part of the cause is from Abraham up to David, because Abraham is the father of faith and David the father of the kingdom — for Saul, having been rendered unworthy, deprived himself of the kingdom —; but from David up to the deportation is another part of the cause, in which the Jews, having been made reprobate, lost the kingdom; and after the deportation of Babylon up to Christ is the third part, of lamentation and misery, of their captivity and dispersion, because, although after the deportation of Babylon and the period of seventy years, when they were released by Cyrus and returned to their own land, yet they never had any pause or rest. For neither could Judea reign afterward, but they were always anxious and wandering about. For assuredly at the time of Christ's nativity they were captives, saying: we have no king but Caesar. For this cause, then, Matthew made three stages of the generation, in order to show the changes both of merits and of causes from the promise that was made up to Christ, but that the seasons of the years continue up to the end in one grace.

VII. WHY, SINCE THERE ARE FORTY-ONE GENERATIONS, DOES THE EVANGELIST SEEM TO HAVE COUNTED FORTY-TWO? FOR WHAT DO THREE TIMES FOURTEEN MAKE BUT FORTY-TWO?

According to the number, forty-one generations are counted, but according to the reckoning, forty-two are proved.

Now Jechoniah was begotten during the transmigration, that is, he was made king, as is contained in Chronicles; he brought the second part to an end, and, because after the transmigration he remained in the kingship by the permission of King Nebuchadnezzar, from him begins the third part, which runs up to Christ. And therefore he is counted twice, so that Jechoniah both concludes the second part and inaugurates the third. For it stands thus: and after the transmigration of Babylon Jechoniah begot Salathiel. Now up to this Salathiel there reigned those who are from Judah, but from whose root Joseph is born. For Jechoniah had a first son by the name of Asyr, but since Joseph traces his origin through the root of Salathiel, Asyr being passed over, Salathiel was joined to Jechoniah his father, so that one might arrive at Joseph, to whom the Virgin Mary had been betrothed. For after Josiah too Jechoniah follows, but because through Joachim the father of Jechoniah one arrives at Joseph, that one, that is Joachim, being passed over, he placed Jechoniah, so as not to exceed the number of fourteen generations, and because after Jechoniah comes Salathiel and his son, through whom Joseph arises.

VIII.

VIII = LXXXV

VIIII.

VIIII = XLVIIII

X.

X = L

XI.

XI = LIIII

XII.

XII = LII

XIII.

XIII = LI

XIIII.

XIIII = LIII

XV.

XV = LV.

XVI.

XVI = LVI

XVII. WHY DID THE SAVIOR, AFTER HIS BAPTISM, FAST FORTY DAYS AND AFTERWARD HUNGER? FOR HE WHO HAD FASTED FORTY DAYS — COULD HE NOT NOW HAVE BEEN WITHOUT HUNGER?

It is written: Son, when you draw near to the service of God, stand in justice and in fear, and prepare your soul for temptation. And therefore the Savior fasted, in order to give us a pattern, so that in like manner we too, applying ourselves to fasts, may by God's help overcome every movement of the devil set against us, knowing by the Savior's example that then the more the snares of the adversary are to be guarded against, when we draw near to the fear of God. For he grieves that we have withdrawn from him; therefore he rages the more against us. This the Savior did for us, not for himself.

Likewise also, that he hungered was not on his own account, but on ours. For when by fasts the temptations of the devil had been overcome — which for that reason were not written down, because they do not pertain to profit — afterward, that is, after the forty days, he permitted himself to suffer hunger, so that — since it belonged to man — he might suffer hunger, so that the devil, seeing it, who had already been overcome, might by the weakness of hunger in him be incited again to tempt, seeing that he was a man, by whom he was being conquered.

For this was the mystery of the Savior: that, because [Satan] insulted and lorded it over conquered man, he should be conquered by man, so that the power of God might win the victory for man. Thus Satan was rendered all the more contemptible, since he both beholds a lowly man and perceives a power that is not man's. For, dulled by amazement, he marveled that there was present a mystery which lay hidden from him, such that there was power to approach, but no power to entrap. For he was tormented by two causes. For, seeing the weakness, he approached, and he found power, so that, beholding a man, he was made suspicious about the power of God. To this end, therefore, He hungered: to mock the cunning of Satan. But He did not fast any longer, so that He might be in accord with Moses and Elijah.

XVIII. IF 'THE LAW AND THE PROPHETS WERE UNTIL JOHN,' FROM WHOM THE KINGDOM OF HEAVEN WAS PROCLAIMED — FOR HE IS THE INITIATOR OF THE NEW PREACHING — WHY DID HIS BAPTISM CEASE?

The baptism begun by John did not cease, but that which was lacking to it was added. For John only baptized; he did not give the Holy Spirit to those who believed, just as he himself says concerning the Savior: I indeed baptize you in water unto repentance, but he shall baptize you in the Holy Spirit. This is what he means: *through me is the remission of sins, yet the Holy Spirit is not given, so that the purified might be called sons; for this was reserved to the Savior, inasmuch as He is Lord, that they should not become sons of God unless they had received the Holy Spirit from the Son of God'. The effect, therefore, of John's baptism — which he worked silently, without questioning, yet not without mention of the Savior, so that what he did might be firm — is the gift of the Trinity, which the condescension of the Savior displayed in giving the form of the three names, which from the beginning under the name or person of the one God wrought a single and undivided work. An interpretation, therefore, was added to John's baptism, with the signifying of the mystery that had long lain hidden; and a further gift was added, so that the baptized, having received the Holy Spirit, might be sons of God. It was therefore amplified, not emptied.

XVIIII. WHY ARE 'THE LAW AND THE PROPHETS UNTIL JOHN' RIGHTLY SAID TO HAVE CEASED? BECAUSE HE OF WHOM THEY PROPHESIED HAS NOW COME. WHY 'THE LAW UNTIL JOHN,' WHEN THE APOSTLE SAYS THAT WE OUGHT TO BE SUBJECT TO THE LAW? 'FOR THE THINGS THAT ARE,' HE SAYS, 'ARE ORDAINED BY GOD.'

Of the Law there is indeed one name, but it has a threefold meaning. For the first part of the Law is about God; for if the Law is named from choosing (lectio), so that out of many things you may know what to choose, then men, being set amid errors, received it so that they might choose the true, that is, that rejecting the devil they might choose God. But the second part of the Law is this: the one contained in six precepts, which begins thus: Honor father and mother. The third, however, lies in the new moons and in the Sabbath, and in the distinguishing and choosing of foods, and in circumcision, and in the offering of victims of cattle. This Law, therefore, he says is to be kept up until John, not beyond. For it was given in such a way that, when its time was completed, it should cease. For it was not from the beginning, but a cause for it was given for a time, so that at the coming of the Savior the Law should cease. Therefore, concerning God there remains both the Law of the precepts and its nature, which is without doubt from him and cannot be abolished. For through it one advances toward the promised reward. For fear begets carefulness.

XX.

XX = LXVIIII

XXI.

XXI = LX

XXII. THE SABBATH IS CERTAINLY THE LAW, OR A PART OF THE LAW: HOW THEN IS THE LAW NOT VOIDED, WHEN THE SABBATH HAS BEEN DISSOLVED, SINCE THE EVANGELIST SAYS: 'BECAUSE HE NOT ONLY BROKE THE SABBATH' AND THE REST?

The Savior did indeed break the Sabbath, but without prejudice to the Sabbath that had been given. For while the time of the Sabbath was now ceasing, yet the Jews asserted that the Sabbath still remained, the Savior, by working on the Sabbaths and by commanding the sick man to carry his bed on the Sabbaths, was breaking the Sabbath according to the understanding of the Jews. For according to the faith of the truth, how is such a thing valid if it be said of what had already ceased—just as it is if it be said of someone who is to be called a former prefect and has suffered a wrong, because the prefecture has suffered a wrong? Therefore the Sabbath was not dissolved, but the men who imprudently claimed the authority of the Sabbath were deluded, since a new law had already succeeded the Sabbath.

XXIII. WHY DID THE SAVIOR CALL HIS DISCIPLES SONS OF THUNDER, WHO WOULD SEEM RATHER TO BE CALLED SONS OF GOD? FOR THUNDERS ARE SAID TO BE PRODUCED BY THE COLLISION OF CLOUDS.

Since thunders strike terror, for this reason the Lord called the disciples sons of thunder, as it were the sons of him who is to be feared. For although thunders are produced by the collision of clouds, yet they are produced by the will of God and are terrible.

XXIIII.

XXIIII = LVIIII

XXV.

XXV = LXI

XXVI.

XXVI = LXIII

XXVII.

XXVII = LXII

XXVIII. IT IS MANIFEST THAT THE SAVIOR, BEING BORN THE SON OF GOD, WAS ALSO CALLED CHRIST: WHY THEN, AFTER THE BAPTISM, DID THE TEMPTER APPROACH HIM SAYING: 'IF YOU ARE THE SON OF GOD' AND THE REST?

Thus the Savior was born from a virgin in such a way that he was both Christ and the Son of God, not by manufacture, but by birth. But while still in his younger age, emptying his power — that is, concealing it from his authority — he did not provoke the impudence of the devil. But when he had been baptized, with the Holy Spirit descending upon him, since it had been made plain to men by the testimony of God the Father who he was, the enemy was led by the rivalry of jealousy to envy, seeing that this deed stood against his own interest; for he understood that the ordinance of baptism had been provided for the salvation of men. And therefore he approaches the Savior, the author of this thing, not to approve it, but to find an opening for overturning it. For temptation sometimes takes place for proving, sometimes cunningly for subversion. For he supposed that he had, as it were, drawn out from the Savior — according to the guile of his own will — an answer whereby he might possess his whole kingdom, secure that, with the leader of the Savior's teaching brought into subjection, no one would escape his death.

XXVIIII. WHY DID THE SAVIOR RESIST THE DEVIL WHO TEMPTED HIM IN NO OTHER WAY THAN BY THE EXAMPLES OF THE LAW?

The Savior answered not only the devil who tempted him, but also the Jews, through whom he carried out the impiety of his cruelty against the Savior. For, foreknowing that the Jews would call him an enemy of the law, he restrained the impudence of their father [the devil] by the testimonies of the law, so that he might condemn the father in the sons and the sons in the father.

XXX.

XXX = LVII.

XXXI.

XXXI = LVIII.

XXXII. THE GOSPEL TESTIFIES THAT «NO ONE HAS SEEN GOD»: BUT AGAINST THIS, JACOB AND MOSES AND ISAIAH ASSERT THAT THEY SAW GOD. BUT SOMEONE SAYS: «NO ONE HAS SEEN THE FATHER». WHAT OF IT? IF THE SON WAS SEEN, THE FATHER TOO WAS SEEN, SINCE INDEED THE FATHER AND THE SON ARE BOTH ONE GOD, BOTH IN SUBSTANCE AND IN IMAGE, BECAUSE THE IMAGE OF THE TWO IS ONE, AND, AS HE HIMSELF SAYS: «HE WHO SEES ME, SEES THE FATHER ALSO.» HOW THEN HAS NO ONE SEEN THE FATHER GOD, WHEN THE SON SAYS THAT THE FATHER IS SEEN WHEN HE HIMSELF IS SEEN, SINCE NEITHER IS THERE ANOTHER GOD? IF THEREFORE THERE IS NOT ANOTHER, HE HIMSELF IS THE SAME, BECAUSE HE IS ONE, GOD WAS SEEN.

As far as it pertains to the evangelist, he said this concerning God the Father, that no one has seen God, except the only-begotten Son, who is in the bosom of the Father, he himself has declared him. Let us believe the Son; for he himself has declared that no one, except himself, has seen God. But he said this for this reason, to teach that he himself had always appeared to the patriarchs and prophets. This, therefore, does not pertain to the one God, but to the person of the Father, of whom it cannot be said otherwise than God the Father. But the Son says that he was seen in such a way that he is nevertheless invisible, in that very thing, when he is thought to be seen. For here "seen" is understood, because he was surely not seen with the eyes, but understood with the mind, and this was to have seen — to have understood — in that which appeared that God was there present. But when the Savior says: he who sees me, sees the Father also, he by no means referred this to the sight of the eyes, but to that of the mind, and so that nothing might be thought to be different between the Father and the Son. Neither of them, therefore, was seen according to substance; but in the surrounding appearance the Son alone was seen with the mind, not with carnal eyes, because he is invisible just as the Father.

XXXIII. HOW IS IT THAT THE SAVIOR, AT FIRST, DENIED MERCY TO THE FOREIGN WOMAN — THAT IS, THE CANAANITE — WHEN HE DID NOT REFUSE THE BENEFIT OF GRANTING HEALTH EITHER TO THE FOREIGN CENTURION OR TO THE LEPER, WHOM JESUS HIMSELF CALLED A FOREIGNER?

The reason itself indicates that the Savior's deed was fitting. For it was absurd, and it worked to the injury of the promise made to the fathers, if a woman who had not confessed the God of the Jews were to receive the benefit promised to a people confessing God. Therefore at the outset it was denied to her. But when, with her neck bowed, she did not refuse faith to the Savior's words, but confessed that the Jews who believe are the sons, while the gentiles are dogs or servants — through which she joined herself to the Lord's faith, because servants point to a master, and a master is not without servants — a unity was made of the one subjected and the one subjecting. Hence it is that she deserved to obtain what she prayed for. The centurion, however, who received the benefit from the Savior without delay, had long before steeped himself in the things of God. For the elders of the Jews say to the Lord: he is worthy that you should grant this to him; for he loves our nation, and he himself has built us a synagogue. But the Lord called the leper a foreigner, not by faith, but by nation. For he was a Samaritan, and it is established that they are by origin descended from the Babylonians. And yet even this man did not obtain the benefit without confession, because it had been said to the disciples by the Lord: Go not into the way of the gentiles, and enter not into the city of the Samaritans, but go rather to the sheep that have perished of the house of Israel; that is, that it should not be preached to the Samaritans and the gentiles before the Jews, whose this promise had been. But when the Jews began to reject the faith of Christ, then the Savior offered himself both to the Samaritan woman and, after the cross, to the centurion Cornelius; but when he was entreated by the Canaanite woman, he received her, because it was not yet the time that salvation should be offered to her.

XXXIIII.

XXXIIII = LXIIII,

XXXV.

XXXV = LXV

XXXVI.

XXXVI = LXVII

XXXVII.

XXXVII = LXVI

XXXVIII.

XXXVIII = LXVIII

XXXVIIII. THE APOSTLE PETER SAYS: 'CHRIST DIED FOR US,' BUT THE APOSTLE PAUL ASSERTS THAT HE ALSO DIED FOR HIMSELF: HE WAS, HE SAYS, 'OBEDIENT UNTO DEATH; ON ACCOUNT OF WHICH GOD GAVE HIM A NAME WHICH IS ABOVE EVERY NAME.' AND IF THIS IS SO, HE WILL SEEM TO HAVE BEEN IMPERFECT, HE WHO THROUGH HIS OWN WORKS MADE AN INCREASE.

That the Son of God was born perfect from God, no faithful person doubts. For he received all things of the paternal divinity in being born of God the Father. Then, therefore, he received the name which is above every name — that is, that he should be called what the Father is, God. For nothing is said to be future with him; for he has all things before himself. Therefore he was born to create and restore all these things. For surely order and reason hold this, that to the paternal name every knee should bow. This the Father granted to the Son on account of those things which he was going to do; and he granted it when he begot him. For he begot him in such a way that he should be in the same honor in which the Father himself is.

XL.

XL = LXXII.

XLI.

XLI = LXXIII

XLII. JOHN TO THE LORD: 'ARE YOU THE ONE WHO IS TO COME, OR DO WE AWAIT ANOTHER?'

Under his own person he pleads the cause of his disciples. For John did not doubt, he who had said: behold the lamb of God, behold him who takes away the sin of the world; but, in order to satisfy his disciples, he sends them with his own words, so that he might confirm by the Savior's own mouth what he himself was teaching about him, so that after his own departure his disciples might follow Christ without any doubt.

XLIII. HOW IS IT THAT, THOUGH THE SAVIOR HAD HEALED ALMOST ALL BY A WORD, HE NEVERTHELESS RESTORED THE BLIND MAN'S EYES BY MEANS OF MUD MADE FROM SPITTLE?

The Savior did this for the confounding of those who accuse the creation. For when he mended the defect — an accident — of the body in no other way than the Creator had made it, he commended the author of this work. For he mended it by means of that from which it had been fashioned. For everything that comes to be, if when it has fallen it is restored by means of that from which it had been made, will be proved to have been rightly made. For if the Savior attests himself to be God by these things, because he mended the damages and defects of the body, how much more is he God who is the inventor of the body itself!

XLIIII. HOW IS IT THAT, WHEN HE WAS ABOUT TO PERFORM AN ADMIRABLE AND UNKNOWN MIRACLE — NAMELY, ABOUT TO RAISE LAZARUS FROM THE DEAD — HE WEPT, OR SOUGHT THE PLACE WHERE HE HAD BEEN LAID AS IF IGNORANT OF IT?

The Savior is God and man, and through this he always played two roles, so that he wept because he had the affection of a man, but, because he was also God, he raised up this man whom he had wept over. Therefore one always comes from the least things to the greater, so that, because he was seen to be a man, he might prove himself to be that which he appeared; but he asserted himself to be God by his works.

XLV. HOW IS IT THAT ABOVE WE READ THAT HEROD HAD DIED, BUT BELOW, AND AFTER MANY YEARS, THAT HEROD KILLED JOHN, THOUGH ABOVE, AFTER HEROD'S DEATH, JOHN HAD OUTLIVED HIM?

Herod was king of Judea; he had four sons — Archelaus, Herod, Philip, and Lysanias; but when Herod their father died, Archelaus succeeded him, and when he died the kingdom of the aforesaid Herod was divided into four parts, so that Pilate was admitted into the fourth part of the kingdom as a governor, not as a king, while the sons of Herod were called kings. Then, when Philip in turn had died, Herod his brother took his wife, concerning whom he was rebuked by John the Baptist, for which reason Herod, the son of the aforementioned Herod, killed him. For what does it say? Herod, it says, the tetrarch, that is, one placed in the fourth part of the kingdom of his father Herod. Why then is there any doubt, when the meaning of the added surname shows this to be another Herod? For he is the very one who also killed with the sword James, the brother of John, who afterwards, struck by an angel of God, expired teeming with worms.

XLVI.

XLVI = LXXXVI

XLVII.

XLVII = LXXV.
