XLVIII. WHY DOES THE APOSTLE SAY THAT HE MADE HIMSELF ALL THINGS TO ALL MEN, WHICH SEEMS TO BE THE ACT OF A FLATTERER AND A HYPOCRITE?

That flatterer is the one who gives his consent for this reason: that he may not offend the one whom he desires to keep favorable to himself. For whoever does something on account of a stumbling-block — something not indeed dangerous, but superfluous — is concerned for the salvation of the one whom he does not wish to suffer a stumbling-block. When Paul the apostle circumcised Timothy on account of the Jews and, having been purified, entered into the temple, he assuredly showed himself to be such a man precisely so that those who were zealous for the ancestral tradition might not, having suffered a stumbling-block, either kill him or suppose our religion to be hostile to themselves. He was therefore willing to concede less, that he might be able to gain more. For he could have sinned gravely if he had not made himself a Jew in going into the temple to pray; he therefore subjected himself, that he might profit them. And with those who were under the law, that is, the Samaritans, he consented on this ground, on which it is agreed that they rightly confess those books of Moses to be from God, and circumcision and the sabbaths to have been given by God. And by these things he was showing them the Christ, whom they hoped to be this very one whom he was preaching. For thus you have it when the Samaritan woman says to the Lord: I know that the Messiah is coming; when therefore he shall have come, he will announce all things to us. According to this sense, then, he was pursuing the words of the books of Moses, who said that the Lord your God will raise up for you a prophet from among your brethren. But with those who were without the law, he assented in this respect, because they say that the world was made by God, and the race of men. For he says: as certain ones among you have said: for we too are of his race. Thus therefore he became all things to all, that he might further their salvation.

XLVIIII.

XLVIIII = LXXVI

L. SINCE WE PROFESS THE SAVIOR TO HAVE BEEN BORN, HOW IS IT THAT THE APOSTLE SAYS HE WAS MADE FROM THE SEED OF DAVID, WHEN IT IS ONE THING TO BE MADE AND ANOTHER TO BE BORN?

Although in this passage the making may be understood as the making of a birth — for a making does indeed differ from a generation, but in other cases, not where the reckoning of flesh and body is at issue —, nevertheless the apostle did not without cause speak in words of this kind, because in another place also he says: made from a woman. Therefore by this saying he signified something. For since the flesh of the Lord was not compacted in the womb of the Virgin from human seed and made into a body, but by the working and power of the Holy Spirit, for this reason the apostle spoke thus. For it is one thing to coagulate blood and to generate with seed mingled in, and another to procreate not by admixture but by power. Therefore he said made, because it was unbegotten.

LI. WHY DID THE APOSTLE SAY TO THE GALATIANS, WHOM HE SO REBUKES AND CONVICTS THROUGHOUT THE WHOLE EPISTLE: 'BE', HE SAYS, 'AS I ALSO AM, BECAUSE I AM AS YOU'? IF THEREFORE HE SHOWED HIMSELF TO BE SUCH AS THEY WERE, IT WAS SUPERFLUOUS TO SAY: 'BE AS I ALSO AM'; OR PERHAPS IN CERTAIN THINGS HE HAD BEGUN TO BE AN IMITATOR OF THEM, AND IN CERTAIN THINGS HE WISHES THEM TO BE IMITATORS OF HIMSELF?

Which of them could imitate the apostle? Or what did that teacher of the nations lack? But he urges them to be imitators of him, and since it is not impossible, he thus rebukes them when he says: 'because I am what you are. For you are men, just as I too am; therefore do what I do.' It can also be referred to this, namely that since he says he became all things to all men by way of assent, in those matters in which they did not differ from the truth, especially the Gentiles. For there was none of those things which by now ought least to be done, but which formerly had been rightly done, such as circumcision, the observance of the sabbaths, of new moons, of foods. But if he approved anything of the nations, he shows that it ought not now to cease. For they say that the world was fashioned by God and for men — this always stands —: if therefore in these matters he became one consenting with them, just as they also do, he urges them that in a similar way these too may become, just as he also is, consenting to his teaching.

LII. IN WHAT WAY WE ARE FREE IN CHOICE AND WILL, WHEN THE APOSTLE SAYS: 'THE FLESH LUSTS AGAINST THE SPIRIT, BUT THE SPIRIT AGAINST THE FLESH, SO THAT YOU DO NOT DO THE THINGS THAT YOU WISH'? AND IF THE FLESH LUSTS AGAINST THE SPIRIT, IT IS EVIL; FOR THE SPIRIT SUGGESTS GOOD THINGS.

The flesh) In this place you are not to understand the substance of the flesh, but the evil acts and the perfidy signified in the flesh. For inasmuch as every error is from things visible and worldly, and the flesh is akin to them — for it is composed of the elements —, he calls every error 'flesh.' He calls works of the flesh not only adulteries and fornications and debaucheries, but also idolatry and sorceries and blasphemies and such things. Does the flesh compel blasphemy or idolatry? Are these not vices of the soul, while it consents to error? But to those who act rightly he says that they are not in the flesh, but in the spirit, and yet assuredly they are still in the body. He therefore who walks well and does not despair of the hope of the faithful, though placed in the flesh, is said not to be in the flesh; but he who acts badly and is a blasphemer is said to be in the flesh, because, just as those who act well are spiritual together with the flesh itself, so those who live badly are carnal together with the soul itself. For this is what is meant by what he says.

This error, then, which he calls 'flesh,' lusts against the spirit, that is, it suggests evil things against that same spirit, which is the law of God. For he introduces two laws, that of God and that of the devil. Hence he speaks of the spirit, for the reason that the law of God, which is spiritual, fights against the flesh, that is, against the vices, in order to preserve man for God; but the law of the devil, which is error, contradicts through the enticements of luxury and worldly sweetness. Between these, then, which are at war, stands man in the middle, who, when he consents to the spirit, the flesh does not wish it; but when he gives his hand to the flesh, he spurns the spirit, that is, he despises the law of God. Him, therefore, who consents to the flesh the spirit calls back, lest he do what he wishes; but him who assents to the spirit the flesh solicits, lest he do what it thinks useful. But the spirit rightly and providently contradicts and calls back, in order to snatch man away from the counsels of Satan; whereas the flesh, which is the adverse law, solicits and resists the law of the spirit for this reason, that through deceit it may deceive.

Therefore the apostle makes these things public in order to show to human choice the thing to which it should commit its will—not to empty out the choice of liberty, but he teaches choice the thing to which it should join itself. But if there were no choice of the will, then neither the law of the devil, which is the flesh, nor the law of God, which is the spirit, would, by opposing one another, be plying man with their counsels. For he who plies, persuades; and he who persuades, does not apply force but circumvents; and he who is circumvented—his will is changed by certain deceptions. But if there were no free choice, man would be dragged unwilling to those things which he does not will.

LIII. FOR WHAT REASON IS THE WISDOM OF THE FLESH AN ENEMY TO GOD, OR WHAT IS THAT WISDOM WHICH IS NOT SUBJECT TO THE LAW OF GOD?

This question does not differ in sense from the preceding one. For in saying what "the flesh" signifies, it has been made clear; whence what its wisdom is can easily be discerned. We have said, then, that all the elements are called flesh, that is, the substances of visible things, whose reasoning is such that they judge nothing can come to be without a spiritual commixture, shuddering at and deeming foolish the account of a simple power. For these men, who, distrusting spiritual things, follow after carnal things, have no other wisdom and think nothing true except what the reasoning of the elements contains, so that they believe neither that a virgin gave birth nor in the resurrection of the flesh, because the reasoning of the flesh, that is, of the elements, does not admit this—for all things that are begotten in time are generated by the working of a mixture—and that bodies which have died and been dissolved cannot again be restored to life, because each single element, once the mixture has been separated out, returns into its own nature. Puffed up in these ways, they deny that what we believe either will be or has been done, and through this their assertion is an enemy to God, since it deems foolish and empty what God has made and what He has promised He will do.

LIIII.

LIIII = LXXVII

LV. HOW IS IT THAT, WHEN JOHN THE BAPTIST HAD FIRST BORNE WITNESS TO THE SAVIOR, HE AFTERWARD DOUBTED, SAYING THROUGH HIS DISCIPLES: 'ART THOU HE THAT IS TO COME, OR DO WE LOOK FOR ANOTHER?'

Those who think that John doubted disparage the Savior. For either they rightly claim that he doubted, or else they surely charge the Savior with ignorance, since — as they think — he praised a man who thought ill of himself. But since it is impossible for the Savior to be deceived, John was rightly praised by him. If he was rightly praised, he did not doubt; for at the very time when John sent his disciples from prison to Jesus, saying, "Are you the one who is to come, or do we await another?" then Jesus answered his disciples, saying, "Go, tell John what you have seen and heard: the blind see, the deaf hear, the lepers are cleansed, the lame walk, the dead rise again; and blessed is he who has not been scandalized in me." And as John's messengers were departing, Jesus began to speak to the crowds concerning John the Baptist: "What did you go out into the desert to see? A reed shaken by the wind, or a man clothed in soft garments? Behold, those who are clothed in soft things are in the houses of kings. But what did you go out to see? A prophet? I tell you, more than a prophet. This is he of whom it is written: Behold, I send my angel before your face, who shall prepare your way." Then even the tax collectors praised God, having been baptized with the baptism of John.

But how great is the praise given to John, that he was said to be more than a prophet! And the Savior calls blessed the man who is not scandalized in him: how was John being praised if by doubting he was scandalized? But he assuredly did not doubt. For the praise proves that he is blessed, because he was not scandalized. For the Savior also, for this reason, at that very time burst forth into the praise of John, that he might show John's mind to be free from the doubt of his disciples. For John, wishing to strengthen his wavering disciples in the faith of the Savior, knowing that his own end was imminent, wished those things which he was saying about him to be confirmed by his mouth. Therefore, that he might be a true witness, he calls upon the testimony of a greater one, so that by their agreement all ambiguity might be removed. This was John's device: to send his own, as it were with words of doubt, so that, hearing from the Savior the same things which they had heard from John, they might be confirmed, knowing that the testimony of a worthy steward and of the heavenly Lord could not be called into doubt. Therefore the Savior also answered, as it were, John, so that between John questioning and the Savior answering they might learn the truth.

LVI.

LVI = LXXVIII

LVII. WHAT DOES THE APOSTLE MEAN WHEN HE SAYS TO THE GALATIANS: 'I MARVEL THAT YOU ARE SO QUICKLY TRANSFERRED FROM HIM WHO CALLED YOU INTO GRACE, TO ANOTHER GOSPEL, WHICH IS NOT ANOTHER? IF IT IS SURELY ANOTHER, IT IS NOT THE SAME; IF IT IS NOT THE SAME, HOW IS IT NOT ANOTHER?

For this reason he said "another" gospel, to which they had been called, because this is not the one that the Galatians had afterward begun to follow. For, drawn away from the gospel of Christ, they had been dragged into Judaism under the name of Christ, as though this were the thing to be followed, and they affirmed something other than what the apostle preached. Whence it is subjoined: "as they persuade you." For the false apostles, when they were deceiving the believing Gentiles, used to say that what they themselves were teaching had been handed down by the Savior, as is contained in the Acts of the Apostles: that unless you be circumcised after the manner of Moses, you cannot be saved. Hence the apostle, moved by grief at their overthrow, writes to them, saying: I marvel that you are thus so quickly removed from him who called you into grace, unto another gospel, which is not another.

He marvels, then, that after a mild and less burdensome matter they should be zealous for harshness and for a burden — that is, that after the simplicity of faith they should permit themselves to be circumcised in place of the rudiments, and should observe the new moons and the sabbath, being made subject to the rudiments. And lest he should seem to have given occasion to the false apostles, who said that what the Galatians had received was other than what the tradition of the truth holds, he at once subjoined: which is not another, unless there are some who trouble you and wish to pervert the gospel of Christ. It was therefore another gospel, to which they had been called by the apostle, than the one they had begun to follow at the instigation of the false apostles; yet it was not other than what Christ had handed down, so that even by this they might recognize that they had been led into error, and might follow faith alone, professing Christ to be God and that he suffices to grant salvation — he through whom they had obtained the remission of sins, not through the law, because without the law the grace of God bestows pardon for transgressions.

LVIII. SINCE MANY SINS ARE SEEN TO BE COMMITTED AGAINST THE BODY — FOR EVERYONE WHO INFLICTS VIOLENCE ON HIMSELF IN ANY PART OF THE BODY ASSUREDLY SINS AGAINST HIS OWN BODY; FOR THERE IS ONE WHO CASTRATES HIMSELF, THERE IS ONE WHO ENDS HIS LIFE BY A NOOSE, THERE IS ONE WHO THRUSTS THE SWORD INTO HIMSELF —, WHY DOES THE APOSTLE SAY: "EVERY SIN," HE SAYS, "WHATSOEVER A MAN SHALL DO, IS WITHOUT THE BODY; BUT HE THAT FORNICATETH SINNETH AGAINST HIS OWN BODY"?

Do you think the apostle was unskilled, or do you desire the force of the saying to be explained? For the apostle, in saying (body), did not signify the single man, but comprehended together also the woman — because the woman is a portion of the man. For all the above-mentioned crimes, which each one violently commits against himself, do not defile the whole body; for he sins as an individual, and by this he alone becomes guilty. But in fornication the whole body is polluted, because the consent to the contamination holds both the man and the woman: therefore fornication is a grave transgression. For sin is committed most wickedly when someone seeks a companion for his crime unto perdition. For if, as one living well shall receive a reward even for him whom he has gained, then the evil man becomes guilty not only in himself, but also in that other whom he wished to have as a partner of his damnation — how much more he who fornicates, who, it is agreed, in a single transgression contaminates himself twice, in a certain manner! For if he sins against the flesh, which is of himself, he assuredly commits adultery against himself twice.

But if anyone thinks he can transform this question so as to apply this statement either to the Church or to the body of Christ, it will not stand. For it would be said improperly, and he will be like the Novatianists, who, in order to buttress the ground of their fury, declare that the one who fornicates sins not against his own body but against Christ's, because Christians are members and the body of Christ, so that fornication would be the same thing as sacrilege, so that such a person is said to sin against Christ, just as one who denies Christ. But this is indeed weak and fragile, because on whatever side you take it, it gets caught in the snare and does not escape. For if the one who fornicates sins against the body of Christ, then the other sins will not be committed against Christ — as when a Christian kills his brother, or sacrifices to idols, or commits some other kind of sin — because every sin is outside the body except fornication. But if not every sin is outside the body, but whatever it may be is committed against Christ, then the one who steals, or who perjures himself, or who lies, or who strikes his brother, or commits some other such thing, is said to sin against Christ or against the Holy Spirit — which is utterly absurd. And yet the Apostle calls the Church the members and the body of Christ, so that we are members one of another; how then does the one who fornicates sin against his own body and not rather against Christ's? Because, according to the mystery of the Church, we are said to be not our own body but Christ's. And this is far from the point, because the one who fornicates for this reason sins against his own body: since the whole of Adam is contaminated.

LVIIII.

LVIIII = LXXVIIII .

LX. WHY THE APOSTLE PAUL REPROVED PETER, HIS FELLOW APOSTLE, BECAUSE, 'FEARING THOSE WHO WERE OF THE CIRCUMCISION, HE WITHDREW HIMSELF' FROM THE GENTILES, WHEN HE HIMSELF ALSO, FEARING THOSE WHO WERE OF THE CIRCUMCISION, 'TAKING TIMOTHY CIRCUMCISED' HIM, WHICH HE FORBADE TO BE DONE — AND SO MUST HE HIMSELF ALSO BE REPROVED?

It is incredible that so great an apostle reproved another in a matter in which he knew he himself had given way. It is not permissible to believe that this was done by so great a man, because such things befit those who live carnally. The deed of the Apostle Paul is therefore free from reproach. For when he preached that circumcision should not be practiced, yet wishing to take Timothy with him — who had been born of a Jewish mother but a Greek father — and this would be a scandal to the Jews, that he should take up an uncircumcised man born of a Jewish woman, then he submitted himself to the constraint for the moment and, taking him, circumcised him. He therefore did a superfluous thing, with the permission of him who suffered. For because he was of a Jewish mother, instructed from infancy in the synagogue in the sacred letters — that is, in the volumes of the Hebrews — he permitted himself to be circumcised, in order to remove the scandal from the Jews, because they had zeal for their own race. Finally, he says, since Titus was a Greek, he was not compelled to be circumcised. But Timothy, because he was of a Jewish mother, as I said, the Jews would not allow to be taken up among the teachers uncircumcised. For this purpose the Apostle took him up, in order to ordain him bishop, as indeed he did. For, instructed in the prophetic letters, he was found a suitable teacher for asserting Christ.

For the apostle Peter would not have been reprehended if it had been out of fear of the Jews that he separated himself from the Gentiles, so as not to be a stumbling block to them. But this was what was reprehended in the apostle Peter: that, although before, among believing Greeks, he lived in the manner of the Gentiles, yet when Jews came from James, being made subject to fear, he taught that men from among the Gentiles ought to Judaize. Hence he hears: "If you, being a Jew, live in the manner of the Gentiles, how do you compel the Gentiles to Judaize?" For he had caused doubt about the evangelical teaching — which is a crime — while he was destroying what he had built. Therefore the apostle Paul calls this "dissimulation," concerning whom it is agreed that, if he circumcised Timothy, he did not keep silent that he was doing a superfluous thing on account of the stumbling of those men, and that he therefore yielded, because he had a Jewish mother. For the Jews would not have pressed the matter, were it not on account of the occasion of the Jewish mother, since by this discovery they would be able to reprove it before the Gentiles, and would not easily take a stumbling block from these things, seeing that they were not of the stock of Israel. Therefore he did this not by dissimulation, but by constraint; for the aforesaid cause held no small dissimulation. For many were reprehended in this cause, so that some of the Jews and Barnabas consented to this dissimulation.

LXI. WHICH SPIRIT THE APOSTLE ASSERTS IS TO BE SAVED, WHEN HE SAYS THAT A MAN OF THIS SORT IS DELIVERED OVER TO SATAN UNTO THE DESTRUCTION OF THE FLESH?

The destruction of the flesh is when someone, given over to carnal pleasures and desires, procures hell for himself; for he acquires destruction from the work of the flesh. For from these things the whole man becomes carnal, because, just as a man living according to the law is made wholly spiritual, so that even the flesh is called that which the soul is, so, if he lives according to the desires of the world and the pleasures of lusts, the whole man becomes flesh, advancing unto the destruction of the flesh; that is, just as the flesh, because it is fluid and corruptible and mortal, when the soul is lost is deformed — lacking form, it dissolves into destruction; for it perishes when it loses the vigor of all its members —, so also the soul is deformed by such a body, by whose vigor it had been despised in the punishment together with the flesh, in which it defiled itself with vices, so that there is no advancement for the flesh through the soul, but the soul has destruction through the flesh, because it did not govern the body spiritually, though it was given as queen to the flesh, but it itself teaches carnal works.

Therefore, when the Church casts out such a man, it keeps its spirit saved, namely the holy one, which is the Church's caretaker, because, if they should permit such a man to be among them — one who has polluted his father's wife with lust —, so that they might be able by the law to compel him, lest he contaminate all, and the Holy Spirit should depart from the Church on account of the polluted one, the Church will not keep the Spirit saved. For what is lost is not saved even on the day of the Lord. Stripped of the Spirit, they cannot be found reckoned among the sons of God. For the Spirit bears witness that we are the sons of God.

This is what the apostle says to the people, because as yet bishops had not been ordained for the churches in all places. Therefore he orders the people to do this, which he himself would have done if a bishop had been appointed there, namely, that acting together they should cast that man out of their assembly, lest they should seem to consent to his deeds, because whoever does not rebuke him whom he can rebuke, but receives him as though he were not erring, supplies him with kindling for going on offending — and by this cause he too is defiled, putting the Holy Spirit to flight from himself.

For to the Thessalonians he says in like manner, among other things: May your spirit be kept whole, and your soul and body, without complaint at the coming of our Lord. The very same meaning is here; for this is what it is to be 'safe,' namely to be 'whole.' For the king says to Daniel: Are the seals safe, Daniel? And he answered: They are safe, O king, that is, 'whole' — so that our spirit may be whole for us, when it does not desert us. For those whom the spirit deserts will no longer be whole in the matter of regeneration, because they do not have him through whom they were called sons of God. Nor is it contrary to this if we say that the spirit itself deserts us when we sin and is not whole. For it is not whole precisely while it deserts us — it who was our leader and ruler — because, if he is our head, we too are his members. But when we do anything against him, it is not he that deserts us, but we who seem to desert him; and then he seems not to be whole, when he loses us. For this is true and manifest, that it is not he who turns himself away from us, but we from him, when we sin. And to the Colossians he says among other things: from whom the whole body, supplied and knit together, grows with the increase of the Lord — which, if we take it according to the sound of the words, does not fit — for God lacks nothing that he should make increase through us — but rather, when we return to the author of our life and confess him, the Godhead acquires us unto salvation and makes increase in us; therefore in those who withdraw from him there is loss. This, then, is to be not whole, which is also to suffer loss.

LXII. WHY THE SAVIOR SAYS, 'PRAY THAT YOUR FLIGHT BE NOT IN WINTER OR ON THE SABBATH,' SINCE THE TIME OF THIS PERSECUTION CANNOT BE DEFERRED, THE APOSTLE SAYING, 'WHO WILL BE REVEALED IN HIS OWN TIME,' AND IN THE ACTS OF THE APOSTLES: 'DETERMINING,' HE SAYS, 'THE TIMES AND THE BOUNDS OF THEIR HABITATION'? AND WHY IS IT NOT PERMITTED TO FLEE IN WINTER OR TO GO OUT ON THE SABBATH [DOES IT SIGNIFY]?

In winter flight is not safe; for there are frosts, constant rains, it snows, it freezes, the rivers overflow; and therefore for those fleeing it is exceedingly hard. For they cannot lie hidden in the woods, nor in the mountains, nor in caves. But on the Sabbath, according to the Jews, it is not permitted to go out from the city a great distance, nor to climb high, and on this account you cannot flee on the Sabbath. And just as these seasons do not make flight safe on account of the impediments mentioned above, so also our flight will not be safe, if the aforesaid persecution finds us bound by fleshly impediments. For worldly desires and mundane resources hold men fast like fetters, and they cannot escape the seduction of the devil. Therefore, then, it must be prayed that at the time when we must flee, the condition of winter and of the Sabbath be not found in us, but that the help of God may render us free from these impediments, so that there be nothing that, having taken us captive by desire of itself, may consign us to the world. Since therefore the Savior was speaking of the last persecution, which is to come in the time of Antichrist, he therefore set down winter, which is the last season; and likewise the Sabbath, which is the last day, so that, just as in these seasons flight is harsh and difficult, so he might signify that in that time the persecutions and afflictions to come would be so grievous that scarcely anyone could escape them.

LXIII.

LXIII = LXXX

LXIIII.

LXIIII = LXXXI

LXV. WHAT DOES IT MEAN THAT THE APOSTLE SAYS: 'NO ONE CAN SAY THE LORD JESUS EXCEPT IN THE HOLY SPIRIT'? THEREFORE CAN PHOTINUS, WHO DENIES THAT CHRIST IS GOD, CONFESS OUR LORD JESUS CHRIST IN THE HOLY SPIRIT? AND CAN MARCION AND MANICHEUS, WHO DENY THAT CHRIST CAME IN THE FLESH, THESE MEN SAY THE LORD JESUS CHRIST IN THE HOLY SPIRIT? OR HARLOTS AND FILTHY MEN, SINCE THE HOLY SPIRIT DOES NOT DWELL IN A BODY SUBJECT TO SINS AND WISDOM DOES NOT ENTER INTO A MALEVOLENT SOUL?

Truth is neither proved nor disproved from the person; for whatever is spoken well and truly is without doubt spoken in the Holy Spirit. Therefore, if someone is guilty in other matters, it ought not on that account to be held against him in this, that he speaks the truth. Nor yet is it held against him, but rather Christ the Lord is contradicted. For he who denies that this man speaks the truth does injury to Christ. But if the truth is spoken, and it is denied that what is true is spoken in the Holy Spirit — which Christ also admits (for no good man refutes one who speaks true things about himself; nor indeed is that learned by revelation which is learned through tradition) — no one speaking the truth will be able to be disproved.

LXVI. IF NO ONE IS JUSTIFIED IN THE LAW BEFORE GOD, WHY IS IT WRITTEN: 'CURSED IS EVERYONE WHO HAS NOT CONTINUED IN ALL THINGS THAT ARE WRITTEN IN THE BOOK OF THE LAW, TO DO THEM'? IF MEN ARE JUSTIFIED BY FAITH AND NOT THROUGH THE LAW, WHY IS HE CURSED WHO HAS NOT FULFILLED THE LAW, SINCE IT DOES NOT AVAIL FOR RIGHTEOUSNESS?

The Law given through Moses did indeed possess a righteousness, but an earthly one, so that those keeping the Law might be righteous for the present, that they might not become guilty. For he is righteous under the Law who harms no one. Hence the Apostle: The Law, he says, is not of faith, but he who does these things shall live in them — that is, the one doing the Law shall not die, but shall live for the present; but the righteousness which is of faith justifies men before God, so that they may be rewarded in the age to come. For to the temporal righteousness they added the righteousness of faith. For it is right to know from whom and through whom we are, so that the true confession of the Father and of the Son and of the Holy Spirit may lead us on to the heavenly kingdoms; for even the ancients, who not only kept the Law of the commandments but also loved God, having hope in the promise, have been justified before God. For he says that the Law alone does not justify men before God, just as neither does faith alone; excepting good works, it commends to God, but when righteousness both earthly and divine has been kept, it makes them perfect. Hence the Lord says: Unless your righteousness shall abound more than that of the scribes and Pharisees, you shall not enter into the kingdom of heaven.

LXVII. IF IN CHRIST ALL THE TREASURES OF WISDOM AND KNOWLEDGE ARE HIDDEN, HOW IS IT THAT THE SAME SAVIOR SAYS CONCERNING THE DAY AND HOUR OF THE FUTURE JUDGMENT THAT HE DOES NOT KNOW IT? BUT IF HE KNOWS IT AND SAYS THAT HE DOES NOT KNOW IT, HE SEEMS TO LIE.

This saying contains a twofold sense. For he says that all the treasures of wisdom and knowledge are hidden in Christ in such a way that whoever has Christ in place of all wisdom and knowledge yields to him, so that to know Christ is to know the whole and to have comprehended all wisdom, as he says to the Colossians: Let no one circumvent you through philosophy and empty seduction according to the elements of this world and not according to Christ, because in him dwells all the fullness of divinity bodily — that is, that he who believes in Christ lacks nothing for salvation, but that this abounds to him, because the fullness of divinity is in his faith. And again in the same epistle he says among other things: Let no one seduce you in humiliation of heart and in the superstition of angels, which he sees, exalting himself, vainly puffed up in the mind of his flesh and not holding the head, from which the whole body, supplied and brought forth, grows into the increase of God. If therefore those believing in Christ venerate the head of every principality and power, they lack nothing, but have all that is needful for salvation; it will in no way harm them not to know the lower things, which contribute nothing to salvation, when they know that which is necessary. In this manner, therefore, those are to be said to know the whole who know that which profits them unto salvation.

There is also this point: that in the Savior all the treasures of wisdom and knowledge are hidden, for all the mysteries of the Father are known to him, and he himself measures every creature; and the Father judges no one, but has given all judgment to the Son, and no one knows what is in God except the Spirit of God, which is also the Spirit of Christ. How, then, is he to be said not to know the day and hour of the coming judgment—he whose Spirit knows the things that are in God, and of whom he said that it "received of his own"? If, then, the one who received of his own knows the things that are to come, how does Christ, who sent us the Holy Spirit, not know them? And can it be that the judge does not know the day of the coming judgment, seeing that he has given all the signs of when it is to be expected? Perhaps too these may be said truly not to be known—those to whom he will say: "Amen I say to you, I know you not." Nor will the foolish and improvident virgins be said not to be known, because he said also—when they themselves said: "Lord, Lord, open to us"—: "Amen I say to you, I know you not." There is, then, a reason why he says he does not know what he does know. Finally, thus he says: "Watch therefore, because you know not at what hour your Lord will come." In order, then, that he might make us anxious and watchful, when questioned he said he did not know the day and hour of the coming judgment, but so that it might profit us, he denied that he knew what he does know. Since, then, we are ignorant, what does it profit us—for we want to know that which, if we know it, will do us harm—but Christ, the provider of our salvation, lest we should hear what would be against us, said that he did not know. Surely, if to someone set in pain and demanding a sword, that he might destroy himself, the one who has it should answer that he does not have it, he will not be liable to be charged with lying for denying it—since, if he did not deny it, he would know the opposite?

LXVIII.

LXVIII = LXXXII

LXVIIII. THE SAVIOR SAYS: "WHOEVER DOES NOT LEAVE ALL HIS POSSESSIONS," THAT IS, HOUSE AND FIELD AND THE REST, "CANNOT BE MY DISCIPLE"; AND ON THE CONTRARY THE EVANGELIST SAYS: "THERE CAME," HE SAYS, "JOSEPH OF ARIMATHEA, A RICH MAN, WHO ALSO HIMSELF WAS A DISCIPLE OF JESUS, WAITING FOR THE KINGDOM OF GOD": HOW IS IT THAT THE EVANGELIST DECLARES HIM A DISCIPLE, WHOM THE SAVIOR DENIED? FOR ZACCHAEUS TOO WAS RICH, AND CORNELIUS THE CENTURION WAS RICH, AND THE WOMEN WHO MINISTERED TO HIM OUT OF THEIR OWN MEANS.

The apostle resolves this meaning in a few words, saying: let those who have be as though not having, and those who use this world as though they used it not, and those who buy as though not possessing. Whoever, then, has these things in such a way as though he had them not, is seen to have left them. For when he does not presume upon them nor boast himself, but walks humbly not only in bearing but also in mind, understanding that he has been given as a steward and minister of them, he is seen to have left all his possessions. For this is said to be left, which withdraws from desire and holds no favor.

LXX. HOW IS IT THAT THE SAVIOR SAYS TO MARY, WHO WISHES TO TOUCH HIM OUT OF JOY: "TOUCH ME NOT; FOR I HAVE NOT YET ASCENDED TO THE FATHER," WHEN IT IS READ THAT BY THE OTHER WOMEN HE WAS BOTH TOUCHED AND ADORED?

They are the words of one indignant, saying: Touch me not. For although she longed for the Savior, yet, while others believed in his resurrection, she stood weeping at the tomb, when she ought already to have rejoiced. For she had heard from the apostles John and Peter that the Lord had risen. For thus you have it: and he saw the linen cloths and the napkin in one place, and he believed; for as yet they did not know the Scriptures, that it behooved him to rise again from the dead. But Mary, because she had not seen him risen, did not believe. For her excessive love made her doubt. In short: those whom we love, if they suffer some misfortune, we do not believe that they escape it; but those whom we hate, if they are already near death, it is incredible to us. To Mary therefore, since she was stunned and was suffering grief through love — she who ought to have followed the faith of the disciples — the Lord first showed himself, that she might cease from grief, yet not without indignation. Hence she hears: Touch me not, that is: 'You are following the carnal sense; abstain from it and follow the spiritual things, which are not seen.' For, he says, I have not yet ascended to the Father.

This is like that saying of John the Baptist sending his disciples to Jesus and saying: Go and say: John the Baptist has sent us to you, saying, Are you he who is to come, or do we look for another? Under his own person he pleads the cause of his disciples. For John did not doubt, he who had said: Behold the Lamb of God, behold him who takes away the sin of the world; but in order to satisfy his disciples, he sends them with his own words, so that he might confirm by the Savior's mouth what he himself was teaching about him, so that after his departure his disciples might follow Christ without any doubt. So also the Savior reproves Mary in himself, saying: I have not yet ascended to the Father, that is: 'Your heart is still pressed to the ground, you who, if you do not see, do not believe.' For if she had lifted up her heart to God, she would have believed with the disciples that the Lord had risen.

LXXI. WHAT DOES IT MEAN THAT THE SAVIOR SAYS: 'I AM THE DOOR; THOSE WHO CAME BEFORE ME ARE THIEVES AND ROBBERS'? WHICH SAYING SEEMS TO STRIKE AT THE PROPHETS.

Because no one goes to God the Father except through faith in Christ, therefore the Savior calls himself the door of the kingdom of heaven. If then he uses the words of a door, what need was there for him to say concerning those before: as many as came before me, since no question was being raised about the past, but rather, as a present door, he was complaining about present matters? For the Savior speaks of the Jews, who thought to enter into the kingdom of God not through faith, but through the righteousness of the law. For coming before the Savior, that is, standing before him, they disparaged him, contradicting him, so as to turn others also away from faith in him, whence they were called thieves and robbers. For at that time these things were said, when the Pharisees were disputing with him who had been born blind, to whom the Savior restored the eyes which nature had denied, whom they wished to turn away from faith in the Savior, saying that it could not be through this one, because he violates the sabbath, but that the kingdom of God is entered through the righteousness of the law. Then the Savior said: I am the door. If anyone enters through me, he shall be saved and shall find pasture; but if anyone wishes to enter by another way, he is a thief and a robber.

How does this pertain to the person of the prophets? Were the prophets thieves and robbers? Did the doctrine of the prophets concerning Christ say that one could earn God's favor through the righteousness of the law without faith — the prophets who knew that Christ would come in the flesh? Therefore he said this about those present, so that whoever existed before him, whether standing or sitting, are to be understood as thieves and robbers. For those who called that man aside, the one who had been blind, wished to turn him away from faith in the Savior, saying: Give honor to God; we know that this man is a sinner — men whom he refuted, saying: From the beginning of the world it was never heard that anyone opened the eyes of one born blind. If this man were not from God, he could do nothing. Concerning this man, then, because he persevered in the faith, and concerning those who said: These words are not those of one who has a demon; can a demon open the eyes of the blind? — the Savior said: But the sheep did not hear them, namely those whom he called thieves and robbers. For how did the sheep not hear the prophets, when it is established in the Scriptures that all the good obey the prophets and the evil obey the false prophets?

LXXII. THE APOSTLE SAYS THAT CHRIST DIED FOR ALL; FOR HE SPEAKS THUS: 'THEREFORE ALL ARE DEAD, AND HE DIED FOR ALL', BUT THE LORD HIMSELF SAYS: 'THE SON OF MAN CAME TO GIVE HIS LIFE AS A REDEMPTION FOR MANY': IN THIS WAY THERE IS A CONTRADICTION.

The words indeed are different, but the sense is one, just as again the same words have a different sense, as for instance: that which is not of faith is sin, and: the law, he says, is not of faith, and yet it is not sin. Those, then, whom the Savior called (many), these the Apostle designated as 'all.' For they are many, since the greatest part out of every people and nation is going to believe in the Savior. These believers the Apostle called (all); for so among other things he says: and he died for all, namely for those who have believed and are going to believe. He also died even for those who are unwilling to believe; but while they do not receive the benefit, they are unwilling that Christ should have died for them, and on this account he does not seem to have died for those to whom he profited nothing but rather did harm. But he is rightly said to have died for those for whom the victory has been won, who, understanding the mystery, give thanks to God through Christ our Lord.

LXXIII. HOW IS IT THAT DAVID SAYS: 'LET THEM BE CONFOUNDED AND ASHAMED WHO SAY TO ME: 'AHA, AHA', BUT THE LORD SAYS, 'WELL DONE, GOOD AND FAITHFUL SERVANT', AND THE REST? THAT WHICH DAVID TOOK AS A REPROACH AND AN EVIL, THIS THE SAVIOR SHOWS TO BE WORTHY OF GOOD MERITS?

This does not differ greatly from the earlier question. For one and the same word has different meanings, as I mentioned above. For the detractors of David, since they favored his ruin, rejoiced at his misfortunes. For while he was suffering pressures and distresses from Saul and from his son Absalom, they said that it was rightly and well done. It is like when someone says of a man who suffers wrongs: 'It is well for him this, to rejoice in evils' — that is to say: 'It is rightly done to him; for so he deserves.' In a similar way the Savior too, glad at good works, pronounces a man's merit, saying: Well done, good and faithful servant. For he exults, because the servant made himself more worthy, saying: 'well,' that is: 'you have done rightly, so as to lay up a greater merit.' Therefore one and the same word is fitted to two contrary cases. For we are accustomed to say of someone: 'he has been sentenced,' but in what sense it is said, the cause either following or foregoing makes clear; for sometimes it is said toward good, sometimes toward evil, just as desire also is sometimes fitted to good, sometimes to evil.

LXXIIII.

LXXIIII = XCVIIII

LXXV. THE APOSTLE CALLS THE LAW GIVEN BY MOSES HOLY AND JUST AND GOOD AND SPIRITUAL, AND ELSEWHERE HE SAYS: 'FOR THE LAW WORKS WRATH' AND: 'WHERE THERE IS NO LAW, NEITHER IS THERE TRANSGRESSION.' FOR TO BE WITHOUT THESE IS SECURITY.

By comparing the sayings themselves you ought to have satisfied yourself sufficiently. For doubters are wont to make questions in such a way that the sayings of one man — and one whom it is not lawful to reprehend — are sought out as conflicting and hostile. For if the apostle calls the law holy and just and good and spiritual, a man preeminent in all praise and on every side fortified with truth, this would suffice for faith; and concerning that which seems contrary, inquiry ought to have been made without obstinacy.

For this proposition wishes for impunity in sinning. For the apostle indeed, when he preferred the law of faith on account of the merit of the time and grace among the Romans, who under the faith of Christ had been led into the law — 'into the law' I say, not that which is natural (for the Romans were indeed under the law which the ten men sent from Athens, and afterward two others, had brought, which was written on two tables, which were buried in the Capitol), but that same one which is called the law 'of works,' that they should be circumcised, observe the sabbath and the new moons, distinguish foods, be anxious about the cleansing of vessels, and the rest which are contained in a law of this kind — this law, then, he says works wrath. For on this account these things were added by an angry God, that they might be a burden to the perfidy of the Jews; for out of so many things it is necessary that something be passed over. Whence the apostle Peter says: Why do you impose a yoke upon the neck of the brethren, which neither our fathers nor we were able to bear? Finally God says through Jeremiah the prophet: I gave them precepts that were not good. For after many offenses and temptings against God and insults done to Moses and disparagements made concerning God, these things were given, which they should observe, that their hard neck might be pressed down and, wherever they turned, they might have the law, so that there might be no refreshment for them.

So in order to remove them from this law, the apostle says: where there is no law, neither is there transgression, so that being untroubled they might be able to serve God spiritually by a shortcut, "(because)," he says, "those who keep the law of the Jews must necessarily transgress; for so many are the precepts that it is impossible to fulfill them."

For how could he say this about that law which is natural? — the law which Moses wrote for this reason, that he might confirm its authority, not because it did not exist before, since we read that vengeance was taken before Moses — but rather that he might set forth that the Romans ought no longer to live under the law, whom it was expedient to conduct themselves under law; for otherwise they could not preserve righteousness. Finally he says to them: do you wish not to fear the power? Do good; and: those who despise it acquire damnation for themselves.

Therefore he calls this law spiritual, which he also shows to be holy and just and good. This is the law which we have called natural, which forbids sinning; for it is the guide of a good life. To this, when the law of faith is added, it makes a person perfect. For because both this law, which I have called natural, and that law, which is called the law of works, are designated by a common name, the apostle seems to speak against the law; <but> lest he should seem to nullify this law, without which this life cannot be governed, he says: we know that the law is spiritual, so that it might be understood as distinct from that law which he nullifies. that the law of the sabbath and of circumcision and of foods and of new moons was to be understood. For that law is called spiritual for this reason, because it lays aside all sins.

LXXVI. IF THE JEWS RECEIVED HARSH COMMANDMENTS ON ACCOUNT OF THEIR DISTRUST AND DISPARAGEMENTS, WHAT HAD THEIR SONS SINNED, THAT THEY TOO SHOULD BE BOUND BY THESE COMMANDMENTS?

The law was given to the fathers, which would be a like burden to the sons. For afterward they sinned against God not less, but perhaps more, than their fathers. For God, being foreknowing, gave this law rather to the sons, because those others all perished in the wilderness.

LXXVII. WHEN THE SAVIOR SAYS: 'I PRAY FOR THEM. FOR THOSE WHOM YOU HAVE GIVEN ME, I DO NOT PRAY FOR THE WORLD', THE APOSTLE JOHN, ON THE CONTRARY, SAYS: 'WE HAVE AN ADVOCATE WITH THE FATHER, AN INTERCESSOR FOR OUR SINS, AND NOT ONLY FOR OURS, BUT FOR THE WHOLE WORLD': THIS SEEMS CONTRADICTORY.

Although the sense does not differ greatly, nevertheless what the Savior says is one thing, and what the apostle John affirms is another. For the Savior prays for his disciples, that they may be kept safe from the assault of the evil one. Finally he says thus: I do not pray that you take them out of the world, but that you keep them from evil. The apostle John, however, introduces another kind of prayer; for he says: we have an advocate, who asks for our sins and for those of the whole world. For Christians, therefore, he asks in a twofold manner, because he both prays for their sins and that they may be kept safe from the assault of the devil. But for those who do not believe, this is the only entreaty: that, when they deserve to be punished for their sins and unbelief, the goodness and patience of God may wait for them, in case they should perhaps be willing to be corrected. For he does not ask that the sins be pardoned to those who distrust, but that they may receive a long delay, so that, if they should amend themselves, their offenses may be forgiven them. This, therefore, the Savior asks for them, as we have said.

LXXVIII. IN THE GOSPEL WE READ THAT THE ANGEL SAYS TO MARY THE MOTHER OF THE LORD THAT 'OF HIS KINGDOM', THAT IS, CHRIST'S, 'THERE SHALL BE NO END', AND DANIEL SPEAKS OF THIS IN THE SAME SENSE; FOR HE SAYS: THEN SHALL ARISE AN ETERNAL KINGDOM, WHICH SHALL NEVER BE DESTROYED'. AGAINST THIS THE APOSTLE SAYS OF THE LORD: 'WHEN HE SHALL HAVE DELIVERED UP', HE SAYS, 'THE KINGDOM TO GOD AND THE FATHER'. HOW WILL HE HAVE AN ETERNAL KINGDOM, WHICH HE IS SAID TO BE ABOUT TO DELIVER UP TO GOD AND THE FATHER?

In the case of the Father and the Son, that is, of God and Christ, a handing over is understood, not an abolition. For consider in what manner the Father handed over the kingdom to the Son, the Lord himself attesting this very thing: for all things, he says, have been handed over to me by my Father. If therefore the Father ceased to have it when he handed it over, it can also be said of the Savior that, when he handed it over, he lost it. For the Son, after he said that the kingdom had been handed over to him by the Father, still preferring the Father, says: but this is eternal life, that they may know thee the only and true God, and Jesus Christ whom thou hast sent. According to this sense, then, the Son too does not lose the kingdom when he hands it over, so that these things may be true, and the angel and Daniel are said not to be at variance with this sense. For whoever thinks that the testimony of these ought to be disparaged is full of faithlessness.

But now let us see, remaining in this faith, what it is for the Son to hand over the kingdom to the Father; for this is what, among other things, the Apostle says in another place: then shall the Son himself also be subject to him who put all things under him, that God may be all in all. This subjection, therefore, is itself the handing over of the kingdom. And so in this place an interpretation seems necessary, so that both the Son may not be denied to be subject to the Father, and nonetheless he may be said to have an eternal kingdom, that is, the kingdom of the Son, because in his name every knee is bowed, of things heavenly, earthly, and infernal. This also the Apostle Peter affirms, saying: there is no other name given under heaven, in which we must be saved. And the Lord himself says to his disciples: hitherto you have not asked anything in my name: ask, and I will do it. Thus the Son reigns, when all things are saved in his name and the things that are asked are given, even to the end of the world.

But after all things shall have confessed Christ, whether by force or by will, and shall have been laid beneath the power of him whom they had resisted, the mystery of the one God shall be made manifest to all, and every giving of thanks shall be referred to the Father, from whom are all things, so that, preaching having ceased, the one God may be known in the mystery of the Trinity. For when all powers and principalities and dominions shall have bowed the knee to Christ, then the Son shall make manifest that it is not he from whom are all things, but the Son of him, and that he is seen in himself. This is the subjection and the handing over of the kingdom. For in showing that it is the Father from whom are all things, he subjects himself to him, when he says that he is from him. For so great a brightness and majesty shall be seen at the coming of the Son, that all the powers and choirs of angels shall think this one to be the singular God. But the Savior, when he shall have said that he is not the one who is called the Father, but his Son, while reigning hands over the kingdom to the Father. In this matter is made manifest both the subjection and the handing over of the kingdom, because, when he professes that he is from the Father, he confesses that whatever he has is the Father's, referring the whole sum to him.

LXXVIIII.

LXXVIIII = LXXXIII

LXXX. WHAT NEED WAS THERE FOR THE APOSTLE, IN REBUKING THE GALATIANS, TO INSERT THIS — FOR, TO THE GALATIANS WHO WERE TAKING REFUGE IN THE LAW AFTER THE GOSPEL, HE SAYS AMONG OTHER THINGS: 'FOR A MEDIATOR IS NOT OF ONE, BUT GOD IS ONE' —, AS THOUGH THE GALATIANS WERE DENYING THE ONE GOD, SEEING THAT THEY HAD BEEN LED INTO THE LAW ON THIS VERY GROUND, THAT THEY SHOULD BELIEVE IN A GOD WHO IS ONE AND SINGULAR, BUT THAT CHRIST HAD COME MERELY AS A MINISTER BY PREDESTINATION TO DISPENSE THE GIFT OF GOD'S GRACE?

It is clear that the Galatians had been led into the law in such a way that they also professed Christ, but not as is fitting, but after the manner of Photinus: namely, that because the law proclaims one God, it is against the law if Christ be called God. This is a contrivance of the Jews, who, overcome by the mighty works of his powers, believed in Christ after the cross, yet in such a way that, while professing Christ, they nevertheless kept the law, as though not all hope were to be placed in Christ. These are they whom the apostle calls false brethren. These men, then, after the tradition of the apostles, had corrupted the understanding of the Galatians, so as to make them Jews under the name of the Savior. Hence the apostle says that Christ came as an arbiter between circumcision and uncircumcision, as he says to the Ephesians: making peace in one new man. If therefore Christ came as an arbiter, and an arbiter makes peace between two parties in such a way that he lays down a new rule, taking away from both their professions, he reconciles them so that, following his verdict, they may lose their former discord. For while the Jew champions circumcision and the Gentile uncircumcision, discord arises; but when this is taken away, peace comes about.

'If, then, these things are so, how is it,' he says, 'that you, O Galatians, having become Jews, corrupt the arbitration of Christ?' Therefore he said to them: you have been made void of Christ. For, spurning the right of reconciliation which he had established between them, they turned back to the former understanding of the Jews, accusing both themselves and the reconciler; for everything that displeases is accused: and because this matter had compelled the Galatians to turn to the law, so as to confess one God without the mystery, as though it were hostile to the law if Christ were called God, he says: an arbiter, that is, a mediator, is not of one — but assuredly of two; but you, having turned to the law, have refused the arbiter —, God nevertheless is one.' By saying this he testifies that he does not proclaim Christ as God in such a way as to make another God or to profess two, but that God is one, as was also said in the law, because, when Christ is proclaimed as God from God, it does not tend toward another God, since that which is from God does not allow itself to be called another God. For whether it be God, or that which is from God, God is one. For there is assuredly no difference between God and that which is from God; yet he is another. For he is another with respect to the person by which he is named Son, but the same with respect to the unity of substance. Lest it seem blameworthy that I have said 'person'; for some are wont to reject it, but let us follow the apostle who says: for what I have granted, if I have granted anything, it was for your sake in the person of Christ.

LXXXI. WHEN THE SAVIOR SAID THAT HE WOULD SUFFER AND WOULD RISE AGAIN AFTER THE THIRD DAY, HE ADDED: 'AND AFTER I HAVE RISEN, I WILL GO BEFORE YOU INTO GALILEE; THERE YOU WILL SEE ME'. AND THE ANGEL SAID THE SAME TO THE WOMEN. YET HE IS FOUND TO HAVE BEEN SEEN BY THE WOMEN AND BY HIS DISCIPLES IN JERUSALEM.

To call the words of the Savior into question is the mark of an unbeliever, but I see that you do not doubt the Savior's words but seek their meaning: why, when he was seen in Jerusalem after his passion, he said that he would be seen by them in Galilee. In Jerusalem he was indeed seen, but piecemeal, to restore the spirits of the disciples; in Galilee, however, by all of them together. Therefore, to those who were going to see him in parts in Jerusalem, he appointed Galilee, so that he might appear to all at once and give precepts for setting the discipline in order.

LXXXII. THE APOSTLE PAUL INDICATES THAT ONE MUST NOT BE GRIEVED OVER THE DEAD; FOR IT BELONGS TO THOSE WITHOUT HOPE TO GRIEVE. YET HE HIMSELF SAYS OF EPAPHRODITUS: 'HE WAS SICK,' HE SAYS, 'NEAR TO DEATH. BUT GOD HAD MERCY ON HIM; AND NOT ON HIM ONLY, BUT ON ME ALSO, LEST I SHOULD HAVE SORROW UPON SORROW'. HOW DOES HE FORBID GRIEVING, WHEN HE HIMSELF DECLARES THAT HE WOULD HAVE GRIEVED, IF EPAPHRODITUS HAD DIED?

The Apostle indicated that he would have grieved over the death of Epaphroditus, had it come to pass, in one way, and he forbids grieving in another way. For here, on account of the comfort of his help, which he was making use of in the gospel, he said that he would have grieved if he had died; but he forbids us to grieve in such a way that we should not mourn the departed as though they were extinguished and lost, despairing of the resurrection. It is therefore one thing to seek comfort as for one absent, and another to grieve over the destruction of one who no longer will be. In the former case consolation ceases; in the latter despair is shut out.

LXXXIII. HOW IS IT THAT, ALTHOUGH THE APOSTLES HEALED ALL THE SICK BROUGHT TO THEM, THEY NEVERTHELESS GAVE NO REMEDY TO THEIR OWN SICK? FOR SURELY EPAPHRODITUS WOULD NOT HAVE BEEN SICK EVEN TO THE POINT OF DEATH, IF THE APOSTLE'S INTERCESSION HAD BEEN HEARD. FOR WHO WOULD DOUBT THAT THE APOSTLE OFTEN PRAYED FOR THIS AND DID NOT OBTAIN IT? FOR IF HE HAD BEEN HEARD, THE SICKNESS WOULD IMMEDIATELY HAVE BEEN TAKEN AWAY.

Signs and mighty works were therefore performed by the apostles in order that unbelief might be compelled to faith, so that, seeing deeds which are impossible for men, they might recognize from this that the preaching was of God and might prove by the mighty work that there is reason in faith; so that, since words are always contradicted, a mighty work was set forth as a witness, and when words yield to it, it signifies that there is in faith a reason which cannot be expressed in words. Among the faithful, therefore, signs and wonders are no longer necessary, but rather a firm hope. For after it is learned that the things which have been promised are true, the mind arms itself for this, that, despising present things, it may prove itself worthy of things to come, laying up merit for itself through toil — as Solomon testifies, who says: Son, when you come to the service of God, stand in righteousness and fear, and prepare your soul for temptation. Because a man makes progress through temptations, these admonitions have been given. Whence the apostle says: Through tribulations we must enter into the kingdom of God. These temptations of tribulations happen in various ways, so that the mind, persevering in the faith it has received, may be crowned. For a man is tempted not only in faith, but also in sickness and in loss and in persecution and in the death of those dear to him, so that, if in these sufferings he has not been led away to the aid of demons, he may earn martyrdom without the price of blood. Not, therefore, to the harm of Epaphroditus did the apostle fail to obtain what he asked, but to his advantage, because in his own case too, when he had made his request, he heard: My grace is sufficient for you; for strength is made perfect in weakness.

XCII. ON THE ETERNITY OF THE SON.

Since there are certain persons who, with the darkness of error not yet dispelled, and with the rot of a lethargic life neither purged nor wiped away, judging by an earthly understanding, doubt either concerning the Son of God or that two are one — for the treacherous and stubborn nation of the Jews, to whom the fountain of the law and the treasuries of the sacraments lay open, does not waver, but denies it with wicked utterances — I shall set forth briefly, as far as a more compressed treatment allows. For hope is left whole to the ignorant, if they are willing to learn; but perpetual punishment is appointed for those who dissemble.

God, the almighty, since he is more excellent in greatness and goodness, was bound to bring forth from himself and to display something great and supremely good; yet, if he had brought forth anything that fell short of the measure of his own excellence, he would seem either to have been unable to do more — which does not apply to one who is almighty — or to have been unwilling — which does not apply to one who is most kind. Assuredly it would have been no great thing for the most high God to have made something which, being placed within himself, was not to possess the force of his full supremacy. Above himself, then, there was nothing — for there is nothing that surpasses God —; below himself would have been too little, because what is less than the greatest was not fitting. And so, creating his Son most like himself, he brought forth from himself as it were a second self, and thus that highest and greatest good, which is supplied to us from God, overflowed to us through the propagation of him to us.

Nay, this reasoning too must at the same time be understood: that God, who founded this world — who among the other marvels of beauty and ordered arrangement also bestowed on the nature of things the power of creating, for the bringing forth of the offspring of each one's own kind — himself certainly first possessed what he gave. For no one gives what he does not himself have. He, therefore, who had a Son corresponding to himself in every part of likeness — that is, being one he had an only one, being upright an upright one, being good a kindly one, being blessed a blessed one, being greatest a greatest one, being everlasting an eternal one — certainly possessed, before the world, the sovereign primacy of his begotten offspring, from which he imparted the pattern to things coming into being, so that the shoots of things begetting were constrained to answer to their own seeds. Nor indeed did lawful order allow it otherwise than that he who was to be the father of things ought first to be a father in his own right — that is, of his own offspring.

But when we said that the Son was brought forth, it is not to be understood as though he had a birth like ours and was born in the way we see mortals born, when, with the offspring bursting forth, things come into existence which before were not. For those things, conceived from seeds implanted from elsewhere, coalesce out of the diversity of the elements into the assembly of their own kind's form; but here, divinely, with no aid of external admixture, he drew out and brought forth from himself that which he had within himself and with himself always. For, just as, if we say that light is born from the sun, because it proceeds and flows forth from it, we by no means say it in such a way as though the sun could at some time be, or ever was, without light, so that it begot the light at some time later than itself, but rather in the sense that, that which it has always held with itself, it — namely light begotten of itself without any distinction of a beginning that was commenced — since it always shines, always illuminates by it out of itself as by a renewing gift: so the Son of God, cleaving to the Father from eternity, held this likeness of him too, that he has neither a rising nor a setting. And as God is everlasting without origin, so also the Son is everlasting; and as God is one, simple and the same, so is he so commingled with the Father that, though distinct in names and in relations, they are nevertheless the same, one in both and each in the other, continuous in inseparable majesty.

God therefore so contains and embraces within himself the Son — not drawn from discordant materials, but brought forth from the simple and single stock and seed-plot of his own nature, always held within himself and never let go from himself by any separation — that nothing in the two is changed from that unity.
